DISCLAIMER Care has been taken to confirm the accuracy of the information presented
and to describe generally accepted practices. However, the authors, editors, and publisher
are not responsible for errors or omissions or for any consequences from application of the
information in this book and make no warranty, expressed or implied, with respect to
the currency, completeness, or accuracy of the contents of the publication. Application
of the information in a particular situation remains the professional responsibility of the
practitioner.
The authors, editors, and publisher have exerted every effort to ensure that drug
selection and dosage set forth in this text are in accordance with current recommenda-
tions and practice at the time of publication.However,in view of ongoing research,changes
in government regulations, and the constant flow of information relating to drug therapy
and drug reactions, the reader is urged to check the package insert for each drug for any
change in indications and dosage and for added warnings and precautions.This is particu-
larly important when the recommended agent is a new or infrequently employed drug.
Some drugs and medical devices presented in the publication have Food and Drug
Administration (FDA) clearance for limited use in restricted research settings. It is the
responsibility of the health care provider to ascertain the FDA status of each drug or
device planned for use in their clinical practice.

--- Fim da P√°gina 10 ---

FOREWORD
To the 1st Edition
It is with the greatest enthusiasm that I introduce Pocket Medicine. In an
era of information glut, it will logically be asked,‚ÄúWhy another manual
for medical house officers?‚Äù Yet, despite enormous information readily
available in any number of textbooks, or at the push of a key on a com-
puter, it is often that the harried house officer is less helped by the
description of differential diagnosis and therapies than one would wish.
Pocket Medicine is the joint venture between house staff and faculty
expert in a number of medical specialties.This collaboration is designed
to provide a rapid but thoughtful initial approach to medical problems
seen by house officers with great frequency. Questions that frequently
come from faculty to the house staff on rounds, many hours after the
initial interaction between patient and doctor, have been anticipated and
important pathways for arriving at diagnoses and initiating therapies are
presented.This approach will facilitate the evidence-based medicine dis-
cussion that will follow the workup of the patient.This well-conceived
handbook should enhance the ability of every medical house officer to
properly evaluate a patient in a timely fashion and to be stimulated to
think of the evidence supporting the diagnosis and the likely outcome of
therapeutic intervention. Pocket Medicine will prove to be a worthy addi-
tion to medical education and to the care of our patients.
DENNIS A. AUSIELLO, MD
Physician-in-Chief, Massachusetts General Hospital
Jackson Professor of Clinical Medicine, Harvard Medical School

--- Fim da P√°gina 11 ---

PREFACE
For Jenny, Matteo, and Natalie with love
Written by residents, fellows, and attendings, the mandate for Pocket
Medicine was to provide,in a concise a manner as possible,the key infor-
mation a clinician needs for the initial approach to and management of
the most common inpatient medical problems.
The tremendous response to the previous editions suggests we
were able to help fill an important need for clinicians.With this fourth
edition come several major improvements including: a thorough updat-
ing of every topic; the addition of several new topics (including acute
aortic syndromes, sepsis, obstructive sleep apnea, hepatic vascular dis-
ease, optimal use of diuretics, viral respiratory infections, infections in
susceptible hosts, intensive glycemic control, approach to the patient
with joint pain, and alcohol withdrawal); incorporation of references to
the most recent reviews and important studies published through the
middle of 2010; and the addition of high-resolution chest radiographs,
chest and abdominal CTs, and echocardiograms, and photomicrographs
of peripheral blood smears and urinalyses.We welcome any suggestions
for further improvement.
Of course medicine is far too vast a field to ever summarize in a
textbook of any size. Long monographs have been devoted to many of
the topics discussed herein. Pocket Medicine is meant only as a starting
point to guide one during the initial phases of diagnosis and management
until one has time to consult more definitive resources. Although the
recommendations herein are as evidence-based as possible, medicine is
both a science and an art.As always, sound clinical judgement must be
applied to every scenario.
I am grateful for the support of the house officers, fellows, and
attendings at the Massachusetts General Hospital.It is a privilege to work
with such a knowledgeable,dedicated,and compassionate group of physi-
cians. I always look back on my time there as Chief Resident as one of
the best experiences I have ever had. I am grateful to several outstanding
clinical mentors, including Hasan Bazari, Denny Ausiello, Larry Friedman,
Nesli Basgoz, Mort Swartz, Eric Isselbacher, Bill Dec, Mike Fifer, and
Roman DeSanctis, as well as the late Charlie McCabe and Peter Yurchak.
Special thanks to my parents for their perpetual encouragement and love
and, of course, to my wife, Jennifer Tseng, who, despite being a surgeon, is
my closest advisor, my best friend, and the love of my life.
I hope that you find Pocket Medicine useful throughout the arduous
but incredibly rewarding journey of practicing medicine.
MARC S. SABATINE, MD, MPH

Initial approach
‚Ä¢ Focused history: quality & severity of pain; location & radiation; provoking & palliating 
factors; duration, frequency & pattern; setting in which it occurred; associated sx
‚Ä¢ Targeted exam: VS (including BP in both arms), cardiac gallops, murmurs, or rubs;
signs of vascular disease (carotid or femoral bruits, T pulses), signs of heart failure;
lung & abdominal exam; chest wall exam for reproducibility of pain
‚Ä¢ 12-lead ECG: obtain w/in 10 min; c/w priors & obtain serial ECGs; consider posterior
leads (V7‚ÄìV9) to reveal isolated posterior MI if hx c/w ACS but ECG unrevealing
‚Ä¢ Cardiac biomarkers (Tn, CK-MB): serial testing at presentation, 6‚Äì12 h after sx onset
troponin (I/T): most Se & Sp marker; level 99th %ile in approp. clinical setting is dx of MI
detectable 3‚Äì6 h after injury, peaks 24 h, may remain elevated for 7‚Äì10 d in STEMI
high-sens. assays: 90‚Äì95% Se & Sp; 85% Se w/in 3 h of sx onset (NEJM 2009;361:858, 868)
‚Äúfalse ‚Äù (non-ACS myonecrosis): myocarditis/toxic CMP, severe CHF, HTN crisis, PE
or severe resp. distress, cardiac trauma/cardioversion, sepsis, SAH, demand ischemia;
? renal failure (T clearance, skeletal myopathy vs. true microinfarctions)
CK-MB: less Se & Sp (skel. muscle, tongue, diaphragm, intestine, uterus, prostate)
‚Ä¢ CXR; other imaging (echo, PE CTA, etc.) as indicated based on H&P and initial testing
‚Ä¢ Coronary CT angiography: 1‚ÅÑ2 free of CAD S 0% w/ ACS; 1‚ÅÑ2 w/ plaque S 17% w/ ACS;
even with signif stenosis, only 35% w/ ACS (JACC 2009;53:1642). ‚à¥good for r/o not r/i.

Exercise tolerance test (stress test) (NEJM 2001;344:1840)
‚Ä¢ Indications: dx CAD, evaluate if known CAD &  in clinical status, risk stratify s/p
ACS, evaluate exercise tolerance, localize ischemia (imaging required)
‚Ä¢ Contraindications
Absolute: AMI w/in 48 h, high-risk UA, acute PE, severe AS, uncontrolled CHF,
uncontrolled arrhythmias, myopericarditis, acute aortic dissection
Relative: left main CAD, mod valvular stenosis, severe HTN, HCMP, high-degree
AVB, severe electrolyte abnl, inability to exercise
‚Ä¢ Exercise: standard Bruce (c speed/incline q3min), modified Bruce (begins w/o tread-
mill incline), submax (if 3 wk post-MI), or sx-limited; hold antianginals if trying to
dx CAD, give if assessing if Pt ischemic on meds
‚Ä¢ Pharmacologic: if unable to exer., low exer. tol or recent MI. Se & Sp  exercise;
Preferred if LBBB. Requires imaging since ECG not specific in this setting. Coronary
vasodilators (will reveal CAD, but not tell you if Pt ischemic): regadenoson, dipyri-
damole, or adenosine (may precipitate bradycardia and bronchospasm).
Chronotropes/inotropes (physiologic): dobutamine (may precipitate tachyarrhythmias).
‚Ä¢ Imaging: used if uninterpretable ECG (paced, LBBB, resting STT 1 mm, dig., LVH,
WPW), after indeterminate ECG test, pharmacologic tests, or localization of ischemia
SPECT (eg, 99mTc-sestamibi), PET (rubidium-82; usually w/ pharm test), echo, MRI
Test results
‚Ä¢ HR (must achieve 85% of max predicted HR [220-age] for exercise test to be dx), BP
response, peak double product (HR  BP), HR recovery (HRpeak‚Äì HR1 min later; nl 12)
‚Ä¢ Max exercise capacity achieved (METS or min)
‚Ä¢ Occurrence of symptoms (at what level of exertion and similarity to presenting sx)
‚Ä¢ ECG changes: downsloping or horizontal ST T (1 mm) predictive of CAD (but dis-
tribution of ST T do not localize ischemic territory); STE highly predictive
‚Ä¢ Duke treadmill score  exercise min ‚Äì (5  max ST dev) ‚Äì (4  angina index) [0 none, 1
nonlimiting, 2 limiting]; score 5 S 1% 1-y mort; ‚Äì10 to 4 S 2‚Äì3%;  ‚Äì11 S 5%
‚Ä¢ Imaging: radionuclide defects or echocardiographic regional wall motion abnormalities
reversible defect  ischemia; fixed defect  infarct
false : breast S ant ‚Äúdefect‚Äù and diaphragm S inf ‚Äúdefect‚Äù

‚Ä¢ Goal: identify hibernating myocardium that could regain fxn after revascularization
‚Ä¢ Options: MRI (Se 95%, Sp 70%), PET (Se 90%, Sp 75%), dobutamine stress
echo (Se 70%, Sp 85%); rest-redistribution thallium (Se 90%, Sp 55%)

‚Ä¢ Image quality best at slower & regular HR (give B if possible, goal HR 55‚Äì60)
‚Ä¢ Calcium generates artifact for CT angiography
‚Ä¢ MRI being studied: angiography, perfusion, LV fxn, hyperenhancement (Circ 2009;119:1671)
Coronary artery calcium score (CACS, NEJM 2008;358:1336; JAMA 2010;303:1610)

ANGIO/PCI
1-5
CORONARY ANGIOGRAPHY AND REVASCULARIZATION
Indications for coronary angiography in stable CAD or asx Pts
‚Ä¢ CCS class III-IV angina despite medical Rx or angina  systolic dysfxn
‚Ä¢ High-risk stress test findings (see prior topic)
‚Ä¢ Uncertain dx after noninvasive testing (& compelling need to determine dx), occupational
need for definitive dx (eg, pilot), or inability to undergo noninvasive testing
‚Ä¢ Systolic dysfxn with unexplained cause
‚Ä¢ Survivor of SCD, polymorphic VT, sustained monomorphic VT
‚Ä¢ Suspected spasm or nonatherosclerotic cause of ischemia (eg, anomalous coronary)
Pre-cath checklist
‚Ä¢ Document peripheral arterial exam (femoral, DP, PT pulses; femoral bruits); NPO 6 h
‚Ä¢ ‚úìCBC, PT, & Cr; give IVF (	 bicarb, acetylcysteine; see ‚ÄúCIAKI‚Äù); blood bank sample
‚Ä¢ ASA 325 mg; consider clopidogrel preRx (300‚Äì600 mg 2‚Äì6 h before) vs. prasugrel
at time of PCI (if ACS)
Coronary revascularization in stable CAD (JACC 2004;44:e213 & 2006;47:e1)
‚Ä¢ CABG: T mortality c/w med Rx (albeit pre statins & ACEI/ARB) in Pts w/ 3VD, LM,
or 2VD w/ critical prox LAD, and espec. if T EF (but viable myocardium); T repeat
revasc and trend toward T D/MI but c stroke c/w PCI in LM/3VD (NEJM 2009;360:961);
CABG vs. PCI being studied in DM (FREEDOM trial)
‚Ä¢ PCI: T angina c/w med Rx; does not T D/MI (COURAGE, NEJM 2007;356:1503); prompt
revasc (PCI or CABG) did not T mortality vs. med Rx in DM (NEJM 2009;360:2503)
‚Ä¢ PCI comparable to CABG in Pts w/o 3VD, w/o DM, and nl EF (Lancet 2009;373:1190)
‚Ä¢ For stable CAD w/o critical anatomy and w/o T EF, initial focus on optimal med Rx
‚Ä¢ If revasc deemed necessary, PCI if limited # of discrete lesions, nl EF, no DM, poor oper-
ative candidate; CABG if extensive or diffuse disease, T EF, DM, or valvular disease
‚Ä¢ Fractional flow reserve [ratio of maximal flow (induced by IV or IC adenosine) distal vs.
proximal to a stenosis]: PCI only if 0.8 S T # stents & T D/MI/revasc (NEJM 2009;360:213)
PCI
‚Ä¢ Balloon angioplasty (POBA): effective, but c/b dissection & elastic recoil & neointimal
hyperplasia S restenosis; now reserved for small lesions & ? some SVG lesions
‚Ä¢ Bare metal stents (BMS): T elastic recoil S 33‚Äì50% T restenosis & repeat revasc (to 
10% by 12 mos) c/w POBA; requires ASA lifelong & clopidogrel  4 wks
‚Ä¢ Drug-eluting stents (DES): T neointimal hyperplasia S 75% T restenosis, 50% T repeat
revasc (to 5% by 1 y), no c D/MI c/w BMS (NEJM 2008;359:1330); 2nd gen. everolimus DES
promising (NEJM 2010;362:1728); require ASA lifelong & clopidogrel  1 y (Circ 2007;115:813)
‚Ä¢ Anticoagulant: UFH (short-acting, rapidly reversible, but need to ‚úìPTT/ACT), LMWH (no
need for monitoring, but t1/2 8‚Äì12 h), bivalirudin (T bleeding, but 	c MI; NEJM 2009;359:688)
Post-PCI complications
‚Ä¢ Postprocedure ‚úìvascular access site, distal pulses, ECG, CBC, Cr, CK-MB
‚Ä¢ Bleeding
hematoma/overt bleeding: manual compression, reverse/stop anticoag
retroperitoneal bleed: may present with T Hct 	 back pain; c HR & T BP late;
Dx: abd/pelvic CT (I‚Äì); Rx: reverse/stop anticoag, IVF/PRBC as required
if bleeding uncontrolled, consult performing interventionalist or surgery
‚Ä¢ Vascular damage
pseudoaneurysm: triad of pain, expansile mass, systolic bruit; Dx: U/S; Rx: manual
compression, U/S-directed compression or thrombin injection, or surgical repair
AV fistula: continuous bruit; Dx: U/S; Rx: surgical repair
T perfusion to LE (embolization, dissection, thrombus): loss of distal pulse; Dx: angio;
Rx: percutaneous or surgical repair
‚Ä¢ Peri-procedural MI: 3 ULN of CK-MB occurs in 5‚Äì10%; Qw MI in 1%
‚Ä¢ Renal failure: contrast-induced manifests w/in 24 h, peaks 3‚Äì5 d (see ‚ÄúCIAKI‚Äù)
‚Ä¢ Cholesterol emboli syndrome (typically in middle-aged & elderly and w/ Ao atheroma)
renal failure (late and progressive, eos in urine); mesenteric ischemia (abd pain,
LGIB, pancreatitis); intact distal pulses but livedo pattern and toe necrosis
‚Ä¢ Stent thrombosis: mins to yrs after PCI, typically p/w AMI. Often due to mechanical
prob. (stent underexpansion or unrecognized dissection, typically presents early) or
d/c of antiplt Rx (espec if d/c both ASA & ADP blocker; JAMA 2005;293: 2126). Risk of
late stent thrombosis may be higher with DES than BMS (JACC 2006;48:2584).
‚Ä¢ In-stent restenosis: mos after PCI, typically p/w gradual c angina (10% p/w ACS).
Due to combination of elastic recoil and neointimal hyperplasia; T w/ DES vs. BMS.

STEMI
Reperfusion
‚Ä¢ Immediate reperfusion (ie, opening occluded culprit coronary artery) is critical
‚Ä¢ In PCI-capable hospital, goal should be primary PCI w/in 90 min of 1st medical contact
‚Ä¢ In non-PCI-capable hospital, consider transfer to PCI-capable hospital (see below), o/w
fibrinolytic therapy w/in 30 min of hospital presentation
‚Ä¢ Do not let decision regarding method of reperfusion delay time to reperfusion
Primary PCI (NEJM 2007;356:47)
‚Ä¢ Superior to lysis: 27% T death, 65% T reMI, 54% T stroke, 95% T ICH (Lancet 2003;361:13)
‚Ä¢ Thrombus aspiration during angio prior to stenting T mortality (Lancet 2008;371:1915)
‚Ä¢ Transfer to center for 1 PCI may also be superior to lysis (NEJM 2003;349:733), see below
Fibrinolysis vs. Hospital Transfer for Primary PCI
Assess Time and Risk
1. Time required for transport to skilled PCI lab: door-to-balloon 90 min &
[door-to-balloon]-[door-to-needle] 1 h favors transfer for PCI
2. Risk from STEMI: high-risk Pts (eg, shock) fare better with mechanical reperfusion
3. Time to presentation: efficacy of lytics T w/ c time from sx onset, espec. 3 h
4. Risk of fibrinolysis: if high risk of ICH or bleeding, PCI safer option
Adapted from ACC/AHA 2004 STEMI Guidelines (Circ 2004;110:e82)
Fibrinolysis
‚Ä¢ Indications: sx 12 h and either STE 0.1 mV (1 mm) in 2 contig. leads or LBBB not
known to be old; benefit if sx 12 h less clear; reasonable if persistent sx & STE
‚Ä¢ Mortality T 20% in anterior MI or LBBB and 10% in IMI c/w ‚àÖreperfusion Rx
‚Ä¢ Prehospital lysis (ie, ambulance): further 17% T in mortality (JAMA 2000;283:2686)
‚Ä¢ 1% risk of ICH; high-risk groups include elderly (2% if 75 y), women, low wt
‚Ä¢ Although age not contraindic., c risk of ICH in elderly (75 y) makes PCI more attractive

Risk stratification
‚Ä¢ Stress test if anatomy undefined or significant residual CAD after PCI of culprit vessel
‚Ä¢ Echocardiogram to assess EF; EF c 6% in STEMI over 6 mos (JACC 2007;50:149)

‚Ä¢ -blocker: 23% T mortality after acute MI
‚Ä¢ Statin: high-intensity lipid-lowering (eg, atorvastatin 80 mg, NEJM 2004;350:1495)
‚Ä¢ ACEI: life-long if CHF, T EF, HTN, DM; 4‚Äì6 wks or at least until hosp. d/c in all STEMI
? long-term benefit in CAD w/o CHF (NEJM 2000;342:145 & 2004;351:2058; Lancet 2003;362:782)
‚Ä¢ Aldosterone antagonist: if EF 40% & signs of HF (see ‚ÄúHeart Failure‚Äù)
‚Ä¢ Nitrates: standing if symptomatic; SL NTG prn for all
‚Ä¢ Oral anticoagulants: beyond indic. for AF and LV thrombus, comb. of warfarin (goal
INR 2‚Äì2.5)  ASA T D/MI/CVA c/w ASA alone, but c bleeding (NEJM 2002;347:969);
addition of oral Xa or IIa inhibitors post-ACS under study (Lancet 2009;374:29)

PA CATHETER AND TAILORED THERAPY 
Rationale
‚Ä¢ Cardiac output (CO)  SV  HR; SV depends on LV end-diastolic volume (LVEDV)
‚à¥manipulate LVEDV to optimize CO while minimizing pulmonary edema
‚Ä¢ Balloon at tip of catheter inflated S floats into ‚Äúwedge‚Äù position. Column of blood extends
from tip of catheter, through pulmonary circulation, to a point just proximal to LA. Under
conditions of no flow, PCWP  LA pressure  LVEDP, which is proportional to LVEDV.
‚Ä¢ Situations in which these basic assumptions fail:
1) Catheter tip not in West lung zone 3 (and ‚à¥PCWP  alveolar pressure Z LA
pressure); clues include lack of a & v waves and if PA diastolic pressure  PCWP
2) PCWP  LA pressure (eg, mediastinal fibrosis, pulmonary VOD, PV stenosis)
3) Mean LA pressure  LVEDP (eg, MR, MS)
4)  LVEDP-LVEDV relationship (ie, abnl compliance, ‚à¥‚Äúnl‚Äù LVEDP may not be optimal)
Indications (JACC 1998;32:840 & Circ 2009;119:e391)
‚Ä¢ Diagnosis and evaluation
Ddx of shock (cardiogenic vs. distributive; espec. if trial of IVF failed / high-risk) and
of pulmonary edema (cardiogenic vs. not; espec. if trial of diuretic failed / high-risk)
Evaluation of CO, intracardiac shunt, pulmonary HTN, MR, tamponade
‚Ä¢ Therapeutics
Tailored therapy to optimize PCWP, SV, SvO2 in heart failure/shock
Guide to vasodilator therapy (eg, inhaled NO, nifedipine) in pulmonary HTN
Guide to perioperative management in some high-risk Pts, pre-transplantation
‚Ä¢ Contraindications
Absolute: right-sided endocarditis, thrombus, or mechanical R-sided valve
Relative: coagulopathy (reverse), recent PPM or ICD (place under fluoroscopy), LBBB
(5% risk of RBBB S CHB, place under fluoro), bioprosthetic R-sided valve
Efficacy concerns
‚Ä¢ No benefit to routine PAC in high-risk surgery or ARDS (NEJM 2006;354:2213)
‚Ä¢ No benefit in decompensated CHF (JAMA 2005;294:1625); untested in cardiogenic shock
‚Ä¢ But: 1‚ÅÑ2 of CO & PCWP clinical estimates incorrect; CVP & PCWP not well correl.; ‚à¥use
PAC to (a) answer hemodynamic ? and then remove, or (b) manage cardiogenic shock
Placement
‚Ä¢ Insertion site: R internal jugular or L subclavian veins for ‚Äúanatomic‚Äù flotation into PA 
‚Ä¢ Inflate balloon (max 1.5 mL) when advancing and to measure PCWP
‚Ä¢ Use resistance to inflation and pressure tracing to avoid overinflation
‚Ä¢ Deflate the balloon when withdrawing and at all other times
‚Ä¢ CXR should be obtained after bedside placement to assess for catheter position and PTX
‚Ä¢ If catheter cannot be successfully floated (typically if severe TR or RV dilatation) or if
another relative contraindication exists, consider fluoroscopic guidance
Complications
‚Ä¢ Central venous access: pneumo/hemothorax (1‚Äì3%), arterial puncture, air embolism
‚Ä¢ Advancement: atrial or ventricular arrhythmias (3% VT), RBBB (5%), catheter knot-
ting, cardiac perforation/tamponade, PA rupture
‚Ä¢ Maintenance: infection (especially if catheter 3 d old), thrombus, pulmonary
infarction (1%), PA rupture/pseudoaneurysm (esp. w/ PHT), balloon rupture
Intracardiac pressures
‚Ä¢ Transmural pressure ( preload)  measured intracardiac pressure-intrathoracic pressure
‚Ä¢ Intrathoracic pressure (usually slightly ) is transmitted to vessels and heart
‚Ä¢ Always measure intracardiac pressure at end-expiration, when intrathoracic pressure
closest to 0; (‚Äúhigh point‚Äù in spont. breathing Pts;‚Äúlow point‚Äù in Pts on  pressure vent.)
‚Ä¢ If c intrathoracic pressure (eg, PEEP), measured PCWP overestimates true transmural
pressures. Can approx by subtracting 1‚ÅÑ2 PEEP (convert cmH2O to mmHg by  3‚ÅÑ4).
‚Ä¢ PCWP: LV preload best estimated at a wave; risk of pulmonary edema from avg PCWP 
Cardiac output
‚Ä¢ Thermodilution: saline injected in RA.  in temp over time measured at thermis-
tor (in PA) is integrated  1/CO. Inaccurate if T CO, sev TR, or shunt.
‚Ä¢ Fick method: O2 consumption (L/min)  CO (L/min)  arteriovenous O2 difference.
CO derived by dividing O2 consumption by observed AV O2 difference [10  1.34 ml
O2/g Hb  Hb g/dl  (SaO2 ‚Äì SvO2)].
Can estimate O2 consumption using wt-based formula, but best to measure (espec
if c metabolism, eg, sepsis).
If SVO2 80%, consider wedged (ie, pulm vein) sat, LSR shunt, impaired O2 utiliza-
tion (severe sepsis, cyanide, carbon monoxide), cc O2 delivery.

‚Ä¢ Goals: optimize both MAP and CO while T risk of pulmonary edema

HEART FAILURE
Definitions (Braunwald‚Äôs Heart Disease, 8th ed., 2008)
‚Ä¢ Failure of heart to pump blood forward at sufficient rate to meet metabolic demands of
peripheral tissues, or ability to do so only at abnormally high cardiac filling pressures
‚Ä¢ Low output (T cardiac output) vs. high output (c stroke volume 	 c cardiac output)
‚Ä¢ Left-sided (pulmonary edema) vs. right-sided (c JVP, hepatomegaly, peripheral edema)
‚Ä¢ Backward (c filling pressures, congestion) vs. forward (impaired systemic perfusion)
‚Ä¢ Systolic (inability to expel sufficient blood) vs. diastolic (failure to relax and fill normally)

Evaluation of the causes of heart failure
‚Ä¢ ECG: evidence for CAD, LVH, LAE, heart block or low voltage (? infiltrative CMP/DCMP) 
‚Ä¢ Coronary angiography (or ? CT coronary angiography)
‚Ä¢ If no CAD, w/u for nonischemic DCMP, HCMP, or RCMP (see ‚ÄúCardiomyopathies‚Äù)
Precipitants of acute heart failure
‚Ä¢ Myocardial ischemia or infarction; myocarditis
‚Ä¢ Renal failure (acute, progression of CKD, or insufficient dialysis) S c preload
‚Ä¢ Hypertensive crisis (incl. from RAS), worsening AS S c left-sided afterload
‚Ä¢ Dietary indiscretion or medical nonadherence
‚Ä¢ Drugs (B, CCB, NSAIDs,TZDs) or toxins (EtOH, anthracyclines)
‚Ä¢ Arrhythmias; acute valvular dysfxn (eg, endocarditis), espec. mitral or aortic regurgitation 
‚Ä¢ COPD or PE S c right-sided afterload
‚Ä¢ Anemia, systemic infection, thyroid disease

CARDIOMYOPATHIES
Diseases with mechanical and/or electrical dysfunction of the myocardium
DILATED CARDIOMYOPATHY (DCMP)
Definition and epidemiology (Circ 2006;113:1807)
‚Ä¢ Ventricular dilatation and T contractility 	 T wall thickness,
‚Ä¢ Incidence: 5‚Äì8 cases/100,000 population per y; prevalence: 1 in 2500
Etiologies (NEJM 1994;331:1564 & 2000;342:1077)
‚Ä¢ Ischemia: systolic dysfxn & dilation out of proportion to CAD (poor remodeling post-MI)
‚Ä¢ Valvular disease: systolic dysfxn due to chronic volume overload in MR & AI
‚Ä¢ Familial (25%): mutations in cytoskeletal, nuclear, and filament proteins (NEJM 1992;362:77)
‚Ä¢ Idiopathic (25% of DCMP, ? undiagnosed infectious, alcoholic, or genetic cause)
‚Ä¢ Infectious myocarditis (10‚Äì15%, autoimmune response to infxn; NEJM 2009;360:1526)
Viruses (coxsackie, adeno, echovirus, CMV): ranging from subacute (dilated LV w/ mild-
mod dysfxn) to fulminant (nondilated, thickened, edematous LV w/ severe dysfxn)
Bacterial, fungal, rickettsial,TB, Lyme (mild myocarditis, often with AVB)
HIV: 8% of asx HIV ; due to HIV vs. other viruses vs. meds (NEJM 1998;339:1093)
Chagas: apical aneurysm 	 thrombus, RBBB, megaesophagus or colon (NEJM 1993;329:639)
‚Ä¢ Toxic
Alcohol (5%) typically 7‚Äì8 drinks/d  5 y, but much interindividual variability
Anthracyclines (risk c 550 mg/m2, may manifest late), cyclophosphamide, trastuzumab 
Cocaine, antiretrovirals, lead, carbon monoxide poisoning, radiation
‚Ä¢ Infiltrative (5%): often mix of DCMP  RCMP (qv) with thickened wall
amyloidosis, sarcoidosis, hemochromatosis, tumor
‚Ä¢ Autoimmune
Collagen vascular disease (3%): polymyositis, SLE, scleroderma, PAN, RA,Wegener‚Äôs
Peripartum (last month S 5 mo postpartum): 0.1% of preg.; c risk w/ multiparity &
c age; 50% will improve; ? c risk w/ next preg. (JAMA 2000;283:1183)
Idiopathic giant cell myocarditis (GCM): avg age 42 y, fulminant,VT (NEJM 1997;336:1860)
Eosinophilic (variable peripheral eosinophilia): hypersensitivity (mild CHF) or
acute necrotizing (ANEM; STE, effusion, severe CHF)
‚Ä¢ Stress-induced (Takotsubo apical ballooning): mimics MI (pain, 	 STE & c Tn; deep TWI
& c QT; mid/apical dyskinesis; ? Rx w/ ACEI; usually improves over wks (NEJM 2005;352:539)
‚Ä¢ Tachycardia-induced: likelihood proportional to rate and duration
‚Ä¢ Arrhythmogenic right ventricular cardiomyopathy (ARVC): fibrofatty replacement
of RV S dilation (dx w/ MRI); ECG: 	 RBBB,TWI V1‚ÄìV3,  wave; risk VT (Circ 2004;110:1879)
‚Ä¢ Metabolic & other: hypothyroidism, acromegaly, pheo, thiamine, sleep apnea
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Heart failure: both congestive & poor forward flow sx; signs of L- & R-sided HF
diffuse, lat.-displaced PMI, S3, 	 MR or TR (annular dilat., displaced pap. muscle)
‚Ä¢ Embolic events (10%), arrhythmias & palpitations
‚Ä¢ Chest pain can be seen w/ some etiologies (eg, myocarditis)
Diagnostic studies and workup
‚Ä¢ CXR: moderate to marked cardiomegaly, 	 pulmonary edema & pleural effusions
‚Ä¢ ECG: may see PRWP, Q waves, or BBB; low voltage; AF (20%)
‚Ä¢ Echocardiogram: LV dilatation, T EF, regional or global LV HK, 	 RV HK, 	 mural thrombi
‚Ä¢ Laboratory evaluation:TFTs, iron studies, HIV, SPEP, ANA; others per clinical suspicion
‚Ä¢ Family hx (20‚Äì35% w/ familial dis.), genetic counseling 	 genetic testing (JAMA 2009;302:2471)
‚Ä¢ Stress test: completely  test useful to r/o ischemic etiology (low false  rate), but
 test does not rule in ischemic etiology (high false  rate, even w/ imaging)
‚Ä¢ Coronary angiography to r/o CAD if risk factors, h/o angina, Qw MI on ECG,
equivocal ETT; consider CT angiography (JACC 2007;49:2044) 
‚Ä¢ ? Endomyocardial biopsy (JACC 2007;50:1914): yield 10% (of these, 75% myocarditis, 25% 
systemic disease); 40% false  rate (patchy dis.) & false  (necrosis S inflammation)
no proven Rx for myocarditis; ‚à¥biopsy if: acute & hemodyn compromise (r/o GCM,
ANEM); arrhythmia or RCMP features (r/o infiltrative); or suspect toxic, allergic, tumor
‚Ä¢ Cardiac MRI: detect myocarditis or infiltrative disease, but nonspecific (EHJ 2005;26:1461)
Treatment (see ‚ÄúHeart Failure‚Äù for standard HF Rx)
‚Ä¢ Implantation of devices may be tempered by possibility of reversibility of CMP
‚Ä¢ Immunosuppression: for giant cell myocarditis (prednisone  AZA), collagen vascular
disease, peripartum (? IVIg), & eosinophilic; no proven benefit for viral myocarditis
‚Ä¢ Prognosis differs by etiology (NEJM 2000;342:1077): postpartum (best), ischemic (worst)

HYPERTROPHIC CARDIOMYOPATHY (HCMP)
Definition and epidemiology
‚Ä¢ LV (usually 15 mm) and/or RV hypertrophy disproportionate to hemodynamic load
‚Ä¢ Prevalence: 1 case/500 population; 50% sporadic, 50% familial
‚Ä¢ Differentiate from 2 LVH: hypertension (espec. elderly women; NEJM 1985;312:277),AS,
elite athletes (wall thickness usually 13 mm & symmetric and nl/c rates of tissue
Doppler diastolic relaxation; NEJM 1991;324:295), Fabry dis. (c Cr, skin findings)
Pathology
‚Ä¢ Autosomal dominant mutations in cardiac sarcomere genes (eg, -myosin heavy chain) 
‚Ä¢ Myocardial fiber disarray with hypertrophy
‚Ä¢ Morphologic hypertrophy variants: asymmetric septal; concentric; midcavity; apical
Pathophysiology
‚Ä¢ Subaortic outflow obstruction: narrowed tract 2 hypertrophied septum  systolic
anterior motion (SAM) of ant. MV leaflet (may be fixed, variable, or nonexistent) and
papillary muscle displacement. Gradient (‚àá) worse w/ c contractility
(digoxin, -agonists), T preload, or T afterload.
‚Ä¢ Mitral regurgitation: due to SAM (mid-to-late, post.-directed regurg. jet) and abnormal
mitral leaflets and papillary muscles (pansystolic, ant.-directed regurg. jet)
‚Ä¢ Diastolic dysfunction: c chamber stiffness  impaired relaxation
‚Ä¢ Ischemia: small vessel dis., perforating artery compression (bridging), T coronary perfusion
‚Ä¢ Syncope: s in load-dependent CO, arrhythmias
Clinical manifestations (70% are asymptomatic at dx)
‚Ä¢ Dyspnea (90%): due to c LVEDP, MR, and diastolic dysfunction
‚Ä¢ Angina (25%) even w/o epicardial CAD; microvasc. dysfxn (NEJM 2003;349:1027)
‚Ä¢ Arrhythmias (AF in 20‚Äì25%;VT/VF) S palpitations, syncope, sudden cardiac death
Physical exam
‚Ä¢ Sustained PMI, S2 paradox. split if severe outflow obstruction,  S4 (occ. palpable)
‚Ä¢ Systolic crescendo-decrescendo murmur at LLSB: c w/ Valsalva & standing
‚Ä¢ 	 mid-to-late or holosystolic murmur of MR at apex
‚Ä¢ Bisferiens carotid pulse (brisk rise, decline, then 2nd rise); JVP w/ prominent a wave
‚Ä¢ Contrast to AS, which has murmur that T w/ Valsalva and T carotid pulses
Diagnostic studies
‚Ä¢ CXR: cardiomegaly (LV and LA)
‚Ä¢ ECG: LVH, anterolateral and inferior pseudo-Qw, 	 apical giant TWI (apical variant)
‚Ä¢ Echo: no absolute cutoffs for degree of LVH but septum / post wall 1.3 suggestive,
as is septum 15 mm; other findings include dynamic outflow obstruction, SAM, MR
‚Ä¢ MRI: hypertrophy  patchy delayed enhancement (useful for dx & prognosis) 
‚Ä¢ Cardiac cath: subaortic pressure ‚àá; Brockenbrough sign  T pulse pressure post-
extrasystolic beat (in contrast to AS, in which pulse pressure c postextrasystole)
Treatment (NEJM 2004;350:1320)
‚Ä¢ Heart failure
 inotropes/chronotropes: -blockers, CCB (verapamil), disopyramide.
Careful use of diuretics.Vasodilators only if systolic dysfxn.Avoid digoxin.
If refractory to drug therapy and there is obstructive physiology (‚àá50 mmHg):
a) Alcohol septal ablation (NEJM 2002;347:1326)
triphasic ‚àáresponse: acute T S 	 partial c back to 50% of baseline S T over months
by 1 y resting ‚àá15 mmHg & stress-induced ‚àá31 mmHg (J Interv Card 2006;19:319)
complications: transient (& occ. delayed) 3 AVB w/ 10‚Äì20% req. PPM;VT
b) Surgical myectomy: long-term sx improvement in 90% (Circ 2005;112:482)
c) ? Dual-chamber pacing, but largely placebo effect (JACC 1997;29:435; Circ 1999;99:2927)
If refractory to drug therapy and there is nonobstructive pathophysiology: transplant
‚Ä¢ Acute HF: can be precip. by dehydration or tachycardia; Rx w/ fluids, B, phenylephrine 
‚Ä¢ AF: rate control with -blockers, maintain SR with disopyramide, amiodarone
‚Ä¢ Sudden cardiac death: ICD (JACC 2003;42:1687). Major risk factors include history of VT/VF,
 FHx SCD, unexplained syncope, NSVT, T SBP or relative HoTN (c SBP 20
mmHg) w/ exercise, LV wall 30 mm; risk 4%/y in high-risk Pts (JAMA 2007;298:405)
‚Ä¢ Counsel to avoid dehydration, extreme exertion
‚Ä¢ Endocarditis prophylaxis no longer recommended (Circ 2007;16:1736)
‚Ä¢ First-degree relatives: periodic screening w/ echo (as timing of HCMP onset variable)

RESTRICTIVE CARDIOMYOPATHY (RCMP)
Definition
‚Ä¢ Impaired ventricular filling due to T compliance in absence of pericardial disease
Etiology (NEJM 1997;336:267; JACC 2010;55:1769)
‚Ä¢ Myocardial processes
autoimmune (scleroderma, polymyositis-dermatomyositis)
infiltrative diseases (see primary entries for extracardiac manifestations, Dx, Rx)
amyloidosis (JACC 2007;50:2101): age at presentation 60 y; male:female  3:2
AL (MM, light-chain, MGUS,WM); familial (transthyretin,TTR);AA/senile (TTR, ANP)
ECG: T QRS amplitude (50%), pseudoinfarction pattern (Qw),AVB (10‚Äì20%),
hemiblock (20%), BBB (5‚Äì20%)
Echo: biventricular wall thickening, granular sparkling texture (30%), biatrial
enlargement (40%), thickened atrial septum, valve thickening (65%), diastolic 
dysfxn, small effusion
normal voltage & normal septal thickness has NPV 90%
MRI: distinct late gadolinium enhancement pattern (JACC 2008;51:1022)
sarcoidosis: age at present. 30 y; more common in blacks, N. Europeans, women
5% of those with sarcoid w/ overt cardiac involvement; cardiac w/o systemic in 10%
ECG:AVB (75%), RBBB (20‚Äì60%),VT
Echo: regional WMA (particularly basal septum) with thinning or mild hypertrophy
nuclear imaging: gallium uptake in areas of sestaMIBI perfusion defects
hemochromatosis: presents in middle-aged men (particularly N. European)
storage diseases: Gaucher‚Äôs, Fabry, Hurler‚Äôs, glycogen storage diseases
diabetes mellitus
‚Ä¢ Endomyocardial processes
chronic eosinophilic: L√∂ffler‚Äôs endocarditis (temperate climates; c eos.; mural thrombi
that embolize); endomyocardial fibrosis (tropical climates; var. eos.; mural thrombi)
toxins: radiation, anthracyclines
serotonin: carcinoid, serotonin agonists, ergot alkaloids
metastatic cancer
Pathology & pathophysiology
‚Ä¢ Path: normal or c wall thickness 	 infiltration or abnormal deposition
‚Ä¢ T myocardial compliance S nl EDV but c EDP S c systemic & pulm. venous pressures
‚Ä¢ T ventricular cavity size S T SV and T CO
Clinical manifestations (Circ 2000;101:2490)
‚Ä¢ Right-sided  left-sided heart failure with peripheral edema  pulmonary edema
‚Ä¢ Diuretic ‚Äúrefractoriness‚Äù
‚Ä¢ Thromboembolic events
‚Ä¢ Poorly tolerated tachyarrhythmias;VT S syncope/sudden cardiac death
Physical exam
‚Ä¢ c JVP, 	 Kussmaul‚Äôs sign (classically seen in constrictive pericarditis)
‚Ä¢ Cardiac: 	 S3 and S4, 	 murmurs of MR and TR
‚Ä¢ Congestive hepatomegaly, 	 ascites and jaundice, peripheral edema
Diagnostic studies
‚Ä¢ CXR: normal ventricular chamber size, enlarged atria, 	 pulmonary congestion
‚Ä¢ ECG: low voltage, pseudoinfarction pattern (Qw), 	 arrhythmias
‚Ä¢ Echo: symmetric wall thickening, biatrial enlarge., 	 mural thrombi, 	 cavity oblit.
w/ diast dysfxn: c early diast (E) and T late atrial (A) filling, c E/A ratio, T decel. time
‚Ä¢ Cardiac MRI: may reveal inflammation or evidence of infiltration (although nonspecific)
‚Ä¢ Cardiac catheterization
atria: M‚Äôs or W‚Äôs (prominent x and y descents)
ventricles: dip & plateau (rapid T pressure at onset of diastole, rapid c to early plateau)
concordance of LV and RV pressure peaks during respiratory cycle (vs. discor-
dance in constrictive pericarditis; Circ 1996;93:2007)
‚Ä¢ Endomyocardial biopsy if suspect infiltrative process
‚Ä¢ Restrictive cardiomyopathy vs. constrictive pericarditis: see ‚ÄúPericardial Disease‚Äù
Treatment (in addition to Rx‚Äôing underlying disease)
‚Ä¢ Gentle diuresis. May not tolerate CCB or other vasodilators.
‚Ä¢ Control HR and maintain SR (important for diastolic filling). Dig proarrhythmic in amyloid.
‚Ä¢ Anticoagulation (particularly with AF or low CO)
‚Ä¢ Transplantation for refractory cases

AORTIC STENOSIS (AS)
Etiology
‚Ä¢ Calcific: predominant cause in Pts 70 y; risk factors include HTN, c chol., ESRD
‚Ä¢ Congenital (ie, bicuspid AoV w/ premature calcification): cause in 50% of Pts 70 y
‚Ä¢ Rheumatic heart disease (AS usually accompanied by AI and MV disease)
‚Ä¢ AS mimickers: subvalvular (HCMP, subAo membrane), supravalvular
Clinical manifestations (usually indicates AVA 1 cm2 or concomitant CAD)
‚Ä¢ Angina: c O2 demand (hypertrophy)  T O2 supply (T cor perfusion pressure) 	 CAD
‚Ä¢ Syncope (exertional): peripheral vasodil. w/ fixed CO S T MAP S T cerebral perfusion
‚Ä¢ Heart failure: outflow obstruct  diastolic dysfxn S pulm. edema; precip. by AF (T LV filling)
‚Ä¢ Acquired von Willebrand disease (20% of sev.AS): destruction of vWF (NEJM 2003;349:343)
‚Ä¢ Natural hx: usually slowly progressive (AVA T 0.1 cm2 per y, but varies; Circ 1997;95:2262),
until sx develop; mean survival based on sx: angina  5 y; syncope  3 y; CHF  2 y 
Physical examination
‚Ä¢ Midsystolic crescendo-decrescendo murmur at
RUSB, harsh, high-pitched, radiates to carotids, apex
(holosystolic  Gallavardin effect) c w/ passive leg raise,
T w/ standing & Valsalva
‚Ä¢ In contrast, dynamic outflow obstruction (eg, HCMP) T
w/ passive leg raise & c w/ standing & Valsalva
‚Ä¢ Ejection click after S1 sometimes heard with bicuspid AoV
‚Ä¢ Signs of severity: late-peaking murmur, paradoxically split S2
or inaudible A2, small and delayed carotid pulse (‚Äúpulsus
parvus et tardus‚Äù), LV heave,  S4 (occasionally palpable)
Diagnostic studies
‚Ä¢ ECG: LVH, LAE, LBBB,AF (in late disease)
‚Ä¢ CXR: cardiomegaly,AoV calcification, poststenotic dilation of
ascending Ao, pulmonary congestion
‚Ä¢ Echo: valve morphology, estim pressure gradient & calculate AVA, EF 
‚Ä¢ Cardiac cath: pressure gradiant (‚àá) across AoV,AVA, r/o CAD (in 50% of calcific AS)
‚Ä¢ Dobutamine challenge during echo or cath if low EF and ‚àá30 to differentiate:
afterload mismatch: 20% c SV & c ‚àá, no  AVA (implies contractile reserve & c EF post-AVR) 
pseudostenosis: 20% c SV, no  in ‚àá, c AVA (implies low AVA artifact of LV dysfxn)
limited contractile reserve: no  SV, ‚àá, or AVA (implies EF prob. will not improve w/ AVR)

AORTIC INSUFFICIENCY (AI)
Etiology (Circ 2006;114:422)
‚Ä¢ Valve disease (43%)
rheumatic heart disease (usually mixed AS/AI and concomitant MV disease)
bicuspid AoV: natural hx: 1/3 S normal, 1/3 S AS, 1/6 S AI, 1/6 S endocarditis S AI
infective endocarditis
valvulitis: RA, SLE; anorectics (fen/phen) & other serotoninergics (NEJM 2007;356:29,39)
‚Ä¢ Root disease (57%)
HTN
aortic aneurysm or dissection, annuloaortic ectasia, Marfan syndrome
aortic inflammation: giant cell,Takayasu‚Äôs, ankylosing spond., reactive arthritis, syphilis
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Acute: sudden T forward SV and c LVEDP (noncompliant ventricle) S pulmonary edema
	 hypotension and cardiogenic shock
‚Ä¢ Chronic: clinically silent while LV dilates (to c compliance to keep LVEDP low) more
than it hypertrophies S chronic volume overload S LV decompensation S CHF
‚Ä¢ Natural hx: variable progression (unlike AS, can be fast or slow); once decompensation
begins, prognosis poor w/o AVR (mortality 10%/y)
Physical examination
‚Ä¢ Early diastolic decrescendo murmur at LUSB
(RUSB if dilated Ao root); c/ sitting forward, expir,
handgrip; severity of AI  duration of murmur (except in 
acute and severe late); Austin Flint murmur:mid-to-late diastolic 
rumble at apex (AI jet interfering w/ mitral inflow) 
‚Ä¢ Wide pulse pressure due to c stroke volume, hyperdy-
namic pulse S many of classic signs (see table); pulse
pressure narrows in late AI with T LV fxn; bisferiens
(twice-beating) arterial pulse
‚Ä¢ PMI diffuse and laterally displaced; soft S1 (early closure of
MV); 	 S3 (Z T EF but rather just volume overload in AI)

Diagnostic studies
‚Ä¢ ECG: LVH, LAD, abnl repolarization; CXR: cardiomegaly 	 ascending Ao dilatation
‚Ä¢ Echo: severity of AI (severe  width of regurgitant jet 65% LVOT, vena contracta
0.6 cm, regurg fraction 50%, regurg orifice 0.3 cm2, flow reversal in descending

Treatment (Circ 2008;118:e523)
‚Ä¢ Acute decompensation (consider ischemia and endocarditis as possible precipitants)
surgery usually urgently needed for acute severe AI which is poorly tolerated by LV
IV afterload reduction (nitroprusside) and inotropic support (dobutamine)
	 chronotropic support (c HR S T diastole S T time for regurgitation)
pure vasoconstrictors and IABP contraindicated
‚Ä¢ In chronic AI, management decisions based on LV size and fxn (and before sx occur) 
‚Ä¢ Surgery (AVR, replacement or repair if possible)
sx (if equivocal, consider stress test) severe AI (if not severe, unlikely to be cause of sx)
asx severe AI and EF 50% or LV dilation (end syst. diam. 55 mm or end diast. diam.
75 mm, or 50 & 70, respectively, if progression) or undergoing cardiac surgery
‚Ä¢ Medical therapy: vasodilators (nifedipine,ACEI, hydralazine) if severe AI w/ sx or LV
dysfxn & Pt not operative candidate or to improve hemodynamics before AVR;
no clear benefit on clinical outcomes or LV fxn when used to try to prolong compen-
sation in asx severe AI w/ mild LV dilation & nl LV fxn (NEJM 2005;353:1342)

MITRAL REGURGITATION (MR)
Etiology (Lancet 2009;373:1382) 
‚Ä¢ Leaflet abnormalities: myxomatous degeneration (MVP), endocarditis, calcific
RHD, valvulitis (collagen-vascular disease), congenital, anorectic drugs
‚Ä¢ Functional: inferoapical papillary muscle displacement due to ischemic LV
remodeling or other causes of DCMP; LV annular dilation due to LV dilation 
‚Ä¢ Ruptured chordae tendinae: myxomatous, endocarditis, spontaneous, trauma
‚Ä¢ Acute papillary muscle dysfxn b/c of ischemia or rupture during MI [usu. posteromedial
papillary m. (supplied by PDA only) vs. anterolateral (suppl. by diags & OMs)]
‚Ä¢ HCMP: (see ‚ÄúCardiomyopathy‚Äù)
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Acute: pulmonary edema, hypotension, cardiogenic shock (NEJM 2004;351:1627)
‚Ä¢ Chronic: typically asx for yrs, then as LV fails S progressive DOE, fatigue,AF, PHT
‚Ä¢ Prognosis: 5-y survival w/ medical therapy is 80% if asx, but only 45% if sx
Physical examination
‚Ä¢ High-pitched, blowing, holosystolic murmur at apex;
radiates to axilla; 	 thrill; c w/ handgrip (Se 68%, Sp 92%),
T w/ Valsalva (Se 93%) (NEJM 1988;318:1572)
ant. leaflet abnl S post. jet heard at spine 
post. leaflet abnl S ant. jet heard at sternum
‚Ä¢ Lat. displ. hyperdynamic PMI, obscured S1, widely split S2
(A2 early b/c T LV afterload, P2 late if PHT); 	 S3
‚Ä¢ Carotid upstroke brisk (vs. diminished and delayed in AS)
Diagnostic studies
‚Ä¢ ECG: LAE, LVH, 	 atrial fibrillation
‚Ä¢ CXR: dilated LA, dilated LV, 	 pulmonary congestion
‚Ä¢ Echo: MV anatomy (ie, cause of MR); MR severity: jet area
(can underestimate eccentric jets), jet width at origin (‚Äúvena
contracta‚Äù), or effective regurgitant orifice (ERO; predicts survival,
NEJM 2005;352:875); LV fxn (EF should be supranormal if compensated, ‚à¥EF 60% w/ sev.
MR  LV dysfxn);TEE if TTE inconclusive or pre/intraop to guide repair vs. replace
‚Ä¢ Cardiac cath: prominent PCWP cv waves (not spec. for MR), LVgram for MR severity & EF

‚Ä¢ Congenital, infectious endocarditis w/ large lesion, myxoma, thrombus 
‚Ä¢ Valvulitis (eg, SLE, amyloid, carcinoid) or infiltration (eg, mucopolysaccharidoses)

MITRAL VALVE PROLAPSE (MVP)
Definition and Etiology
‚Ä¢ Billowing of MV leaflet 2 mm above mitral annulus in parasternal long axis echo view
‚Ä¢ Leaflet redundancy from myxomatous proliferation of spongiosa of MV apparatus 
‚Ä¢ Idiopathic, familial, and a/w connective tissue diseases (eg, Marfan‚Äôs, Ehlers-Danlos)
‚Ä¢ Prevalence 1‚Äì2.5% of gen. population,  (NEJM 1999;341:1), most common cause of MR
Clinical manifestations (usually asymptomatic)
‚Ä¢ MR (from leaflet prolapse or ruptured chordae); infective endocarditis; embolic events
‚Ä¢ Arrhythmias, rarely sudden cardiac death
Physical exam
‚Ä¢ High-pitched, midsystolic click 	 mid-to-late systolic murmur
T LV volume (standing) S click earlier; c LV volume or afterload S click later, softer
Treatment
‚Ä¢ Endocarditis prophylaxis no longer recommended (Circ 2007:116:1736)
‚Ä¢ Aspirin or anticoagulation if prior neurologic event or atrial fibrillation

PROSTHETIC HEART VALVES
Mechanical (60%)
‚Ä¢ Bileaflet (eg, St. Jude Medical); tilting disk; caged-ball
‚Ä¢ Characteristics: very durable (20‚Äì30 y), but thrombogenic and ‚à¥require anticoagulation
consider if age 65 y or if anticoagulation already indicated (JACC 2010;55:2413)
Bioprosthetic (40%)
‚Ä¢ Bovine pericardial or porcine heterograft (eg, Carpentier-Edwards), homograft 
‚Ä¢ Characteristics: less durable, but minimally thrombogenic
consider if age 65 y, lifespan 20 y, or contraindication to anticoagulation
Physical examination
‚Ä¢ Normal: crisp sounds, 	 soft murmur during forward flow (normal to have small ‚àá)
‚Ä¢ Abnormal: regurgitant murmurs, absent mechanical valve closure sounds
Anticoagulation (Circ 2008;118:e523)
‚Ä¢ Warfarin
low-risk mech AVR: INR 2‚Äì3 (consider 2.5‚Äì3.5 for 1st 3 mo)
mech MVR or high-risk (defined below) mech AVR: INR 2.5‚Äì3.5
high-risk bioprosthetic: INR 2‚Äì3 (and consider for 1st 3 mo in low-risk)
high-risk features: prior thromboembolism,AF, T EF, hypercoagulable 
‚Ä¢ ASA (75‚Äì100 mg) indicated for all Pts with prosthetic valves; avoid adding to 
warfarin if h/o GIB, uncontrolled HTN, erratic INR, or 80 y
Periprocedural ‚ÄúBridging‚Äù of Anticoagulation in Pts with Mechanical Valve(s)
AVR w/o risk factors
d/c warfarin 48‚Äì72 h before surg; restart 24 h after surg
MVR or AVR w/ risk factors
Preop: d/c warfarin, start UFH when INR 2
4‚Äì6 h preop: d/c UFH; postop: restart UFH & warfarin ASAP
Procedures include noncardiac surgery, invasive procedures, and major dental work (Circ 2008;118:e523)
Correction of overanticoagulation (Circ 2008;118:e626)
‚Ä¢ Risk from major bleeding must be weighed against risk of valve thrombosis
‚Ä¢ Not bleeding & INR 5: withhold warfarin, do not give vit K, ‚úìserial INRs
‚Ä¢ Not bleeding & INR 5‚Äì10: withhold warfarin, vit K 1‚Äì2.5 mg PO, ‚úìserial INRs
‚Ä¢ Bleeding or INR 10: FFP 	 low-dose (1 mg) vit K IV
Endocarditis prophylaxis (see ‚ÄúEndocarditis‚Äù)
‚Ä¢ Indicated for all prosthetic valves to T IE risk during transient bacteremia
Complications 
‚Ä¢ Structural failure (r/o endocarditis); mechanical valves: rare except for Bjork-Shiley;
bioprosthetic valves: up to 30% fail rate w/in 10‚Äì15 y, mitral  aortic
‚Ä¢ Paravalvular leak (r/o endocarditis); small central jet of regurg is normal in mech. valves
‚Ä¢ Obstruction from thrombosis or pannus ingrowth: ‚úìTTE,TEE and/or fluoroscopy if ? clot
significantly sx pannus ingrowth: remove w/ surgery
thrombosis: surgery if left-sided valve & either severe sx or lg (? 1 cm) clot burden;
fibrinolytic Rx often ineffective for left-sided thrombosis & 12‚Äì15% risk of stroke;
consider UFH 	 lytic if mild sx and small clot burden or poor surg candidate;
fibrinolytic therapy reasonable for right-sided thrombosis 
‚Ä¢ Infective endocarditis 	 valvular abscess and conduction system disruption
‚Ä¢ Embolization (r/o endocarditis); risk 1%/y w/ warfarin (vs. 2% w/ ASA, or 4% w/o meds)
mech MVR 2 risk of embolic events vs. mech AVR (Circ 1994:89:635)
‚Ä¢ Bleeding (from anticoag), hemolysis (especially w/ caged-ball valves or paravalvular leak)

PERICARDIAL DISEASE
GENERAL PRINCIPLES
Anatomy
‚Ä¢ 2-layered (parietal & visceral) tissue sac surrounding heart & proximal great vessels
Disease states
‚Ä¢ Inflammation (w/ or w/o fluid accumulation) S pericarditis
‚Ä¢ Fluid accumulation (usually in setting of inflammation) S effusion 	 tamponade
‚Ä¢ Change in compliance (sequela of inflammation) S constriction
‚Ä¢ Tamponade and constriction characterized by increased ventricular interdependence
PERICARDITIS AND PERICARDIAL EFFUSION
Etiologies of Pericarditis (Lancet 2004;363:717)
Infectious
Viral: Coxsackie, echo, adeno, EBV,VZV, HIV, influenza
(50%)
Bacterial (from endocarditis, pneumonia, or s/p cardiac surgery):
S. pneumococcus, N. meningitidis, S. aureus, Borrelia (Lyme)
Tuberculous (extension from lung or hematogenous)
Fungal: Histo, Coccidio, Candida; Parasitic: Entamoeba, Echino
Neoplastic
Common: metastatic (lung, breast, lymphoma, leukemia, renal cell)
(35%)
Rare: primary cardiac & serosal tumors (mesothelioma)
Autoimmune
Connective tissue diseases: SLE, RA, scleroderma, Sj√∂gren‚Äôs
Vasculitides: PAN, Churg-Strauss,Wegener‚Äôs
Drug-induced: procainamide, hydralazine, INH, CsA
Uremia
Develops in 20% of Pts, especially if on HD. May be transudative.
Cardiovascular
Acute transmural MI (5‚Äì20%); late post-MI (Dressler‚Äôs syndrome)
Proximal aortic dissection (up to 45%)
Chest trauma or s/p cardiac procedure or surgery
Radiation
4,000 cGy to mediastinum; acute or delayed; may be transudative
Idiopathic
Most presumed to be undiagnosed viral
Effusions w/o
CHF, cirrhosis, nephrotic syndrome, hypothyroidism, amyloidosis.;
pericarditis
Transudative.
Clinical manifestations (NEJM 2004;351:2195)
‚Ä¢ Pericarditis: chest pain that is pleuritic, positional (T by sitting forward), radiates to
trapezius; may be absent in tuberculous, neoplastic, post-XRT, and uremic pericarditis;
	 fever; 	 s/s of systemic etiologies
‚Ä¢ Effusion: ranges from asx to tamponade (see below)
Physical exam
‚Ä¢ Pericarditis: multiphasic friction rub best heard at LLSB w/ diaphragm of stethoscope
(leathery sound w/ up to 3 components: atrial contraction, ventricular contraction,
ventricular relaxation) that is notoriously variable and evanescent
‚Ä¢ Effusion: distant heart sounds, dullness over left posterior lung field due to 
compressive atelectasis from pericardial effusion (Ewart‚Äôs sign)
Diagnostic studies (EHJ 2004;25:587; Circ 2006;113:1622)
‚Ä¢ ECG: may show diffuse STE (concave up) & PR depression (except in aVR: ST T & PR c),
TWI; classically and in contrast to STEMI,TWI do not occur until STs normalize
Stages: STE & PR T (I); ST & PR normalize (II); diffuse TWI (III);Tw normalize (IV).
May show evidence of large effusion w/ low voltage & electrical alternans (beat-to-
beat  in QRS amplitude and/or axis).

PERICARDIAL TAMPONADE
Etiology
‚Ä¢ Any cause of pericarditis but especially malignancy, uremia, idiopathic,
proximal aortic dissection with rupture, myocardial rupture
‚Ä¢ Rapidly accumulating effusions most likely to cause tamponade as no time for 
pericardium to stretch (c compliance) and accommodate fluid
Pathophysiology (NEJM 2003;349:684)
‚Ä¢ c intrapericardial pressure, compression of heart chambers, T venous return S T CO
‚Ä¢ Diastolic pressures c & equalize in all cardiac chambers S minimal flow of blood from
RA to RV when TV opens S blunted y descent
‚Ä¢ c ventricular interdependence S pulsus paradoxus (pathologic exaggeration of nl physio) 
Inspiration S T intrapericardial & RA pressures S c venous return S c RV size S
septal shift to left.Also, c pulmonary vascular compliance S T pulm venous return.
Result is T LV filling S T LV stroke volume & blood pressure.
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Cardiogenic shock (hypotension, fatigue) without pulmonary edema
‚Ä¢ Dyspnea (seen in 85%) may be due to c respiratory drive to augment venous return
Physical exam (JAMA 2007;297:1810)
‚Ä¢ Beck‚Äôs triad (present in minority): distant heart sounds, c JVP, hypotension
‚Ä¢ c JVP (76%) w/ blunted y descent
‚Ä¢ Reflex tachycardia (77%), hypotension (26%; occasionally hypertensive), cool extremities
‚Ä¢ Pulsus paradoxus (Se 82%, Sp 70%)  T SBP 10 mmHg during inspiration
LR 3.3 (5.9 if pulsus 12), LR 0.03
Ddx  PE, hypovolemia, severe obstructive lung disease, constriction (1/3), CHF
Can be absent if pre-existing c LVEDP, cardiac arrhythmia, or regional tamponade
‚Ä¢ Distant heart sounds (28%), 	 pericardial friction rub (30%)
‚Ä¢ Tachypnea but clear lungs
Diagnostic studies
‚Ä¢ ECG: T voltage (seen in 42%), electrical alternans, 	 signs of pericarditis
‚Ä¢ CXR: c cardiac silhouette (89%)
‚Ä¢ Echocardiogram:  effusion, IVC plethora, septal shift with inspiration,
diastolic collapse of RA (Se 85%, Sp 80%) and/or RV (Se 80%, Sp 90%)
respirophasic ‚Äôs in transvalvular velocities (c across TV & T across MV w/ inspir.)
postsurgical tamponade may be localized and not easily visible 
‚Ä¢ Cardiac cath (right heart and pericardial): elevation (15‚Äì30 mmHg) and equalization of
intrapericardial and diastolic pressures (RA, RV, PCWP), blunted y descent in RA
c in stroke volume postpericardiocentesis ultimate proof of tamponade
if RA pressure remains elevated after drainage, may have effusive-constrictive disease
(NEJM 2004;350:469) or myocardial dysfxn (eg, from concomitant myocarditis)
Treatment
‚Ä¢ Volume (but be careful as overfilling can worsen tamponade) and  inotropes (avoid B)
‚Ä¢ Pericardiocentesis (except if due to aortic or myocardial rupture, in which cases 
consider removing just enough fluid to reverse PEA while awaiting surgery)

CONSTRICTIVE PERICARDITIS
Etiology
‚Ä¢ Any cause of pericarditis but especially postviral, radiation, uremia, TB, post-
cardiac surgery, and idiopathic
Pathophysiology
‚Ä¢ Rigid pericardium limits diastolic filling S c systemic venous pressures
‚Ä¢ Venous return is limited only after early rapid filling phase; ‚à¥rapid T in RA pressure with
atrial relaxation and opening of tricuspid valve and prominent x and y descents
‚Ä¢ Kussmaul‚Äôs sign: JVP does not decrease with inspiration (c venous return with inspi-
ration, but negative intrathoracic pressure not transmitted to heart because of
rigid pericardium)
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Right-sided  left-sided heart failure
Physical exam
‚Ä¢ c JVP with prominent y descent,  Kussmaul‚Äôs sign (Ddx:TS, acute cor pulmonale,
RV infarct, RCMP)
‚Ä¢ Hepatomegaly, ascites, peripheral edema
‚Ä¢ PMI usually not palpable, pericardial knock, usually no pulsus paradoxus
Diagnostic studies
‚Ä¢ ECG: nonspecific,AF common in advanced cases
‚Ä¢ CXR: calcification (MTb most common cause), especially in lateral view (although does
not necessarily  constriction)
‚Ä¢ Echocardiogram: 	 thickened pericardium, ‚Äúseptal bounce‚Äù  abrupt posterior dis-

‚Ä¢ Cardiac catheterization
atria: Ms or Ws (prominent x and y descents)
ventricles: dip-and-plateau or square-root sign (rapid T pressure at onset of
diastole, rapid c to early plateau)
discordance between LV & RV pressure peaks during respiratory cycle (Circ 1996;93:2007)
‚Ä¢ CT or MRI: thickened pericardium (4 mm on CT) with tethering
Treatment
‚Ä¢ Diuresis for intravascular volume overload, surgical pericardiectomy

AORTA
1-30
AORTIC ANEURYSM
Definitions
‚Ä¢ True aneurysm (involves all 3 layers of aorta) vs. false (rupture contained in adventitia)
‚Ä¢ Location: root (annuloaortic ectasia), thoracic aortic aneurysm (TAA), thoracoab-
dominal aortic aneurysm, abdominal aortic aneurysm (AAA)
‚Ä¢ Type: fusiform (circumferential dilation) vs. saccular (localized dilation)
Epidemiology (Circ 2005;111:816 & 2008;117:242)
‚Ä¢ Aortic aneurysms 13th leading cause of death in U.S. (15,000 deaths/y from ruptures)
‚Ä¢ TAA: : 1.7:1; usually involves root/asc Ao or descending Ao (arch & thoracoabd rare)
Risk factors: HTN; atherosclerosis; aortitis (Takayasu‚Äôs, GCA, spondyloarthritis,
syphilis); congenital (bicuspid AoV,Turner‚Äôs); connective tissue diseases
(Marfan, Ehlers-Danlos type IV); familial; chronic Ao dissection; trauma
‚Ä¢ AAA: 5% prev. in individuals 65 y; 5‚Äì10 more common in  than ; most infrarenal
Risk factors  risk factors for atherosclerosis: smoking, HTN, hyperlipidemia, age, FHx
Pathophysiology (NEJM 2009;361:1114; Nat Med 2009;15:649)
‚Ä¢ LaPlace‚Äôs law: tension across a cylinder ‚àù[(P  r) / (wall thickness)]
‚Ä¢ TAA: cystic medial necrosis (medial degeneration, mucoid infiltration, apoptosis) 
‚Ä¢ AAA: atherosclerosis & inflammation S matrix degeneration S medial weakening
‚Ä¢ Inflammatory and infectious (‚Äúmycotic‚Äù) aneurysms rare
Screening (JAMA 2009;302:2015)
‚Ä¢ TAA: no established population screening guidelines
‚Ä¢ AAA: ‚úìfor pulsatile abdominal mass in all Pts; U/S for all men 60 y w/ FHx of AAA
and all men 65‚Äì75 y w/ prior tobacco use ( J Vasc Surg 2004;39:267; Annals 2005;142:203;)
Diagnostic studies (Circ 2005;111:816)
‚Ä¢ Contrast CT: quick, noninvasive, good Se & Sp for all aortic aneurysms
‚Ä¢ CXR: often abnormal, but not definitive in TAA
‚Ä¢ Abdominal ultrasound: screening and surveillance test of choice for AAA
‚Ä¢ TTE/TEE: useful for root and rest of TAA
‚Ä¢ MRI: preferred for aortic root imaging for TAA, but also useful in AAA
Treatment (Circ 2006;113;e463; 2008;177:1883; 2010;121:1544)
‚Ä¢ Risk factor modification: smoking cessation, LDL-C 70 mg/dL; ? macrolides or
tetracyclines (inhibit MMPs, anti-Chlamydia)
‚Ä¢ BP control: -blockers (T dP/dt) T aneurysm growth (NEJM 1994;330:1335); ACEI a/w T
risk of rupture (Lancet 2006;368:659),ARB may T rate of aortic root growth in Marfan (NEJM
2008;358:2787);no burst activity/exercise requiring Valsalva maneuvers (eg,heavy lifting)
‚Ä¢ Surgery
TAA: symptomatic, ascending 5.5 cm; descending 6 cm; Marfan Pt 4.0‚Äì4.5 cm;
growing 0.5 cm/y; aneurysm 4.5 cm and planned AoV surgery
AAA: 5 cm; rapidly growing; infrarenal/juxtarenal 5.5 cm (NEJM 2002;346:1437)
‚Ä¢ Endovascular aneurysm repair (EVAR) (NEJM 2008;358:494)
T short-term mortality, bleeding, and length of stay, but, long-term graft complica-
tions (2‚Äì5%/y; leak, rupture) necessitating periodic surveillance and no  in
overall mortality (JAMA 2009;302:1535; NEJM 2010;362:1863 & 1881). Consider for high-
risk operative Pts or in descending thoracic aneurysms 5.5 cm (JACC 2010;55:986).
In Pts unfit for surgery: T aneurysm mortality but no  in overall mortality over
medical Rx (NEJM 2010;362:1872). EVAR noninferior (? superior) to open repair in
ruptured AAA w/ favorable anatomy (Ann Surg 2009;250:818).
Complications
‚Ä¢ Pain: gnawing chest, back, or abdominal pain
‚Ä¢ Rupture: risk c w/ diameter, female sex, current smoking, HTN
TAA: 2.5%/y if 6 cm vs. 7% if 6 cm; AAA: 1%/y if 5 cm vs. 6.5% if 5‚Äì5.9 cm
may be heralded by c pain; once occurs, usually fatal or Pt may p/w severe constant
pain and in hemorrhagic shock; 90% mortality
‚Ä¢ Aortic Dissection (see following section)
‚Ä¢ Thromboembolic ischemic events
‚Ä¢ Compression of adjacent structures (eg, SVC, trachea, esophagus)
Follow-up
‚Ä¢ Expansion rate 0.1 cm/y for TAA, 0.4 cm/y for AAA
‚Ä¢ Serial imaging first 3, 6, 9, & 12 mo, then annually
‚Ä¢ Screening for CAD, PAD, and aneurysms elsewhere, espec. popliteal. 25% of Pts w/
TAA will also have AAA.

ACUTE AORTIC SYNDROMES
Definitions (Circ 2003;108:628)
‚Ä¢ Classic dissection: intimal tear S extravasation of blood into aortic media
‚Ä¢ Incomplete dissection: intimomedial tear without significant intramural extravasation
‚Ä¢ Intramural hematoma (IMH): vasa vasorum rupture S medial hemorrhage
‚Ä¢ Penetrating ulcer: ulceration of plaque penetrating intima S medial hemorrhage
Classification
‚Ä¢ Proximal: involves ascending Ao, regardless of origin (  Stanford A, DeBakey I & II)
‚Ä¢ Distal: involves descending Ao only, distal to subclavian art. (  Stanford B, DeBakey III)

‚Ä¢ Aortitis:Takayasu‚Äôs, giant cell arteritis, Beh√ßet‚Äôs, syphilis
‚Ä¢ Pregnancy: typically in 3rd trimester; can also see spont. coronary artery dissections
‚Ä¢ Trauma: blunt, IABP, cardiac or aortic surgery, cardiac catheterization

flow down compromised branches; fenestration of false lumen
Complications
‚Ä¢ Rupture: pericardial sac S tamponade (avoid pericardiocentesis unless shock/PEA);
pleural space; mediastinum; retroperitoneum
‚Ä¢ Obstruction of branch artery
can be static (avulsed/thrombosed) or dynamic (s in pressure in true vs. false lumen)
coronary S MI (usually RCA S IMI, since dissection often occurs along outer curvature)
innominate/carotid S CVA, Horner; intercostal/lumbar S spinal cord ischemia/paraplegia
innominate/subclavian S upper extremity ischemia; iliac S lower extremity ischemia
celiac/mesenteric S bowel ischemia; renal S acute renal failure
‚Ä¢ AI: due to annular dilatation or disruption or displacement of leaflet by false lumen
‚Ä¢ Mortality: 1‚Äì2%/h  48 h for acute proximal; 10% at 30 d for acute distal

ARRHYTHMIAS
1-32
ARRHYTHMIAS
BRADYCARDIAS, AV BLOCK, AND AV DISSOCIATION
Sinus bradycardia (SB) (NEJM 2000;342:703)
‚Ä¢ Etiologies: meds (incl B, CCB, amio, Li, dig), c vagal tone (incl. athletes, sleep, IMI),
metabolic (hypoxia, sepsis, myxedema, hypothermia, T glc), OSA, c ICP
‚Ä¢ Treatment: usually none required; atropine or pacing if symptomatic
‚Ä¢ Most common cause of sinus pause is blocked premature atrial beat
Sick sinus syndrome (SSS) 
‚Ä¢ Features may include: periods of unprovoked SB, SA arrest, paroxysms of SB and atrial
tachyarrhythmias (‚Äútachy-brady‚Äù syndrome), chronotropic incompetence w/ ETT
‚Ä¢ Treatment: meds alone usually fail (adeq. control tachy S unacceptable brady); usually
need combination of meds (B, CCB, dig) for tachy & PPM for brady
AV Block
Type
Features
1	
Prolonged PR (200 ms), all atrial impulses conducted (1:1).
2	 Mobitz I
Progressive c PR until impulse not conducted (S ‚Äúgrouped beating‚Äù).
(Wenckebach)
Abnl AV node due to ischemia (IMI), inflammation (myocarditis, MV 
surgery), high vagal tone (athletes), drug-induced.
Classically (50%), absolute c in PR decreases over time (S T RR
intervals, duration of pause 2 preceding RR interval).
AVB usually worsens w/ carotid sinus massage, improves w/ atropine.
Often paroxysmal/asx, no Rx required.
2	 Mobitz II
Occasional or repetitive blocked impulses w/ consistent PR interval.
Abnl His-Purkinje system due to ischemia (AMI), degeneration of
conduction system, infiltrative disease, inflammation/AoV surgery.
AVB usually improves w/ carotid sinus massage, worsens w/ atropine.
Often progresses to 3 AVB. Pacing wire or PPM often required.
3	 (complete)
No AV conduction. Escape, if present, narrow (jxnal) or wide (vent.)
Nb, if 2:1 block, cannot distinguish Type I vs. II 2 AVB (no chance to observe PR prolongation); usually catego-
rize based on other ECG & clinical data. High-grade AVB usually refers to block of 2 successive impulses.
AV dissociation 
‚Ä¢ Default: slowing of SA node allows subsidiary pacemaker (eg,AV junction) to take over
‚Ä¢ Usurpation: acceleration of subsidiary pacemaker (eg,AV junctional tach,VT)
‚Ä¢ 3 AV block: atrial pacemaker unable to capture ventricles, subsidiary pacemaker emerges
distinguish from isorhythmic dissociation (A V rate, ‚à¥some P‚Äôs non-conducting)
SUPRAVENTRICULAR TACHYCARDIAS (SVTS)
Arise above the ventricles, ‚à¥narrow QRS unless aberrant conduction or pre-excitation.
Etiologies of SVT (NEJM 1995;332:162; 2006;354:1039)
Type
Features
Sinus tachycardia (ST)
Caused by pain, fever, hypovolemia, hypoxia,
anemia, anxiety, -agonists, etc.
SA node reentrant tachycardia
Rare. Reentrant loop w/in SA node, discern
(SANRT)
from ST by rapid onset & termination.
Atrial tachycardia (AT)
Originate at site in atria other than SA node.
Seen w/ CAD, COPD, c catechols, EtOH, dig.
Multifocal atrial tachycardia (MAT)
c automaticity at multiple sites in the atria
Atrial flutter (AFL)
Macroreentry, usually w/in tricuspid annulus
Atrial fibrillation (AF)
Wavelets irregularly passing down AVN, often
originate from the pulmonary veins
AV nodal reentrant tach (AVNRT)
Reentrant circuit using dual pathways w/in AVN
Atrioventricular reciprocating
Reentrant circuit using AVN and accessory
tachycardia (AVRT)
pathway. Orthodromic (conducts down AVN;
usually narrow QRS) vs. antidromic (conducts 
down accessory path; wide QRS).
Nonparoxysmal junctional
c automaticity at AV junction
tachycardia (NPJT)

Seen in myo/endocarditis, cardiac surg, IMI, dig.

ACCESSORY PATHWAYS (WOLFF-PARKINSON-WHITE)
Definitions
‚Ä¢ Accessory pathway (bypass tract) of conducting myocardium connect-
ing atria & ventricles, allowing impulses to bypass normal AVN delay
‚Ä¢ Preexcitation (WPW) pattern: T PR interval, c QRS width w/ 
wave (slurred onset, can be subtle), ST & Tw abnl (can mimic old IMI);
only seen w/ pathways that conduct antegrade (if pathway only conducts
retrograde then ECG will be normal during SR;‚Äúconcealed‚Äù bypass tract)
‚Ä¢ WPW syndrome: accessory pathway  paroxysmal tachycardia
Diagnosis of SVT Type (NEJM 2006;354:1039)
Onset
Abrupt onset/offset suggests reentry (AVNRT,AVRT, SANRT)
Rate
Not diagnostic as most SVTs can range from 140‚Äì250 bpm, but:
ST usually 150 bpm;AFL often conducts 2:1 S ventricular rate 150 bpm
AVNRT & AVRT are usually 150 bpm
Rhythm
Irregular S AF,AFL w/ variable block, or MAT
P wave
Before QRS S ST, AT (P different from sinus), MAT (3 morphologies)
morphology
After QRS & inverted in inf. leads S retrograde atrial activation via AVN
AVNRT: buried in or distort terminal portion of QRS (pseudo RSR‚Äô in V1)
AVRT: slightly after but usually distinct from QRS
Usually short RP interval (1/2 RR), but can be long RP 
Fibrillation or no P waves S AF
Saw-toothed ‚ÄúF‚Äù waves (best seen in inferior leads & V1) S AFL
Response to
c automaticity rhythms (ST,AT, MAT) S slow rate or c AV block
vagal stim.
AVN reentry (AVNRT,AVRT) S abruptly terminate (classically with a P
or adenosine
wave after last QRS) or no response
AFL S c AV block S unmasking of ‚ÄúF‚Äù waves

Tachycardias
‚Ä¢ Orthodromic AVRT: narrow-complex SVT (typically), conducting T AVN & c accessory
pathway; requires retrograde conduction and ‚à¥can occur w/ concealed bypass tracts
‚Ä¢ Antidromic AVRT: wide-complex SVT, conducting T accessory pathway & c AVN;
requires antegrade conduction and ‚à¥should see WPW pattern during SR
‚Ä¢ AF with rapid conduction down accessory pathway, ‚à¥wide-complex irregular SVT;
requires antegrade conduction and ‚à¥should see WPW pattern during SR
Treatment
‚Ä¢ AVRT: vagal maneuvers, B, ? CCB; caution w/ adenosine (can precip. AF); have defib-
rillator ready
‚Ä¢ AF/AFL w/ conduction down accessory pathway: need to Rx arrhythmia and c pathway
refractoriness; use procainamide, ibutilide, flecainide or cardiovert; avoid CCB & B
(ineffective) and dig/adenosine (can T refractoriness of pathway S c vent. rate SVF)
‚Ä¢ Long term: Rx tachycardias w/ radiofrequency ablation or antiarrhythmics (IA, IC)
consider pathway ablation if asx but AVRT or AF inducible on EPS (NEJM 2003;349:1803)
risk of SCD related to how short R-R interval is in AF and if SVT inducible w/ exercise
WIDE-COMPLEX TACHYCARDIAS (WCTS)
Etiologies
‚Ä¢ Ventricular tachycardia (VT)
‚Ä¢ SVT conducted with aberrancy: either fixed BBB, rate-dependent BBB (usually
RBBB), conduction via an accessory pathway, or atrially-triggered ventricular pacing 
Monomorphic ventricular tachycardia (MMVT)
‚Ä¢ All beats look similar; predominantly upward in V1  RBBB-type vs. downward  LBBB-type
‚Ä¢ Etiologies in a structurally abnormal heart: prior MI (scar); CMP; myocarditis;
arrhythmogenic RV CMP (ARVC): incomplete RBBB,
 wave (terminal notch in QRS) & TWI in V1‚Äì3 on resting
ECG, LBBB-type VT, dx w/ MRI (Lancet 2009;373:1289)
‚Ä¢ Etiologies in a structurally normal heart:
RVOT VT: normal resting ECG, LBBB-type VT w/ inferior axis;
idiopathic LV VT (responds to verapamil)
Polymorphic ventricular tachycardia (PMVT)
‚Ä¢ QRS morphology changes from beat to beat
‚Ä¢ Etiologies: ischemia; CMP; catecholaminergic;
torsades de pointes (TdP, ‚Äútwisting of the points‚Äù, PMVT  c QT): c QT acquired
(eg, meds, lytes, see ‚ÄúECG‚Äù) or congenital (K/Na channelopathies; Tw abnl; TdP trig-
gered by sympathetic stim [exercise, emotion, sudden loud noises]; Lancet 2008;372:750) 
Brugada syndrome (Na channelopathy): pseudo-RBBB
w/ STE in V1‚Äì3 (provoked w/ IA or IC) on resting ECG
Diagnostic clues that favor VT (assume until proven o/w)
‚Ä¢ Prior MI, CHF, or LV dysfunction best predictors that WCT is VT (Am J Med 1998;84:53)
‚Ä¢ Hemodynamics and rate do not reliably distinguish VT from SVT
‚Ä¢ MMVT is regular, but initially it may be slightly irregular, mimicking AF w/ aberrancy;
grossly irregularly irregular rhythm suggests AF w/ aberrancy
‚Ä¢ ECG features that favor VT (Circ 1991;83:1649)
AV dissociation (independent P waves, capture or fusion beats) proves VT
very wide QRS (140 ms in RBBB-type or 160 in LBBB-type); extreme axis deviation
QRS morphology atypical for BBB
RBBB-type: absence of tall R‚Äô (or presence of monophasic R) in V1, r/S ratio 1 in V6
LBBB-type: onset to nadir 60‚Äì100 ms in V1, q wave in V6
concordance (QRS in all precordial leads w/ same pattern/direction)
Long-term management (JACC 2006;48:1064)
‚Ä¢ Workup: echo to ‚úìLV fxn, cath or stress test to r/o ischemia, ? MRI and/or RV bx to
look for infiltrative CMP or ARVC, ? EP study to assess inducibility
‚Ä¢ ICD: 2 prevention after documented VT/VF arrest (unless due to reversible cause)
1 prevention if high-risk, eg, EF 30‚Äì35% (see ‚ÄúHeart Failure‚Äù), ? ARVD, ? Brugada,
? certain long QT syndromes, severe HCMP
‚Ä¢ Medications: -blockers (espec for LQTS), antiarrhythmics (eg, amiodarone) to suppress
recurrent VT, triggering ICD firing, or if not ICD candidate, anti-tachycardic pacing
‚Ä¢ If med a/w TdP S QT 500 	VPBs: d/c med, replete K, give Mg, 	 pacing (JACC 2010;55:934)
‚Ä¢ Radiofrequency ablation if isolated VT focus, or if recurrent VT triggering ICD 
firing; ablation before ICD implantation T discharge rate by 40% (Lancet 2010;375:31)

ATRIAL FIBRILLATION
Classification (JACC 2006;48:e149)
‚Ä¢ Paroxysmal (self-terminating) vs. persistent (sustained 7 d) vs. permanent (typically
1 y and when cardioversion has failed or is foregone)
‚Ä¢ Valvular (rheumatic MV disease, prosthetic valve, or valve repair) vs. nonvalvular
‚Ä¢ Lone AF  age 60 y and w/o clinical or echo evidence of cardiac disease (including HTN)
Epidemiology and Etiologies (Annals 2008;149:ITC5-2)
‚Ä¢ 1% of population has recurrent AF (8% of elderly); mean age at presentation 75 y 
‚Ä¢ Acute (up to 50% w/o identifiable cause)
Cardiac: CHF, myo/pericarditis, ischemia/MI, hypertensive crisis, cardiac surgery
Pulmonary: acute pulmonary disease or hypoxia (eg, COPD flare, pneumonia), PE
Metabolic: high catecholamine states (stress, infection, postop, pheo), thyrotoxicosis
Drugs: alcohol (‚Äúholiday heart‚Äù), cocaine, amphetamines, theophylline, caffeine
Neurogenic: subarachnoid hemorrhage, ischemic stroke
‚Ä¢ Chronic: c age, HTN, ischemia, valve dis. (MV,TV,AoV), CMP, hyperthyroidism, obesity
Pathophysiology (NEJM 1998;339:659; & Circ 1995;92:1954)
‚Ä¢ Commonly originates from ectopic foci in atrial ‚Äúsleeves‚Äù in the pulmonary veins
‚Ä¢ Loss of atrial contraction S HF; LA stasis S thromboemboli; tachycardia S CMP
Evaluation
‚Ä¢ H&P, ECG, CXR, echo (LA size, ? thrombus, valves, LV fxn, pericardium), K, Mg, FOBT
before anticoag,TFTs, ? r/o ischemia (AF unlikely due to ischemia in absence of other sx)

SYNCOPE
1-37
SYNCOPE
Definition
‚Ä¢ Symptom of sudden transient loss of consciousness due to global cerebral hypoperfusion
‚Ä¢ If CPR or cardioversion required, then SCD and not syncope (different prognosis)
Etiologies (NEJM 2002;347:878; JACC 2006;47:473; Eur Heart J 2009;30:2631)
‚Ä¢ Neurocardiogenic (a.k.a. vasovagal, 20%; NEJM 2005;352:1004): c sympathetic tone S
vigorous contraction of LV S mechanoreceptors in LV trigger c vagal tone (hyperactive
Bezold-Jarisch reflex) S T HR (cardioinhibitory) and/or T BP (vasodepressor)
cough, deglutition, defecation & micturition S c vagal tone and thus can be precipitants
related disorder: carotid sinus hypersensitivity
‚Ä¢ Orthostatic hypotension (10%)
hypovolemia, diuretics, deconditioning
vasodilators (espec. if combined w/  chronotropes)
autonomic neuropathy (1  Parkinson‚Äôs, Shy-Drager, Lewy body dementia, POTS;
2  diabetes, EtOH, amyloidosis, renal failure) (NEJM 2008;358:615)
‚Ä¢ Cardiovascular
Arrhythmia (15%)
Bradyarrhythmias: SSS, high-grade AV block,  chronotropes, PPM malfunction
Tachyarrhythmias:VT, SVT (syncope rare unless structural heart disease or WPW)
Mechanical (5%)
Endocardial:AS, MS, PS, prosthetic valve thrombosis, myxoma
Myocardial: pump dysfxn from MI or outflow obstruction from HCMP (but usually VT)
Pericardial: tamponade
Vascular: PE, PHT, aortic dissection, ruptured AAA, subclavian steal
‚Ä¢ Neurologic (10%): seizure (technically not syncope),TIA/CVA, vertebrobasilar
insufficiency, dissection of cerebral arteries, migraine, narcolepsy
‚Ä¢ No cause identified in 40% of cases
‚Ä¢ Misc. causes of LOC (but not syncope): hypoglycemia, hypoxia, anemia, psychogenic
Workup (etiology cannot be determined in 40% of cases)
‚Ä¢ H&P incl. orthostatic VS have highest yield and most cost effective (Archives 2009;169:1299)
‚Ä¢ History (from Pt and witnesses if available)
activity and posture before the incident
precipitating factors: exertion (AS, HCMP, PHT), positional  (orthostatic hypotension),
stressors such as sight of blood, pain, emotional distress, fatigue, prolonged standing,
warm environment, N/V, cough/micturition/defecation/swallowing (neurocardiogenic),
head turning or shaving (carotid sinus hypersens.); arm exercise (subclavian steal)
prodrome (eg, diaphoresis, nausea, blurry vision): cardiac 5 sec, vasovagal 5 sec
associated sx: chest pain, palp., neurologic, post-ictal, bowel or bladder incontinence
(convulsive activity for 10 sec may occur with transient cerebral hypoperfusion)
‚Ä¢ PMH: prior syncope, previous cardiac or neurologic dis.; no CV disease at baseline S 5%
cardiac, 25% vasovagal; CV disease S 20% cardiac, 10% vasovagal (NEJM 2002;347:878)
‚Ä¢ Medications
vasodilators: -blockers, nitrates,ACEI/ARB, CCB, hydralazine, phenothiazines, antidep.
diuretics;  chronotropes (eg, -blockers and CCB)
proarrhythmic or QT prolonging: class IA, IC or III antiarrhythmics, et al. (see ‚ÄúECG‚Äù)
psychoactive drugs: antipsychotics,TCA, barbiturates, benzodiazepines, EtOH
‚Ä¢ Family history: CMP, SCD
‚Ä¢ Physical exam
VS including orthostatics ( if supine S standing results in 20 mmHg T SBP,
10 mmHg T DBP, or 10‚Äì20 bpm c HR), BP in both arms
cardiac: HF (c JVP, displ. PMI, S3), murmurs, LVH (S4, LV heave), PHT (RV heave, c P2)
vascular exam: ‚úìfor asymmetric pulses, carotid bruits, carotid sinus massage
neurologic exam: focal findings, evidence of tongue biting; fecal occult blood test
‚Ä¢ ECG (abnormal in 50%, definitively identifies cause of syncope in 10%)
sinus bradycardia, sinus pauses,AVB, BBB, SVT,VT
ischemic changes (new or old); atrial or ventricular hypertrophy
markers of arrhythmia: ectopy, c QT, preexcitation (WPW), Brugada,  wave (ARVC)
Other diagnostic studies (consider ordering based on results of H&P and ECG)
‚Ä¢ Ambulatory ECG monitoring: if suspect arrhythmogenic syncope
Holter monitoring (continuous ECG 24‚Äì48 h): useful if frequent events
arrhythmia  sx (4%); asx but signif. arrhythmia (13%); sx but no arrhythmia (17%)
Event recorder (activated by Pt to record rhythm strip): useful for infrequent events, but
problematic if no prodrome; yield 20‚Äì50% over 30‚Äì60 d of monitoring

--- Fim da P√°gina 49 ---

Loop recorders (continuously save rhythm strip and ‚à¥can be activated after an
event): useful for infrequent events including w/o prodrome (Mayo Clin Proc 2008;83:1280).
Implantable loop recorders (inserted SC; can record up to 3 y): useful for very
infrequent events; yield 90% after 1 y (AJC 2003;92:1231)
‚Ä¢ Echo: r/o structural heart disease (eg, CMP [incl HCMP & ARVD], valvular disease
[incl AS, MS, MVP], myxoma, amyloid, PHT, 	 anomalous coronaries)
‚Ä¢ ETT: esp. w/ exertional syncope; r/o ischemia- or catecholamine-induced arrhythmias
‚Ä¢ Cardiac catheterization: consider if noninvasive tests suggest ischemia
‚Ä¢ Electrophysiologic studies (EPS)
consider if arrhythmia detected, if structural heart disease, or if CAD (esp. with low EF)
50% abnl (inducible VT, conduction abnormalities) if heart disease, but ? significance
3‚Äì20% abnl if abnl ECG; 1% abnl if normal heart and normal ECG (Annals 1997;127:76)
‚Ä¢ Tilt table testing (provocative test for vasovagal syncope): r/o other causes first
 in 50% w/ recurrent unexplained syncope; Se 26‚Äì80%, Sp 90%; reprod. 80%
‚Ä¢ Cardiac MRI: helpful to dx ARVC if suggestive ECG, echo (RV dysfxn), or  FH of SCD
‚Ä¢ Neurologic studies (cerebrovascular studies, CT, MRI, EEG): if H&P suggestive; low yield

‚Ä¢ Vasovagal syncope: ? midodrine, fludrocortisone, disopyramide, SSRI

‚Ä¢ ‚úìstate driving laws and MD reporting requirements. Consider appropriateness of Pt
involvement in exercise/sport, operating machinery, high-risk occupation (eg, pilot).

VVI

DDD

Cardiac Resynch Therapy (CRT) / Biventricular (BiV) Pacing (JACC 2008;51:2085)
‚Ä¢ 3-lead pacemaker (RA, RV, coronary sinus); R S in V1 suggests appropriate LV capture

‚Ä¢ Goal: terminate VT/VF w/ shock or burst of pacing, prevent sudden cardiac death (SCD)
‚Ä¢ Patient selection (JACC 2008;51:2085)
2 prevention: survivors of VF arrest, unstable VT w/o reversible cause (NEJM

‚Ä¢ ICD discharge: ‚úìdevice to see if appropriate; r/o ischemia; 6 mos driving ban; if recur-
rent VT, ? drug Rx (eg, amio  B, JAMA 2006;295:165) or VT ablation (NEJM 2007;357:2657).
Nb, ablation at time of ICD T risk of VT by 40% (Lancet 2010;375:31).

Pre-operative testing and therapy
‚Ä¢ ECG if 1 risk factor and planned vascular surgery, or if known vascular disease and

‚Ä¢ Coronary revascularization should be based on standard indications (eg,ACS,

‚Ä¢ Given need for dual antiplatelet Rx after stenting, wait 4 wk after BMS and ideally

‚Ä¢ If possible, wait 4‚Äì6 wk after MI (even if  ETT or  ETT & revascularized). If no

‚Ä¢ Preop statins: T ischemia & CV events in Pts undergoing vascular surg (NEJM 2009;361:980)

‚Ä¢ Conflicting evidence regarding efficacy of B for T periop events. Some studies have
shown T cardiac death & MI (NEJM 1996;335:1713 & 1999;341:1789) whereas another T MI
but c death & stroke and c bradycardia/HoTN (Lancet 2008;371;1839).
‚Ä¢ Consider periop B if CAD,  stress test, or 1 cardiac risk factor, espec if vascular surgery  
‚Ä¢ Ideally initiate 1 wk prior to surgery and titrate during preop, intraop, and postop peri-
ods to achieve HR 55‚Äì65 bpm and BP control. Avoid bradycardia and hypotension.
