ASTHMA
2-2
ASTHMA
Definition and epidemiology
‚Ä¢ Chronic inflam. disorder w/ airway hyperrespons.  var. airflow obstruction
‚Ä¢ Affects 5% population; 85% of cases by age 40 y
Clinical manifestations (NEJM 2001;344:350)
‚Ä¢ Classic triad  wheezing, cough, and dyspnea; others include chest tightness,
sputum; symptoms typically chronic with episodic exacerbation
‚Ä¢ Precipitants (triggers)
respiratory irritants (smoke, perfume, etc.) & allergens (pets, dust mites, pollen, etc.)
infections (URI, bronchitis, sinusitis)
drugs (eg, ASA & NSAIDs via leukotrienes, B via bronchospasm, MSO4 via histamine)
emotional stress, cold air, exercise
‚Ä¢ Exacerbations: important to note frequency, severity, duration, and required treatment
(need for steroids, ED visits, hospitalizations, and intubations)
Physical examination
‚Ä¢ Wheezing and prolonged expiratory phase
‚Ä¢ Presence of nasal polyps, rhinitis, rash S allergic component
‚Ä¢ Exacerbation S c RR, c HR, accessory muscle use, diaphoresis, pulsus paradoxus
Diagnostic studies
‚Ä¢ Peak exp flow (PEF): 60 L/min c after bronchodil or 20% diurnal variation sug-
gests asthma. 80% personal best c/w poor control, 50% c/w severe exacerbation.
‚Ä¢ Spirometry: T FEV1, T FEV1/FVC, coved flow-volume loop; lung volumes:  c RV & TLC
 bronchodilator response (c FEV1 12%) strongly suggestive of asthma
methacholine challenge (T FEV1 20%) if PFTs nl: Se 90% (AJRCCM 2000;161:309)
‚Ä¢ Sputum: eos 3% has 86% Se, 88% Sp; can also see Curschmann‚Äôs spirals (mucus casts
of distal airways) and Charcot-Leyden crystals (eosinophil lysophospholipase);
 in sputum eos count may guide outPt Rx (Lancet 2002;360:1715)
‚Ä¢ Allergy suspected S consider ‚úìserum IgE, eos, skin testing/RAST 
Ddx (‚Äúall that wheezes is not asthma‚Äù)
‚Ä¢ Hyperventilation & panic attacks
‚Ä¢ Upper airway obstruction or inh foreign body; laryngeal/vocal cord dysfxn (eg, 2	 to GERD)
‚Ä¢ COPD, bronchiectasis; ILD (including sarcoidosis); vasculitis; PE
‚Ä¢ CHF
‚ÄúAsthma plus‚Äù syndromes (Lancet 2002;360:1313)
‚Ä¢ Atopy

‚Ä¢ ASA-sensitive asthma (Samter‚Äôs syndrome)

‚Ä¢ ABPA

‚Ä¢ Churg-Strauss 
 asthma  eosinophilia  granulomatous vasculitis
‚ÄúReliever‚Äù medications (used prn to quickly relieve sx)
‚Ä¢ Short-acting inhaled 2-agonists: albuterol Rx of choice; levoalbuterol (R-isomer) 2x
potency, no outcome benefit, ? less tachycardia (J Allergy Clin Immunol 2008;122:544)
‚Ä¢ Inhaled anticholinergics (ipratropium) improve 2-agonist delivery S c bronchodilation
‚ÄúController‚Äù meds (taken daily to keep control) (NEJM 2009;360:1002)
‚Ä¢ Inh corticosteroids: Rx of choice (JAMA 2001;285:2583). PRN ? as good as daily for mild
asthma (NEJM 2005;352:1519 & 2007;356:2040). PO steroids may be needed for severely
uncontrolled asthma, but avoid if possible b/c systemic side effects.
‚Ä¢ Long-acting inh 2-agonists (eg, salmeterol): improves PEF when added to inh steroid
(Lancet 2009;374:1754). Except for control of exercise-induced asthma, should not be
used w/o inh steroid (may c mortality; Chest 2006;129:15 & Annals 2006;144:904). Clinical
relevance of 2-receptor pharmacogenetic interaction not validated 
(Lancet 2009;374:1754).
‚Ä¢ Nedocromil/cromolyn: limited use in adults. Useful in young Pts, exercise-induced
bronchospasm; ineffective unless used before trigger or exercise exposure.
‚Ä¢ Theophylline: useful in hard to control Pts, PO convenience, but high side effect pro-
file
‚Ä¢ Leukotriene modifiers: some Pts very responsive, especially aspirin-sensitive
(AJRCCM 2002;165:9) and exercise-induced  asthma (Annals 2000;132:97).Transcription of
genes for 5-lipoxygenase pathway predicts response (Nat Genet 1999;22:168).
‚Ä¢ Anti-IgE: allergic asthma (c IgE) uncontrolled on inh steroids (NEJM 2006;354:2689), not
cost-effective for most cases of severe asthma (JACI 2007;120:1146)

Other
‚Ä¢ Behavior modification: identify and avoid triggers
‚Ä¢ Immunotherapy (eg, desensitization): may be useful if significant allergic component
‚Ä¢ TNF antagonists may be helpful in Pts w/ refractory asthma (NEJM 2006;354:697)
‚Ä¢ Anti-IL5 found to spare steroids in uncontrolled Pts w/ sputum eos (NEJM 2009;360:985)
‚Ä¢ Bronchial thermoplasty (exp‚Äôtal): radiofrequency destruction of airway smooth muscle
no  in FEV1, but T in sx and # of exacerbations (NEJM 2007;356:1327)
‚Ä¢ PPI: not found to improve asthma sx, even if asx GERD (NEJM 2009;360:1487)
Principles of treatment
‚Ä¢ Education and avoidance of environmental triggers for all Pts
‚Ä¢ Use quick-relief rescue medication as needed for all Pts
‚Ä¢ Goal is to achieve complete control

HEMOP/SPN
2-7
HEMOPTYSIS
Definition and pathophysiology
‚Ä¢ Expectoration of blood or blood-streaked sputum
‚Ä¢ Massive hemoptysis: 600 mL/24‚Äì48 h;gas exchange more important than blood loss
‚Ä¢ Massive hemoptysis usually from tortuous or invaded bronchial arteries
Etiologies
Infection/
Bronchitis (most common cause of trivial hemoptysis)
Inflammation
Bronchiectasis incl. CF (common cause of massive hemoptysis)
Tuberculosis or aspergilloma (can be massive)
Pneumonia or lung abscess
Neoplasm
Usually primary lung cancer, sometimes metastasis (can be massive)
Cardiovascular
PE (can be massive), pulmonary artery rupture (2	 to instrumentation),
CHF, mitral stenosis, trauma/foreign body, bronchovascular fistula
Other
Vasculitis (Wegener‚Äôs, Goodpasture‚Äôs, Beh√ßet‚Äôs; can be massive),
AVM, anticoagulation (w/ underlying lung disease), coagulopathy,
cocaine, idiopathic pulmonary hemosiderosis, catamenial (lung 
endometriosis)
(Crit Care Med 2000;28:1642)
Diagnostic workup
‚Ä¢ Localize bleeding site
Rule out GI or ENT source by exam, history; may require endoscopy
Pulmonary source: determine whether unilateral or bilateral, localized or
diffuse, parenchymal or airway by CXR or chest CT, bronchoscopy if necessary
‚Ä¢ PT, PTT, CBC to rule out coagulopathy
‚Ä¢ Sputum culture/stain for bacteria, fungi, and AFB; cytology to r/o malignancy
‚Ä¢ ANCA, anti-GBM, urinalysis to ‚úìfor vasculitis or pulmonary-renal syndrome
Treatment
‚Ä¢ Mechanism of death is asphyxiation not exsanguination; maintain gas exchange, reverse
coagulation and treat underlying condition; cough supp. may c risk of asphyxiation
‚Ä¢ Massive hemoptysis: put bleeding side dependent; selectively intubate nl lung if needed
Angiography: used for Dx & Rx (vascular occlusion balloons or selective embolization
of bronchial circulation)
Rigid bronchoscopy: allows more interventional options (electrocautery, laser) than flex.
Surgical resection

Initial evaluation
‚Ä¢ History: h/o cancer, smoking, age (30 y 
 2% malignant, 15% ea. decade 30)
‚Ä¢ CT: size/shape, Ca, LAN, effusions, bony destruction, c/w old studies
‚àÖCa S c likelihood malignant; laminated S granuloma; ‚Äúpopcorn‚Äù S hamartoma
Diagnostic studies
‚Ä¢ PET: detects metab. activity of tumors, 97% Se & 78% Sp for malig. (espec if 8 mm)
also useful for surgical staging b/c may detect unsuspected mets (Lancet 2001;2:659)

‚Ä¢ Transthoracic needle biopsy: if tech. feasible, 97% will obtain definitive tissue dx

OBSTRUCTIVE SLEEP APNEA (OSA)
Definition and pathophysiology
‚Ä¢ Repetitive pharyngeal collapse during sleep causing apnea (10 s) or hypopnea (airflow
reduction)  desaturation, arousals from sleep S daytime sleepiness
‚Ä¢ Apnea-hypopnea index (AHI) 
 avg # apneas and hypopneas per hr of sleep
‚Ä¢ Sleep-induced loss of activity of pharyngeal dilator muscles S pharyngeal collapse S
arousal S activation of sympathetic nervous system; phenotypes vary across OSA Pts
‚Ä¢ Apnea S negative intrathoracic pressure S c preload, c afterload S HTN, CV sequelae
‚Ä¢ Risk factors: obesity (present in 70%), male, age, alcohol, smoking, black race
Clinical manifestations (Lancet 2002;360:237)
‚Ä¢ Snoring, witnessed apneas/gasping, daytime sleepiness
‚Ä¢ Cardiovascular
HTN (JAMA 2000;283:1829; NEJM 2000;342:1378)
Associated with c risk of stroke and death (NEJM 2005;353:2034) and possibly CAD and
CHF (AJRCCM 2001;163:19)
‚Ä¢ Neurocognitive
T cognitive performance, T QOL
c MVA and work accidents (NEJM 1999;340:847; AJRCCM 2001;164:2031)
Diagnosis and treatment
‚Ä¢ Polysomnography (sleep study); can do home-testing. If , trial of CPAP.
‚Ä¢ CPAP: TT apnea/hypopnea, T BP (Lancet 2002;359:204), T sleepiness, c performance (AJRCCM
2001;164:608), c EF in Pts with heart failure (NEJM 2003;348:1233)
‚Ä¢ Oral appliances can prevent retroglossal collapse. Offer if refusing CPAP.
‚Ä¢ Avoid alcohol and sedatives
‚Ä¢ Surgery (eg, uvulopalatopharyngoplasty, UPPP) of limited benefit (Chest 1997;111:265)

WORKUP OF ILD
Rule out mimickers of ILD
‚Ä¢ Congestive heart failure (‚úìBNP, trial of diuresis)
‚Ä¢ Infection: viral, atypical bacterial, fungal, mycobacterial, parasitic
‚Ä¢ Malignancy: lymphangitic carcinomatosis, bronchoalveolar, leukemia, lymphoma

‚Ä¢ CXR and high-resolution chest CT: reticular, nodular, or ground glass pattern

‚Ä¢ Serologies: ‚úìACE,ANA, RF, ANCA, anti-GBM, HIV

‚Ä¢ Hypersensitivity pneumonitides (organic dusts): loose, noncaseating granulomas

PLEURAL EFFUSION
Pathophysiology
‚Ä¢ Systemic factors (eg, c PCWP, T oncotic pressure) S transudative effusion
‚Ä¢ Local factors (ie,  pleural surface permeability) S exudative effusion
Transudates
‚Ä¢ Congestive heart failure (40%): 80% bilateral,  cardiomegaly on CXR
occasionally exudative (especially after aggressive diuresis or if chronic), but 75% of
exudative effusions in CHF Pts found to have non-CHF cause (Chest 2002;122:1518)
‚Ä¢ Constrictive pericarditis (knock on exam, calcification or thickening on imaging)
‚Ä¢ Cirrhosis (‚Äúhepatic hydrothorax‚Äù): diaphragmatic defect w/ passage of ascitic fluid
often right-sided (2‚ÅÑ3) & massive (even w/o marked ascites)
‚Ä¢ Nephrotic syndrome: usually small, bilateral, asymptomatic (r/o PE b/c hypercoag)
‚Ä¢ Other: PE (usually exudate), malignancy (lymphatic obstruction), myxedema, CAPD
Exudates
‚Ä¢ Lung parenchymal infection (25%)
bacterial (parapneumonic): can evolve along spectrum of exudative (but sterile) S
fibropurulent (infected fluid) S organization (fibrosis & formation of rigid pleural peel).
Common causes: Strep pneumo, Staph aureus, Strep milleri, Klebsiella, Pseudomonas,
Haemophilus, Bacteroides, Peptostreptococcus, mixed flora in aspiration pneumonia.
mycobacterial: 50% lymphs 80% of the time, ADA 40, pleural bx 70% Se
fungal, viral (usually small), parasitic (eg, amebiasis, echinococcosis, paragonimiasis)
‚Ä¢ Malignancy (15%): primary lung cancer most common, metastases (especially breast,
lymphoma, etc.), mesothelioma (‚úìserum osteopontin levels; NEJM 2005;353:15)
‚Ä¢ Pulmonary embolism (10%): effusions in 40% of PEs; exudate (75%)  transudate
(25%); hemorrhagic‚Äîmust have high suspicion b/c presentation highly variable
‚Ä¢ Collagen vascular disease: RA (large), SLE (small),Wegener‚Äôs, Churg-Strauss
‚Ä¢ Gastrointestinal diseases: pancreatitis, esophageal rupture, abdominal abscess
‚Ä¢ Hemothorax (Hcteff/Hctblood 50%): trauma, PE, malignancy, coagulopathy, leaking aortic

‚Ä¢ Chylothorax (triglycerides 110): thoracic duct damage due to trauma, malignancy, LAM
‚Ä¢ Other: post-CABG: left-sided; initially bloody, clears after several wks
Dressler‚Äôs syndrome (pericarditis & pleuritis post-MI), uremia, post-radiation therapy

Drug-induced (eg, nitrofurantoin, methysergide, bromocriptine, amiodarone):  eos

VENOUS THROMBOEMBOLISM (VTE)
Definitions
‚Ä¢ Proximal deep venous thrombosis (DVT): thrombosis of popliteal, femoral, or iliac veins
(nb,‚Äúsuperficial‚Äù femoral vein part of deep venous system)
‚Ä¢ Pulmonary embolism (PE): thrombosis originating in venous system and embolizing
to pulmonary arterial circulation; 1 case/1,000 person y; 250,000/y (Archives 2003;163:1711)
Risk factors 
‚Ä¢ Virchow‚Äôs triad for thrombogenesis
stasis: bed rest, inactivity, CHF, CVA w/in 3 mo, air travel 6 h (NEJM 2001;345:779)
injury to endothelium: trauma, surgery, prior DVT, inflammation
thrombophilia:APC resistance, protein C or S deficiency, APS, prothrombin gene
mutation,c factor VIII, hyperhomocysteinemia, HIT, OCP, HRT, tamoxifen, raloxifene
‚Ä¢ Malignancy (12% of ‚Äúidiopathic‚Äù DVT/PE)
‚Ä¢ History of thrombosis (greater risk of recurrent VTE than genetic thrombophilia)
‚Ä¢ Statin therapy T risk (NEJM 2009;360:1851)

Risk stratification for Pts with PE
‚Ä¢ Clinical: hypotension and/or tachycardia (30% mortality), hypoxemia
‚Ä¢ CTA: RV / LV dimension ratio 0.9 (Circ 2004;110:3276)
‚Ä¢ Biomarkers: c troponin (Circ 2002;106:1263), c BNP (Circ 2003;107:1576) a/w c mortality
‚Ä¢ Echocardiogram: RV dysfxn (controversial in absence of hypotension)
Treatment of VTE (Chest 2008;133;454S; NEJM 2008;359:2804)
‚Ä¢ LE DVT: calf or proximal S anticoagulate (even if asx)
‚Ä¢ UE DVT: anticoagulate (same guidelines as LE). If catheter-associated, need not
remove if catheter fxnal and ongoing need for catheter
‚Ä¢ Superficial venous thrombosis: anticoagulate (especially if extensive clot) as 10%
experience thromboembolic event w/in 3 mo (Annals 2010;152:218)
‚Ä¢ Acute anticoagulation (initiate immediately if high clinical suspicion!)

No need to monitor anti-factor Xa unless concern re: dosing (eg, renal insuffic)
Attractive option as outPt bridge to long-term oral anticoagulation

Direct thrombin inhibitors (eg, argatroban, lepirudin) used in HIT  Pts

Consider if extensive (eg, iliofemoral) acute DVT

PULMONARY HYPERTENSION (PHT)
PA mean pressure 25 mm Hg at rest or 30 mm Hg with exertion
Pathobiology (NEJM 2004;35:1655)
‚Ä¢ Smooth muscle & endothelial cell proliferation: c VEGF, ET-1, 5-HT; T PGI2, NO,VIP;
mutations in bone morphogenic protein receptor 2 (BMPR2; gene involved in prolif. &
apoptosis) seen in 50% familial and 26% sporadic cases of PPH (NEJM 2001;345:319) 
‚Ä¢ Imbalance between vasoconstrictors and vasodilators

‚Ä¢ In situ thrombosis: c TXA2, 5-HT, PAI-1; T PGI2, NO,VIP, tissue plasminogen activator

MECHANICAL VENTILATION
Indications
‚Ä¢ Improve gas exchange
c oxygenation
c alveolar ventilation and/or reverse acute respiratory acidosis
‚Ä¢ Relieve respiratory distress
T work of breathing (can account for up to 50% of total oxygen consumption)
T respiratory muscle fatigue
‚Ä¢ Apnea, airway protection, pulmonary toilet
Choosing settings (NEJM 2001;344:1986)
1. Choose method (including potentially noninvasive ventilation, see later)
2. Pick ventilator mode, and (if appropriate) volume targeted or pressure targeted
3. Set or ‚úìremaining variables (eg, FiO2, PEEP, I:E time, flow, airway pressures)
Step 1: Pick Ventilator Mode
Mode
Description
Assist control
Vent delivers a minimum number of supported breaths
(AC)
Additional Pt-initiated breaths trigger fully-assisted vent breaths
‚à¥Vent-triggered breaths identical to Pt-triggered breaths
Tachypnea S ? respiratory alkalosis, breath-stacking, & auto-PEEP
May be pressure targeted or volume targeted
Synchronized
Vent delivers min. # supported breaths (synch. to Pt‚Äôs efforts)
intermittent
Additional Pt-initiated breaths S VT determined by Pt‚Äôs efforts
mandatory vent
‚à¥Vent-assisted breaths Z spontaneous breaths
(SIMV)
Must overcome resp. circuit during spont. breaths S c fatigue
SIMV

Pressure support
Support Pt-initiated breaths w/ a set inspiratory pressure & PEEP
vent (PSV)
A mode of partial vent support because no set rate
Continuous
Pt breathes spont. at his/her own rate while vent maintains constant
positive airway
positive airway pressure throughout respiratory cycle
pressure (CPAP)
(7 cm H2O overcomes 7 Fr ETT)

Step 2: Choose Volume Targeted or Pressure Targeted
Volume 
Vent delivers a set VT
targeted
Airway pressures depend on airway resist. & lung/chest wall compliance
Benefit: c control over ventilation (ideal initial ventilator setting);
evidence-based benefit in ALI/ARDS; easy to measure mechanical
respiratory properties (PIP, Pplat, airway resistance, compliance)
Risk: Patient at risk for c pressures S barotrauma (and volutrauma if set
volume too high!)
Volume control (VC) : in AC or SIMV mode, vent delivers variable pressure 
(depending on real-time lung compliance) to achieve set VT. Volume support 
(VS) sample principle in spontaneous mode.
Pressure 
Vent delivers a fixed inspiratory pressure regardless of VT
targeted
VT depends on airway resistance and lung/chest wall compliance
Benefit: May c patient comfort (PSV) requiring less sedation
Risk: Pt at risk for T volumes S inadequate VE
Other
Proportional assist ventilation (PAV): vent delivers variable pressure 
(depending on real-time lung mech.) to achieve targeted % of work of 
breathing
Airway pressure release ventilation (APRV): vent keeps lungs inflated at high 
pressure w/ intermittent release to allow for exhalation via passive recoil
Neurally adjusted ventilator assist (NAVA): vent support proportional to 
diaphragmatic electrical activity sensed using esophageal electrode
General 
Institutional/practitioner preference and patient comfort usually
principles
dictate ventilator strategy; no strategy has proven superior
Common reasons for  include: dyssynchrony, poor gas exchange,  mech.
resp properties, goals of care (eg, sedation, weaning, lung protection)
Alarms can be set for c volumes and c airway pressures in pressure-
targeted and volume-targeted strategies, respectively

Tailoring the ventilator settings
‚Ä¢ To improve oxygenation: options include c FiO2, c PEEP
First, c FiO2. If 0.6 and oxygenation remains suboptimal, then try c PEEP:
If c PaO2/FiO2 and Pplat stable, suggests recruitable lung (ie, atelectasis). Continue to
c PEEP until either can  T FiO2 to 0.6 or Pplat 30 cm H2O. If PEEP 20 & FiO2 1.0
and oxygenation remains suboptimal, consider rescue/expt strategies (see ARDS).
If c PEEP S ‚àÖ or T PaO2/FiO2 or c PaCO2, suggests additional lung not recruitable
and instead overdistending lung S c shunt & dead space; ‚à¥T PEEP
‚Ä¢ To improve ventilation: c VT or inspiratory pressure, c RR (may need to T I time). Nb,
tolerate c PaCO2 (permissive hypercapnia) in ALI/ARDS (qv) as long as pH 7.15.

Weaning from the ventilator
‚Ä¢ Weaning strategy: spontaneous breathing trial (SBT) for Pts who meet screening 
criteria (qv) better than gradual weaning of PSV or SIMV (NEJM 1995;332:345). Daily
awakening (d/c all sedation; pass if open eyes & w/o: agitation, RR 35, SaO2
88%, resp distress or arrhythmias) followed by SBT better than SBT alone 
(Lancet 2008;371:126).
‚Ä¢ Identify Pts who can breathe spontaneously (NEJM 1991;324:1445 & 1996;335:1864)
screening criteria: sedation reversed, VS stable, minimal secretions, adequate cough,
cause of respiratory failure or previously failed SBT reversed
vent parameters: PaO2/FiO2 200, PEEP 5, f/VT 105, VE 12 L/min, VC 10 mL/kg
rapid shallow breathing index (f/VT) 105 predicts failure; NPV 0.95 (NEJM 1991;324:1445)
‚Ä¢ SBT

failure if: deteriorating ABGs, c RR, c or T HR, c or T BP, diaphoresis, anxiety
‚Ä¢ Tolerate SBT S extubation. Fail SBT S ? cause S work to correct S retry SBT qd
Complications
‚Ä¢ Barotrauma and volutrauma (eg, PTX, pneumomediastinum)
high PIPs usually safe unless c Pplat (30 cm H2O, but lower better) S alveolar damage
‚Ä¢ Oxygen toxicity (theoretical); proportional to duration  degree of c oxygen (FiO2 0.6)
‚Ä¢ Alterations in cardiac output (eg, PEEP can T preload S hypotension)
‚Ä¢ Ventilator-associated pneumonia (1%/day, mortality rate 30%)
typical pathogens: MRSA, Pseudomonas, Acinetobacter, and Enterobacter species
preventive strategies (AJRCCM 2005;171:388): wash hands, semirecumbent position, 
non-nasal intub, enteral nutrition rather than TPN, routine suction of subglottic
secretions, avoid unnecessary abx & transfusions, routine oral antiseptic, 
stress-ulcer prophylaxis w/ ? sucralfate (TVAP, c GIB) vs. H2RA/PPI, ? silver-coated
tubes (JAMA 2008;300:805)
‚Ä¢ Laryngeal
edema: for Pts vent 36 h; ? predicted by  cuff leak test. Methylprednisolone 20 mg IV
q4h starting 12 h pre-extub S TT edema and 50% T in reintubation (Lancet 2007;369:1003)
ulceration: consider tracheostomy for patients in whom expect 14 d of mech. vent S
T duration mech. vent, T # ICU days (BMJ 2005;330:1243); no benefit to performing at
1 wk vs. waiting until 2 wk (JAMA 2010;303:1483)

ARDS
2-22
ACUTE RESPIRATORY DISTRESS SYNDROME
Definition & Presentation (NEJM 2000;342:1334; American-Euro Consensus Conf 1994)
‚Ä¢ Acute onset (24 h)
‚Ä¢ Bilateral patchy airspace disease (need not be diffuse)
‚Ä¢ Noncardiogenic pulmonary edema (PCWP 18 or no clinical evidence of c LAP)
‚Ä¢ Severe hypoxemia: PaO2/FiO2 200 S ARDS; PaO2/FiO2 300 S ALI (acute lung injury)
‚Ä¢ Chest CT: heterogeneous lung with densities greater in dependent areas
‚Ä¢ Lung bx: classically shows diffuse alveolar damage (DAD); bx not required but often
provides useful dx information (Chest 2004;125:197)
Pathophysiology
‚Ä¢ c intrapulmonary shunt S hypoxemia (‚à¥Rx w/ PEEP to prevent derecruitment)
‚Ä¢ c increased dead space fraction (see appendix), predicts c mort. (NEJM 2002;346:1281)
‚Ä¢ T compliance: VT/(Pplat ‚Äì PEEP) 50 mL/cm H2O
Etiologies
Direct Injury
Indirect Injury
‚Ä¢ Pneumonia (40%)
‚Ä¢ Inhalation injury
‚Ä¢ Sepsis (25%) ‚Ä¢ Pancreatitis

SEPSIS
2-23
SEPSIS
Definitions
Systemic 
2 or more of the following:
Inflammatory Response 
(1) Temp 38 or 36	C,
Syndrome (SIRS)
(2) HR 90,
(3) RR 20 or PaCO2 32,
(4) WBC 12,000 or 4000 or 10% bands
Sepsis
SIRS  suspected infection
Severe Sepsis
Sepsis  organ dysfunction, hypoperfusion or hypotension
Signs of hypoperfusion may incl. lactic acidosis, oliguria, MS
Septic Shock
Sepsis-induced hypotension despite adequate fluid 
resuscitation, along with signs of hypoperfusion
(Chest 1992;101;1644)
Fluids & Vasoactive Drugs
‚Ä¢ Early goal-directed therapy (‚ÄúRivers Protocol‚Äù, NEJM 2001;345:1368)
Insert arterial & central venous lines (NEJM 2007;356:e21; PAC not needed) and ‚úìMAP,
CVP, & central venous (no need for mixed venous) O2 sat
Target MAP 65 mm Hg, CVP 8‚Äì12 mm Hg, & UOP 0.5 mL/kg/h using fluid (eg,
500 mL NS q30min) and vasopressors as needed
Target ScvO2 70% using PRBCs & inotropes (dobutamine, c dose as needed q15min)
When done w/in first 6 h for severe sepsis & septic shock, 42% T mortality
‚Ä¢ Normal saline as good as albumin for resuscitation (NEJM 2004;350:2247)
‚Ä¢ Norepinephrine preferred to dopamine (T arrhythmias & trend T mort.; NEJM 2010;362:779)
‚Ä¢ Vasopressin added to low-dose norepinephrine not superior to high-dose norepineph-
rine (NEJM 2008;358:877); consider if HoTN refractory to catecholamine vasopressors
‚Ä¢ Use PRBC w/ caution, may c mortality/morbidity, c risk of ARDS (Crit Care Med
2005;33:1191); ‚à¥goal Hb 7 unless active cardiac ischemia (NEJM 1999;340:409)
‚Ä¢ After early resuscitation, if ALI/ARDS, target CVP 4‚Äì6 mm Hg as additional fluids
may be harmful S c ventilator/ICU days (NEJM 2006;354:2564; Chest 2008;133:252)
‚Ä¢ Pulse pressure variation 13% with respiration S likely volume-responsive (Chest
2008;133:252); only validated in passive, intubated Pts
Antibiotics
‚Ä¢ If possible, obtain 2 sets of BCx before starting abx
‚Ä¢ Start empiric IV abx w/in 1 h of recognition of severe sepsis or septic shock
‚Ä¢ Typically want broad gram-positive and gram-negative coverage, including MRSA and
highly resistant gram-negative bacilli  anaerobes
Steroids (NEJM 2003;348:727; JAMA 2009;301:2362)
‚Ä¢ ACTH stimulation test helps predict mortality in sepsis, does not predict benefit
from corticosteroid therapy (JAMA 2000;283:1038; NEJM 2008;358:111)
‚Ä¢ Earlier study showed possible mortality benefit w/in 8 h of severe septic shock (SBP 90 for
1 h despite fluids & pressors) if post ACTH stim cortisol   9 g/dL (JAMA 2002;288:862)
‚Ä¢ No mortality benefit to early (72 h) empiric corticosteroids in all Pts w/ septic
shock, regardless of ACTH stim; faster resolution of shock, more superinfection
(NEJM 2008;358:111)
‚Ä¢ ? hydrocortisone 50‚Äì100 q6‚Äì8h  fludrocortisone 50 g daily in septic shock refrac-
tory to fluids & pressors, regardless of ACTH stim (Crit Care Med 2008;36:296)
Activated Protein C
‚Ä¢ Remains controversial: 6% absolute T mort., but c bleeding (NEJM 2001;344:699); no mort.
benefit if low risk of death (APACHE 25 or single organ failure, NEJM 2005;353:1332)
‚Ä¢ ? if APACHE 25 or multi-organ failure w/o contraindic. (Crit Care Med 2008;36:296)
Intensive Glycemic Control
‚Ä¢ No evidence of improved outcomes in MICU population w/ intensive glycemic control
‚Ä¢ Intensive glycemic control to goal 80‚Äì110 mg/dL in surgical ICU population S
mortality benefit, greatest if 3-d ICU stay (NEJM 2001;345:1359)
‚Ä¢ More recently, intensive glycemic control S either no  or c increased mortality, and
definite c hypoglycemia (JAMA 2008;300:933; NEJM 2006;354:449; 2008;358:125; 2009;360:1283)
‚Ä¢ Reasonable to keep glc 150 mg/dL in severe sepsis, using validated protocol (Crit Care
Med 2008;36:296)

Achalasia
‚Ä¢ Etiologies: idiopathic (most common), pseudoachalasia (due to GE jxn tumor), Chagas
‚Ä¢ Sx: dysphagia (solid & liquid), chest pain (1‚ÅÑ3 of Pts), regurgitation

GASTROESOPHAGEAL REFLUX DISEASE (GERD)
Pathophysiology
‚Ä¢ Excessive transient relaxations of lower esophageal sphincter (LES) or incompetent LES
‚Ä¢ Mucosal damage (esophagitis) due to prolonged contact w/ acid can evolve to stricture
‚Ä¢ Risk factors: hiatal hernia, obesity, gastric hypersecretory states, delayed emptying 
‚Ä¢ Precipitants: supine position, fatty foods, caffeine, alcohol, cigarettes, CCB, pregnancy
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Esophageal: heartburn, atypical chest pain, regurgitation, water brash, dysphagia
‚Ä¢ Extraesophageal: cough, asthma (often poorly controlled), laryngitis, dental erosions
Diagnosis (Gastro 2008;135:1383)
‚Ä¢ Based on hx and empiric trial of PPI (Se & Sp: 78% & 54%) (Annals 2004;140:518)
‚Ä¢ EGD if failure to respond to BID PPI or alarm features: dysphagia, odynophagia, vomit-
ing, wt loss, Fe-defic anemia,  FOBT, palpable mass or adenopathy, age 55 y
‚Ä¢ If dx uncertain & EGD nl S high res manometry w/ 24-h esophageal pH monitoring
Treatment (NEJM 2008;359:1700)
‚Ä¢ Lifestyle: avoid precipitants, lose weight, avoid large & late meals, elevate head of bed
‚Ä¢ Medical: PPI (up to BID)  H2RA, espec. if esophagitis (Coch 2007;2:003244), antacids
PPI achieve relief in 80‚Äì90%; side effects: diarrhea, H/A, c risk of C. diff & hip fx
‚Ä¢ Surgical: fundoplication if refractory sx on meds: success 90%, but 1‚ÅÑ2 on meds
after 10 y

DYSPEPSIA (‚ÄúINDIGESTION‚Äù)

‚Ä¢ Upper abdominal sx: discomfort, pain, fullness, bloating, burning

PEPTIC ULCER DISEASE (PUD)
Epidemiology & Etiologies (Lancet 2009;374:1449)
‚Ä¢ Lifetime prevalence 10%, but incidence T‚Äôing b/c T incidence of H. pylori and potent acid
suppression Rx. However, incidence of hospitalization for complications un‚Äôd (in fact c
in elderly; likely 2 to c NSAID use).
‚Ä¢ H. pylori infection: 80% of duodenal ulcers (DU) and 60% of gastric ulcers (GU)
50% of population colonized w/ H. pylori, but only 5‚Äì10% will develop PUD
‚Ä¢ ASA & NSAIDs: 45% erosions, 15‚Äì30% GU, 0.1‚Äì4% UGIB
‚Ä¢ Hypersecretory states (often mult. recurrent ulcers): gastrinoma (Zollinger-Ellison
syndrome, also p/w diarrhea, 1% of PUD), carcinoid, mastocytosis 
‚Ä¢ Malignancy: 5‚Äì10% of GU
‚Ä¢ Other: smoking, stress ulcers (if CNS process  ‚ÄúCushing‚Äôs‚Äù; if burn  ‚ÄúCurling‚Äôs‚Äù),
XRT, chemo, CMV or HSV (immunosupp), bisphosphonates; steroids alone not a
risk factor, but may exacerbate NSAID-induced ulceration
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Epigastric abdominal pain: relieved with food (DU) or worsened by food (GU)
‚Ä¢ Complications: UGIB, perforation & penetration, gastric outlet obstruction
Diagnostic studies
‚Ä¢ Test for H. pylori
Serology: Se 80%, Sp 90%; not useful to confirm erad. as can stay  wks to y
Stool antigen: Se & Sp 90%; use to confirm erad.; high false  in acute GIB
EGD  rapid urease test (Se & Sp 95%) or histo: false  if on abx, bismuth, PPI 
‚Ä¢ EGD req to def make dx; consider if fail empiric Rx or alarm features; bx GUs to r/o malig;
relook in 6‚Äì12 wks if apparently benign ulcer is lg or complicated or sx persist despite Rx
Treatment (NEJM 2010;362:1597)
‚Ä¢ If H. pylori , eradicate:
Triple Rx: clarith 500 bid  amox 1 g bid  PPI bid 	 10‚Äì14 d (but c clarith resist rates) 
Quadruple Rx: MNZ TCN  bismuth  PPI (H. pylori resist to clarith or amox allergy)
Sequential Rx (PPI  abx 	 5 d S PPI  2 different abx 	 5 d): erad. rates 90%
emerging as poss 1st-line Rx (Annals 2008;148:923)
Besides PUD, test & Rx if: gastric MALT lymphoma, atrophic gastritis, FHx gastric ca 
‚Ä¢ If H. pylori : gastric acid suppression w/ PPI
‚Ä¢ Discontinue ASA and NSAIDs; add PPI
‚Ä¢ Lifestyle changes: d/c smoking and probably EtOH; diet does not seem to play a role
‚Ä¢ Surgery: if refractory to med Rx (1st r/o NSAID use) or for complications (see above)
Prophylaxis if ASA/NSAID required (JACC 2008;52:1502)

GIB
3-3
GASTROINTESTINAL BLEEDING
Definition
‚Ä¢ Intraluminal blood loss anywhere from the oropharynx to the anus
‚Ä¢ Classification: upper  above the ligament of Treitz; lower  below the ligament of Treitz
‚Ä¢ Signs: hematemesis  blood in vomitus (UGIB); hematochezia  bloody stools
(LGIB or rapid UGIB); melena  black, tarry stools from digested blood (usually
UGIB, but can be anywhere above and including the right colon)
Etiologies of upper GI bleed (UGIB)
‚Ä¢ Peptic ulcer disease (50%): H. pylori, NSAIDs, gastric hypersecretory states
‚Ä¢ Varices (10‚Äì30%): esophageal  gastric, 2 to portal HTN. If isolated gastric S r/o
splenic vein thrombosis.
‚Ä¢ Gastritis/gastropathy/duodenitis (15%): NSAIDs,ASA, alcohol, stress, portal
hypertensive 
‚Ä¢ Erosive esophagitis/ulcer (10%): GERD, XRT, infectious (CMV, HSV, or Candida if
immunosuppressed), pill esophagitis (bisphosphonate, NSAIDs;  odynophagia)
‚Ä¢ Mallory-Weiss tear (10%): GE junction tear due to retching against closed glottis
‚Ä¢ Vascular lesions (5%)
Dieulafoy‚Äôs lesion: superficial ectatic artery usually in cardia S sudden, massive UGIB
AVMs, angioectasias, hered. hemor. telangectasia: submucosal, anywhere in GI tract
Gastric antral vascular ectasia (GAVE): ‚Äúwatermelon stomach,‚Äù tortuous, dilated ves-
sels; a/w cirrhosis, atrophic gastritis, CREST
Aorto-enteric fistula: AAA or aortic graft erodes into 3rd portion of duodenum;
p/w ‚Äúherald bleed‚Äù; if suspected, diagnose by endoscopy or CT
‚Ä¢ Neoplastic disease: esophageal or gastric carcinoma, GIST
‚Ä¢ Oropharyngeal bleeding and epistaxis S swallowed blood
Etiologies of lower GI bleed (LGIB)
‚Ä¢ Diverticular hemorrhage (33%): 60% of diverticular bleeding localized to right colon
‚Ä¢ Neoplastic disease (19%): usually occult bleeding, rarely severe
‚Ä¢ Colitis (18%): infectious, ischemic, radiation, inflammatory bowel disease (UC  CD)
‚Ä¢ Angiodysplasia (8%): most commonly located in ascending colon and cecum
‚Ä¢ Anorectal (4%): hemorrhoids, anal fissure, rectal ulcer
‚Ä¢ Other: post-polypectomy, vasculitis 
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ UGIBLGIB: N/V, hematemesis, coffee-ground emesis, epigastric pain, vasovagal, melena
‚Ä¢ LGIBUGIB: diarrhea, tenesmus, BRBPR, hematochezia (11% UGIB; Gastro 1988;95:1569)
Initial Management
‚Ä¢ Assess severity: tachycardia suggests 10% volume loss, orthostatic hypotension 
20% loss, shock 30% loss
‚Ä¢ Resuscitation: placement of 2 large-bore (18-gauge or larger) intravenous lines
Volume replacement: NS or LR to achieve normal VS, UOP, & mental status
‚Ä¢ Transfuse: blood bank sample for type & cross; use O-neg if emergent 
Transfuse 2‚Äì8 U and target Hct 25‚Äì30 depending on comorbidities
‚Ä¢ Reverse coagulopathy: FFP & vit K to normalize PT; plts to keep count 50,000
‚Ä¢ Triage: consider ICU if unstable VS or poor end organ perfusion
Intubation for emergent EGD, if ongoing hematemesis, shock, poor resp status,  MS
? OutPt management if SBP 
110, HR 100, Hb 
13 () or 
12 (), BUN 18,
‚àÖmelena, syncope, heart failure, liver disease (Lancet 2009;373;42)
Workup
‚Ä¢ History: where (anatomic location) & why (etiology)
acute or chronic, prior GIB, # of episodes, other GI dx 
hematemesis, vomiting prior to hematemesis (Mallory-Weiss), melena, hematochezia
abdominal pain, wt loss, anorexia,  in stool caliber
ASA/NSAIDs, clopidogrel, anticoagulants, known coagulopathy
alcohol (gastritis, varices), cirrhosis, known liver disease, risk factors for liver disease
abdominal/rectal radiation, history of cancer, prior GI or aortic surgery
‚Ä¢ Physical exam: VS most important, orthostatic s, JVP
localizable abd tenderness, peritoneal signs, masses, LAN, signs of prior surgery
signs of liver disease (hepatosplenomegaly, ascites, etc.)
rectal exam: masses, hemorrhoids, anal fissures, stool appearance, color, occult blood 
pallor, jaundice, telangiectasias (alcoholic liver disease or hered. hemor. telangectasia)
‚Ä¢ Laboratory studies: Hct (may be normal in first 24 h of acute GIB before equilibration) 
T 2‚Äì3% S 500 mL blood loss; low MCV S Fe deficient and chronic blood loss; plt, PT,

CLOSTRIDIUM DIFFICILE
Pathogenesis
‚Ä¢ Ingestion of C. difficile spores S colonization when colonic flora d by abx or 
chemo S release of toxin A/B S colonic mucosal necrosis & inflammation 
S pseudomembranes
‚Ä¢ c toxigenic strain (NAP-1/027) c mort. & length of hosp. (esp in elderly) (NEJM 2008;359:1932)
‚Ä¢ Additional risk factors: elderly, nursing home residents, IBD, ? PPI exposure
Clinical manifestations (a spectrum of disease)
‚Ä¢ Asx colonization: 3% healthy adults; 20% in hospitalized patients on antibiotics
‚Ä¢ Acute watery diarrhea (occ bloody)  mucus, often w/ lower abd pain, fever, ccc WBC
‚Ä¢ Pseudomembranous colitis: above sx  pseudomembranes  bowel wall thickening
‚Ä¢ Fulminant colitis (2‚Äì3%): toxic megacolon (colon dilatation 
6 cm on KUB, colonic

‚Ä¢ Stool cytotoxin assay: gold standard, highly Se & Sp, but takes 24‚Äì48 h
‚Ä¢ Consider flex sig if dx uncertain and/or evidence of no improvement w/ standard Rx

‚Ä¢ In everyone: start contact precautions, if possible d/c abx ASAP; stop antimotility agents
‚Ä¢ Mild (6 BM/d, temp 101F, WBC 15 k, no peritoneal sx or SIRS, and age 65 y)

‚Ä¢ Severe (12 BM/d, temp 103F, WBC 25k, c abd pain, sepsis, or no bowel sounds)

toxic megacolon; ? tigecycline (CID 2009;48:1732);Ab CT, urgent surgery consult re:

‚Ä¢ If Pt needs to stay on original abx, continue C. diff. Rx for

CONSTIPATION & ADYNAMIC ILEUS
Constipation (NEJM 2003;349:1360)
‚Ä¢ Definition (Rome III): 
2 of the following during last 3 mo at least 25% of time:
straining, lumpy/hard stools, incomplete evacuation, sensation of anorectal obstruction,
manual maneuvers to facilitate defecation, stool frequency 3 per wk
‚Ä¢ Etiology
Functional: normal transit, slow transit, pelvic floor dysfunction, constipation-predom IBS
Meds: opioids, anticholinergics (TCAs & antipsychotics), Fe, CCB, diuretics, NSAIDs
Obstruction: cancer, stricture, rectocele, anal stenosis, extrinsic compression
Metabolic/endo: DM, hypothyroid, uremia, preg, panhypopit, porphyria, c Ca, T K, T Mg
Neuro: Parkinson‚Äôs, Hirschsprung‚Äôs, amyloid, MS, spinal injury, autonomic neuropathy
‚Ä¢ Diagnosis: H&P w/ DRE. Labs: consider CBC, electrolytes w/ Ca,TSH
Colonoscopy if alarm sx: wt loss,  FOBT, fevers, FHx of IBD or colon cancer.
Sigmoidoscopy if no alarm sx & 50 y/o.
For functional constipation: sitzmark study, anorectal manometry, defecography
‚Ä¢ Treatment: Bulk laxatives (fiber 20 g/d) S osmotic laxative S stimulant laxative
Bulk laxatives (psyllium, methylcellulose, polycarbophil): c colonic residue, c peristalsis 
Osmotic laxatives (Mg, sodium phosphate (avoid if CKD), lactulose): c water in colon 
Stimulant laxatives (senna, castor oil, bisacodyl, docusate sodium): c motility & secretion
Enema/suppository (phosphate, mineral oil, tap water, soapsuds, bisacodyl) 
Lubiprostone (see IBS). Methylnaltrexone for opioid-induced (NEJM 2008;358:2332).
Adynamic ileus
‚Ä¢ Definition: loss of intestinal peristalsis in absence of mechanical obstruction
Ogilvie‚Äôs  acute colonic adynamic ileus in presence of competent ileocecal valve
‚Ä¢ Precipitants: intra-abd process (surgery, pancreatitis, peritonitis); severe medical illness
(eg, PNA, sepsis); intestinal ischemia; meds (opiates, anticholinergics); electrolyte abnl
‚Ä¢ Clinical manifestations: abd. discomfort, N/V, hiccups, abd. distention, T or absent bowel
sounds, no peritoneal signs (unless perforation); cecum 
10‚Äì12 cm S c rupture
‚Ä¢ Dx: supine & upright KUB vs. CTS gas-filled loops of small & large intestine. Must
exclude mechanical obstruction (absence of gas in rectum).
‚Ä¢ Treatment: NPO, mobilize (walk, roll), d/c drugs that T intestinal motility, enemas;
decompression (NGT, rectal tubes, colonoscope); erythromycin, neostigmine

DIVERTICULAR DISEASE
DIVERTICULOSIS
Definition and Pathobiology (Lancet 2004;363:631)
‚Ä¢ Acquired herniations of colonic mucosa and submucosa through the colonic wall 
‚Ä¢ May be a consequence of low-fiber diet S c stool transit time and T stool volume S
c intraluminal pressure S herniation at site of relative muscle weakness where
vasa recta penetrate to supply blood to colonic mucosa and submucosa
Epidemiology 
‚Ä¢ Prevalence higher w/ c age (10% if 40 y; 50‚Äì66% if 80 y);‚ÄúWesternized‚Äù societies 
‚Ä¢ Left side (90%, mostly sigmoid)  right side of colon (except in Asia, where RL)
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Usually asx, but 5‚Äì15% develop diverticular hemorrhage and 10‚Äì25% diverticulitis 
‚Ä¢ Nuts/seeds/popcorn intake in asx diverticulosis does not c risk of 1st case of diverticulitis
or diverticular bleeding (JAMA 2008;300:907)
DIVERTICULITIS
Pathophysiology (NEJM 2007;357:2057)
‚Ä¢ Retention of undigested food and bacteria in diverticulum S fecalith formation S
obstruction S compromise of diverticulum‚Äôs blood supply, infection, perforation
‚Ä¢ Uncomplicated: microperforation S localized infection
‚Ä¢ Complicated (25%): macroperforation S abscess, peritonitis, fistula (65% w/ bladder),
obstruction, stricture
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ LLQ abdominal pain, fever, nausea, vomiting, constipation
‚Ä¢ PEx ranges from LLQ tenderness  palpable mass to peritoneal signs & septic shock
‚Ä¢ Ddx includes IBD, infectious colitis, PID, tubal pregnancy, cystitits, colorectal cancer
Diagnostic studies
‚Ä¢ Plain abdominal radiographs to r/o free air, ileus, or obstruction
‚Ä¢ Abdominal CT (IO): 95% Se & Sp; assess complicated disease (abscess, fistula)
‚Ä¢ Colonoscopy contraindicated acutely c risk of perforation; do 6 wks after to r/o neoplasm
Treatment (Am J Gastro 2008;103:1550)
‚Ä¢ Mild: outPt Rx indicated if Pt has few comorbidities and can tolerate POs
PO abx: (MNZ  FQ) or amox/clav for 7‚Äì10 d; liquid diet until clinical improvement
‚Ä¢ Severe: inPt Rx if cannot take POs, narcotics needed for pain, or complications
NPO, IV fluids, NGT (if ileus)
IV abx (GNR & anaerobic coverage): amp/gent/MNZ or piperacillin-tazobactam
‚Ä¢ Abscesses 4 cm should be drained percutaneously or surgically
‚Ä¢ Surgery: if progression despite med Rx, undrainable abcess, free perforation, or
recurrent disease (2 severe episodes)
‚Ä¢ Colonic stricture: late complication of diverticulitis; Rx w/ endoscopic dilation vs. resection
Prevention
‚Ä¢ Low-fiber diet immediately after acute episode; high-fiber diet when 6 wks w/o sx
‚Ä¢ Consider mesalamine  rifaximin if multiple episodes
‚Ä¢ Risk of recurrence 10‚Äì30% w/in 10 y of 1st episode; more likely 2nd episode complicated
DIVERTICULAR HEMORRHAGE (also see ‚ÄúGastrointestinal Bleeding‚Äù)
Pathophysiology
‚Ä¢ Intimal thickening and medial thinning of vasa recta as it courses over dome of diver-
ticulum S weakening of vascular wall S arterial rupture
‚Ä¢ Diverticula more common in left colon; but bleeding diverticula more often in right colon
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Painless hematochezia/BRBPR, but can have abdominal cramping
‚Ä¢ Usually stops spontaneously (75%) but resolution may occur over hrs‚Äìdays; 20% recur
Diagnostic studies
‚Ä¢ Colonoscopy: rapid prep w/ Go-Lytely via NGT (4‚Äì6 L over 2‚Äì4 h)
‚Ä¢ Arteriography  tagged RBC scan if severe bleeding
Treatment
‚Ä¢ Colonoscopy: epinephrine injection  electrocautery (NEJM 2000;342:78), hemoclip, banding
‚Ä¢ Arteriography: intra-arterial vasopressin infusion or embolization
‚Ä¢ Surgery: if above modalities fail & bleeding is persistent & hemodynamically significant

INFLAMMATORY BOWEL DISEASE
Definition
‚Ä¢ Ulcerative colitis (UC): idiopathic inflammation of the colonic mucosa
‚Ä¢ Crohn‚Äôs disease (CD): idiopathic transmural inflammation of the GI tract, skip areas
‚Ä¢ Indeterminate colitis: in 5‚Äì10% of chronic colitis, cannot distinguish UC vs. CD even w/ bx 
Epidemiology & Pathophysiology (Lancet 2007;369:1627 & NEJM 2009;361:2066)
‚Ä¢ 1.4 million people in US; prev 1:1000 UC and 1:3000 CD; c incidence in Caucasians, Jews
‚Ä¢ Age of onset 15‚Äì30 y in UC and CD; CD is bimodal and has second peak at 50‚Äì70 y
‚Ä¢ Smokers at c risk for CD, whereas nonsmokers & former smokers at c risk for UC
‚Ä¢ Genetic predisposition  disruption of intestinal barrier (epithelial or T immune)   in gut
microbiome S acute inflam w/o immune downregulation or tolerance S chronic inflam
ULCERATIVE COLITIS (Lancet 2007;369:1641)
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Grossly bloody diarrhea, lower abdominal cramps, urgency, tenesmus
‚Ä¢ Severe colitis (15%): progresses rapidly over 1‚Äì2 wks with T Hct, c ESR, fever,
hypotension, 6 bloody BMs per day, distended abdomen with absent bowel sounds
‚Ä¢ Extracolonic (25%): erythema nodosum, pyoderma gangrenosum, aphthous ulcers,
uveitis, episcleritis, thromboembolic events (espec during a flare; Lancet 2010;375:657),
AIHA, seroneg arthritis, chronic hepatitis, cirrhosis, PSC (c risk of cholangiocarcinoma)
Diagnosis
‚Ä¢ Colonoscopy: involves rectum (95%) & extends proximally and contiguously within colon
‚Ä¢ Classify by location: proctitis (25‚Äì55%), left-sided colitis (ie, sigmoid  descending
colon, 50‚Äì70%), and pancolitis (20%)
‚Ä¢ Appearance: granular, friable mucosa with diffuse ulceration; pseudopolyps
‚Ä¢ Microscopy: superficial chronic inflammation; crypt abscess and architectural distortion
Complications
‚Ä¢ Toxic megacolon (5%): colon dilatation (
6 cm on KUB), colonic atony, systemic
toxicity, & c risk of perf. Rx w/ IV steroids & broad-spectrum abx; surgery if fail to
improve w/in 48‚Äì72 h
‚Ä¢ Stricture (5%): occurs in rectosigmoid after repeated episodes of inflammation
Prognosis
‚Ä¢ 50% of Pts in remission at any given time; intermittent exacerbations in 90%; contin-
ual active disease in 18%. Rate of colectomy at 10 y is 24%.
‚Ä¢ Mortality rate of severe UC flare is 2%, & overall life expectancy in UC  non-UC Pts 
CROHN‚ÄôS DISEASE (Lancet 2007;369:1641)
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Smoldering disease with abd pain, fevers, malaise, wt loss
‚Ä¢ Mucus-containing, non-grossly bloody diarrhea; n/v, bloating, obstipation
‚Ä¢ T albumin, c ESR/CRP, T Hct (due to Fe, B12, folate deficiency; chronic inflammation)
‚Ä¢ Extracolonic as in UC 
Diagnosis
‚Ä¢ EGD/Colonoscopy  small bowel imaging (eg, video capsule endoscopy or CT
enterography); CD can affect any portion of GI tract with skip lesions
‚Ä¢ Classify by location: small bowel (47%), ileocolonic (21%), colonic (28%); upper tract rare
‚Ä¢ Appearance: nonfriable mucosa, cobblestoning, aphthous ulcers, deep & long fissures 
‚Ä¢ Microscopy: transmural inflammation with mononuclear cell infiltrate, noncaseat-
ing granulomas (seen in 25% of mucosal biopsies), fibrosis, ulcers, fissures
Complications
‚Ä¢ Perianal disease: fissures, fistulas, perirectal abscesses (up to 30% of Pts)
‚Ä¢ Stricture: small bowel, postprandial abd pain; can lead to complete SBO
‚Ä¢ Fistulas: perianal, enteroenteric, rectovaginal, enterovesicular, enterocutaneous
‚Ä¢ Abscess: fever, tender abd mass, c WBC; steroids mask sx, ‚à¥need high-level suspicion
‚Ä¢ Malabsorption: illeal disease/resection: T bile acids abs S gallstones; T fatty acid abs S
Ca oxalate kidney stones; T fat soluble vitamin abs SVit D deficiency S osteopenia
Prognosis
‚Ä¢ Highly variable: 1 y following dx, 55‚Äì65% in remission, 10‚Äì30% have flared, 15‚Äì25%
have low activity, and 13‚Äì20% have chronic active course 
‚Ä¢ At 20 y, majority will have required some surgery; overall life expectancy is slightly T

MANAGEMENT (Lancet 2007;369:1641 & Gastro 2007;133:1670)
Initial Evaluation 
‚Ä¢ H&P (‚úìfor intestinal & extraintestinal manifestations) and endoscopy as above
‚Ä¢ Laboratory: ESR, CRP, CBC, LFTs, Fe, B12, folate, Vit D. Anti-Saccharomyces cerevisiae
Abs (ASCA) for CD & p-ANCA for UC have low Se, higher Sp; ‚à¥not dx.
‚Ä¢ Exclude other etiologies: infectious/ischemic colitis, med adverse effect, intestinal 
lymphoma/carcinoma, colon cancer, IBS, vasculitis, Behcet‚Äôs, sprue, bacterial overgrowth
‚Ä¢ Rule out infection before treating with immunosuppressants and biologics
Goals of Treatment 
‚Ä¢ Avoid NSAIDs (both UC and CD)
‚Ä¢ Induce remission of acute flare S maintain remission; mucosal healing 1 goal
‚Ä¢ Convention has been step-up Rx (least S most toxic). Recent shift to early and/or combined
immunomodulation to improve disease outcome (Lancet 2008;371:660 & NEJM 2010;362:1383).
Induction of Remission (Acute Flare Treatment)

flexure. PO if more extensive: 2.4‚Äì4.8 g/d mesalamine (released in 
ileum/colon), olsalazine (colon), sulfasalazine (avoid if sulfa allergy).
Steroids
Mod dis. (6 BMs/d w/ minimal systemic tox) S PO prednisone. Severe dis.
(6 BMs/d w/ systemic tox) S IV. PR only in mild distal dis.
Anti-TNF
Mod/Severe flare unresponsive to steroids, flare on immunomodulators.

Surgery
J pouch anal anastamosis; 6% fail; pouchitis: Rx w/ abx, probiotics
Crohn‚Äôs Disease
Abx
FQ/MNZ or amox/clav; best for perianal disease, limited data for colonic CD
Steroids
Mild dis. (tolerating POs, w/o 10% wt loss): consider budesonide w/ ileal dis.
(1st-pass metabolism limits systemic adverse effects)
Mod dis. (wt loss, abd pain, nausea, anemia): PO prednisone
Severe dis. (fever, obstruction, cachexia): IV pred 1 mg/kg
w/o immunomod at 1 y 30% remission, 30% steroid-depend, 40% surgery
Anti-TNF
Indic as above for UC, though early Rx prior to steroid failure gaining favor.
Infliximab (chimeric), adalimumab (humanized), or certolizumab (pegylated).
Inflixi  AZA S 60% steroid-free clin remission by 6 mos (NEJM 2010;362:1383).
Other
Surgery: diverting ileostomy, especially good for perineal disease
anti-41 integrin (natalizumab): rare risk of PML, restricted use in CD
In clinical trials: anti-IL12/23 (ustekinamab), anti-47 integrin (vedolizumab)

Complications of therapy (Clin Gastro Hep 2009:7:874)
‚Ä¢ Anti-TNF: reactivation TB; must doc  PPD prior to Rx. If c LFTs, must exclude viral
hepatitis. Small c‚Äôd risk of NHL. Other: infusion rxn; lupus-like rxn, psoriasis, MS, CHF.
‚Ä¢ 6MP/AZA: BM suppression, lymphoma, pancreatitis, hepatitis; ‚úìTPMT genotype
prior to dosing to T risk of generation of toxic metabolites 
‚Ä¢ 5-ASA: diarrhea, abd pain, pancreatitis
Cancer screening (Gastro 2010;138:738)
‚Ä¢ Colon cancer: risk in UC 2% at 10 y, 8% at 20 y, 18% at 30 y. Similar for colonic
CD, except risk of small bowel cancer as well. Dysplasia best marker for risk. Other
risk factors include: PSC,  FHx, greater extent of disease, stricture, & pseudopolyps.
‚Ä¢ Surveillance: colonoscopy with random bxs 8 y after dx to eval for dysplasia, q1‚Äì3y
thereafter based on risk factors. If high-grade dysplasia or dysplasia assoc. lesion/mass
S colectomy. Chemoprophylaxis: 5-ASA & ursodeoxycholic acid (if PSC) beneficial.

INTEST ISCHEM
3-12
INTESTINAL ISCHEMIA
ACUTE MESENTERIC ISCHEMIA (25%)
Etiologies
‚Ä¢ SMA embolism (50%): from LA (AF), LV (T EF), or valves; SMA most prone to embolism
‚Ä¢ Nonocclusive mesenteric ischemia (25%): transient intestinal hypoperfusion due to
T CO, atherosclerosis, sepsis, drugs that T gut perfusion (pressors, cocaine, dig, diuretic)
‚Ä¢ SMA thrombosis (10%): usually at site of atherosclerosis, often at origin of artery
‚Ä¢ Venous thrombosis (10%): hypercoagulable states, portal hypertension, IBD,
malignancy, inflammation (pancreatitis, peritonitis), pregnancy, trauma, surgery
‚Ä¢ Focal segmental ischemia of the small bowel (5%): vascular occlusion to small
segments of the small bowel (vasculitis, atheromatous emboli, strangulated hernias, XRT)
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Occlusive: sudden abd pain out of proportion to abdominal tenderness on exam
at least initially (2‚Äì4 h) until severe ischemia S frank infarction w/ peritoneal signs 
‚Ä¢ Nonocclusive: abd distension & pain, though up to 25% may be pain-free,  N/V;
often in setting of CHF  h/o chronic mesenteric ischemia sx
‚Ä¢ Hematochezia due to mucosal sloughing (right colon supplied by SMA)
‚Ä¢ ‚ÄúIntestinal angina‚Äù: postprandial abd pain, early satiety, & T wt from gastric vascular ‚Äústeal‚Äù;
may occur wks to mos before onset of acute pain in pts w/ chronic mesenteric ischemia
Physical exam
‚Ä¢ May be unremarkable, or may only show abdominal distention;  FOBT 75% of Pts
‚Ä¢ Bowel infarction suggested by peritoneal signs (diffuse tenderness, rebound, guarding)
Diagnostic studies
‚Ä¢ Dx relies on high level of suspicion; rapid dx essential to avoid infarction (occurs w/in h)
‚Ä¢ Laboratory: often nl; 75% c WBC; c amylase & LDH; 50% acidosis w/ c lactate (late)
‚Ä¢ KUB: nl early before infarct;‚Äúthumbprinting,‚Äù ileus, pneumatosis in later stages
‚Ä¢ CT angiogram (arterial phase imaging): non-invasive test of choice; can detect
thrombi in mesenteric vessels, colonic dilatation, bowel wall thickening, pneumatosis/
portal venous gas; venous phase imaging for dx of mesenteric vein thrombosis
‚Ä¢ Angiography: gold standard; potentially therapeutic; indicated if suspect occlusion
Treatment
‚Ä¢ Fluid resuscitation, optimize hemodynamics (minimize pressors); broad-spectrum abx
‚Ä¢ Emergent surgery for prompt resection of necrotic bowel if evidence of peritonitis
‚Ä¢ Anticoagulation for arterial & venous thrombosis and embolic disease
‚Ä¢ Papaverine (vasodilator) catheter-directed infusion into SMA, typically in nonocclu-
sive ischemia when spasm is considered the primary cause of the ischemia
‚Ä¢ SMA embolism: consider fibrinolytics; if no quick improvement S surgical
embolectomy if possible, o/w aortomesenteric bypass
‚Ä¢ SMA thrombosis: percutaneous or surgical revascularization (J Vasc Surg 2009;50:341)
‚Ä¢ Nonocclusive: correct underlying cause (especially cardiac)
‚Ä¢ Consider angioplasty/stent vs. surg revasc in cases of chronic mesenteric ischemia if:

2 vessels or occl SMA, supportive clinical hx, & other etiologies for abd pain excluded
Prognosis
‚Ä¢ Mortality 20 to 70% if bowel infarcted; dx prior to infarction strongest predictor of survival
ISCHEMIC COLITIS (75%)
Definition and pathophysiology
‚Ä¢ Non-occlusive disease 2 to s in systemic circulation or anatomic/fxnal s in local
mesenteric vasculature; often underlying etiology unknown, frequently seen in elderly 
‚Ä¢ ‚ÄúWatershed‚Äù areas (splenic flexure & recto-sigmoid) most susceptible, 25% involve R side
Clinical manifestations, diagnosis, and treatment
‚Ä¢ Disease spectrum: reversible colopathy (35%), transient colitis (15%), chronic ulcerating
colitis (20%), stricture (10%), gangrene (15%), fulminant colitis (5%)
‚Ä¢ Usually p/w cramping LLQ pain w/  FOBT or overtly bloody stool; fever
and peritoneal signs should raise clinical suspicion for infarction
‚Ä¢ Dx: r/o infectious colitis; consider flexible sig/colonoscopy if sx persist and no alternative
etiology identified (only if peritonitis not present, o/w avoid overdistension of colon)
‚Ä¢ Treatment: bowel rest, IV fluids, broad-spectrum abx, serial abd exams; surgery for
infarction, fulminant colitis, hemorrhage, failure of medical Rx, recurrent sepsis, stricture
‚Ä¢ Resolution w/in 48 h w/ conservative measures occurs in 50% of cases

PANCREATITIS
Pathogenesis
‚Ä¢ Acinar injury via direct or indirect toxicity S release or impaired secretion (ie, duct
obstruction) of enzymes S autodigestion S fat necrosis
‚Ä¢ Profound acute inflammatory response
Etiologies
‚Ä¢ Gallstones (40%): , usually small stones (5 mm) are culprit, also microlithiasis
‚Ä¢ Alcohol (30%): , usually chronic, w/ acute flares
‚Ä¢ Drugs (occur via hypersensitivity, toxic metab, or direct toxicity): furosemide, thiazides,
sulfa, DDI, asparaginase, estrogen, 6-MP/AZA,ACEI, dapsone, 5-ASA, valproic acid 
‚Ä¢ Obstructive: panc/ampullary tumors, mets (breast, lung), annular pancreas, divisum w/
concurrent minor papilla stenosis, and ascaris 
‚Ä¢ Metabolic: hypertriglyceridemia (TG need to be 1000 and usually 4500; seen w/
type I and type V familial hypertriglyceridemia), hypercalcemia
‚Ä¢ Infections: coxsackie, mumps, EBV, CMV, HAV, HBV, mycoplasma,TB, candida/toxo/crypto
‚Ä¢ Autoimmune: can p/w chronic disease or panc mass; c IgG4,  ANA, duct abnl
‚Ä¢ Ischemia: vasculitis, cholesterol emboli, hypovolemic shock, cardiopulmonary bypass
‚Ä¢ Post ERCP: 5% w/ clinical, overt pancreatitis; 35‚Äì70% with asx c amylase
‚Ä¢ Post trauma: blunt abd trauma, pancreatic/biliary surgery
‚Ä¢ Familial: autosomal dominant w/ variable penetrance (PRSS1, CFTR, SPINK1 genes)
‚Ä¢ Scorpion sting (in Trinidad): mechanism believed to be hyperstimulation of pancreas
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Epigastric abdominal pain, radiating to back, constant, some relief w/ leaning forward
‚Ä¢ Nausea and vomiting
‚Ä¢ Ddx: acute cholecystitis, perforated viscus such as DU, intestinal obstruction, mesen-
teric ischemia, IMI,AAA leak, distal aortic dissection, ruptured ectopic pregnancy
Physical exam
‚Ä¢ Abdominal tenderness and guarding, T bowel sounds (adynamic ileus) 
 palpable abdominal mass;  jaundice if biliary obstruction
‚Ä¢ Signs of retroperitoneal hemorrhage (Cullen‚Äôs  periumbilical; Grey Turner‚Äôs  flank) rare
‚Ä¢ Fever, tachycardia, hypotension  shock
Diagnostic studies (Gastro 2007;132:2022)
‚Ä¢ Laboratory
c amylase: levels 3 	 ULN suggestive of pancreatitis; level Z severity
false : acute on chronic (eg, alcoholic); hypertriglyceridemia (T amylase activity)
false : other abd or salivary gland process, acidemia, renal failure, macroamylasemia 
(amylase binds to other proteins in serum, cannot be filtered out)
c lipase: more specific than amylase
false : renal failure, other abd process, diabetic ketoacidosis, HIV, macrolipasemia
ALT 3 	 ULN suggests gallstone pancreatitis (Am J Gastro 1994;89:1863); A, bili not helpful
Other labs (see Prognosis): c WBC, c or T Hct, c BUN, T Ca, c glc
‚Ä¢ Imaging studies
KUB/CXR: can see ‚Äúsentinel loop‚Äù air in small bowel in LUQ, atelectasis, effusion
Abd CT: not required for dx, but test of choice to make dx. Helps exclude other
dx, stage severity, & r/o complications. CT w/ IV contrast on day 3 of presenta-
tion in severe cases to evaluate for pancreatic necrosis (avoid on presentation
b/c theoretical concern of c necrosis w/ IV contrast; defer if concomitant ARF).
Abd U/S: typically not useful to visualize pancreas (obscured by bowel gas), but help-
ful to investigate biliary etiology, ie, gallstones and BD dilatation; can see pseudocyst
MRI/MRCP: can detect necrosis; also used to assess for stones & ductal disruption
Endoscopic U/S (EUS):limited role acutely;useful for occult biliary disease (microlithiasis)
Treatment (Lancet 2008;371:143)
‚Ä¢ Supportive therapy: in mild cases, bowel rest is usually sufficient
Fluid resuscitation (may need up to 10 L/d if hemodynamically severe pancreatitis)
Nutrition: enteral preferred over TPN if NPO 7 d; T infectious complications &
disease severity, & trend toward T mortality (BMJ 2004;328:1407). Ideally NJ tube, but NG okay.
Analgesia: IV meperidine, morphine (theoretical risk of sphincter of Oddi spasm,
but has not been shown to adversely affect outcome), hydromorphone
‚Ä¢ Prophylactic systemic abx (eg, imipenem) to T mortality & prevent conversion of
sterile necrosis to infected necrosis remains controversial (Am J Surg 2009;197:806 &
Gastro 2007; 132:2022); ? reserve for severe pancreatitis w/ 30% necrosis by CT, & no
more than 14 d
‚Ä¢ Surgery: infected necrosis (qv) nearly always requires debridement. Improved outcomes by
delaying (if possible) surgery 
2 wks to allow organization of necrosis. Cholecystectomy
if gallstones (w/in 48 h if mild, o/w w/in 14 d; Surg 2009;145:260; Ann Surg 2010;251:615)

‚Ä¢ ERCP  sphincterotomy: in acute setting, reserved for severe cholangitis/sepsis and T
bili 5 (ie., presumptive obstructive BD stone). Otherwise, early ERCP does not
reduce risk of local or systemic pancreatitis complications (Ann Surg 2007;245:10).
Complications
‚Ä¢ Systemic: shock, ARDS, renal failure, GI hemorrhage, DIC
‚Ä¢ Metabolic: hypocalcemia, hyperglycemia, hypertriglyceridemia
‚Ä¢ Acute fluid collection (30‚Äì50%): seen early, no capsule, no Rx required
‚Ä¢ Pseudocyst (10‚Äì20%): fluid collection, persists for 4‚Äì6 wks, encapsulated
suggested by persistent pain & elevation of amylase or lipase, or mass on exam
most resolve spont.; if 6 cm or persists 6 wks  pain S endo/perc/surg drainage
‚Ä¢ Sterile pancreatic necrosis (20%): area of nonviable pancreatic tissue
? prophylactic abx (see above); supportive measures, surgery if Pt unstable
‚Ä¢ Infection (5% of all cases, 30% of severe): usually 2 enteric GNR
infected pancreatic necrosis: fever & c WBC not specific; ‚à¥FNA in deteriorating Pt
w/ necrosis (small risk of seeding sterile necrosis); if gram stain/cx  S abx  evacu-
ation (percutaneously, followed by surgical debridement after 4 wks; NEJM 2010;362:1491)
pancreatic abscess: circumscribed collection of pus (usually w/o pancreatic tissue)
treat with abx  drainage (CT-guided if possible), usually seen 
4 wks into course
‚Ä¢ Ascites or pleural effusion: occurs due to disrupted pancreatic duct; consider early ERCP
w/ stent across duct; can also occur from draining pseudocyst
Prognosis (Gastro 2007;132:2022)
‚Ä¢ Severe pancreatitis (20%)  organ failure or local complications (necrosis, pseudocyst) 
‚Ä¢ Scoring systems: HAPS, BISAP,APACHE II, Ranson‚Äôs criteria, CT Severity Index
HAPS: no abd tenderness or rebound on exam plus nl Hct and Cr on admission
predicts non-severe course w/ 98% accuracy (Clin Gas Hep 2009;6:702)
BISAP: 5-point scoring system on admission (BUN 25, GCS 15, SIRS, age 60,
and pleural effusion) identifies Pts at risk for c‚Äôd mortality (Am J Gastro 2009;104:966)
APACHE II (www.mdcalc.com/apache-ii-score-for-icu-mortality): severe if score 
8

LFTS
3-15
ABNORMAL LIVER TESTS
Tests of hepatocellular injury or cholestasis
‚Ä¢ Aminotransferases (AST,ALT): intracellular enzymes released 2 necrosis/inflammation
ALT more specific for liver than is AST (heart, skeletal muscle, kidney, brain, RBC/WBC)
ALT  AST S viral hepatitis or fatty liver/nonalcoholic steatohepatitis (pericirrhotic)
AST:ALT 2:1 S alcoholic hepatitis, cirrhosis, NAFLD; nonhepatic source
ALT/AST 15	 ULN S etiologies of acute liver failure (ccc LDH S ischemia/toxic)
‚Ä¢ Alkaline phosphatase (A): enzyme bound in hepatic canicular membrane
besides liver, also found in bone, intestines, kidney, and placenta
confirm liver origin with: c 5‚Äô-NT, c GGT, or A heat fractionation
c levels seen with biliary obstruction or intrahepatic cholestasis (eg, hepatic infiltration)
Tests of hepatic function
‚Ä¢ Albumin: marker for liver protein synthesis, T slowly in liver failure (t1/2 20 d)
‚Ä¢ Prothrombin time (PT): depends on synthesis of coag factors by the liver (except FVIII);
b/c t1/2 of some of these factors (eg,V,VII) is short, c PT can occur w/in hrs of liver dysfxn
‚Ä¢ Bilirubin: product of heme metabolism carried by albumin to liver where it must be
taken up for conjugation (to make soluble) and then excreted into bile (most sensitive
to detect parenchymal disease); either conjugated (direct) or unconjugated (indirect)
Patterns of liver injury
‚Ä¢ Hepatocellular: cc aminotransferases,  c bilirubin or A
ccc ALT & AST (1000): severe viral hepatitis, acetaminophen, ischemia, Wilson‚Äôs, AIH 
‚Ä¢ Cholestasis: cc A and bilirubin,  c aminotransferases
‚Ä¢ Isolated hyperbilirubinemia: cc bilirubin (direct or indirect), nl A and aminotransferases
‚Ä¢ Infiltrative: c A,  c bilirubin or aminotransferases
‚Ä¢ Jaundice is a clinical sign seen when bilirubin 2.5 mg/dL (especially in sclera or under
tongue); if hyperbilirubinemia conjugated S c urine bilirubin

HEPATITIS
VIRAL
Hepatitis A (ssRNA; accounts for 30‚Äì45% of acute viral hepatitis)
‚Ä¢ Transmission: fecal-oral route; contaminated food, water, shellfish; day-care ctr outbreaks
‚Ä¢ Incubation: 2‚Äì6 wks; no chronic carrier state
‚Ä¢ Sx: T appetite, malaise, fever, N/V, RUQ pain,  jaundice; rarely fulminant
‚Ä¢ Diagnosis: acute hepatitis   IgM anti-HAV; past exposure   IgG anti-HAV ( IgM)
‚Ä¢ Treatment for acute HAV supportive. Prevention: vaccinate children & Pts w/ chronic
HBV, HCV, or other chronic liver disease (2 doses at 0, 6‚Äì12 mos)
‚Ä¢ Postexposure ppx: age 1‚Äì40 y S vaccine; age 1 or 40 y or immunosupp S Ig
Hepatitis B (dsDNA; accounts for 45% of acute viral hepatitis; Lancet 2009;373:582) 
‚Ä¢ Transmission: blood, sexual, perinatal
‚Ä¢ Incubation: 6 wks‚Äì6 mos (mean 12‚Äì14 wks)
‚Ä¢ Acute infxn: 70% subclinical, 30% jaundice, 1% fulminant hepatitis (up to 60% mortality)
‚Ä¢ Chronic infxn: 5% (adult-acquired; higher if immunosupp), 90% (perinatally acquired);
40% chronic carriers S cirrhosis; c risk of cirrhosis if HCV, HDV, or HIV coinfection
‚Ä¢ Risk of hepatocellular carcinoma: 25‚Äì40%; highest risk w/ perinatal transmission & c‚Äôd HBV
DNA; risk of HCC w/ or w/o concurrent cirrhosis. Screen w/ AFP & US vs MRI q6mo.
‚Ä¢ Extrahepatic syndromes: PAN (1%), MPGN, arthritis, dermatitis, PMR
‚Ä¢ Serologic and virologic tests
HBsAg: appears before sx; used to screen blood donors; persists 6 mo  chronic HBV
HBeAg: evidence of viral replication and c infectivity
IgM anti-HBc: first Ab to appear; indicates acute infection
window period  HBsAg become , anti-HBs not yet , anti-HBc only clue to infection
IgG anti-HBc: indicates previous (HBsAg ) or ongoing (HBsAg ) HBV infection
anti-HBe: indicates waning viral replication, T infectivity
anti-HBs: indicates resolution of acute disease & immunity (sole marker after vac)
HBV DNA: presence in serum correlates w/ active viral replication in liver

‚Ä¢ Treatment for acute HBV: supportive; hospitalize for  MS or c INR (liver transplant ctr)
‚Ä¢ Treatment for chronic HBV if: (1) HBeAg  w/ DNA 20,000 IU/mL & elevated ALT;
(2) HBeAg  w/ DNA 2,000 IU/mL & elevated ALT or liver bx demonstrates
stage 2 fibrosis (NEJM 2008;359:1486 & Hep 2009;50;661)
‚Ä¢ PEG IFN-2a: best rate of HBeAg seroconversion at 1 y (27%), low tolerability limits use
‚Ä¢ 1st line is entecavir or tenofovir; well-tolerated & low resistance, HBeAg sero-
conversion at 1 yr is 21%; seroconversion at 3 y for entecavir is 39%; lamivudine
15‚Äì30% resis at 1 y; telbivudine c CK & neuropathy; adefovir (add to lamivudine-
resistant Pts) nephrotoxic & resistance occurs, too
‚Ä¢ Goal: if HBeAg  S HBeAg , anti-HBe ; if HBeAg  or ‚àÖseroconversion S
indefinite
‚Ä¢ If undergo liver transplant: HBIG  nucleoside analog effective in preventing reinfection
‚Ä¢ HIV/HBV coinfection: Rx w/ 2 drugs active against both HBV & HIV (NEJM 2007;356:1445)
‚Ä¢ If inactive carrier scheduled to receive immunosuppresion/chemotherapy S Rx
‚Ä¢ Prevention: vaccinate high-risk Pts (3 doses 0, 1 & 6 mos)
‚Ä¢ Postexposure (risk infxn 30%) ppx: HBIG S vaccine (if unvac. or known nonresponder)
Hepatitis C (RNA; accounts for 10‚Äì30% of acute viral hepatitis; Lancet 2008;372:321)
‚Ä¢ Transmission: blood  sexual; 20% w/o clear precipitant
‚Ä¢ Incubation: 1‚Äì5 mos; mean 6‚Äì7 wks
‚Ä¢ Natural hx
acute infection: 80% subclinical; 10‚Äì20% symptomatic hepatitis w/ jaundice; fulminant
hepatitis very rare; spontaneous clearance in up to 30%
chronic:up to 80% S chronic hepatitis,20‚Äì30% of whom develop cirrhosis (after 20 y)
c risk of cirrhosis in men, EtOH, HIV; HCC in 2‚Äì5% of cirrhotics/y
‚Ä¢ Extrahepatic syndromes: cryoglobulinemia, porphyria cutanea tarda (blistering rash in sun-
exposed areas), MPGN, MGUS, IPF, NHL, and DM
‚Ä¢ Serologic and virologic tests
anti-HCV (ELISA):  in 6 wks,does not  recovery or immunity;can be  after recovery
HCV RNA:  w/in 2 wks, marker of active infection
HCV RIBA: used to confirm  anti-HCV ELISA in Pts w/ undetectable HCV RNA
HCV genotype (1‚Äì4): guides duration & predicts response to Rx (genotype 2,3  1,4)
‚Ä¢ Diagnosis: acute hepatitis   HCV RNA,  anti-HCV 
resolved hepatitis   HCV RNA,  anti-HCV
chronic hepatitis   HCV RNA,  anti-HCV
‚Ä¢ Treatment indications (Hep 2009;49:1335)
Acute: if no spont clearance at 8‚Äì12 wks, consider PEG-IFN	-2a/b 
 12‚Äì24 wks 
Chronic:RNA ,plus bx w/ either chronic hepatitis & fibrosis stage 1 or compensated
liver disease (in genotype 2 or 3, may proceed to Rx w/o bx b/c high response rate)
‚Ä¢ Drugs: PEG-IFN-2a  ribavirin (Gastro 2010;138:108). Goal is sustained virologic
response (SVR)  absence of viremia 24 wks after completion of Rx. PIs (telaprevir,
boceprevir) promising (NEJM 2009;360:1827 & 1839; 2010;362:1292).
‚Ä¢ Genotypes 1 or 4: Rx 48 wks. If early vir resp (EVR) not achieved by wk 12 (ie, RNA
T 2 log) stop Rx, as EVR best predictor of lack of SVR. If partial EVR (RNA T 2
log at 12 wks & undetectable at 24 wks), consider prolonging Rx to 72 wks.
Overall SVR rate 50‚Äì60%.
‚Ä¢ Genotypes 2 or 3: Rx 24 wks; SVR rate 80%
‚Ä¢ Predictors of response: RNA 400k IU/mL, rapid vir resp (‚àÖRNA at wk 4),
‚àÖcirrhosis, , age 40 y, wt 75 kg, white/Hispanic, ‚àÖHIV, SNPs in IL28B
(Nat Gen 2009;41:1100; Gastro 2010;138:2307)
‚Ä¢ Risks of Rx: flu-like sx, psych sx (if depressed can give SSRI), thyroid dysfxn, marrow
suppression (can give EPO & GCSF), hemolysis (ribavirin), sexual dysfxn
‚Ä¢ Contraindic.: decompensated cirrhosis, preg., severe psych illness, active substance
abuse, severe cardiac/pulm disease, uncontrolled DM, seizure d/o, autoimmune disease
‚Ä¢ Vaccinate all chronic HCV patients against HBV and HAV if not immune
‚Ä¢ Postexposure (needlestick risk 3%) ppx: none; if HCV RNA S , consider Rx w/in 3 mos
Hepatitis D (RNA)
‚Ä¢ Transmission: blood or sexual; endemic in Africa & E. Europe
‚Ä¢ Pathogenesis: requires HBV to cause either simultaneous or superimposed infection
‚Ä¢ Natural hx: in HBV c severity of infxn and c progression to cirrhosis; clears w/ HBV 
‚Ä¢ Serologic/virologic tests: anti-HDV; follow HDV RNA during Rx (high relapse rate)
Hepatitis E (RNA)
‚Ä¢ Transmission: fecal-oral; travelers to Pakistan, India, SE Asia, Africa, and Mexico
‚Ä¢ Natural hx: acute hepatitis w/ c mortality (10‚Äì20%) if pregnant; rare chronic in transplant Pts
‚Ä¢ Diagnosis: IgM anti-HEV (through CDC)

AUTOIMMUNE HEPATITIS (AIH)
Classification (NEJM 2006;354:54)
‚Ä¢ Type 1: anti‚Äìsmooth muscle Ab (ASMA), ANA; anti‚Äìsoluble liver antigen (anti-SLA)
a/w more severe disease and relapsing disease
‚Ä¢ Type 2: anti‚Äìliver/kidney microsome type 1 (anti-LKM1); children (age 2‚Äì14 y);
Mediterranean
‚Ä¢
Overlap syndrome: autoimmune hepatitis  PBC or PSC
Diagnosis and Treatment 
‚Ä¢
3‚ÅÑ4 female; 40% present acutely (occ fulminant); 34% asymptomatic; ALT can be 1,000 
‚Ä¢ Extrahepatic syndromes: thyroiditis, arthritis, UC, Sj√∂gren‚Äôs, Coombs,  hemolytic anemia
‚Ä¢ Dx: scoring system combining serologies, c IgG, ‚àÖviral hepatitis, & characteristic liver bx
(lymphoplasmocytic infiltrate & interface hepatitis) has high Sp & mod Se (Hep 2008;48:169)
‚Ä¢ Rx: if LFTs 10	 ULN, or if 5	 ULN w/ IgG 2	 ULN, or bridging/multiacinar necrosis on bx

OTHER CAUSES OF HEPATITIS OR HEPATOTOXICITY
Alcoholic hepatitis (NEJM 2009;360:2758)
‚Ä¢ Sxs: can range from asx hepatomegaly to decompensation w/ ascites, encephalopathy,
and death. AST & ALT usually 300‚Äì500 w/ AST:ALT 2:1, in part b/c concomitant
B6 defic (ALT can be normal); T plt, c iron sat, c‚Äôd Tbili & INR indicate severe hepatitis.
‚Ä¢ Rx: if discriminant fxn ( 4.6 	 [PT-control]  Tb in mg/dL) 32 or encephalopathy
methylprednisolone 32 mg/d 	 4 wks S 4‚Äì6 wk taper; T death (NEJM 1992;326:507)
contraindications: GIB, chronic HBV, sepsis 
pentoxifylline 400 mg tid T mortality due to reduction in HRS (Coch 2009;4:CD007339)
‚Ä¢ Lille model predicts nonresponse to corticosteroids & mortality, powered by Tb from
day 1S7; nonresponders have 6-mo survival of 25% (www.lillemodel.com & Hep
2007;45:1348)
Acetaminophen hepatotoxicity (Clin Liv Dis 2007;11:525 & NEJM 2008;359:285)
‚Ä¢ Normal metabolism via glucuronidation and sulfation S nontoxic metabolites
‚Ä¢ Overdose (usually 10 g): CYP2E1 hydroxylation S reactive electrophilic species
(NAPQI) that are scavenged by glutathione until reserves exhausted S hepatotoxicity
‚Ä¢ CYP2E1 induced by fasting and alcohol allowing for ‚Äútherapeutic misadventure‚Äù in
malnourished alcoholics taking even low doses (2‚Äì6 g) of acetaminophen
‚Ä¢ Liver dysfunction may not be apparent for 2‚Äì6 d
‚Ä¢ Rx: NG lavage, activated charcoal if w/in 4 h. Consider early transfer to transplant ctr.
N-acetylcysteine: administer up to 72 h after ingestion, if time of ingestion
unknown, or if chronic ingestion 4 g per day
Rumack-Matthew nomogram predicts risk of hepatotoxicity from a given
serum level of acetaminophen when time of ingestion is known (see Appendix) 
Low threshold to start NAC even w/ low or undetectable serum acetaminophen levels
PO NAC (preferred): 140 mg/kg loading dose S 70 mg/kg q4h 	 17 additional doses
IV NAC: 150 mg/kg over 1h S 50 mg/kg over 4 h S 100 mg/kg over 16 h;
risk of anaphylaxis; use if unable to tolerate POs, GIB, preg, fulminant hepatic failure
Ischemic hepatitis
‚Ä¢ ‚ÄúShock liver‚Äù w/ AST & ALT 1000  cc LDH; delayed cc Tbili
‚Ä¢ Often requires c venous pressure  T portal/arterial pressure  hypoxia
‚Ä¢ Typically seen in hypotension, sepsis, CHF
Nonalcoholic fatty liver disease (NAFLD) (Gastro 2008;134:1682)
‚Ä¢ Fatty infiltration of the liver and absence of EtOH or other cause of liver disease.
Nonalcoholic steatohepatitis (NASH)  NAFLD  inflammation  fibrosis on liver bx
‚Ä¢ Prevalence: 20‚Äì30% of U.S. population. Risk factors: DM & metabolic syndrome
(hyperinsulinemia, obesity, c TGs), HAART, tamoxifen, amiodarone,TPN, rapid wt loss.
Variants in apolipoprotein C3 gene a/w c TG & risk of NAFLD (NEJM 2010;362:1082).
‚Ä¢ Clinical: 80% asx,ALT AST; NAFLD can progress to cirrhosis in 1‚Äì3% of Pts w/
NAFLD and up to 25% of Pts w/ NASH
‚Ä¢ Dx: U/S, MRI, CT suggest fatty infiltration but liver bx only way to dx NASH vs.
NAFLD
‚Ä¢ Rx: wt loss, glycemic/lipid control; both pioglitazone and vitamin E T steatosis &
inflammation, but not fibrosis (NEJM 2010;362:1675)

LIVER FAILURE
3-20
ACUTE LIVER FAILURE
Definition
‚Ä¢ Acute hepatic disease  coagulopathy  encephalopathy; w/o known pre-existing liver dis.
‚Ä¢ Fulminant  develops w/in 8 wks; subfulminant  develops between 8 wks and 6 mos
Etiology (Hep 2008;47:1401)
‚Ä¢ Viral
HAV, HBV, HCV (rare), HDV  HBV, HEV (especially if pregnant)
HSV (immunosupp. Pt), EBV, CMV, adenovirus, paramyxovirus, parvovirus B19
‚Ä¢ Drugs/Toxins
Drugs: acetaminophen (most common cause; 40% of all cases), phenytoin, INH,
rifampin, sulfonamides, tetracycline, telithromycin, amiodarone, PTU, valproate
Toxins: fluorinated hydrocarbons, CCl4, Amanita phalloides
‚Ä¢ Vascular: ischemic hepatitis, Budd-Chiari syndrome, hepatic SOS, malignant infiltration
‚Ä¢ Autoimmune hepatitis (usually initial presentation)
‚Ä¢ Misc.: Wilson‚Äôs, acute fatty liver of pregnancy (HELLP, Reye‚Äôs), idiopathic (20%)
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Initial presentation usually nonspecific, w/ nausea, vomiting, malaise, followed by jaundice
‚Ä¢ Neurologic
encephalopathy: stage I  MS; stage II  lethargy, confusion; stage III  stupor;
stage IV  coma
asterixis in stage I/II/III encephalopathy; hyperreflexia, clonus, rigidity in stage III/IV
cerebral edema S c ICP, T CPP S cerebral hypoxia, uncal herniation, Cushing‚Äôs reflex
(hypertension  bradycardia), pupillary dilatation, decerebrate posturing, apnea
‚Ä¢ Cardiovascular: hypotension with low SVR
‚Ä¢ Pulmonary: respiratory alkalosis, impaired peripheral O2 uptake, pulm edema, ARDS
‚Ä¢ Gastrointestinal: GIB (T clotting factors, T plt, DIC), pancreatitis (? due to ischemia)
‚Ä¢ Renal:ATN, hepatorenal syndrome, hyponatremia, hypokalemia, hypophosphatemia
‚Ä¢ Hematology: coagulopathy (due to T synthesis of clotting factors  DIC)
‚Ä¢ Infection (90% of Pts): especially with Staph, Strep, GNRs, and fungi (T immune fxn,
invasive procedures); SBP in 32% of Pts; fever and c WBC may be absent
‚Ä¢ Endocrine: hypoglycemia (T glc synthesis), metabolic acidosis (c lactate), adrenal insuf.
Workup
‚Ä¢ Viral serologies (HBV DNA, HCV RNA)
‚Ä¢ Autoimmune hepatitis serologies, ceruloplasmin & urine copper
‚Ä¢ Toxicology screen (acetaminophen levels q1-2h until peak determined)
‚Ä¢ Imaging studies (RUQ U/S or abd CT, Doppler studies of portal and hepatic veins)
‚Ä¢ Liver biopsy (unless precluded by coagulopathy S in which case consider transjugular)
Treatment (Hep 2005;41;1179 & Nat Rev Gastro Hep 2009;6:542) 
‚Ä¢ ICU care at liver transplant ctr for hemodynamic & ventilatory support; CVVH for ARF
‚Ä¢ IV N-acetylcysteine (same dose as for acetaminophen): all Pts w/ hepatic failure and
grade 1‚Äì2 enceph: c cerebral blood flow and c transplant-free survival (Gastro 2009;137:856)
‚Ä¢ Cerebral edema: CT Se only 60%, ‚à¥consider ICP monitoring if stage III/IV enceph; head of
bed 30o and hypertonic saline for goal Na 145‚Äì155 mEq/L; other potential measures:
hyperventilation, mannitol, barbiturates, ? induction of hypothermia, IV indomethacin
‚Ä¢ Encephalopathy: intubate for grade III or IV; ? lactulose, but no efficacy data
‚Ä¢ Coagulopathy: vit K; FFP/plts/cryo if active bleeding; ? recomb. factor VIIa; PPI prophylaxis
‚Ä¢ Infection: low threshold for abx (broad spectrum, eg, vancomycin & 3rd-gen ceph.),
albeit no proven mortality benefit to empiric abx
‚Ä¢ Treatment of specific causes: nucleosides for HBV; corticosteroids for autoimmune
hepatitis; chelation Rx for Wilson‚Äôs; IV acyclovir for HSV; gastric lavage and 
charcoal  penicillin and silymarin for Amanita phalloides; delivery of child for 
pregnancy-related;TIPS and anticoagulation for Budd-Chiari
‚Ä¢ Liver transplantation if poor prognosis w/ grade II or III encephalopathy (see below)
‚Ä¢ Extracorporeal liver assist devices (cell-based vs. non) under study as bridge to transplant
Prognosis
‚Ä¢ Non-acetaminophen ALF mortality 80%, acetaminophen-induced ALF mortality 30%
‚Ä¢ Predictors of poor outcome
acetaminophen-induced: pH 7.3 after fluids or INR 6.5, Cr 3.4, or grade III/IV enceph.
non‚Äìacetaminophen-induced: INR 6.5 or 3 of the following: non-A/B viral hep;
non-acetaminophen drug toxicity; time from jaundice to enceph. 7 d; age 10 or
40 y; INR 3.5;T bili 17.4
‚Ä¢ 25‚Äì30% of Pts w/ ALF undergo liver transplantation w/ 5-y survival rate of 70%
