CIRRHOSIS
Definition (Lancet 2008;371:838)
‚Ä¢ Definition: fibrosis and nodular regeneration resulting from hepatocellular injury
‚Ä¢ Decompensated  jaundice, variceal bleed, encephalopathy, ascites; worse prognosis
Etiologies
‚Ä¢ Alcohol (60‚Äì70%): Laennec‚Äôs cirrhosis; micronodular 
‚Ä¢ Viral hepatitis (10%): chronic HBV, HCV, HDV infection
‚Ä¢ Autoimmune hepatitis: female, c IgG,  ANA, anti‚Äìsmooth muscle Ab
‚Ä¢ Metabolic diseases (5%): hemochromatosis,Wilson‚Äôs disease, 1-antitrypsin deficiency
‚Ä¢ Biliary tract diseases (5%): primary biliary cirrhosis, secondary biliary cirrhosis
(calculus, neoplasm, stricture, biliary atresia), primary sclerosing cholangitis
‚Ä¢ Vascular diseases: Budd-Chiari syndrome, R-sided CHF, constrictive pericarditis
‚Ä¢ Nonalcoholic fatty liver disease (NAFLD, 10‚Äì15%) cause of most ‚Äúcryptogenic
cirrhosis‚Äù 
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Subclinical or may p/w liver dysfunction (jaundice, coagulopathy, encephalopathy) and/or
portal HTN (ascites, varices); 35% p/w fever (SBP, acute EtOH); 25% p/w hematemesis
Physical exam
‚Ä¢ Liver: initially enlarged, palpable (L lobe predom), firm; eventually shrunken and 
nodular
‚Ä¢ Signs of liver failure: jaundice (bili 2), spider angiomata & palmar erythema (c estra-
diol), Dupuytren‚Äôs contractures, white nail lines (Muehrcke‚Äôs lines) & proximal nail
beds (Terry‚Äôs nails), c parotid & lacrimal glands, gynecomastia, testicular atrophy,
asterixis, encephalopathy, fetor hepaticus, clubbing, hypertrophic osteoarthropathy
‚Ä¢ Signs of portal hypertension: splenomegaly, ascites, dilated superficial abdominal veins
(caput medusae), epigastric Cruveilhier-Baumgarten venous hum
Laboratory studies
‚Ä¢ c bilirubin, c PT (poor correlation w/ bleeding; factor VIII nl as not synthesized by liver),
T alb,  c aminotransferases (AST  ALT if late) and c A (variable), T Na, c gamma glob
‚Ä¢ Anemia (marrow suppression, hypersplenism, Fe and/or folate deficiencies), neutropenia
(hypersplenism), thrombocytopenia (hypersplenism, TTpo production by liver, EtOH tox)
Workup
‚Ä¢ Abdominal U/S w/ Doppler: liver size (c L & caudate lobe), r/o HCC, ascites,
‚úìpatency of portal, splenic, and hepatic veins 
‚Ä¢ Assess fibrosis: biomarkers (FibroSURE  panel of 6 markers, c score predictive of
fibrosis, esp in Hep C); US/MR elastography 
‚Ä¢ Determine etiology: hepatitis serologies (HBsAg, anti-HBs, anti-HCV), autoimmune
hepatitis studies (IgG,ANA, anti‚Äìsmooth muscle Ab), Fe and Cu studies, 1-AT, AMA
‚Ä¢  Liver bx: percutaneous or transjugular (consider if ascites or coagulopathy) used
to dx etiology and presence of cirrhosis
Ascites (see ‚ÄúAscites‚Äù for details on dx eval)
‚Ä¢ Due to portal HTN (defined as hepatic venous pressure gradient [HVPG] 5 mmHg)
‚Ä¢ Develops in 60% w/in 10 y; 50% mortality at 5 y (Hepatology 2009;29:2087)
‚Ä¢ Treatment (Am J Gastro 2009;104:1802): T Na intake (1‚Äì2 g/d) in all
free H2O restriction only if Na 125
Diuretics: goal 1 L/d; urine Na/K 1 implies effective aldo block
spironolactone (100 mg PO qd)  furosemide (40 mg PO qd); c doses in proportion
‚àÖNSAID as interferes w/ diuretic action (common cause of refractory ascites)
‚Ä¢ Refractory ascites  ascites despite med/diet compliance; 20% mortality at 3 mo
Large-volume paracentesis (LVP) remove 4‚Äì6 L per session until dry or T sx
? albumin replacement: T chemical abnl; no  mortality (Gastro 1988;94:1493)
Beware LVP if SBP as c risk of ARF S consider dx tap to r/o SBP first
Transjugular intrahepatic portosystemic shunt (TIPS)
T ascites in 75%, c CrCl, c transplant-free survival (NEJM 2000;342:1701)
c encephalopathy (‚à¥TIPS contraindic. if  mild at baseline), no  quality of life
(Gastro 2003;124:634); by 1 y 40% occlude (metal stent); new coated stent T (20%)
occlusion and ? T mortality (Gastro 2004;126:469)
LVP 1st line Rx b/c TIPS complications (metal stent), but TIPS T mort (Gastro 2007;133:825) 
‚Ä¢ Hepatic hydrothorax: 2 diaphragmatic defect; often unilateral, R L,  ascites
Treatment: ‚àÖchest tube due to c complications; Rx same as ascites
Spontaneous bacterial empyema can occur (even w/o SBP) S consider dx thora-
centesis; Rx same as for SBP (see later)

Spontaneous bacterial peritonitis (SBP; see ‚ÄúAscites‚Äù for details on dx)

q1‚Äì2wk until gone S survey EGD at 3 mo S q6‚Äì12mo; adding B only c side effects

B  EBL  either alone (Annals 2008;149:109); if refractory S TIPS or liver transplant
Portosystemic (hepatic) encenphalopathy (PSE)
‚Ä¢ Pathogenesis: failure of liver to detoxify NH3  other substances that cause cerebral
edema and/or act as false neurotrasmitters (GABA-like)
‚Ä¢ Preciptants: c dietary protein, constipation, GIB, med noncompliance, infection, azotemia,

‚Ä¢ Stages: (1) confusion; (2) drowsiness; (3) stupor; (4) coma
‚Ä¢ Dx: asterixis can be seen; NH3 poor Se for dx & monitoring Rx; remains a clinical dx
‚Ä¢ Acute treatment: identify/correct precipitants, restrict dietary protein acutely (60‚Äì80 g/d),
lactulose (acidification of colon: NH3 S NH4
); goal 2‚Äì4 stools/d or rifaximin 400 mg
tid (T gut bacteria S T NH3 prod); rifaximin and lactulose similar efficacy ( J Hep 2003;38:51)
‚Ä¢ 2 prevention: lactulose  rifaximin 550 bid (Gastro 2009;137:885 NEJM 2010;362:1071)
Hepatorenal syndrome (NEJM 2009;361:1279)
‚Ä¢ Pathobiology unknown, though kidney is pathologically normal; ? vascular s
‚Ä¢ Definition: progressive azotemia (Cr 1.5 or 1.5 	 base) despite volume challenge
(1 g/kg/d of albumin 	 2 d), and exclusion of other causes (drugs, ATN, obstruction);
(nb, often overestimate renal fxn in cirrhotics b/c T muscle mass [‚à¥less creatine], c Cr
renal tubular secretion, and T conversion of creatine S creatinine) 
Type I: Cr 2.5 or 1.5	 baseline in 2 wk; usually occurs in severe liver failure,
often following precipitating event (see later); median survival 2 wk
Type II: more indolent course, median survival 6 mo; liver failure present but  type I
Both a/w ascites (usually h/o refractory ascites), oliguria, UNa 10 mEq/L and T Na 
‚Ä¢ Precipitants: GIB, overdiuresis, infection, paracentesis, drugs (aminoglycosides, NSAIDs) 
‚Ä¢ Rx: octreotide (200 mcg SC tid)  midodrine (12.5 mg PO tid)  albumin (Hep 1999;29:1690);
albumin  terlipressin (Gastro 2008;134:1352 & 1360); ? TIPS; definitive Rx  liver transplant
Other complications
‚Ä¢ Hepatopulmonary syndrome (NEJM 2008;358:2378)

S/S: platypnea-orthodeoxia, clubbing, cyanosis

‚Ä¢ Portopulmonary hypertension: c PAP; unclear pathogenesis though some

‚Ä¢ ‚ÄúCirrhotic‚Äù cardiomyopathy: T inotopic & chronotropic response, T systolic and

‚Ä¢ Infxns: Kuppfer cell (hepatic m) dysfxn, T opsonic activity; vaccinate for HAV &

‚Ä¢ Diabetes (15‚Äì30%): due to altered glc & insulin metabolism

‚Ä¢ MELD (Model for End-Stage Liver Disease): used to stratify Pts on liver transplant

10
‚Ä¢ Indications: recurrent or severe encephalopathy, refractory ascites, SBP, recurrent variceal

OTHER ETIOLOGIES OF CIRRHOSIS
Hereditary hemochromatosis (Lancet 2007;370:1855; Hepatology 2008;48:991)
‚Ä¢ Recessive disorders of iron sensing (hepcidin) and transport (transferrin) 
S tissue iron overload; nonhereditary: ineffect. erythropoiesis  transfusions
‚Ä¢ HFE mutations (85% of cases), typically C282Y homozygotes (0.5% of N. European
Caucasians), rarely C282Y/H63D compound heterozygotes; HJV mut. S juvenile onset.
C282Y homozygotes: 28% of  develop sx (88% lab abnl), and 1% of  develop sx
(due to menses T Fe load S later presentation). C282Y/H63D: only 1.5% manifest dis.
‚Ä¢ Sx: fatigue & arthralgias. In advanced disease (rare): bronze skin (melanin  iron),
hypogonadism (espec. in juvenile onset), DM, arthropathy (MCP), CHF, infxns (Vibrio,
Listeria, Yersinia), cirrhosis (c risk if EtOH/fatty liver disease; 15% risk of HCC). Disease
also a/w ALS & porphyria.
‚Ä¢ Dx: iron sat 45% (iron/TIBC 	 100%; most Se & Sp), c ferritin (acute phase reactant,
so poor Sp; often nl in young Pts); MRI (shows ‚Äúblack liver‚Äù and can ‚úìiron stores).
If c iron saturation S ‚úìHFE gene mutation (C282Y/C282Y or C282Y/H63D are ).
Liver bx to assess damage if: HFE  and: ferritin 1000 ng/ml, c LFTs, or c liver size.
‚Ä¢ Treatment: phlebotomy (500 mL  1 unit) qwk until Fe sat 50% and ferritin 50, then prn;
PPI (T intestinal iron transport); deferoxamine if phleb. contraindic.; genetic counseling
Wilson‚Äôs disease (Lancet 2007;369:397 & Hepatology 2008;6:2089)
‚Ä¢ Recessive disorder of copper transport (mutation in ATP7B) S copper overload;
primarily affects liver, but also other tissues (brain, eye)
‚Ä¢ Epidemiology: 1 in 40,000, usually manifests before age 30 y; almost always before 40 y
‚Ä¢ Extrahepatic s/s: neuro  disease, parkinsonism and movement disorder (hepatolen-
ticular disease), Kayser-Fleischer rings ( in 99% w/ neuro  but in 50% w/
hepatic disease), hemolytic anemia, renal disease
‚Ä¢ Dx: c 24-h urine Cu, T serum ceruloplasmin (Se 90%), penicillamine challenge w/ c urine Cu
excretion. In acute liver failure, A/bili 4  AST/ALT 2 better Se & Sp than urine Cu
or ceruloplasmin (Hepatology 2008;4:1167). Gold standard  liver bx w/ hepatic Cu content.
‚Ä¢ Treatment: chelation therapy w/ penicillamine  pyridoxine; 2nd line trientine (T tox-
icity w/ similar efficacy). Zinc: T intestinal Cu transport and can help delay disease;
best used if asx or in conjuction w/ chelation (must give 4‚Äì5 h apart from chelators).

1-antitrypsin deficiency (1-AT) (NEJM 2009;360:2749)
‚Ä¢ Abnl 1-AT S polymerization in liver (cirrhosis) & uninhibited protease activity in
lung (emphysema). Affects 1/3000 of European ancestry; 1% of all COPDers
(onset before 40 y)
‚Ä¢ Extrahepatic disease: emphysema, necrotizing panniculitis, ANCA vasculitis (Wegener)
‚Ä¢ Dx: absence of 1-AT globulin on SPEP,  PAS inclusion bodies on liver bx
gold standard  protein phenotyping of protease inhibitor (Pi); ZZ, null/null, or null/Z S
clinical sx; null/null makes no 1-AT, ‚à¥only COPD and not liver dz (no polymerization)
‚Ä¢ Treatment: standard Rx for cirrhosis/chronic liver dis.; 1-AT replacement for emphysema
Primary biliary cirrhosis (PBC) (NEJM 2005;353:1261; Hepatology 2009;50:291)
‚Ä¢ Autoimmune destruction of intrahepatic bile ducts; may be triggered by certain infections
or toxins; a/w X monosomy, variants in IL12 & IL12 receptor genes (NEJM 2009;360:2544)
‚Ä¢ Epidemiology: middle-aged women; a/w Sj√∂gren‚Äôs, Raynaud‚Äôs, scleroderma, celiac dis.
‚Ä¢ Sx: fatigue, pruritus, jaundice, steatorrhea, xanthelasma, autonomic and cognitive dysfxn
‚Ä¢ Ddx: biliary stricture/cancer, PSC, autoimmune hepatitis (overlap syndrome), sarcoid,
meds, idiopathic adult ductopenia, eosinophilic cholangitis, AIDS cholangiopathy,
ischemic damage. Imaging of biliary tree (MRCP, CT, ERCP)  serology can help.
‚Ä¢ Dx: c A, c bili, c chol,  anti-mitochondrial Ab (AMA) in 95%. If  AMA, liver bx not needed
due to high Se & Sp. 0.5% gen pop  AMA & normal LFTs S 10% develop PBC at 6 y.
If AMA , liver bx (Pts often  ANA, anti‚Äìsmooth muscle; same prognosis as  AMA).
‚Ä¢ Rx: ursodeoxycholic acid (13‚Äì15 mg/kg/d) regardless of stage, 25% complete response
c survival & T histologic change and complications (eg, varices) (Gastro 2005;128:297)
? colchicine, methotrexate, budesonide if refractory 
Pruritus: cholestyramine (give 2‚Äì4 h after UDCA); if refractory sx: naltrexone, sertraline
Fat-soluble vitamins; screen/Rx osteoporosis (risk indepedent of vit D deficiency) 
Liver transplant: 20% recur but no impact on long-term survival
Primary sclerosing cholangitis (PSC) (Liver Transpl 2008;14:735)
‚Ä¢ Idiopathic cholestasis w/ fibrosis, stricturing, and dilatation of intrahepatic and extra-
hepatic bile ducts; a/w HLA types, autoantibodies but poor response to
immunomodulator Rx suggesting nonautoimmune pathogenesis
‚Ä¢ Epidemiology: young men (age 20‚Äì50 y), 70% a/w ulcerative colitis (rarely Crohn‚Äôs disease)
‚Ä¢ Clinical manifestations: fatigue, pruritus, jaundice, fevers, RUQ pain, cholangiocarcinoma
‚Ä¢ Ddx: same as PBC, may also have overlap w/ autoimmune hepatitis and similar pre-
sentation to IgG4 autoimmune cholangitis (steroid responsive) (Gastro 2008;134:706)
‚Ä¢ Dx: c bilirubin, c A,  p-ANCA in 70% but nonspecific
MRCP S multifocal beaded bile duct strictures, but may miss dx if confined to small
intrahepatic ducts (2% ‚Äúsmall duct PSC‚Äù: better prognosis, ? different disease) 
ERCP w/ liver bx gold standard:‚Äúonion-skin‚Äù fibrosis around bile ducts
‚Ä¢ Treatment: supportive care, fat-soluble vitamins; no meds have improved survival
Ursodeoxycholic acid may T colon CA risk in Pts w/ UC and improve LFTs in Pts w/o UC
Dominant stricture: endoscopic dilation, short-term stenting, or surgical resection
Cholangiocarcinoma (20%): ? annual surveillance w RUQ U/S and CA 19-9; ? PET
Liver transplantation: 30% recurrence, though if UC, colectomy may T recurrence

HEPATIC VASCULAR DISEASE
Portal vein thrombosis (PVT) (Al Phar Ther 2009;30:881)
‚Ä¢ Definition: thrombosis, constriction, or invasion of portal vein S portal HTN S varices.
Isolated splenic vein thrombosis (eg, 2 to pancreatitis) S isolated gastric varices.
‚Ä¢ Etiologies: cirrhosis, neoplasm (pancreas, HCC), abdominal infxn S pylephlebitis (infected
thrombosis of PVT), hypercoag state (incl MPS), panceatitis, IBD, surgery, trauma
‚Ä¢ Clinical manifestations
acute PVT: can p/w pain; often asx and dx as incidental finding on U/S or CT 
if mesenteric vein involved may p/w intestinal infarct; if fevers consider pylephlebitis 
chronic PVT: asx/incidental finding; may p/w s/s of portal HTN S hematemesis 2
variceal bleeding, splenomegaly, mild encephalopathy; ascites rare unless cirrhosis
‚Ä¢ Diagnostic studies: LFTs usually normal; U/S w/ Doppler, MRA, CT (I), angiography;
‚Äúportal cavernoma‚Äù network of hepatopedal collaterals in chronic PVT‚Äîcan rarely cause
biliary obstruction and cholestatic LFT  portal cholangiopathy (may require surgery)
‚Ä¢ Treatment: eval for underlying cause (cirrhosis, MDS, hypercoag); if cirrhotic, Rx less clear
acute: anticoagulation 6 mo unless irreversible etiology (not cirrhosis), then indefinite
chronic: anticoagulation if hypercoag. state (not cirrhosis); unclear if benefit  bleed risk 
Varices: screen at dx; no evidence for 1 ppx of bleed; if bleed endoscopic Rx and B.
If refractory bleed consider TIPS, shunt. Isolated gastric varices 2 splenic vein
thrombosis: splenectomy is curative.
Budd-Chiari syndrome (NEJM 2004;350:578)
‚Ä¢ Occlusion of hepatic veins or IVC S sinusoidal congestion and portal HTN
‚Ä¢ Etiologies: 50% due to myeloproliferative disorder a/w JAK2 mutations (esp P. vera),
hypercoag. state, tumor invasion (HCC, renal, adrenal), IVC webs, trauma, 1‚ÅÑ4 idiopathic
‚Ä¢ Symptoms: hepatomegaly, RUQ pain, ascites, dilated venous collaterals
‚Ä¢ Dx:  c aminotransferases & A; Doppler U/S of hepatic veins (85% Se & Sp); CT (I)
or MRI/MRA S vein occlusion or c caudate lobe (separate venous drainage);‚Äúspider-
web‚Äù pattern on hepatic venography; liver bx showing congestion (r/o right-sided CHF)
‚Ä¢ Treatment: anticoagulation (heparin S warfarin), thrombolysis if acute clot;TIPS preferred
over surgical shunt; angioplasty w/ metallic stent if web or small clot; liver transplant
Sinusoidal obstruction syndrome (SOS) (Mayo 2003;78:589)
‚Ä¢ Occlusion of hepatic venules and sinusoids (formerly veno-occlusive disease)
‚Ä¢ Etiologies: HSCT, chemo (esp cyclopho), XRT, Jamaican bush tea
‚Ä¢ Clinical manifestations: hepatomegaly, RUQ pain, ascites, weight gain, c bilirubin
‚Ä¢ Dx: U/S w/ reversal of portal flow, but often not helpful; dx made clinically (c bili, wt
gain/ascites, and RUQ pain) or, if necessary, by liver bx or HVPG (10 mmHg)
‚Ä¢ Treatment (20% mortality): supportive; ? defibrotide (adenosine agonist c TPA levels)
‚Ä¢ Ppx: ursodeoxycholic acid for high risk HSCT pop; ? use of low-dose heparin

‚Ä¢ ‚ÄúUnderfill‚Äù theory: portal hypertension S transudation of fluid into peritoneum S
T plasma volume S renal Na retention
‚Ä¢ ‚ÄúOverflow‚Äù theory: hepatorenal reflex S Na retention
‚Ä¢ Peripheral vasodilatation theory (favored): portal hypertension S systemic vasodilatation
(? due to release of NO) S T effective arterial volume S renal Na retention
‚Ä¢ Other: T serum oncotic pressure from hypoalbuminemia; c hepatic lymph production

BILIARY TRACT DISEASE
CHOLELITHIASIS (GALLSTONES)
Epidemiology & Pathogenesis (J Hep 2008;48:S124)
‚Ä¢ 10% adults in the U.S. have gallstones
‚Ä¢ Bile  bile salts, phospholipids, cholesterol; c cholesterol saturation in bile  accelerated
nucleation  gallbladder hypomotility S gallstones
‚Ä¢ Risk factors: female; South, Central, Native American, c age (40 y), obesity, pregnancy,
TPN, rapid T wt, drugs (OCPs, estrogen, clofibrate, octreotide, ceftriaxone), ileal disease
‚Ä¢ ? statin use 1 y T risk of sx gallstones & cholecystectomy ( JAMA 2009;302:2001)
Types of gallstones
‚Ä¢ Cholesterol (90%): 2 subtypes
mixed: contain 50% cholesterol; typically smaller, multiple stones
pure: 100% cholesterol; larger, yellow, white appearance
‚Ä¢ Pigment (10%)
Black: unconjugated bilirubin (chronic hemolysis, cirrhosis) and calcium
Brown: stasis & infection in bile ducts S bacteria deconjugate bilirubin S precipitates w/
calcium; seen w/ duodenal diverticula, biliary strictures, parasites 
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ May be asx; biliary colic in 2%/y; once sx, rate of complications 2%/y
‚Ä¢ Biliary colic  episodic RUQ or epigastric abd pain that begins abruptly, is con-
tinuous, resolves slowly, and lasts for 30 min to 3 h;  radiation to scapula; nausea
‚Ä¢ May be precipitated by fatty foods
‚Ä¢ Physical exam: afebrile,  RUQ tenderness or epigastric pain
Diagnostic studies
‚Ä¢ RUQ U/S: Se & Sp 95% for stones 5 mm; can show complications (cholecystitis);
should be performed only after fasting 
8 h to ensure distended, bile-filled gallbladder
Treatment
‚Ä¢ Cholecystectomy (CCY), usually laparoscopic, if symptomatic
‚Ä¢ CCY in asx Pts w/: selective mucosal GB calcification (7% risk of ca) (Surgery
2001;129:699), GB polyps 10 mm, Native American, stones 3 cm
‚Ä¢ Ursodeoxycholic acid (rare) for cholesterol stones w/ uncomplicated biliary pain or if
poor surgical candidate; also reduces risk of gallstone formation with rapid weight loss
Complications
‚Ä¢ Cholecystitis: 20% of sx biliary pain S cholecystitis w/in 2 y
‚Ä¢ Choledocholithiasis S cholangitis or gallstone pancreatitis
‚Ä¢ Mirizzi‚Äôs syndrome: common hepatic duct compression by cystic duct stone S jaundice,
biliary obstruction
‚Ä¢ Cholecystoenteric fistula: stone erodes through gallbladder into bowel
‚Ä¢ Gallstone ileus: SBO (usually at term ileum) due to stone in intestine that passed thru fistula
‚Ä¢ Gallbladder carcinoma (1% in U.S.)
CHOLECYSTITIS (NEJM 2008;358:2804)
Pathogenesis
‚Ä¢ Acute cholecystitis: stone impaction in cystic duct S inflammation behind 
obstruction S GB swelling  secondary infection (50%) of biliary fluid
‚Ä¢ Acalculous cholecystitis: gallbladder stasis and ischemia S inflammatory response; occurs
mainly in critically ill, hosp. Pts (postop major surgery, TPN, sepsis, trauma, burns,
opiates, immunosuppression, infxn [eg, CMV, Crypto, Campylobacter, typhoid fever])
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ History: RUQ/epigastric pain  radiation to R shoulder/back, nausea, vomiting, fever 
‚Ä¢ Physical examination: RUQ tenderness, Murphy‚Äôs sign  c RUQ pain and inspiratory
arrest with deep breath during palpation of R subcostal region,  palpable gallbladder
‚Ä¢ Laboratory evaluation: c WBC,  mild c bilirubin, A,ALT/AST, and amylase;
AST/ALT 500 U/L, bili 4 mg/dL, or amylase 1,000 U/L S choledocholithiasis
Diagnostic studies
‚Ä¢ RUQ U/S: high Se & Sp for stones, but need specific signs of cholecystitis: GB wall
thickening 5 mm, pericholecystic fluid, and a sonographic Murphy‚Äôs sign

‚Ä¢ HIDA scan: most Se test (80‚Äì90%) for acute cholecystitis. IV inj of HIDA, which is
selectively secreted into biliary tree. In acute cholecystitis, HIDA enters BD but
not GB. 10‚Äì20% false  (cystic duct obstructed from chronic cholecystitis, lengthy
fasting, liver disease).
Treatment
‚Ä¢ NPO, IV fluids, nasogastric tube if intractable vomiting, analgesia
‚Ä¢ Antibiotics (E. coli, Klebsiella, and Enterobacter sp. are usual pathogens) 
([2nd- or 3rd-generation cephalosporin or FQ]  MNZ) or piperacillin-tazobactam
‚Ä¢
Early CCY (usually w/in 72 h). Delaying surgery 2‚Äì3 mos T operative time w/o 
rate of complications or conversion to open procedure (Am J Surg 2008;194:40).
‚Ä¢ Cholecystostomy and percutaneous drainage if too sick for surgery
‚Ä¢ Intraoperative cholangiogram or ERCP to r/o choledocholithiasis in Pts w/ jaundice,
cholangitis, or stone in BD on U/S
Complications
‚Ä¢ Gangrenous cholecystitis: necrosis w/ risk of empyema and perforation
‚Ä¢ Emphysematous cholecystitis: infection by gas-forming organisms (air in GB wall)
‚Ä¢ Post CCY: bile duct leak, BD injury or retained stones, cystic duct remnant, sphincter of
Oddi dysfxn
CHOLEDOCHOLITHIASIS
Definition
‚Ä¢ Gallstone lodged in bile duct (BD)
Epidemiology
‚Ä¢ Occurs in 15% of Pts w/ gallbladder stones; can form de novo in BD
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Asymptomatic (50%)
‚Ä¢ RUQ/epigastric pain due to obstruction of bile flow S c BD pressure, jaundice, pruritis,
nausea
Diagnostic studies
‚Ä¢ Labs: c bilirubin,A; transient spike in ALT or amylase suggests passage of stone
‚Ä¢ RUQ U/S: BD stones seen 50% of cases; usually inferred from dilated BD (6 mm)
‚Ä¢ ERCP preferred dx modality; cholangiogram (percutaneous, operative) when ERCP
unavailable or unsuccessful; EUS/MRCP to exclude BD stones when suspicion low
Treatment
‚Ä¢ ERCP & papillotomy w/ stone extraction
‚Ä¢ CCY typically w/in 6 wks unless contraindication (15% Pts will develop indication
for CCY if left unRx‚Äôd)
Complications
‚Ä¢ Cholangitis, cholecystitis, pancreatitis, stricture
CHOLANGITIS
Definition and Etiologies
‚Ä¢ BD obstruction S infection proximal to the obstruction
‚Ä¢ Etiologies: BD stone (85%)
Malignant (biliary, pancreatic) or benign stricture
Infiltration w/ flukes (Clonorchis sinensis, Opisthorchis viverrini)
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Charcot‚Äôs triad: RUQ pain, jaundice, fever/chills; present in 70% of Pts
‚Ä¢ Reynold‚Äôs pentad: Charcot‚Äôs triad  shock and  MS; present in 15% of Pts
Diagnostic studies
‚Ä¢ RUQ U/S
‚Ä¢ Labs: c WBC, bilirubin, A, amylase;  BCx
‚Ä¢ ERCP; percutaneous transhepatic cholangiogram (if ERCP unsuccessful) 
Treatment
‚Ä¢ Antibiotics (broad spectrum) to cover common bile pathogens (see above)
ampicillin  gentamicin (or levofloxacin)  MNZ (if severe); carbapenems; pip/tazo
‚Ä¢ 80% respond to conservative Rx and abx S biliary drainage on elective basis
‚Ä¢ 20% require urgent biliary decompression via ERCP (papillotomy, stone extrac-
tion, and/or stent insertion). If sphincterotomy cannot be performed (larger stones),
decompression by biliary stent or nasobiliary catheter can be done; otherwise 
percutaneous transhepatic biliary drainage or surgery.

ACID-BASE

ACID-BASE DISTURBANCES
GENERAL
Definitions
‚Ä¢ Acidemia S pH 7.36, alkalemia S pH 7.44
‚Ä¢ Acidosis S process that increases [H]; alkalosis S process that decreases [H]
‚Ä¢ Primary disorders: metabolic acidosis or alkalosis, respiratory acidosis or alkalosis
‚Ä¢ Compensation
respiratory: hyper- or hypoventilation alters PaCO2 to counteract 1 metabolic process
renal: excretion/retention of H/HCO3 to counteract 1 respiratory process
respiratory compensation occurs in min; renal compensation takes hrs to days
compensation never fully corrects pH; if pH normal, consider mixed disorder

Treatment of severe metabolic acidoses (pH 7.2) (NEJM 1998;338:26)
‚Ä¢ DKA: insulin & IVF;AKA: dextrose, IVF, replete K, Mg, PO4 as needed
‚Ä¢ Lactic acidosis: treat underlying condition, avoid vasoconstrictors
‚Ä¢ Renal failure: hemodialysis
‚Ä¢ Methanol & ethylene glycol: early fomepizole, vit. B6 (ethylene glycol), folate
(methanol), hemodialysis (especially if late presentation) (NEJM 2009;360:2216)
‚Ä¢ Alkali therapy: NaHCO3 (eg, 3 50-mmol amp in 1 L D5W) to get serum HCO3 8
and pH 7.2 (estimate mmol of HCO3 needed as 8-[HCO3]serum  wt  0.5)
side effects: volume overload, hypernatremia, T ICa, c PaCO2 (and ‚à¥possibly
intracellular acidosis), overshoot; no proven benefit in lactic acidosis (Annals 1990;112:492)
METABOLIC ALKALOSIS
Pathophysiology
‚Ä¢ Saline-responsive etiologies require initiating event and maintenance factors, whereas
saline-resistant etiologies develop from various causes
‚Ä¢ Initiating event
loss of H from GI tract or kidneys
exogenous alkali
contraction alkalosis: diuresis S excretion of HCO3-poor fluid S extracellular fluid
‚Äúcontracts‚Äù around fixed amount of HCO3 S c HCO3 concentration
posthypercapnia: respiratory acidosis S renal compensation with HCO3 retention;
rapid correction of respiratory disorder (eg, with intubation) S transient excess HCO3
‚Ä¢ Maintenance factors
volume depletion S c proximal reabsorption of NaHCO3 and c aldosterone (see next)
hyperaldosteronism (either 1 or 2) S distal Na reabsorption in exchange for K and
H excretion (and consequent HCO3 retention)
hypokalemia S transcellular K/H exchange; intracellular acidosis in renal proximal
tubular cells promotes bicarbonate reabsorption and ammoniagenesis

SODIUM

SODIUM AND WATER HOMEOSTASIS
OVERVIEW
General
‚Ä¢ Disorders of serum sodium are generally due to s in total body water, not sodium
‚Ä¢ Hyper- or hypoosmolality S rapid water shifts S s in brain cell volume S  MS, seizures
Key hormones
‚Ä¢ Antidiuretic hormone (ADH): primary hormone that regulates sodium concentration
stimuli for secretion: hyperosmolality, TT effective arterial volume (EAV)
action: insertion of aquaporin-2 channels in collecting ducts S passive water reabsorption
urine osmolality is an indirect functional assay of the ADH-renal axis
Uosm range: 60 mOsm/L (no ADH) to 1200 mOsm/L (maximal ADH)
‚Ä¢ Aldosterone: primary hormone that regulates total body sodium (and ‚à¥volume)
stimuli for secretion: hypovolemia (via renin and angiotensin II), hyperkalemia
action: iso-osmotic reabsorption of sodium in exchange for potassium or H
HYPONATREMIA
Pathophysiology
‚Ä¢ Excess of water relative to sodium; almost always due to c ADH
‚Ä¢ c ADH may be appropriate (eg, hypovolemia or hypervolemia with T EAV)
‚Ä¢ c ADH may be inappropriate (SIADH)
‚Ä¢ Rarely, T ADH (appropriately suppressed), but kidneys unable to maintain nl [Na]serum
primary polydipsia: ingestion of massive quantities (usually 12 L/d) of free H2O
overwhelms diluting ability of kidney (normal solute load 750 mOsm/d, min Uosm
 60 mOsm/L S excrete in 12 L; if H2O ingestion exceeds this, H2O retention)
‚Äútea & toast‚Äù and ‚Äúbeer potomania‚Äù: TT daily solute load, c free H2O S insufficient
solute to excrete H2O intake (eg, if only 250 mOsm/d, minimum Uosm  60
mOsm/L S excrete in 4 L; if H2O ingestion exceeds this, H2O retention)
Workup (NEJM 2000;342:1581)
‚Ä¢ Measure plasma osmolality
Hypotonic hyponatremia most common scenario; true excess of free H2O relative to Na
Hypertonic hyponatremia: excess of another effective osmole (eg, glc, mannitol) that
draws H2O intravascularly; each 100 mg/dL c glc 100 mg/dL S T [Na] by 2.4 mEq/L
Isotonic hyponatremia: rare lab artifact from hyperlipidemia or hyperproteinemia
‚Ä¢ For hypotonic hyponatremia, ‚úìvolume status (vital signs, orthostatics, JVP, skin
turgor, mucous membranes, peripheral edema, BUN, Cr, uric acid)
‚Ä¢ Uosm diagnostically useful in limited circumstances, because almost always 300
exceptions: Uosm 100 in 1 polydipsia & T solute intake
moreover, Uosm 300 Z SIADH; must determine if c ADH appropriate or inappropriate
however, Uosm important when deciding on treatment (see below)
‚Ä¢ If euvolemic and c Uosm, evaluate for glucocorticoid insufficiency and hypothyroidism

Hypovolemic hypotonic hyponatremia (ie, TT total body Na, T TBW)
‚Ä¢ Renal losses (UNa 20 mEq/L, FENa 1%): diuretics (espec. thiazides, as loop diuretics
T tonicity of medullary interstitium and impair urine concentrating ability), salt-
wasting nephropathy, cerebral salt wasting, mineralocorticoid deficiency
‚Ä¢ Extrarenal losses (UNa 10 mEq/L, FENa 1%): GI losses (eg, diarrhea), third-spacing
(eg, pancreatitis), inadequate intake, insensible losses
Euvolemic hypotonic hyponatremia (ie, nl total body Na, c TBW)
‚Ä¢ SIADH (eu- or mild hypervolemia, inapprop c UOsm, normal UNa, T BUN & UA)
malignancy: lung, brain, GI, GU, lymphoma, leukemia, thymoma, mesothelioma
pulmonary: pneumonia, asthma, COPD, PTX,  pressure ventilation
intracranial: trauma, stroke, hemorrhage, infxn, hydrocephalus
drugs: antipsychotics, antidepressants, chemotherapy, vasopressin, dDAVP, MDMA
miscellaneous: pain, nausea, postoperative state
‚Ä¢ Endocrinopathies: c ADH activity seen in glucocorticoid deficiency (co-secretion of
ADH & CRH) and hypothyroidism (T CO & T GFR)
‚Ä¢ Psychogenic polydipsia (Uosm 100, T uric acid): usually requires intake 12 L/d
‚Ä¢ Low solute:‚Äútea & toast‚Äù;‚Äúbeer potomania‚Äù
‚Ä¢ Reset osmostat: chronic malnutrition (T intracellular osmoles) or pregnancy (hormonal
effects) S ADH physiology reset to regulate a lower [Na]serum
Hypervolemic hypotonic hyponatremia (ie, c total body Na, cc TBW)
‚Ä¢ CHF (T CO S T EAV; UNa 10 mEq/L, FENa 1%)
‚Ä¢ Cirrhosis (splanchnic arterial vasodilation and ascites S T EAV; UNa 10 mEq/L,
FENa 1%)
‚Ä¢ Nephrotic syndrome (hypoalbuminemia S edema S T EAV; UNa 10 mEq/L,
FENa 1%)
‚Ä¢ Advanced renal failure (diminished ability to excrete free H2O; UNa 20 mEq/L)
Treatment
‚Ä¢ Goals of treatment
Asymptomatic hyponatremia: correct [Na]serum at rate of 0.5 mEq/L/h
Symptomatic hyponatremia: initial rapid correction of Na (2 mEq/L/h for the first 
2‚Äì3 h) until sx resolve
Rate of c Na should not exceed 10‚Äì12 mEq/L/d to avoid osmotic demyelination 
syndrome (spastic quadriplegia, dysarthria, dysphagia), espec if hypoNa chronic
‚Ä¢ Effect of IV fluids
initial [Na]serum per L infusate 
eg, 1 L hypertonic saline (513 mEq Na) given to 70-kg man w/ [Na]  110 mEq/L
will c [Na]serum by 9.4 mEq
however, above assumes entire infusate retained without any output of Na or H2O
if Pt is euvolemic, as in SIADH, then infused Na will be excreted
eg, 1 L NS (154 mEq of Na or 308 mOsm of solute in 1 L free H2O) given to Pt with

‚Ä¢ Hypovolemic hyponatremia: volume repletion with normal saline

HYPERNATREMIA
Pathophysiology (NEJM 2000;342:1493)
‚Ä¢ Deficit of water relative to sodium; by definition, all hypernatremic Pts are hypertonic 
‚Ä¢ Usually loss of hypotonic fluid; occasionally infusion of hypertonic fluid
‚Ä¢ And impaired access to free water (eg, intubation,  MS, elderly): hypernatremia
is a powerful thirst stimulus, ‚à¥usually only develops in Pts w/o access to H2O
Workup
‚Ä¢ ‚úìUosm, UNa, volume status (vital signs, orthostatics, JVP, skin turgor, BUN, Cr)
Uosm 700‚Äì800 S extrarenal loss or Na overload; ‚úìUNa to differentiate
Uosm 700‚Äì800 S renal loss; differentiate DI vs. diuresis based on Uosm and clinical hx 
Figure 4-5 Approach to hypernatremia
SODIUM
4-8
Extrarenal H2O loss (Uosm 700‚Äì800)
‚Ä¢ GI H2O loss: vomiting, NGT drainage, osmotic diarrhea, fistula
‚Ä¢ Insensible loss: fever, exercise, ventilation 
Renal H2O loss (Uosm 700‚Äì800)
‚Ä¢ Diuresis: osmotic (glc, mannitol, urea), loop diuretics
‚Ä¢ Diabetes insipidus:ADH deficiency (central) or resistance (nephrogenic)
Central: hypothalamic or posterior pituitary disease (congenital, trauma/surgery,
tumors, infiltrative); also idiopathic, hypoxic encephalopathy, anorexia, EtOH
Nephrogenic (Annals 2006;144:186)
congenital (ADH receptor V2 mutation, aquaporin-2 mutation)
drugs: Li, amphotericin, demeclocycline, foscarnet, cidofovir
metabolic: hypercalcemia, severe hypokalemia, protein malnutrition, congenital
tubulointerstitial: postobstruction, recovery phase of ATN, PKD, sickle cell,
Sj√∂gren‚Äôs, amyloid, pregnancy (placental vasopressinase)
DI usually presents as severe polyuria and mild hypernatremia
Other (Uosm 700‚Äì800)
‚Ä¢ Na overload: hypertonic saline (eg, resuscitation w/ NaHCO3), mineralocorticoid excess
‚Ä¢ Seizures, cexercise: c intracellular osmoles S H2O shifts S transient c [Na]serum
Treatment
‚Ä¢ Restore access to H2O or supply daily requirement of H2O (1 L/d)
‚Ä¢ Replace free H2O deficit (also replace concurrent volume deficit if appropriate):
Free H2O deficit (L) 
 TBW
shortcut: for typical 70-kg man, free H2O deficit (L)  ([Na]serum ‚Äì 140)/3
 [Na]serum per L infusate 

POLYURIA
Definition and pathophysiology
‚Ä¢ Polyuria defined as 3 L UOP per day
‚Ä¢ Due to an osmotic or a water diuresis; almost always due to osmotic diuresis in inpatients
Workup
‚Ä¢ Perform a timed urine collection (6 h sufficient) and measure Uosm
‚Ä¢ 24-h osmole excretion rate  24-h UOP (actual or estimate)  Uosm
1000 mOsm/d S osmotic diuresis
800 mOsm/d S water diuresis

‚Ä¢ Etiologies
Glucose (uncontrolled diabetes mellitus)

‚Ä¢ Etiologies: diabetes insipidus (DI) (Naserum 140) or 1 polydipsia (Naserum 140)
see ‚ÄúHypernatremia‚Äù above for list of causes of central and nephrogenic DI
‚Ä¢ Workup of DI: Uosm 300 (complete) or 300‚Äì600 (partial)
water deprivation test (start in a.m., ‚úìNaserum, Posm, Uosm, UOP q1‚Äì2h)
Deprive until Posm 295, then ‚úìUosm. If Uosm 300, then administer vasopressin (5 U SC)
or dDAVP (10 g intranasal), then check Uosm in 1‚Äì2 hrs:
Uosm c by 50%  central DI
Uosm unchanged  nephrogenic DI
‚úìADH level before and after water deprivation to evaluate proper response

‚Ä¢ 1 polydipsia: treat psychiatric illness, check meds, restrict access to free H2O
‚Ä¢ Osmotic diuresis: address underlying cause, replace free H2O deficit (see
‚ÄúHypernatremia‚Äù for formula to calculate) and ongoing losses
‚Ä¢ DI:

depletion S T delivery of filtrate to dysfxnal diluting segment of kidney)
pregnancy-induced DI: due to vasopressinase from placenta, ‚à¥ Rx w/ dDAVP

POTASSIUM
4-10
POTASSIUM HOMEOSTASIS
Overview (Annals 2009;150:619)
‚Ä¢ Renal: potassium excretion regulated at distal nephron (collecting tubule)
distal Na delivery & urine flow: Na absorption S lumen electronegative S K secretion
aldosterone: increases Na absorption, K secretion
‚Ä¢ Transcellular shifts: most common cause of acute change in serum potassium
Acid-base disturbance: K/H exchange across cell membranes
Insulin S stimulates Na-K ATPase S hypokalemia (mitigates postprandial c K)
Catecholamines S stimulate Na-K ATPase S hypokalemia; reversed by -blockers
Digoxin S blocks Na-K ATPase S hyperkalemia
Massive necrosis (eg, tumor lysis, rhabdo, ischemic bowel) S release of intracellular K
Hypo- or hyperkalemic periodic paralysis: rare disorders due to channel mutations
HYPOKALEMIA
Transcellular shifts
‚Ä¢ Alkalemia, insulin, catecholamines, hypokalemic periodic paralysis, acute c in
hematopoiesis (megaloblastic anemia Rx w/ B12, AML crisis), hypothermia
GI potassium losses (UK 25 mEq/d or 15 mEq/L or TTKG 3)
‚Ä¢ GI losses plus metabolic acidosis: diarrhea, laxative abuse, villous adenoma
‚Ä¢ Vomiting & NGT drainage usually manifest as renal losses due to 2 hyperaldo & met. alk.
Renal potassium losses (UK 30 mEq/d or 15 mEq/L or TTKG 7)
‚Ä¢ Hypotensive or normotensive
acidosis: DKA, RTA [proximal RTA (type II) and some distal RTAs (type I)]
alkalosis
diuretics, vomiting/NGT drainage (via 2 hyperaldosteronism)
Bartter‚Äôs syndrome (loop of Henle dysfxn S furosemide-like effect; NEJM 1999;340:1177)
Gitelman‚Äôs syndrome (distal convoluted tubule dysfxn S thiazide-like effect)
Mg depletion: ? c distal K secretion (JASN 2007,18:2649)
‚Ä¢ Hypertensive: mineralocorticoid excess
1 hyperaldosteronism (eg, Conn‚Äôs syndrome)
2 hyperaldosteronism (eg, renovascular disease, renin-secreting tumor)
nonaldosterone mineralocorticoid (eg, Cushing‚Äôs, Liddle‚Äôs, exogenous mineralocort.,
licorice)
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Nausea, vomiting, ileus, weakness, muscle cramps, rhabdomyolysis, polyuria
‚Ä¢ ECG: U waves,  c QT interval, ventricular ectopy (PVCs, VT, VF)
Workup (NEJM 1998;339:451)
‚Ä¢ Rule-out transcellular shifts
‚Ä¢ ‚úì24-hr UK and transtubular potassium gradient (TTKG)  (UK/PK) / (Uosm/Posm)
UK 30 mEq/d or 15 mEq/L or TTKG 7 S renal loss
UK 25 mEq/d or 15 mEq/L or TTKG 3 S extrarenal loss
‚Ä¢ If renal losses, ‚úìBP, acid-base, UCl (UNa unreliable for volume status w/ alkalemia)

Treatment

Transcellular shifts
‚Ä¢ Acidemia, insulin defic. (DM), -blockers, dig intox., massive cellular necrosis (tumor

‚Ä¢ Any cause of oligouric or anuric AKI or any cause of end stage renal disease

‚Ä¢ Normal aldosterone function

T response to aldosterone
meds: K-sparing diuretics,TMP-SMX, pentamidine, calcineurin inhibitors

‚Ä¢ Weakness, nausea, paresthesias, palpitations

Definition (Crit Care 2007;11:R31)
‚Ä¢ AKI: an abrupt (48 h) c Cr 0.3 mg/dL, c Cr 50%, or UOP 0.5 mL/kg/hr for 6 h

Treatment
‚Ä¢ Treat underlying disorder (see relevant sections); ? steroids if AIN (KI 2008;73:940)
‚Ä¢ Avoid nephrotoxic insults; review dosing of renally cleared drugs
‚Ä¢ Optimize hemodynamics (both MAP & CO); may take 1‚Äì2 wks to recover from ATN 
‚Ä¢ Watch for and correct volume overload, electrolyte (c K, c PO4), & acid/base status
‚Ä¢ If obstruction is diagnosed and relieved, watch for:
Hypotonic diuresis (2 buildup of BUN, tubular damage); Rx w/ IVF (eg, 1‚ÅÑ2 NS)
Hemorrhagic cystitis (rapid  in size of bladder vessels); avoid by decompressing slowly
‚Ä¢ Indications for urgent dialysis (when condition refractory to conventional therapy)
Acid-base disturbance: acidemia
Electrolyte disorder: generally hyperkalemia; occasionally hypercalcemia, tumor lysis
Intoxication: methanol, ethylene glycol, lithium, salicylates
Overload of volume (CHF)
Uremia: pericarditis, encephalopathy, bleeding
‚Ä¢ No benefit to dopamine (Annals 2005;142:510), diuretics (JAMA 2002;288:2547), or mannitol
CHRONIC KIDNEY DISEASE (CKD)
Definition and Etiologies (Annals 2009;150:ITC2-1 & Lancet 2010;375:1296)
‚Ä¢ 3 mos of reduced GFR (60) and/or kidney damage (path, markers, imaging)
‚Ä¢ Prevalence 13% in U.S.; Cr poor estimate of GFR; ‚à¥use prediction equation,
eg, MDRD equation: www.kidney.org/professionals/KDOQI/gfr_calculator.cfm
nb, equation may underestimate GFR in Pts w/ normal renal fxn
‚Ä¢ Etiologies: DM (45%), HTN/RAS (27%), glomerular (10%), interstitial (5%), PKD (2%)
(NEJM 2008;359:1477), congenital, drugs, myeloma, progression of AKI (JAMA 2009;302:1179)
‚Ä¢ Rates of all-cause mortality and CV events increase with each stage of CKD and are
significantly higher than the rate of progression to kidney failure (NEJM 2004;351:1296)

DIURESIS
General considerations 
‚Ä¢ Increases Na excretion for treatment of HTN or edema in CHF, renal failure and cirrhosis 
‚Ä¢ Daily wt most effective method of documenting successful diuresis
Loop diuretics (NEJM 1998;339:387)
‚Ä¢ Drugs: furosemide (Lasix), torsemide, bumetanide (Bumex), ethacrynic acid
‚Ä¢ Mechanism: inhibit Na-K-2Cl transporter in thick ascending limb (ThAL)
Response is fxn of amt of drug excreted; ‚à¥c dose needed in renal insufficiency, CHF
Sigmoidal dose response curve; ‚à¥c dose until induce diuresis, cc dose beyond that
point yields diminishing returns compared with c frequency of dosing.
‚Ä¢ Dosing: PO bioavailability of furosemide 50%, ‚à¥IV dose 2 as potent as PO dose
torsemide & bumetanide 90% bioavailability; use ethacrynic acid if sulfa allergy
40 mg furosemide PO ‚âà20 mg furosemide IV ‚âà20 mg torsemide PO ‚âà1 mg bumetanide
dose furosemide bid-qid; qd dosing can lead to initial diuresis S antinatriuresis
? c diuresis w/ contin. infusion (bolus S titrate drip) vs. bolus alone (Annals 1991;115:360)
? c diuresis w/ co-administration of albumin if T serum albumin (Crit Care Med 2005;33:1681)
Thiazide diuretics (NEJM 2009;361:2153)
‚Ä¢ Drugs: hydrochlorothiazide (HCTZ), chlorothiazide (Diuril), metolazone (Zaroxolyn)
‚Ä¢ Mechanism: inhibit Na-Cl cotransporter in the distal convoluted tubule (DCT)
synergistic with loop diuretic, esp. if chronic loop use
T effect when GFR 30, except metolazone which is still effective in renal insufficiency
‚Ä¢ Dosing: give prior to loop diuretic, typically 30 min before 
K-sparing diuretics
‚Ä¢ Drugs: spironolactone (Aldactone), amiloride, triamterene, eplerenone
‚Ä¢ Mechanism: T Na reabsorption in collecting duct (amiloride/triamterene inhibit principal
cell Na channel [ENaC]; spironolactone/eplerenone inhibit mineralocorticoid receptor).
Relatively weak natriuretic activity, useful in combination with thiazide or in cirrhosis.
Approach to Diuresis (if inadequate diuresis, go to next step)
Step
Action
1
Loop diuretic PO: ‚úìresponse at 3 h, redose at 2 prior dose if needed
2
Add thiazide diuretic PO (potentiates response to loop diuretic)
3

c dose needed w/ c Cr; initial effective IV lasix dose ‚âà30  Cr
(ie, if Cr  4 S 120 mg IV lasix)
4
Loop diuretic infusion: bolus  continuous IV infusion 	 thiazide
5

Disease state specific regimens
‚Ä¢ Renal insufficiency: loop diuretic (c dose to achieve effective delivery to ThAL) 	 thiazide
‚Ä¢ CHF: loop diuretic (c frequency over c dose)  thiazide (watch K & Mg)
‚Ä¢ Nephrotic syndrome: urinary albumin binds secreted loop diuretic, use 2‚Äì3  normal dose
‚Ä¢ Cirrhosis: spironolactone (blocks 2 hyperaldosteronism)  lasix in 2.5:1 ratio
‚Ä¢ Severe metabolic alkalosis: acetazolamide & treat underlying cause
Adverse effects
‚Ä¢ Loop: T Na, T K, T Mg, T Ca, hyperuricemia, ototoxicity
‚Ä¢ Thiazide: T Na, T K, T Mg, c Ca, hyperlipidemia, pancreatitis
‚Ä¢ K-sparing: c K (especially w/ ACEI), metabolic acidosis

DIALYSIS
General
‚Ä¢ Substitutes for renal solute and fluid removal 
‚Ä¢ Acute: CVVH vs. HD (Chest 2007;132:1379); Chronic: PD vs. HD
Hemodialysis (HD) (NEJM 1998;338:1428; 339:1054)
‚Ä¢ Physiology: blood flows along one side of semipermeable membrane, dialysate along other
Fluid removal (ie, Na  H2O) via transmembrane pressure (TMP) gradient
Solute removal via transmembrane concentration gradient and inversely proportional
to size (‚à¥effective removal of K, urea, and Cr, but not PO4)
‚Ä¢ Typical orders: duration, volume removal goals, K and Ca in dialysate bath, anticoagulation
‚Ä¢ Complications: hypotension, arrhythmia, access complications, disequilibrium syndrome
Vascular Access
Advantages
Disadvantages
AV Fistula
Highest patency
Long maturation time (2‚Äì6 mo)
Lowest risk of bacteremia
Primary nonfunction (20%)
AV Graft
Easier to create than AVF
Poor 1 patency, often requiring
Maturation time (2‚Äì3 wks)
thrombectomy or angioplasty
Catheter
Immediate use
Highest risk of bacteremia
Use as bridge to AVF/AVG
T blood flow S T HD efficiency
Continuous Veno-Venous Hemofiltration (CVVH) (NEJM 1997;336:1303)
‚Ä¢ Physiology: based on hemofiltration rather than dialysis. Blood under pressure passes
down one side of a highly permeable membrane allowing water and solutes to pass
across the membrane via TMP gradient (convective clearance). Filtrate is discarded.
Replacement fluid is infused (solute concentrations similar to plasma, except no K,
urea, Cr, PO4). Fluid balance precisely controlled by adjusting amounts of filtrate
and replacement fluid.
‚Ä¢ Access: double-lumen central venous catheter
‚Ä¢ Typical orders: volume removal goals, replacement fluid buffer: HCO3 (requires heparin 
to prevent machine from clotting) vs. citrate (hepatically metabolized to HCO3;
provides anticoagulation w/ in machine via Ca chelation; ‚à¥need Ca infusion to
maintain serum Ca)
‚Ä¢ Complications: hypotension, T PO4, access complications; T ICa (citrate toxicity in Pts
with hepatic dysfunction S look for T ICa but normal/ c serum Ca and AG meta-
bolic acidosis)
‚Ä¢ Potential advantages over HD: less hypotension, better volume control, removal of
inflammatory mediators; however, no survival advantage (Lancet 2006;368:379)
‚Ä¢ No advantage for high intensity CVVH over standard intensity (NEJM 2008;359:7)
Peritoneal Dialysis (PD) (Perit Dial Int 2001;21:25)
‚Ä¢ Physiology: peritoneum acts as membrane. Fluid balance controlled by choosing
dialysate glucose concentration (higher concentrations pull more fluid into peri-
toneum); longer dwell times pull less fluid as glucose equilibrates across peritoneum
‚Ä¢ Access: permanent catheter inserted in OR
‚Ä¢ Typical orders for CAPD (continuous ambulatory peritoneal dialysis):
PD fluid  1.5%, 2.5%, or 4.25% dextrose
buffer (lactate), Na, K, Ca2, Mg2
infuse 10 min, dwell 30 min‚Äì5.5 h, drain 20 min
‚Ä¢ Can use overnight cycler device that infuses & drains more rapidly, with shorter dwells,
while Pt sleeps. Called automated or continuous cycling peritoneal dialysis (APD, CCPD).
‚Ä¢ Complications
Peritonitis (abdominal pain, tenderness, cloudy drainage)
diagnosis:WBC 100 and 50% PMNs
spectrum: 60‚Äì70% GPC, 15‚Äì20% GNR, remainder no bacteria or fungal
Rx: abx IV or in PD, catheter removal for certain pathogens (eg, yeast, Pseudomonas)
Hyperglycemia: exacerbated by inflammation, long dwell times, and higher [glc]
T albumin; right-sided pleural effusion

ASYMPTOMATIC GLOMERULAR HEMATURIA
Definition and Etiologies
‚Ä¢ Hematuria 	 proteinuria of glomerular origin w/o renal insufficiency or systemic 
disease (nonglomerular hematuria more common; see ‚ÄúHematuria‚Äù)
‚Ä¢ Ddx: any cause of GN, especially IgA; also consider Alport‚Äôs (X-linked, deafness, renal
failure) and thin basement membrane nephropathy (autosomal dominant, benign course)
IgA Nephropathy (NEJM 2002;347:738 & JASN 2005;16:2088)
‚Ä¢ Most common cause of GN; male predominance w/ peak incidence 20‚Äì30s
‚Ä¢ Wide range of clinical presentations: asx hematuria (30‚Äì40%), gross hematuria 1‚Äì3 d
after URI (30‚Äì40%), chronic GN (10%), nephrotic syndrome (5%), RPGN (5%)

‚Ä¢ Prognosis: 25‚Äì30% will reach ESRD w/in 20‚Äì25 y of presentation
‚Ä¢ Treatment:ACEI/ARB, 	 fish oils (NEJM 1994;331:1194); steroids (JASN 2004;15:157) 	 cytotoxic
therapy for crescentic GN and nephrotic sx, consider for progressive chronic GN
NEPHROTIC SYNDROME
Definition
‚Ä¢ Proteinuria 3.5 g/d, albumin 3.5 mg/dL, edema, c cholesterol
Primary glomerular diseases (grouped by pathology)
‚Ä¢ Focal segmental glomerulosclerosis (40%)
idiopathic, HIV (collapsing variant), pamidronate, heroin, congenital, hyperfiltration due
to prior nephron loss, obesity, vesicoureteral reflux
‚Ä¢ Membranous nephropathy (30%)
idiopathic (phospholipase A2 receptor Abs; NEJM 2009;361:11), infxn (espec. HBV, also
HCV, syphilis), autoimmune (espec. SLE), carcinomas, drugs (NSAIDs, penicillamine)
‚Ä¢ Minimal change disease (20%, more common in children)
idiopathic, NSAIDs, Hodgkin‚Äôs disease & other lymphoproliferative disorders
‚Ä¢ Membranoproliferative GN (5%, mixed nephrotic/nephritic features)
Type I: infection (especially HCV 	 cryos; IE, HBV, other chronic infxns), immune
complex disease (SLE, cryos, Sj√∂gren‚Äôs), lymphoproliferative disorders, idiopathic
Type II: very rare; autoAb blocks inactivation of C3 convertase  C3 nephritic factor
‚Ä¢ Fibrillary-immunotactoid glomerulopathy (1%)
‚Ä¢ Mesangial proliferative GN (likely atypical forms of MCD or FSGS, 5%)
Systemic diseases
‚Ä¢ Diabetes mellitus: nodular glomerulosclerosis (Kimmelstiel-Wilson lesion); large kidneys
hyperfiltration S microalbuminuria S dipstick  S nephrotic range (10‚Äì15 y)
concomitant proliferative retinopathy seen in 90% of type 1 and 60% of type 2
‚Ä¢ Amyloidosis:AL or light chain amyloid or AA amyloid secondary to inflammation
‚Ä¢ SLE: typically with membranous nephropathy (WHO class V)
‚Ä¢ Cryoglobulinemia: typically with membranoproliferative GN
Workup (Archives 2001;161:25 & BMJ 2008;336:1185)
‚Ä¢ Urine sediment: usually benign w/o concurrent nephritis; 	 oval fat bodies (‚ÄúMaltese
crosses,‚Äù NEJM 2007;357:806)
‚Ä¢ Measure proteinuria: 24-h urine collection or urine prot/Cr ratio (not accurate in AKI)
‚Ä¢ r/o secondary causes: c HbA1C  retinopathy S presumpt. dx of diabetic nephropathy;
‚úìANA, anti-dsDNA, C3, C4, SPEP/UPEP, fat pad bx, cryocrit, HBV, HCV, HIV, RPR,
phospholipase A2 receptor Ab
‚Ä¢ Renal biopsy

‚Ä¢ 1¬∫ glomerular dis: steroids 	 cytotoxic therapy; cancer screening if membranous neph.
‚Ä¢ Secondary causes: treat underlying disease
‚Ä¢ General: watch for malnutrition (protein loss), thrombosis (esp. renal vein, b/c loss of ATIII

NEPHROLITHIASIS
Types of stones and risk factors (Lancet 2006;367:333 & Annals 2009;151:ITC2)
‚Ä¢ Calcium (Ca oxalate  Ca phosphate): 70‚Äì90% of kidney stones
Urine characteristics: c Ca, c oxalate, c urate, c pH, T citrate, T volume
2 hypercalciuria: 1 hyperparathyroidism, type 1 RTA, sarcoid
2 hyperoxaluria: Crohn‚Äôs, ileal disease w/ intact colon, gastric bypass
Diet: c animal protein, c sucrose, c Na, T K, T fluid, T fruits/vegetables
‚Ä¢ Uric acid: 5‚Äì10% of kidney stones, radiolucent on plain film
Urine characteristics: c uric acid (eg, gout), T pH (eg, from chronic diarrhea)
‚Ä¢ Magnesium ammonium phosphate (‚Äústruvite‚Äù or ‚Äútriple phosphate‚Äù)
Chronic UTI w urea-splitting organisms (eg, Proteus, Klebs) S c urine NH3 and pH (7)
‚Ä¢ Cystine: inherited defects of tubular amino acid reabsorption
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Hematuria (absence does not exclude diagnosis), flank pain, N/V, dysuria, frequency
‚Ä¢ Ureteral obstruction (stones 5 mm unlikely to pass spont.) S AKI if solitary kidney
‚Ä¢ UTI: c risk of infection proximal to stone; urinalysis of distal urine may be normal
Workup
‚Ä¢ Noncontrast helical CT scan (ureteral dilation w/o stone suggests recent passage) 
‚Ä¢ Strain urine for stone to analyze; U/A & UCx, electrolytes, BUN/Cr, Ca, PO4, PTH, UA
‚Ä¢ 24-h urine  2 (6 wk after acute setting) for Ca, PO4, UA, oxalate, citrate, Na, Cr
Acute treatment (NEJM 2004;350:684)
‚Ä¢ Analgesia (narcotics 	 NSAIDs; combination superior, Ann Emerg Med 2006;48:173), aggressive
PO/IV hydration, antibiotics if UTI
‚Ä¢ Consider CCB or alpha blocker to promote ureteral relaxation (Lancet 2006;368:1171)
‚Ä¢ Indications for immediate urologic evaluation and/or hospitalization: obstruction
(especially solitary or transplant kidney), urosepsis, intractable pain or vomiting, AKI
‚Ä¢ Urologic Rx: lithotripsy, cystoscopic stent, percutaneous nephrostomy, stone removal
Chronic treatment
‚Ä¢ Increase fluid intake (2 L/d) for goal UOP 2 L/d
‚Ä¢ Calcium stones: 24-h urine identifies specific urinary risk factors to treat
T Na and meat intake (NEJM 2002;346:77), thiazides: decrease urine Ca
Depending on 24-h urine: K-citrate, dietary oxalate restriction, allopurinol
High dietary Ca is likely beneficial by T oxalate absorp., unclear role of Ca supplements
‚Ä¢ Uric acid: urine alkalinization (K-citrate), allopurinol
‚Ä¢ Magnesium ammonium phosphate: antibiotics to treat UTI, urologic intervention
‚Ä¢ Cystine: urine alkalinization (K-citrate), D-penicillamine, tiopronin, captopril

ANEMIA
T in RBC mass: Hct 41% or Hb 13.5 g/dL (men); Hct 36% or Hb 12 g/dL (women)

MICROCYTIC ANEMIAS
Iron deficiency (NEJM 1999;341:1986 & Hematology ASH Educ Prog 2003;40)
‚Ä¢ T marrow iron & depleted body iron stores S T heme synthesis S microcytosis S anemia
‚Ä¢ Special clinical manifestations: angular cheilosis, atrophic glossitis, pica (consumption of

antiendomysial Ab)
‚Ä¢ Treatment (Fe supplementation): oral Fe tid (6 wk to correct anemia; 6 mo to
replete Fe stores); in cases of excessive/persistent GI losses or for dialysis or cancer
Pts prior to EPO Rx, IV iron (Fe-sucrose, -gluconate, -dextrose) should be considered

erythroid precursors; ‚à¥anemia from hemolysis and ineffective erythropoiesis
‚Ä¢ -thalassemia: deletions in -globin gene complex on chr. 16 (nl 4  genes)
3  S -thal-2 trait  silent carrier; 2  S -thal-1 trait or -thal minor  mild anemia
1  S HbH (4) disease  severe anemia, hemolysis, and splenomegaly
0  genes S Hb Barts (4)  intrauterine hypoxia and hydrops fetalis
‚Ä¢ -thalassemia: mutations in -globin gene on chr. 11 S absent or T gene product
1 mutated  gene S thal minor (or trait)  mild anemia (no transfusions)
2 mutated  genes S thal intermedia (occasional transfusions) or thal major (  Cooley‚Äôs
anemia; transfusion-dependent) depending on severity of mutations

‚Ä¢ Associated with thymoma, CLL, and parvovirus infection
‚Ä¢ Diagnostic studies: lack of erythroid precursors on BM bx, other lines normal
‚Ä¢ Treatment: thymectomy if thymus enlarged; IVIg if parvovirus infection;
immunosuppression if CLL or idiopathic; supportive care with PRBC transfusions;
? erythropoietin receptor agonist if due to antierythropoietin Ab (NEJM 2009;361:1848)

MACROCYTIC ANEMIAS
includes megaloblastic and nonmegaloblastic causes
Megaloblastic anemia
‚Ä¢ Impaired DNA synthesis S cytoplasm matures faster than nucleus S ineffective
erythropoiesis and macrocytosis; due to folate or B12 deficiency
‚Ä¢ ‚úìfolate and vitamin B12, c LDH & indirect bilirubin (due to ineffective erythropoiesis)
‚Ä¢ Smear: neutrophil hypersegmentation, macro-ovalocytes, anisocytosis, poikilocytosis
Folate deficiency
‚Ä¢ Folate present in leafy green vegetables and fruit; total body stores sufficient for 2‚Äì3 mo
‚Ä¢ Etiologies: malnutrition (alcoholics, anorectics, elderly), T absorption (sprue),
impaired metabolism (methotrexate, pyrimethamine, trimethoprim), c requirement
(chronic hemolytic anemia, pregnancy, malignancy, dialysis)
‚Ä¢ Diagnosis: T folate; T RBC folate, c homocyst. but nl methylmalonic acid (unlike B12 defic.)
‚Ä¢ Treatment: folate 1‚Äì5 mg PO qd for 1‚Äì4 mo or until complete hematologic recovery;
critical to r/o B12 deficiency first (see below)
Vitamin B12 deficiency
‚Ä¢ B12 present only in foods of animal origin; total body stores sufficient for 2‚Äì3 y
‚Ä¢ Binds to intrinsic factor (IF) secreted by gastric parietal cells; absorbed in terminal ileum
‚Ä¢ Etiologies: malnutrition (alcoholics, vegans), pernicious anemia (PA, autoimmune dis-
ease against gastric parietal cells, a/w polyglandular endocrine insufficiency and c
risk of gastric carcinoma), other causes of T absorption (gastrectomy, sprue, Crohn‚Äôs
disease), c competition (intestinal bacterial overgrowth, fish tapeworm)
‚Ä¢ Clinical manifestations: neurologic changes (subacute combined degeneration)
affecting peripheral nerves, posterior and lateral columns of the spinal cord, and cortex
S numbness, paresthesias, T vibratory and positional sense, ataxia, dementia
‚Ä¢ Dx: T B12; c homocysteine and methylmalonic acid; anti-IF Ab; Schilling test; c gastrin in PA
‚Ä¢ Treatment: 1 mg B12 IM qd  7 d S q wk  4‚Äì8 wk S q month for life
neurologic abnormalities are reversible if treated w/in 6 mo
folate can reverse hematologic abnormalities of B12 deficiency but not neurologic
changes (and can lead to ‚Äústeal‚Äù of B12 stores S worsening of neuro complications)
oral supplementation (2 mg qd) appears feasible as well (Blood 1998;92:1191) even w/o IF
Nonmegaloblastic macrocytic anemias
‚Ä¢ Liver disease: often macrocytic, may see target cells
‚Ä¢ Alcoholism: BM suppression & macrocytosis independent of folate/B12 defic. or cirrhosis
‚Ä¢ Reticulocytosis
‚Ä¢ Other causes: hypothyroidism; MDS; meds that impair DNA synthesis (zidovudine,
5-FU, hydroxyurea,Ara-C); hereditary orotic aciduria; Lesch-Nyhan synd.
PANCYTOPENIA
Etiologies
‚Ä¢ Hypocellular bone marrow (nl cellularity 100 ‚Äì age): aplastic anemia, hypoplastic MDS
‚Ä¢ Cellular bone marrow: MDS, aleukemic leukemia, PNH, severe megaloblastic anemia
‚Ä¢ Marrow replacement (myelophthisis): myelofibrosis, metastatic solid tumors, granulomas
‚Ä¢ Systemic diseases: hypersplenism, sepsis, alcohol, toxins
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Anemia S fatigue
‚Ä¢ Neutropenia S recurrent infections
‚Ä¢ Thrombocytopenia S mucosal bleeding & easy bruisability
Aplastic anemia  stem cell failure (Lancet 2005;365:1647)
‚Ä¢ Epidemiology: 2‚Äì5 cases/106/y; biphasic (major peak in adolescents, 2nd peak in elderly)
‚Ä¢ Diagnosis: pancytopenia w/ T retics, BM bx w/ cytogenetics showing hypocellularity
‚Ä¢ Etiologies: idiopathic (1‚ÅÑ2‚Äì2‚ÅÑ3 of cases)
stem cell destruction: radiation, chemotherapy, chemicals (eg, benzene)
idiosyncratic med rxn (eg, chloramphenicol, NSAIDs, sulfa drugs, gold,
carbamazepine, antithyroid)
viruses (HHV-6, HIV, EBV, parvovirus B19); also posthepatitis (non A, B, or C)
immune disorders (SLE, GVHD post HSCT, thymoma)
PNH (see below); Fanconi‚Äôs anemia (congenital disorder w/ pancytopenia, macrocytic
anemia, c risk of MDS,AML, & SCC of head & neck, and multiple physical anomalies);
telomerase (hTERT) mutation (NEJM 2005;352:1413)

‚Ä¢ Treatment and prognosis
allogeneic HSCT: for young Pts S 80% long-term survival and significantly T risk
of malignant evolution, but has risk of transplant-related morbidity & mortality; if
possible avoid transfusions (and alloimmunization) pretransplant
immunosuppression (CsA/tacrolimus,ATG): 70‚Äì80% respond, with 80‚Äì90% 5-y survival
in responders; 15‚Äì20% 10-y incidence of clonal disorders (mostly MDS, AML, PNH)
supportive care: transfusions, antibiotics, possible utility of G-CSF and epo
Myelodysplastic syndromes (MDS) (qv) 
Paroxysmal nocturnal hemoglobinuria (PNH)

complement-mediated RBC lysis, plt aggreg., & hypercoagulability
‚Ä¢ Clinical: intravascular hemolytic anemia, hypercoagulability (venous 

‚Ä¢ Dx: peripheral blood flow cytometry (T CD55 & CD59); urine hemosiderosis

allogeneic HSCT for hypoplasia or severe thrombosis

Myelophthisic anemia (see ‚ÄúPrimary Myelofibrosis‚Äù)
‚Ä¢ Infiltration of bone marrow by cancer, leukemia, infection, fibrosis (primary myelofi-
brosis), granulomas, lysosomal storage disorders

Diagnostic evaluation

Glucose-6-phosphate dehydrogenase (G6PD) deficiency (Lancet 2008;371:64)
‚Ä¢ X-linked defect of metabolism (G6PD mutations) w/ c susceptibility to oxidative damage
‚Ä¢ Most common in males of African or Mediterranean descent (malaria-endemic areas)
‚Ä¢ Hemolysis precipitated by drugs (sulfonamides, dapsone, primaquine, doxorubicin,

‚Ä¢ Diagnosis: smear may show RBC Heinz bodies (oxidized Hb) that result in bite cells

RBCs have already lysed and young RBCs may still have near normal levels)
Sickle cell anemia (NEJM 1999;340:1021; Hematology ASH Educ Program 2004;35)
‚Ä¢ Recessive -globin mutation S structurally abnl hemoglobin (HbS);
8% of African Americans are heterozygotes (‚Äúsickle trait‚Äù; usually w/o sx)
1 in 400 are homozygotes (sickle cell disease)
‚Ä¢ Deoxygenated HbS polymerizes S RBC sickles and T RBC deformability S hemolysis and
microvascular occlusion
‚Ä¢ Anemia: chronic hemolysis 	 acute aplastic (parvo. B19) or splenic sequestration crises
‚Ä¢ Vaso-occlusion and infarction: painful crises, acute chest syndrome, CVA, splenic

‚Ä¢ Infection: splenic infarction S overwhelming infection by encapsulated organisms;
infarcted bone S osteomyelitis (Salmonella, Staph. aureus)
‚Ä¢ Diagnosis: sickle-shaped RBCs and Howell-Jolly bodies on smear; Hb electrophoresis
‚Ä¢ Treatment: hydroxyurea causes c HbF S T painful crises, acute chest episodes and

Hereditary spherocytosis (HS) (Br J Hematol 2004;126:455)
‚Ä¢ Defect in a cytoskeletal protein of RBC membrane S membrane loss

Autoimmune hemolytic anemia (AIHA)
‚Ä¢ Acquired, antibody-mediated RBC destruction
‚Ä¢ Warm AIHA: IgG Abs opsonize RBCs at body temp S removal by spleen
Etiologies: idiopathic, lymphoproliferative disorders (CLL, NHL), autoimmune diseases
(SLE), drugs (see below)
‚Ä¢ Cold AIHA: IgM Ab bind to RBCs at temp 37C S complement fixation
S intravascular hemolysis and acrocyanosis on exposure to cold
Etiologies: idiopathic, lymphoproliferative (eg,Waldenstr√∂m‚Äôs) disorders (monoclonal),
Mycoplasma pneumoniae infection and infectious mononucleosis (polyclonal)
‚Ä¢ Diagnosis: spherocytes on smear,  Coombs‚Äô; ‚úìcold agglutinin titer, splenomegaly
‚Ä¢ Treatment: treat underlying disease; warm AIHA: corticosteroids 	 splenectomy, IVIg,
cytotoxic agents, rituximab; cold AIHA: cold avoidance, steroids often ineffective,
rituximab (Blood 2004;103:2925)
Drug-induced hemolytic anemia
‚Ä¢ Acquired, antibody-mediated, RBC destruction precipitated by a medication:
abx: cephalosporins, sulfa drugs, rifampin, ribavirin
CV: methyldopa, procainamide, quinidine, thiazides
other:TCAs, phenothiazines, NSAIDs, sulfonylureas, MTX, 5-FU
‚Ä¢ Diagnosis: Coombs‚Äô usually negative, c LDH
‚Ä¢  Treatment: discontinue offending agent
Microangiopathic hemolytic anemia (MAHA)
‚Ä¢ Intraarteriolar fibrin damages RBCs S acquired intravascular hemolysis
‚Ä¢ Etiologies: hemolytic-uremic syndrome (HUS), thrombotic thrombocytopenic 
purpura (TTP), disseminated intravascular coagulation (DIC), malignancy,
malignant HTN, eclampsia/HELLP, mech. cardiac valves, infected vascular prostheses
‚Ä¢ Diagnosis: schistocytes 	 thrombocytopenia 	 abnormalities associated with specific
disorders, eg, c PT in DIC, c Cr in HUS, c LFTs in HELLP
‚Ä¢ Treatment: treat underlying abnormality; urgent plasma exchange for TTP
Hypersplenism
‚Ä¢ Splenomegaly S stasis and trapping in the spleen S macrophagic attack and 
remodeling of RBC surface S spherocytosis S hemolysis

‚Ä¢ Primary ITP is diagnosis of exclusion; no robust clinical or lab parameters, but typically:

‚Ä¢ Treatment of type II (NEJM 2006;355:809; Chest 2008;133:340S)
Discontinue heparin (including flushes, LMWH prophylaxis, heparin-impregnated lines)
Avoid plt transfusions if not actively bleeding (anecdotally linked w/ thrombotic events)
Nonheparin anticoag. (argatroban, lepirudin, bivalirudin) regardless if thrombosis; initiate
warfarin when plt 150,000, overlap 5 days (‚úìchromogenic Xa to titrate)
 thrombosis (HITT):  anticoagulate for  3‚Äì6 mo
 thrombosis (isolated HIT): screen for LE DVT; no consensus on duration of subse-
quent anticoag. (at least until plt count recovers, more often 2‚Äì3 mo if no clot)
‚Ä¢ Heparin use if h/o HIT: if PF4 Ab  (typically 100 d after dx) S re-exposure to UFH
reasonable (eg, for surgery); HIT recurrence low (NEJM 2001;344:1286; Chest 2008;133:340S)
Hemolytic-uremic syndrome (HUS) & thrombotic thrombocytopenic purpura (TTP)
‚Ä¢ Definition: vascular occlusive disorders w/ systemic (TTP) or intrarenal (HUS) plt aggreg.
S thrombocytopenia & mechanical injury to RBCs (MAHA) (NEJM 2002;347:589)
HUS triad  thrombocytopenia  MAHA  renal failure
TTP pentad  thrombocytopenia  MAHA 	  MS 	 renal failure 	 fever
‚Ä¢ Pathophysiology: mechanism in most HUS cases is distinct from TTP (NEJM 1998;339:1578)
HUS: Shiga toxin binds & activates renal endothelial cells & plts S intrarenal thrombi
TTP: T ADAMTS13 protease activity S persistence of large vWF multimers on endothelial
surface S adhesion and aggregation of passing platelets S thrombosis
‚Ä¢ Clinical manifestations and associations
HUS: usually in children; prodrome of bloody diarrhea due to enterohemorrhagic E. coli
TTP: usually in adults; idiopathic, drugs (CsA, gemcitabine, mitomycin C, ticlopidine,
clopidogrel, quinine), HIV, pregnancy, HSCT, autoimmune disease, familial
‚Ä¢ Diagnosis: unexplained thrombocytopenia (typically 20 k)  MAHA S sufficient for dx
 schistocytes (2‚Äì3/hpf),  Coombs, normal PT/PTT & fibrinogen, TT ADAMTS13
cc LDH (tissue ischemia  hemolysis), c indirect bili., cc haptoglobin, c Cr (esp. in HUS)
Biopsy: arterioles filled with platelet hyaline thrombi
Ddx: DIC, vasculitis, malignant hypertension, preeclampsia/HELLP syndrome
‚Ä¢ Treatment: urgent plasma exchange 	 glucocorticoids in all adults w/ suspected
TTP-HUS; FFP if delay to plasma exchange
platelet transfusions contraindicated S c microvascular thrombosis (NEJM 2006;354:1927)
Disseminated intravascular coagulation (DIC): see ‚ÄúDisorders of Coagulation‚Äù

Tests of platelet function
‚Ä¢ Bleeding time: global screen of platelet function; not reliable and rarely used
‚Ä¢ Platelet aggregation tests: measure aggregation in response to agonists (eg,ADP)
von Willebrand‚Äôs disease (vWD) (NEJM 2004;351:683)
‚Ä¢ von Willebrand‚Äôs factor (vWF) function  platelet glue & plasma carrier of factor VIII
‚Ä¢ vWD is the most common inherited bleeding disorder.
Type 1 (autosomal dominant; 85% of cases): partial quantitative deficiency in vWF
Type 2 (autosomal dominant; 15% of cases): qualitative deficiency of vWF
Type 3 (autosomal recessive; rare): near complete deficiency of vWF
‚Ä¢ Acquired vWD: associated with many disorders (malignancy, autoimmune, hypothyroidism,
drugs) and caused by different mechanisms (anti-vWF Abs, c clearance, T synthesis)
‚Ä¢ Diagnosis: T vWF:Ag, T vWF activity (measured by ristocetin cofactor assay), T factor
VIII, 	 c PTT, 	 T platelets; confirm with vWF multimer analysis
‚Ä¢ Clinical condition, factor VIII levels and ristocetin cofactor assay useful to guide Rx decision
‚Ä¢ Treatment: desmopressin (dDAVP, IV/IN) S c endothelial cell release of vWF; variable
efficacy depending on type, ‚à¥‚úìresponse before use w/ subseq. bleeding or procedures;
vWF replacement: cryoprecipitate, factor VIII concentrates rich in vWF, recomb. vWF
Uremic Bleeding
‚Ä¢ Uremia S platelet dysfunction including T aggregation, impaired adhesiveness
‚Ä¢ Treatment: dDAVP, cryoprecipitate, correct anemia (improves plt aggregation and
adhesion by increasing plt interactions with endothelium),consider holding anti-plt agents

Hemophilias (NEJM 2001;344:1773)
‚Ä¢ X-linked factor VIII (hemophilia A) or factor IX (hemophilia B) deficiency
‚Ä¢ Classification: mild (5‚Äì25% normal factor activity), moderate (1‚Äì5%), or severe (1%)
‚Ä¢ Clinical manifestations: hematomas, hemarthroses, bruising, bleeding (mucosal, GI, GU)
‚Ä¢ Diagnosis: c PTT (normalizes w/mixing study), normal PT & vWF, T factor VIII or IX
‚Ä¢ Treatment: purified/recomb. factor VIII or IX concentrate, desmopressin (mild disease),
aminocaproic acid; recomb. factor VIIa if factor inhib., cryo (only has factor VIII)
Coagulation factor inhibitors
‚Ä¢ Etiologies: hemophilia (treated with factor replacement); postpartum; lymphoproliferative
disorders and other malignancies; autoimmune diseases; most commonly anti‚Äìfactor VIII
‚Ä¢ Diagnosis: c PTT (does not normalize w/mixing study); Bethesda assay quantitates titer
‚Ä¢ Treatment: high titer S recomb. factor VIIa, porcine factor concentrates, activated
prothrombin complex; others S high-purity human factor, plasmapheresis, immuno-
supp. w/ steroids, cyclophosphamide, and/or rituximab (Curr Opin Hematol 2008;15:451)
Disseminated intravascular coagulation (DIC) (NEJM 1999;341:586)
‚Ä¢ Etiologies: trauma, shock, infection, malignancy (esp.APL), obstetric complications
‚Ä¢ Pathogenesis: massive activation of coagulation that overwhelms control mechanisms
thrombosis in microvasculature S ischemia  microangiopathic hemolytic anemia
acute consumption of coagulation factors and platelets S bleeding
chronic DIC S able to replete factors and platelets S thrombosis
‚Ä¢ Diagnosis: c PT, c PTT, T fibrinogen (may be nl b/c acute phase),  FDP/D-dimer, T
plts,  schisto, c LDH, T hapto; chronic DIC:  FDP/D-dimer, variable plts, other labs nl
‚Ä¢ Treatment: treat underlying process; support with FFP, cryoprecipitate (goal fibrinogen
100 mg/dL), and platelets; consider activated protein C in severe sepsis
Vitamin K deficiency
‚Ä¢ Etiologies: malnutrition, T absorption (antibiotic suppression of vitamin K-producing
intestinal flora or malabsorption), liver disease (T stores), warfarin

Antiphospholipid syndrome (APS) (NEJM 2002;346:752)
‚Ä¢ Definition: dx requires 1 clinical & 1 laboratory criteria
Clinical: thrombosis (any) or complication of pregnancy (3 spont. abortions before 
10 wk or 1 fetal loss after 10 wk or premature birth before 34 wk)

‚Ä¢ Antiphospholipid antibodies (APLA)
‚úì if: SLE, age 40 y & arterial thromb, recurrent venous thromb, spontaneous abortion 
ACL: Ab against cardiolipin, a mitochondrial phospholipid; IgG more specific than IgM
LA: Ab that prolongs phospholipid-dependent coagulation reactions; ‚à¥c PTT that
does not correct with mixing study but does correct with excess phospholipids
or platelets; PT not affected b/c the reaction contains much more phospholipid
2-GP-I:  Ab against 2-glycoprotein-I, IgG or IgM
False VDRL: nontreponemal test for syphilis in which cardiolipin is part of Ag complex
Clinical significance of different Abs in pathogenesis uncertain
Risk of thromboembolic phenomena may increase with titer of APLs
‚Ä¢ Etiologies: primary (idiopathic) or secondary due to autoimmune syndromes
(eg, SLE), malignancy, infections, drug reactions
‚Ä¢ Treatment: UFH/LMWH S warfarin after thromboembolic event (lifelong for most Pts)

INR 2‚Äì3 for an initial venous thrombosis (NEJM 2003;349:1133; J Thromb Haemost 2005;3:848)

Transfusion reactions
‚Ä¢ For all reactions (except minor allergic): stop transfusion; send remaining blood
product and fresh blood sample to blood bank
‚Ä¢ Acute hemolytic: fever, hypotension, flank pain, renal failure 24 h after transfusion
Due to ABO incompatibility S preformed Abs against donor RBCs
Treatment: vigorous IVF, maintain UOP with diuretics, mannitol, or dopamine
‚Ä¢ Delayed hemolytic: generally less severe than acute hemolytic; 5‚Äì7 d after transfusion
Due to undetected allo-Abs against minor antigens S anamnestic response
Treatment: usually no specific therapy required; dx is important for future transfusion
‚Ä¢ Febrile nonhemolytic: fever and rigors 0‚Äì6 h after transfusion
Due to Abs against donor WBCs and cytokines released from cells in blood product
Treatment: acetaminophen 	 meperidine; rule out infection and hemolysis
‚Ä¢ Allergic: urticaria; rarely, anaphylaxis: bronchospasm, laryngeal edema, hypotension
Reaction to transfused proteins; anaphylaxis seen in IgA-deficient Pts w/ anti-IgA Abs
Treatment: urticaria S diphenhydramine; anaphylaxis S epinephrine 	 glucocorticoids
‚Ä¢ Transfusion-related acute lung injury (TRALI): noncardiogenic pulmonary edema
Due to donor Abs that bind recipient WBCs, which then aggregate in pulmonary
vasculature and release mediators causing c capillary permeability. Rx: see ‚ÄúARDS.‚Äù

MYELODYSPLASTIC SYNDROMES (MDS)
Myeloid neoplasm overview (Blood 2009;114:937)
‚Ä¢ Myeloid neoplasms are classified into 5 categories based on bone marrow morphology,
clinical characteristics and genetics (WHO 2008 system)
WHO 2008 Myeloid Neoplasm Categories

20% blasts, but risk of leukemic transformation

Myelodysplastic syndromes (MDS) overview (NEJM 2009;361:1872)
‚Ä¢ Acquired clonal stem cell disorder S ineffective hematopoiesis S cytopenias,
dysmorphic blood cells and precursors, variable risk of leukemic transformation
‚Ä¢ Epidemiology: 100 cases/106/y; median age 65 y; male predominance (1.8x)
‚Ä¢ Idiopathic or 2 to chemo w/ alkylating agents, topo II inhib.; c risk w/ radiation, benzene
‚Ä¢ Clinical manifestations: anemia (85%), neutropenia (50%), thrombocytopenia (25%)
‚Ä¢ Diagnosis: dysplasia (usually multilineage) in peripheral smear (ovalomacrocytes,
pseudo-Pelger-Hu√´t anomaly) and bone marrow (10% dysplasia with blasts 	 RS) 
‚Ä¢ Cytogenetic abnormalities: several are characteristic of MDS and have prognostic
significance [eg, del(5q), monosomy 7, del(7q), trisomy 8, del(20q)]
‚Ä¢ Prior to dx MDS: exclude AML (20% blasts) and CMML (monocyte count 1  109/L);
r/o 2 BM s due to defic. of B12, folate, copper; viral infections (eg, HIV);
chemotherapy; alcohol abuse; lead or arsenic toxicity

MPN
5-15
MYELOPROLIFERATIVE NEOPLASMS (MPN)
General (NEJM 2006;355:2452; Nat Rev Clin Oncol 2009;6:627)
‚Ä¢ Results from clonal expansion of multipotent hematopoietic stem cell
‚Ä¢ A type of myeloid neoplasm (see MDS for classification)
‚Ä¢ Different from MDS in that the cells are not dysplastic (ie, normally developed)
‚Ä¢ 8 categories of MPN: polycythemia vera (PV); essential thrombocythemia (ET); primary
myelofibrosis (PM); chronic myelogenous leukemia (CML), BCR-ABL1‚Äì; chronic
neutrophilic leukemia; chronic eosinophilic leukemia, not otherwise specified; masto-
cytosis; myeloproliferative neoplasms, unclassifiable
‚Ä¢ Gain of fxn mutations in JAK2 (Janus kinase) present in most cases of MPN (PV 100%,
ET 50%, PMF 50%; NEJM 2005;352:1779) and BCR-ABL fusion in all cases of CML;
KIT mutations in virtually all mastocytosis; MPL and TET2 mutations w/ lower 
frequency; genetic lesions are useful as a clonal marker and dx tool
POLYCYTHEMIA VERA (PV)
Definition
‚Ä¢ c in RBC mass 	 c granulocytes and platelets in the absence of physiologic stimulus
Etiologies of erythrocytosis
‚Ä¢ Relative c RBC (T plasma): dehydration;‚Äústress‚Äù erythrocytosis (Gaisb√∂ck‚Äôs syndrome)
‚Ä¢ Absolute c RBC: 1 (PV, other MPD) or 2 due to hypoxia; carboxyhemoglobinemia;
inappropriate erythropoietin (renal, hepatic, cerebellar tumors); Cushing‚Äôs syndrome
Clinical manifestations (common between PV and ET)
‚Ä¢ Symptoms S often termed ‚Äúvasomotor symptoms‚Äù
hyperviscosity (erythrocytosis): headache, dizziness, tinnitus, blurred vision
thrombosis (hyperviscosity, thrombocytosis): transient visual disturbances (amaurosis,
ocular migraine); Budd-Chiari syndrome; erythromelalgia  intense burning, pain, and
erythema of extremities due to microvascular thrombi; c risk of DVT, MI, stroke.
Risk of thrombosis is highly correlated with elevated WBC in PV and ET (see below).
bleeding (abnormal platelet function): easy bruising, epistaxis, GI bleeding
c histamine from basophils S pruritus, peptic ulcers; c uric acid (cell turnover) S gout
‚Ä¢ Signs: plethora, splenomegaly, hypertension, engorged retinal veins
Diagnostic evaluation
‚Ä¢ Hb 18.5 g/dL (men), 16.5 g/dL (women)
‚Ä¢ ‚úìEpo to rule out secondary causes of erythrocytosis; if Epo T, PV likely
If Epo c, then ‚úìSaO2 or PaO2, carboxyhemoglobin
‚Ä¢ JAK2 V617F mutation screen on peripheral blood is positive in 95% of PV and
JAK2 exon 12 mutations are present in the remainder of Pts
‚Ä¢ 	 c WBC, platelets, basophils; c uric acid, leukocyte alkaline phosphatase, vitamin B12
‚Ä¢ Peripheral smear S no morphologic abnormalities
‚Ä¢ BM bx S hypercellular, megakaryocytic hyperplasia, T iron, absence of Ph chromosome
Treatment
‚Ä¢ Phlebotomy (espec if sx) to moderate degree of Fe defic. S Hct 45% () or
42% ()
‚Ä¢ Low-dose ASA in all Pts (NEJM 2004;350:114)
‚Ä¢ Hydroxyurea if high risk of thrombosis (age 60, prior thrombosis) or sx throm-
bocytosis (plt 1.5  106/L)
‚Ä¢ Supportive: allopurinol (gout), H2-blockers/antihistamines (pruritus)
Prognosis
‚Ä¢ Median survival if treated is 9‚Äì12 y
‚Ä¢ Risk of transformation into acute leukemia (2% for untreated Pts, higher if previous chemo)
‚Ä¢ Post-PV myelofibrosis (spent phase) occurs in 15% of cases, usually after 10 y

PRIMARY MYELOFIBROSIS (PMF)
Definition
‚Ä¢ Clonal myeloproliferation with reactive marrow fibrosis & extramedullary hematopoiesis
‚Ä¢ Formerly known as agnogenic myeloid metaplasia with myelofibrosis
Etiologies of myelophthisis (marrow replacement)
‚Ä¢ 1 	 primary myelofibrosis; post-PV/ET myelofibrosis
‚Ä¢ 2 	 hematologic (eg, leukemia, MDS) or metastatic malignancies (eg, breast, prostate)
collagen vascular disorders (eg, SLE)
toxins (eg, benzene, radiation)
granulomas from infection (eg,TB, fungal) or sarcoid
deposition diseases (eg, Gaucher‚Äôs disease)

‚Ä¢ Ineffective erythropoiesis S anemia; extramedullary hematopoiesis S massive

‚Ä¢ Tumor bulk and c cell turnover S fatigue, weight loss, fever, sweats

‚Ä¢ Anemia with variable WBC and platelet counts
‚Ä¢ Peripheral smear S ‚Äúleukoerythroblastic‚Äù (teardrop cells, nucleated RBCs,

‚Ä¢ BM aspirate S ‚Äúdry‚Äù tap; BM bx S severe fibrosis, replacement by reticulin & collagen

‚Ä¢ Does not meet WHO criteria for CML (absence of BCR-ABL translocation), PV, MDS

‚Ä¢ In absence of adverse prognostic factors (eg, anemia or sx) S no treatment
‚Ä¢ Allogeneic HSCT only potential cure S consider in young Pts with poor prognosis

ACUTE LEUKEMIA
Definition
‚Ä¢ Clonal proliferation of hematopoietic progenitor with T ability to differentiate into mature
elements S c blasts in bone marrow and periphery S T RBCs, platelets, and neutrophils
Epidemiology and risk factors
‚Ä¢ Acute myelogenous leukemia (AML): 12,000 cases/y; median age 65 y; 80% of
adult acute leukemia cases
‚Ä¢ Acute lymphocytic leukemia (ALL): 4000 cases/y; median age 10 y; bimodal with
2nd peak in elderly
‚Ä¢ Risk factors: radiation, chemo (alkylating agents, topo II inhib), benzene, smoking
‚Ä¢ Acquired hematopoietic diseases: MDS, MPN (especially CML), aplastic anemia, PNH
‚Ä¢ Inherited: Down‚Äôs & Klinefelter‚Äôs, Fanconi‚Äôs anemia, Bloom syndrome, ataxia telangiectasia
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Cytopenias S fatigue (anemia), infection (neutropenia), bleeding (thrombocytopenia)
‚Ä¢ More common in AML:
leukostasis (when blast count 50,000/L): occluded microcirculation S local
hypoxemia and hemorrhage S headache, blurred vision,TIA/CVA, dyspnea, hypoxia;
look for hyperviscosity retinopathy (vascular engorgement, exudates, hemorrhage)
DIC (especially with APL)
leukemic infiltration of skin, gingiva (especially with monocytic subtypes)
chloroma: extramedullary tumor of leukemic cells, virtually any location
‚Ä¢ More common in ALL:
bone pain, lymphadenopathy, hepatosplenomegaly (also seen in monocytic AML)
CNS involvement (15%): cranial neuropathies, nausea and vomiting, headache
anterior mediastinal mass (especially in T-cell); tumor lysis syndrome (qv)
Diagnostic evaluation (Blood 2009;114:937)
‚Ä¢ Peripheral smear: anemia, thrombocytopenia, variable WBC (50% p/w c WBC,
50% p/w normal or T WBC)  circulating blasts (seen in 95%;  Auer Rods in AML)
‚Ä¢ Bone marrow: hypercellular with 20% blasts; cytogenetics, flow cytometry
‚Ä¢ Presence of certain cytogenetic anomalies, ie, t(15;17), t(8;21), inv(16) or
t(16;16), are sufficient for dx of AML regardless of the blast count
‚Ä¢ ‚úìfor tumor lysis syndrome (rapid cell turnover): c UA, c LDH, c K, c PO4, T Ca
‚Ä¢ Coagulation studies to r/o DIC: PT, PTT, fibrinogen, D-dimer
‚Ä¢ LP (w/ co-admin of intrathecal chemotherapy to avoid seeding CSF w/ circulating
blasts) for ALL Pts (CNS is sanctuary site) and for AML w/ CNS sx
‚Ä¢ TTE if prior cardiac history or before use of anthracyclines
‚Ä¢ HLA typing of Pt, siblings, and parents for potential allogeneic HSCT candidates

Treatment (Blood 2009;113:1875 & 2010;115:453)
‚Ä¢ Induction chemo followed by consolidation Rx

‚Ä¢ ‚úìfor complete remission (CR)  normal peripheral counts, 5% BM blasts
CR Z cure ‚à¥must always f/u induction with consolidation Rx
‚Ä¢ If  CR: consolidation Rx based on risk stratification (age, genetics, PS): chemo or
allogeneic HSCT or autologous HSCT (JAMA 2009;301:2349)
‚Ä¢ If  CR: reinduction with similar chemotherapy (25) or alternative regimen
