Acute Promyelocytic Leukemia (APL) (Blood 2009;113:1875)
‚Ä¢ Rare disease w/ only 600‚Äì800 cases/y in US, but biologically and clinically distinct
‚Ä¢ Atypical promyelocytes (large, granular cells; creased nuclei) in blood and bone marrow
‚Ä¢ Defined by translocation of retinoic acid receptor: t(15;17); PML-RAR (95% of cases)
‚Ä¢ Medical emergency with DIC and bleeding common; supportive care measures crucial
‚Ä¢ Remarkable responses to all-trans-retinoic acid (ATRA), which induces differentiation,
and arsenic trioxide (ATO); early initiation of ATRA is critical as soon as APL suspected
‚Ä¢ Induction chemo typically anthracycline  ATRA 	 cytarabine S CR in 90% of Pts
‚Ä¢ Consolidation Rx (eg,ATO S anthracycline  ATRA) followed by prolonged mainte-
nance Rx (eg,ATRA  6MP  MTX); ATO highly active in induction and consolida-
tion and is promising as 1st-line Rx or for treatment of refractory disease
‚Ä¢ Overall best prognosis of all AMLs w/ 80% cure rate; WBC 10,000/L 
is adverse prognostic factor (Blood 2000;96:1247)

CHRONIC MYELOGENOUS LEUKEMIA (CML)
Definition (Blood 2009;114:937)
‚Ä¢ Myeloproliferative neoplasm with clonal overproduction of hematopoietic
myeloid stem cells that can differentiate
‚Ä¢ Philadelphia chromosome (Ph) 	 t(9;22) S BCR-ABL fusion S c Abl kinase activity
BCR-ABL required for Dx of CML
‚Ä¢ ‚ÄúAtypical CML‚Äù (BCR-ABL ) now considered a separate disease and reclassified as
MDS/MPN (see ‚ÄúMyelodysplastic Syndromes‚Äù)
Epidemiology and risk factors
‚Ä¢ 4300 new cases/y in U.S.; median age 50 at presentation; 15% of adult leukemias
‚Ä¢ c risk with irradiation; no clear relation to cytotoxic drugs
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Triphasic clinical course; 85% present in the chronic phase
‚Ä¢ Chronic phase: often asymptomatic but common features are fatigue, malaise, weight
loss, night sweats, abdominal fullness (splenomegaly 50%)
‚Ä¢ Accelerated phase: refractory leukocytosis and worsening symptoms S fever, weight
loss, progressive splenomegaly, bone pain, bleeding, infections, pruritus (basophilia)
‚Ä¢ Blastic phase  acute leukemia S severe constitutional symptoms, infection, bleeding
and possible leukostasis (see ‚ÄúAcute Leukemia‚Äù)
Diagnostic evaluation
‚Ä¢ Peripheral smear: leukocytosis (often 100,000/L), left-shifted with all stages of
myeloid maturation; anemia, thrombocytosis, basophilia
‚Ä¢ Bone marrow: hypercellular, c myeloid to erythroid ratio, T leuk alkaline phosphatase
‚Ä¢ Chronic: 10% blasts (peripheral or BM)
‚Ä¢ Accelerated: 10‚Äì20% blasts, 20% basos, plts 100K, c spleen size, karyotypic prog.
‚Ä¢ Blastic: 20% blasts (2/3 myeloid, 1/3 lymphoid), may see extramedullary leukemia
Treatment (NEJM 2006;355:2408; Lancet 2007;370:342; NEJM 2007;357:258)
‚Ä¢ Tyrosine kinase inhib: 1st line Rx chronic phase; continued indef in responders
imatinib, dasatinib & nilotinib are selective inhib of BCR-ABL (Blood 2008;112:4808)
imatinib active in chronic, accelerated, blastic phases (but less as disease advances)
imatinib resistance is associated with BCR-ABL mutation or amplification 
dasatanib and nilotinib are more potent BCR-ABL inhibitors and yield higher
response rates than imatinib as initial therapy (NEJM 2010:362:2251 & 2260); both are effec-
tive against most imatinib resistance mutations except T315I (NEJM 2006:354:2531 & 2542)
side effects include nausea, diarrhea, muscle cramps, cytopenias, T PO4, rarely CHF;
dasatinib also a/w pericardial & pleural effusions, nilotinib w/ c bili & lipase
‚Ä¢ Allogeneic HSCT: consider for Pts w/ available donor who present in accelerated or
blastic phase; reasonable option for Pts with relapsed/refractory disease to imatinib
(especially Pts w/ BCR-ABL T315I mutation)

CHRONIC LYMPHOCYTIC LEUKEMIA (CLL)
Definition (NEJM 2005;352:804; Br J Haematol 2007;139:672)
‚Ä¢ Monoclonal accumulation of functionally incompetent mature B-lymphocytes
‚Ä¢ CLL & small lymphocytic lymphoma (SLL) now classified as same disease
Epidemiology and risk factors
‚Ä¢ 10,000 new cases/y; median age at dx is 65 y; most common adult leukemia
‚Ä¢ c incidence in 1st-degree relatives; no known association with radiation, chemicals, drugs

‚Ä¢ Symptoms: often asx & identified when CBC reveals lymphocytosis; 10‚Äì20% p/w fatigue,
malaise, night sweats, weight loss (ie, lymphoma ‚ÄúB‚Äù sx)
‚Ä¢ Signs: lymphadenopathy (80%) and hepatosplenomegaly (50%)
‚Ä¢ Autoimmune hemolytic anemia (AIHA) or thrombocytopenia (ITP)
‚Ä¢ Hypogammaglobulinemia 	 neutropenia S c susceptibility to infections
‚Ä¢ Bone marrow failure
‚Ä¢ Monoclonal gammopathy in 5%
‚Ä¢ Aggressive transformation: 5% develop Richter‚Äôs syndrome  transformation into
high-grade lymphoma (usually DLBCL) and sudden clinical deterioration

‚Ä¢ Peripheral smear: lymphocytosis (5000/L, mature-appearing small cells)
‚Äúsmudge‚Äù cells from damage to abnl lymphs from shear stress of making blood smear
‚Ä¢ Flow cytometry: clonality with dim surface Ig (sIg); CD5, CD19, CD20, CD23.
CD38 or ZAP70 a/w unmutated Ig variable heavy chain region & worse prognosis
‚Ä¢ Bone marrow: normo- or hypercellular; infiltrated w/ small B-cell lymphocytes (30%)
‚Ä¢ Lymph nodes: infiltrated w/ small lymphocytic or diffuse small cleaved cells  SLL
‚Ä¢ Cytogenetics: 11q22-23 & 17p13 unfavorable; trisomy 12 neutral; 13q14 favorable

LYMPHOMA
Definition
‚Ä¢ Malignant disorder of lymphoid cells that reside predominantly in lymphoid tissues
‚Ä¢ Hodgkin lymphoma (HL) is distinguished from non-Hodgkin‚Äôs lymphoma (NHL) by
the presence of Reed-Sternberg (RS) cells
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Lymphadenopathy (nontender)
HL: superficial (usually cervical/supraclavicular) 	 mediastinal lymphadenopathy;
nodal disease with orderly, anatomic spread to adjacent nodes
NHL: diffuse; nodal and extranodal disease with noncontiguous spread;
symptoms reflect involved sites (abdominal fullness, bone pain)
‚Ä¢ Constitutional (‚ÄúB‚Äù) symptoms: fever (38), sweats, weight loss (10% over 6 mos)
HL: periodic, recurrent ‚ÄúPel-Ebstein‚Äù fever; 10‚Äì15% have pruritus
NHL: ‚ÄúB‚Äù symptoms less common than in HL
Diagnostic and staging evaluation
‚Ä¢ Physical exam: lymph nodes, liver/spleen size, Waldeyer‚Äôs ring, testes (1% of NHL), skin
‚Ä¢ Pathology: excisional lymph node bx (not FNA, need surrounding architecture) with
immunophenotyping and cytogenetics; BM bx (except in HL clinical stage IA/IIA with
favorable features); LP if CNS involvement is clinically suspected
‚Ä¢ Lab tests: CBC, BUN/Cr, LFTs, ESR, LDH, UA, Ca, alb; ‚úìHBV & HCV (and must 
‚úìHBsAg & anti-HBc if planning rituximab Rx as can lead to HBV reactivation); 
consider HIV, HTLV, & EBV serologies and connective tissue diseases autoAbs
‚Ä¢ Imaging: chest/abd/pelvic CT (but don‚Äôt reliably detect spleen/liver involvement)
‚à¥also need PET scans
head CT/MRI if neurological symptoms; bone scan if bony pain or if A elevated

PLASMA CELL DYSCRASIAS
MULTIPLE MYELOMA (MM)
Definition and epidemiology
‚Ä¢ Malignant neoplasm of plasma cells producing a monoclonal Ig  ‚ÄúM protein‚Äù
‚Ä¢ 20,580 new cases and 10,580 deaths/y in U.S. (2009); median age at diagnosis 66 y
‚Ä¢ African American:Caucasian ratio 2:1
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Anemia (normocytic) due to bone marrow involvement and autoimmune Ab
‚Ä¢ Bone pain and hypercalcemia due to c osteoclast activity S lytic lesions, pathologic fx
‚Ä¢ Recurrent infections due to relative hypogammaglobulinemia as clonal plasma cells
suppress nl immunoglobulin
‚Ä¢ Renal disease: multiple mechanisms include toxic effect of filtered light chains S renal
failure (cast nephropathy) or type II RTA; amyloidosis or light chain deposition disease S
nephrotic syndrome: hypercalcemia, urate nephropathy, type I cryoglobulinemia
‚Ä¢ Neurologic: cord compression; POEMS (polyneuropathy, organomegaly, endocrinopa-
thy, M protein, skin changes) syndrome
‚Ä¢ Hyperviscosity: usually when IgM 4 g/dL, IgG 5 g/dL, or IgA 7 g/dL
‚Ä¢ Coagulopathy: inhibition of or Ab against clotting factor; Ab-coated platelets
‚Ä¢ Amyloidosis (see ‚ÄúAmyloidosis‚Äù)
Diagnostic and staging evaluation (NCCN Version 3.2010)
‚Ä¢ Sx MM criteria  M protein in serum (usually 3 g/dL) or urine, marrow plasmacyto-
sis (usually 90%) or presence of a plasmacytoma, and myeloma-related organ or tis-
sue impairment (ROTI)  lytic bone lesions, Ca 11.5 g/dL, Cr 2 mg/dL, or Hb 10
‚Ä¢ Variants
smoldering MM: M protein 3 g/dL and/or plasmacytosis 10%, but asx & no ROTI
risk of progression related to level of plasmacytosis and M protein (NEJM 2007;356:2582)
solitary bone plasmacytoma: 1 lytic lesion w/o M protein, plasmacytosis, or other ROTI
extramedullary plasmacytoma: usually upper respiratory tract
plasma cell leukemia: plasma cell count 2000/L
nonsecretory MM: no M protein, but marrow plasmacytosis & ROTI
‚Ä¢ Ddx of M component: MM, MGUS (see below), CLL, lymphoma, cirrhosis, sarcoidosis, RA
‚Ä¢ Peripheral smear S rouleaux (see Peripheral Smear insert); ‚úìCa, alb, Cr; T anion gap,
c globulin, c ESR
‚Ä¢ Protein electrophoresis and immunofixation
serum protein electrophoresis (SPEP): quantitates M component;  in 80% of Pts
urine protein electrophoresis (UPEP): detects the 20% of Pts who secrete only light
chains (  Bence Jones proteins), which are filtered rapidly from the blood
immunofixation: shows component is monoclonal and identifies Ig type S IgG (50%),
IgA (20%), IgD (2%), IgM (0.5%), light chain only (20%), nonsecretors (5%)
serum-free light chain assay: important test for dx and to follow treatment response
‚Ä¢ 2-microglobulin and LDH levels reflect tumor burden 
‚Ä¢ Bone marrow bx: better prognosis  hyperdiploidy; worse prognosis  del. of
chromosome 17p13 (10% of Pts) & certain translocations
‚Ä¢ Skeletal survey (plain radiographs) to identify lytic bone lesions and areas at risk for
pathologic fracture; bone scan is not useful for detecting lytic lesions

Treatment (NEJM 2004;351:1860; Lancet 2009;374:324; NEJM 2009;360:2645)
‚Ä¢ Treatment not indicated for smoldering MM or asx stage I disease
‚Ä¢ In general, Pts divided into two groups: transplant eligible vs. not eligible
‚Ä¢ If not transplant eligible: systemic chemotherapy (c median survival, but not curative)
regimens include melphalan  prednisone  either thalidomide/lenalidomide (Lancet
2007;370:1209) or proteasome inhibitor if high risk (bortezomib; NEJM 2008;359:906)     
lenalidomide  dexamethasone for relapsing/refractory MM (NEJM 2007;357:2123, 2133)
‚Ä¢ If transplant eligible: high-dose chemo  auto-HSCT. Not curative, but c survival
c/w conv. chemo (NEJM 2009;360:2645). Offer if 70 y w/ good performance status.  
Many chemo regimens used incl. thalidomide or lenalidomide  dex, bortezomib 
dex, vincristine/doxorubicin/dex, etc.
Double auto-HSCT c survival (NEJM 2003;349:2495),  thalidomide S c response
rate but no  in overall survival (NEJM 2006;354:1021)
‚Ä¢ Local radiation for solitary or extramedullary plasmacytoma
‚Ä¢ Adjunctive Rx
bone: bisphosphonates (NEJM 1996;334:488; & JCO 2007;25:2464); XRT for sx bony lesions
renal: avoid NSAIDs & IV contrast; consider plasmapheresis for acute renal failure
hyperviscosity syndrome: plasmapheresis
infections: consider IVIg for recurrent infections
‚Ä¢ Common toxicities of Rx: melphalan  prednisone S infxn, myelosuppression;
thalidomide S thromboembolism, bradycardia, periph. neuropathy, neutropenia, rash
(Blood 2008;111:3968); bortezomib S periph. neuropathy
MONOCLONAL GAMMOPATHY OF UNCERTAIN SIGNIFICANCE (MGUS)
Definition and epidemiology (NEJM 2006;355:2765)
‚Ä¢ M protein 3 g/dL, no urinary Bence Jones proteins, marrow plasmacytosis 10%, no ROTI
‚Ä¢ Prevalence 3% in population 50 y of age, 5% in population 70 y of age, and
7.5% in population 85 y of age (NEJM 2006;354:1362)
Management
‚Ä¢ ‚úìCBC, Ca, Cr, SPEP and UPEP with immunofixation to exclude MM
‚Ä¢ Close observation: repeat SPEP in 6 mos and then yearly thereafter if stable
Prognosis (NEJM 2002:346:564)
‚Ä¢ 1%/y or 25% lifetime risk S MM, WM, amyloidosis, or malign. lymphoproliferative dis.
‚Ä¢ Abnormal serum-free light chain ratio: c risk of progression to MM (Blood 2005;105:812)
WALDENSTR√ñM‚ÄôS MACROGLOBULINEMIA (WM)

‚Ä¢ Circulating monoclonal IgM

neurologic: blurred vision (‚Äúsausage‚Äù retinal veins on funduscopy), HA, dizziness,  MS

type I cryoglobulinemia S Raynaud‚Äôs phenomenon
platelet dysfxn S mucosal bleeding

‚Ä¢ Relative serum viscosity: defined as ratio of viscosity of serum to H2O (nl ratio

HEMATOPOIETIC STEM CELL TRANSPLANTATION (HSCT)
Transplantation of donor pluripotent cells that can reconstitute all recipient blood lineages
Categories of Stem Cell Transplantation
Feature
Allogeneic (Allo)
Autologous (Auto)
Donor-recipient relationship
Immunologically distinct
Donor is also recipient
Graft-versus-host disease
Yes
No
Graft-versus-tumor effect
Yes
No
Risk of graft contam. w/ tumor
No
Yes
Relapse risk (leukemia)
Lower
Higher
Transplant-related mortality
Higher
Lower
‚Ä¢ Types of Allo HSCT: based on donor/recipient matching of major HLA antigens on
Chr. 6 (3 principal genes for serotyping: HLA-A, -B, & -DR; each w/ 2 alleles ‚à¥6 major Ag)
Matched related (sibling matched at 6/6 major Ag): lowest risk of GVHD; preferred donor
Mismatched related (eg, 1/6 Ag mismatch) or haploidentical (mismatch at 3/6 Ag):
easiest to find, but c risk of GVHD; ‚à¥need to deplete T cells first
Matched unrelated: c risk of GVHD; ‚à¥adv. molecular matching of 8 HLA alleles to T risk
Umbilical cord blood: HSC processed at birth & stored; T risk of GVHD; tolerate mismatch
‚Ä¢ Graft-versus-host disease (GVHD): undesirable side effect of allo HSCT
allogeneic T cells view host cells as foreign; c incid. w/ mismatch or unrelated donors
‚Ä¢ Graft-versus-tumor (GVT) effect: desirable consequence of allo HSCT;
allogeneic T cells attack host tumor cells
Indications (NEJM 2006;354:1813)
‚Ä¢ Malignant disease:
Auto HSCT allows higher doses of chemo by rescuing hematopoietic system
(used for lymphoma, multiple myeloma, testicular cancer)
Allo HSCT produces graft-versus-tumor (GVT) effect, in addition to
hematopoietic rescue (used for AML, ALL, CML, CLL, MDS, lymphoma)
‚Ä¢ Nonmalignant disease: allo HSCT replaces abnl lymphohematopoietic system w/ one
from nl donor (eg, immunodef., aplastic anemia, hemoglobinopathies, ? autoimmune dis.)
Transplantation procedure
‚Ä¢ Preparative regimen: chemotherapy and/or immunosuppression prior to transplantation
myeloablative (traditional): chemotherapy and/or total body irradiation. Goal is
eradication of underlying disease for which transplant is being performed.
nonmyeloablative (‚Äúmini‚Äù): reduced-intensity conditioning regimens S T toxicity to
allow Pts w/ comorbidities or c age to tolerate HSCT. Goal to proceed w/
transplant when in disease remission, harnessing GVT effect while tolerating GVHD.
‚Ä¢ Sources of stem cells:
bone marrow (BM): original source of HSCT but now less common than PBSC
peripheral blood stem cells (PBSC): easier collection, most commonly used source
umbilical cord blood (UCB): less stringent HLA-matching requirements, though fewer
available cells from single donor (‚à¥2 donors typically combined), slower engraftment
‚Ä¢ Engraftment: absolute neutrophil count (ANC) recovers to 500/L w/in 2 wk w/ PBSC,
3 wk w/ BM, 4 wk w/ UCB. G-CSF accelerates recovery by 3‚Äì5 d in all scenarios.
Engraftment syndrome: fever, rash, noncardiogenic pulm edema, abnl LFTs, AKI, wt gain.
Dx of exclusion, r/o infection, GVHD; Rx w/ IV steroids.
Complications
‚Ä¢ Either direct chemoradiotoxicites associated with preparative regimen or consequences
of interaction between donor and recipient immune systems
Timing and Mechanism of Noninfectious Complications of HSCT

‚Ä¢ Sinusoidal obstruction syndrome (SOS; incidence 10%, mortality 30%)
Previously known as veno-occlusive disease (VOD)
Mechanism: direct cytotoxic injury to hepatic venules S in situ thrombosis
Symptoms: tender hepatomegaly, ascites, jaundice, fluid retention
with severe disease S liver failure, encephalopathy, hepatorenal syndrome
Diagnosis: c ALT/AST, c bilirubin; c PT with severe disease; Doppler U/S may show
reversal of portal vein flow; c hepatic wedge pressure; abnl liver bx
Treatment: supportive; prophylaxis with ursodiol; defibrotide
‚Ä¢ Idiopathic interstitial pneumonitis (IIP, up to 70% mortality; Curr Opin Oncol 2008;20:227)
Mech: alveolar injury due to direct toxicity S fever, hypoxia, diffuse pulmonary infiltrates
Diffuse alveolar hemorrhage (DAH): subset of IIP
Diagnosis: bronchoscopy to exclude infection; c bloody lavage fluid seen with DAH
Treatment: high-dose corticosteroids (limited data)
‚Ä¢ Acute GVHD (within 3 mos of transplant; Lancet 2009;373:1550)
Clinical grade I-IV based on scores for skin (severity of maculopapular rash), liver
(bilirubin level), and GI (volume of diarrhea); bx supports diagnosis
Prevention: immunosuppression (MTX  CsA or tacrolimus) or T-cell depletion of graft
Treatment: grade I S none; grade II‚ÄìIV  S associated with T survival and ‚à¥treated with
immunosuppressants (corticosteroids, CsA, tacrolimus, rapamycin, rituximab, MMF)
‚Ä¢ Chronic GVHD (developing or persisting beyond 3 mos posttransplant)
Clinical: malar rash, sicca syndrome, arthritis, obliterative bronchiolitis, bile duct
degeneration and cholestasis. More common w/ PBSC than BM.
Treatment: immunosuppressants as above; photopheresis
‚Ä¢ Graft failure
Primary  persistent neutropenia without evidence of engraftment
Secondary  delayed pancytopenia after initial engraftment; either immune mediated due
to attack by immunocompetent host cells in the allogeneic setting (termed graft
rejection) or non‚Äìimmune mediated (eg, CMV infection)
‚Ä¢ Infectious complications
due to regimen-induced pancytopenia and immunosuppression
auto HSCT recipients do not require immunosuppression and ‚à¥remain at c risk only
during the pre-engraftment and immediate postengraftment phases
both primary infections and reactivation events occur (eg, CMV, HSV, VZV)

LUNG
5-28
LUNG CANCER
Pathology and genetics (NEJM 2008;359:1367)
‚Ä¢ Non‚Äìsmall cell lung cancer (NSCLC, 85%)
Adenocarcinoma: typically peripheral; tumor cells can have mutations in KRAS,
EGFR, p53, & LKB1, and EML4-ALK fusion protein
Squamous cell: typically central; tumor cells can have mutations in p53, MET, &
LKB1, and/or amplifications in EGFR, MET, & PIK3CA
Large cell: typically peripheral
Bronchioalveolar carcinoma: track along airways, can be multifocal
‚Ä¢ Small cell lung cancer (SCLC, 15%): typically central; mutations in p53 & MET
Epidemiology and risk factors
‚Ä¢ Most common cause of cancer-related death for both men and women in U.S.
‚Ä¢ Cigarette smoking: 85% of lung cancers occur in smokers; risk  total pack-yrs, 
T risk after quitting/reducing, but not to baseline (JAMA 2005;294:1505)
squamous & small cell almost exclusively in smokers
adenocarcinoma most common type in nonsmokers
bronchioalveolar carcinoma associated with females, nonsmokers, EGFR mutations
‚Ä¢ Asbestos: when combined with smoking, synergistic c in risk of lung cancer
‚Ä¢ Radon: risk to general population unclear
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ 10% are asx at presentation and are detected incidentally by imaging
‚Ä¢ Endobronchial growth of 1 tumor: cough, hemoptysis, dyspnea, wheezing, post‚Äì
obstructive pneumonia; more common with squamous or small cell (central location)
‚Ä¢ Regional spread
pleural effusion, pericardial effusion, hoarseness (recurrent laryngeal nerve palsy),
dysphagia (esophageal compression), stridor (tracheal obstruction)
Pancoast‚Äôs syndrome: apical tumor S brachial plexus involvement (C8, T1, T2) S
Horner‚Äôs syndrome, shoulder pain, rib destruction, atrophy of hand muscles
SVC syndrome (NEJM 2007;356:1862): central tumor S SVC compression S face or
arm swelling (80%), venous distention of neck & chest wall (60%),
dyspnea/cough (50%), HA (10%); Rx  steroids & diuretics, XRT 	 chemo
after tissue dx, SVC stent for severe sx, fibrinolytic  anticoag if thrombus  
‚Ä¢ Extrathoracic metastases: brain, bone, liver, adrenal, skin
‚Ä¢ Paraneoplastic syndromes
Endocrine
ACTH (SCLC) S Cushing‚Äôs syndrome; ADH (SCLC) S SIADH
PTH-rP (squamous cell) S hypercalcemia
Skeletal: digital clubbing (non‚Äìsmall cell), hypertrophic pulmonary
osteoarthropathy (adenocarcinoma)  symmetric polyarthritis and proliferative
periostitis of long bones
Neurologic (usually small cell): Eaton-Lambert, periph. neuropathy, cerebellar degen.
Cutaneous: acanthosis nigricans, dermatomyositis
Hematologic: hypercoagulable state (adenocarcinoma), DIC, marantic endocarditis
Screening (NEJM 2005;352:2714)
‚Ä¢ No proven survival benefit to screening CXR or sputum cytology, even in high-risk Pts
‚Ä¢ Survival benefit of screening chest CT in observational studies controversial (NEJM
2006;355:1763; JAMA 2007;297:953); await RCTs
Diagnostic and staging evaluation (AJCC Cancer Staging Manual, 7th ed, 2010)
‚Ä¢ Imaging: CXR, chest CT (include liver and adrenal glands)
‚Ä¢ Tissue
bronchoscopy (for central lesions) or CT-guided needle bx (for peripheral
lesions or accessible sites of suspected metastasis)
mediastinoscopy (lymph node bx), VATS (eval. of pleura peripheral lesions),
thoracentesis (cell block for cytology), or sputum cytology (for central lesions)
‚Ä¢ Staging
Intrathoracic: mediastinoscopy or VATS; thoracentesis if pleural effusion
Extrathoracic:
PET scan or integrated PET-CT more Se than CT alone for detecting mediastinal
and distant mets as well as bone mets (NEJM 2000;343:254; 2003;348:2500; 2009;361:32)
brain MRI for all Pts and bone scan for those w/ localizing sx or lab abnormalities
BM bx for SCLC if peripheral smear abnl
‚Ä¢ PFTs with quantitative V/Q if planned treatment includes surgical resection; need to
have 30% of normal, predicted lung fxn after resection

5-30
BREAST CANCER
Epidemiology and genetics (Risk assessment tool: www.cancer.gov/bcrisktool/)
‚Ä¢ Most common cancer in U.S. women; 2nd leading cause of cancer death in women
‚Ä¢ Age: incidence rates c with age, with decrease in slope at age of menopause
‚Ä¢ Genetics (NEJM 2007;357:154 & 2008;359:2143): 15‚Äì20% have  FHx. Risk depends on # of
affected 1 relatives and their age at dx. 45% of familial cases a/w known germline mut.
BRCA1/2: 35‚Äì85% lifetime risk of breast cancer & c risk of ovarian cancer; ? c colon &
prostate cancer; prog not worse than in non-carriers w/ breast cancer (NEJM
2007;357:115); BRCA2: a/w c male breast cancer & pancreatic cancer; rare muta-
tions in CHEK2 or TP53 a/w c risk in familial breast cancer (JAMA 2006;295:1379)
‚Ä¢ Estrogen: c risk with early menarche, late menopause, late parity, or nulliparity (NEJM
2006;354:270); c risk with prolonged HRT (RR  1.24 after 5.6 y, JAMA 2003;289:3243);
no c risk shown with OCP use (NEJM 2002;346:2025)
‚Ä¢ Benign breast conditions: c risk w/ atypia (atypical ductal or lobular hyperplasia) &
proliferative (ductal hyperplasia, papilloma, radial scar, or sclerosing adenosis) features;
no c risk w/ cysts, fibroadenoma, or columnar changes (NEJM 2005;352:229)
‚Ä¢ c risk with h/o ionizing radiation to chest for treatment of Hodgkin‚Äôs lymphoma 
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Breast mass (hard, irregular, fixed, nontender), nipple discharge (higher risk if unilateral,
limited to one duct, bloody, associated with mass)
‚Ä¢ Special types: Paget‚Äôs disease S unilateral nipple eczema  nipple discharge;
inflammatory breast cancer S skin erythema and edema (peau d‚Äôorange)
‚Ä¢ Metastases: lymph nodes, bone, liver, lung, brain
Screening
‚Ä¢ Self breast exam (SBE): no proven mortality benefit (JNCI 2002;94:1445); not recommended 
‚Ä¢ Clinical breast exam (CBE): benefit independent of mammography not established
‚Ä¢ Mammography: 20‚Äì30% T in breast cancer mortality (smaller absolute benefit
in women 50 y) (Lancet 2001;358:1340 & 2002;359:909; Annals 2002;137:347; Lancet 2006;368:2053);
75% of all abnl finding benign; suspicious: clustered microcalc., spiculated, enlarging;
adding U/S c Se, but T PPV (JAMA 2008;299:3151)
‚Ä¢ ACS/NCI recommend annual mammo  CBE beginning at age 40; USPSTF recommends
begin at 50 and biennially (Annals 2009;151:716), controversial (NEJM 2009;361:2499)
‚Ä¢ c risk: screen earlier w/ CBE and mammo (age 25 in BRCA1/2 carrier, 5‚Äì10 y before
earliest FHx case, 8‚Äì10 y after thoracic XRT, upon dx of c risk benign disease)
‚Ä¢ MRI: superior to mammo in high-risk Pts; consider annually if 20% lifetime risk (eg, 
FHx, BRCA 1 or 2, prior chest XRT) (NEJM 2004;351:427; Lancet 2005;365:1769 & 2007;370:485)
‚Ä¢ Genetic testing should be considered in women with strong FHx
Diagnostic evaluation
‚Ä¢ Palpable breast mass:
Age 30 y S observe for resolution over 1‚Äì2 menstrual cycles
Age 30 y, unchanging mass S U/S S aspiration if mass not simple cyst
Age 30 y or solid mass on U/S or bloody aspirate or recurrence after aspiration S
mammo (detect other lesions) and either fine-needle aspir. or core-needle bx
Clearly cancerous on exam or indeter. read/atypia on needle bx S excisional bx
‚Ä¢ Suspicious mammogram with normal exam: stereotactically-guided bx
‚Ä¢ MRI: detects contralateral cancer in 3% of women w/ recently dx breast cancer &
negative contralateral mammogram (but PPV only 21%) (NEJM 2007;356:1295);
whether to use routinely remains unclear
Staging
‚Ä¢ Anatomic: tumor size, chest wall invasion, axillary LN mets (strongest prognostic factor)
‚Ä¢ Histopathologic: type (little prognostic relevance) & grade; lymphatic/vascular invasion
In situ carcinoma: no invasion of surrounding stroma
Ductal (DCIS): c risk of invasive cancer in ipsilateral breast (30%/10 y)
Lobular (LCIS): marker of c risk of invasive cancer in either breast (1%/y)
Invasive carcinoma: infiltrating ductal (70‚Äì80%); invasive lobular (5‚Äì10%); tubular, 
medullary, and mucinous (10%, better prognosis); papillary (1‚Äì2%); other (1‚Äì2%)
Inflammatory breast cancer (see above): not a histologic type but a clinical reflection
of tumor invasion of dermal lymphatics; very poor prognosis
Paget disease: ductal cancer invading nipple epidermis 	 associated mass
‚Ä¢ Biomarkers: determine estrogen, progesterone receptor (ER/PR) and HER2/neu
status for all invasive breast cancers
‚Ä¢ Recurrence score and risk with Oncotype DX 21-gene assay in ER , node  Pts
(NEJM 2004;351:2817; 2006:355:560)

PROSTATE
5-32
PROSTATE CANCER
Epidemiology and risk factors (NEJM 2003;349:366)
‚Ä¢ Most common cancer in U.S. men; 2nd most common cause of cancer death in men
‚Ä¢ Lifetime risk of prostate cancer dx 16%; lifetime risk of dying of prostate cancer 3%
‚Ä¢ More common with c age (rare if 45 y), in African Americans, and if  FHx
Clinical manifestations (usually asymptomatic at presentation)
‚Ä¢ Obstructive sx (more common with BPH): hesitancy, T stream, retention, nocturia
‚Ä¢ Irritative sx (also seen with prostatitis): frequency, dysuria, urgency
‚Ä¢ Periprostatic spread: hematuria, hematospermia, new-onset erectile dysfunction
‚Ä¢ Metastatic disease: bone pain, spinal cord compression, cytopenias
Screening (NEJM 2009;360:1351)
‚Ä¢ Mortality benefit from screening has not been established, with one recent trial
showing a 20% T mortality, but another no benefit (NEJM 2009;360:1310 & 1320)
‚Ä¢ Digital rectal exam (DRE): size, consistency, lesions
‚Ä¢ PSA: 4 ng/mL cutpoint neither Se nor Sp; can c with BPH, prostatitis, acute retention, after
bx or TURP, and ejaculation (no significant c after DRE, cystoscopy);
15% of men 62 y w/ PSA 4 & nl DRE have bx-proven T1 cancer (NEJM 2004;350:2239)
‚Ä¢ Per American Cancer Soc., offer PSA  DRE screening to men age 50 (45 if high risk)
with life expectancy 10 y; USPSTF has no rec. for PSA if 75 y, rec. against if 75 y
Diagnostic and staging evaluation
‚Ä¢ Transrectal ultrasound (TRUS) guided biopsy, with 6‚Äì12 core specimens
‚Ä¢ Histology: Gleason grade (2‚Äì10; low grade 6)  sum of the differentiation score (1 
best, 5  worst) of the two most prevalent patterns in the bx; correlates with prognosis
‚Ä¢ Imaging: to evaluate extraprostatic spread
bone scan: for PSA 10 ng/mL, high Gleason grade, or clinically advanced tumor
abdomen-pelvis CT: inaccurate for detecting extracapsular spread and lymph node mets
endorectal coil MRI: improves assessment of extracapsular spread

COLORECTAL
5-33
COLORECTAL CANCER (CRC)
Epidemiology and risk factors
‚Ä¢ 4th most common cancer in U.S men and women; 2nd leading cause of cancer death
overall
‚Ä¢ Rare before age 40, w/ 90% of cases occurring after age 50. 70% are sporadic.
‚Ä¢ Family History: up to 25% of Pts have  FHx. Risk depends on # of 1st degree rela-
tives (w/ CRC or polyp) and their age at dx; 5% have an identifiable germline mutation
Familial adenomatous polyposis (FAP): mutation of APC tumor suppressor S 1000s
of polyps at young age S 100% lifetime risk; c risk of thyroid, stomach, SI cancers
Hereditary nonpolyposis colorectal cancer (HNPCC): mutations in DNA mis-
match repair genes S c tumor progression S 80% lifetime risk; predom. right-sided
tumors; c risk of endometrial, ovarian, stomach, small bowel cancers.
Amsterdam criteria: 3 family members w/ HNPCC-related cancer, one of
which dx before age 50, affecting 2 successive generations.
‚Ä¢ Inflammatory bowel disease: c risk with c extent and duration of disease
‚Ä¢ Other factors a/w c risk of CRC: diet rich in animal fat, ? smoking, ? diabetes/obesity
‚Ä¢ T risk of adenomas w/ ASA & NSAIDs, incl. COX-2 (NEJM 2006;355:873, 885), but c bleeding
and c CV events w/ COX-2; T COX-2-expressing CRC after prolonged ASA (NEJM
2007;356:2131 & Lancet 2007;369:1603); currently not recommended (Annals 2007;146:361)
Pathology and Genetics (Hematol Oncol Clin North Am 2002;16:127)
‚Ä¢ Adenoma S carcinoma sequence reflects accumulation of multiple genetic mutations
c risk of malignancy with large (2.5 cm), villous, sessile adenomatous polyps
Adenomas typically observed 10 y prior to onset of cancer (both sporadic and familial)
‚Ä¢ Genetic profile in sporadic CRC: APC (80%), KRAS (50%), TP53 (50‚Äì70%), DCC or
SMAD4, chromosomal instability (majority) or mismatch repair deficiency (10‚Äì15%)
‚Ä¢ Upfront genotyping at dx may guide Rx (eg, KRAS, see below)
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Distal colon:  bowel habits, obstruction, colicky abdominal pain, hematochezia
‚Ä¢ Proximal colon: iron defic. anemia, dull vague abd pain; obstruction atypical
due to larger lumen, liquid stool, and polypoid tumors (vs. annular distal tumors)
‚Ä¢ Metastases: nodes, liver, lung, peritoneum S RUQ tenderness, ascites, supraclavicular LN
‚Ä¢ Associated with Streptococcus bovis bacteremia and Clostridium septicum sepsis
Screening (NEJM 2009;361:1179)
‚Ä¢ Average risk: colonoscopy starting at age 50 & repeat q10y strongly preferred method
‚Ä¢ c risk: earlier and/or more frequent screening.  FHx: age 40 or 10 y before index
dx, then q5y. IBD: 8‚Äì10 y after dx, then q1‚Äì2y. Known or suspected familial syn-
drome: genetic counseling & very early screening (eg, age 20-25), then q1‚Äì2y.
‚Ä¢ Colonoscopy: test of choice as examines entire colon; 90% Se for lesions 1 cm. Flex
sig less Se but better than no endoscopy (NEJM 1992;326:653). If polyp found, re ‚úìin 3‚Äì5 y.
‚Ä¢ Fecal occult blood test (FOBT): T mortality (NEJM 1993;328:1365 & 2000;343:1603); 3 card
home testing more Se (24% vs. 5%) than DRE/FOBT (Annals 2005;142:81). Repeat q1y.
‚Ä¢ Fecal DNA: c Se,  Sp c/w FOBT, but less Se than colonoscopy (NEJM 2004;351:2704)
‚Ä¢ CT colonography (CTC): c/w colonoscopy, 90% Se for lesions 1 cm but consid-
erably less for smaller lesions (NEJM 2008;359:1207). Strategy of CTC followed by
colonoscopy for lesions 6 mm diagnoses similar # of advanced neoplasms as
colonoscopy alone, but with only 8% needing colonoscopy (NEJM 2007;357:1403).
However, in high-risk Pts, Se only 85% for advanced neoplasia 6 mm (JAMA
2009;301;2453). ‚à¥? consider CTC if avg risk (w/o personal or FH of polyps/CRC)
(Gastro 2008;134:1578) or if cannot obtain colonoscopy.
Staging (AJCC Cancer Staging Manual, 7th ed, 2010)
‚Ä¢ TNM staging: Size/depth of primary (T), locoregional nodes (N), distant metastases (M).
Staging is complex and based on pathologic correlation with observed survival data.
‚Ä¢ Colonoscopy  biopsy/polypectomy  intraoperative and pathologic staging
essential for evaluating extracolonic spread
‚Ä¢ CT scans of chest and abdomen/pelvis (inaccurate for depth of invasion & malignant LN) 
‚Ä¢ Baseline CEA in Pt with known CRC has prognostic significance and is useful to fol-
low response to therapy and detect recurrence; not a screening tool

5-35
PANCREATIC TUMORS
Pathology and genetics (Annu Rev Pathol 2008;3:157; Genes Dev 2006;20:1218)
‚Ä¢ Histologic types:  adenocarcinoma (85%), acinar cell carcinoma, endocrine tumors, cys-
tic neoplasms (eg, IPMN, see below), rare mets to pancreas (eg, lung, breast, renal cell)
‚Ä¢ Pancreatic adenocarcinoma accounts for majority of pancreatic cancer (85%)
‚Ä¢ Location: 60% in head, 15% in body, 5% in tail; in 20% diffuse infiltration of pancreas
‚Ä¢ Mutations in adenocarc.: KRAS (90%), p16 (80‚Äì95%), p53 (50‚Äì75%), SMAD4 (55%)
Epidemiology and risk factors (Lancet 2004;363:1049)
‚Ä¢ Pancreatic adenocarcinoma is 4th leading cause of cancer death in U.S. men and women
‚Ä¢ 80% of pancreatic adenocarcinomas occur in Pts 60‚Äì80 y
‚Ä¢ Acquired risk factors: smoking (RR 1.5), obesity, chronic pancreatitis, ? diabetes
‚Ä¢ Hereditary risk factors: genetic susceptibility may play a role in 5‚Äì10% of cases
Hereditary chronic pancreatitis: mutation in cationic trypsinogen gene (PRSS1)
Familial cancer syndromes and gene mutations with c risk: familial atypical multiple mole
melanoma (CDKN2A/p16), familial breast and ovarian cancer (BRCA2), Peutz-Jeghers
(LKB1), ataxia-telangiectasia (ATM), ? hereditary colorectal cancer (HNPCC and FAP) 
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Painless jaundice (w/ pancreatic head mass), pain (radiating to back), weight loss
‚Ä¢ New-onset atypical diabetes mellitus; unexplained malabsorption; unexplained pancreatitis
‚Ä¢ Migratory thrombophlebitis (Trousseau‚Äôs sign)
‚Ä¢ Exam: abdominal mass; nontender, palpable gallbladder (Courvoisier‚Äôs sign, but more often
seen with biliary tract cancers); hepatomegaly; ascites; left supraclavicular (Virchow‚Äôs)
node & palpable rectal shelf (both nonspecific signs of carcinomatosis)
‚Ä¢ Laboratory tests may show c bilirubin, c A, anemia
Diagnostic and staging evaluation
‚Ä¢ Pancreatic protocol CT scan (I w/ arterial & venous phase imaging)
‚Ä¢ If no lesion seen S EUS, ERCP, MRI/MRCP may reveal mass or malignant ductal strictures
‚Ä¢ Biopsy pancreatic lesion via EUS-guided FNA (preferred in potential surgical candidates)
or CT-guided (potential risk of seeding) or biopsy metastasis
‚Ä¢ Tumor markers: c CA 19-9 (nb, falsely c in liver failure); may be useful to follow dis. postop

ONC EMERG

ONCOLOGIC EMERGENCIES
FEVER AND NEUTROPENIA (FN)
Definition
‚Ä¢ Fever: single oral temp 38.3C (101F) or 38C (100.4F) for 1 h
‚Ä¢ Neutropenia: ANC 500 cells/L or 1,000 cells/L with predicted nadir 500 cells/L
Pathophysiology and microbiology
‚Ä¢ Predisposing factors: catheters, skin breakdown, GI mucositis, obstruction (lymphat-
ics, biliary tract, GI, urinary tract), immune defect a/w malignancy
‚Ä¢ Most episodes thought to result from seeding of bloodstream by GI flora
‚Ä¢ Neutropenic enterocolitis (typhlitis): RLQ pain, watery/bloody diarrhea, cecal wall thickening
‚Ä¢ GNRs (especially P. aeruginosa) were historically most common
‚Ä¢ Gram  infections have recently become more common (60‚Äì70% of identified organisms)
‚Ä¢ Fungal superinfection often results from prolonged neutropenia & antibiotic use
‚Ä¢ Infection with atypical organisms and bacterial meningitis is rare
Prevention
‚Ä¢ Levofloxacin (500 mg qd) T febrile episodes & bacterial infections in chemo-related
high-risk neutropenic patients; no difference in mortality (NEJM 2005;353:977, 988)
Diagnostic evaluation
‚Ä¢ Exam: skin, oropharynx, lung, perirectal area, surgical & catheter sites; avoid DRE
‚Ä¢ Labs: CBC with differential, electrolytes, BUN/Cr, LFTs, U/A
‚Ä¢ Micro: blood (peripheral & through each indwelling catheter port), urine, & sputum cx;
for localizing s/s S ‚úìstool (C. difficile, cx), peritoneal fluid, CSF (rare source)
‚Ä¢ Imaging: CXR; for localizing s/s S CNS, sinus, chest, or abdomen/pelvis imaging
‚Ä¢ Caveats: neutropenia S impaired inflammatory response S exam and radiographic
findings may be subtle; absence of neutrophils by Gram stain does not r/o infection
Risk stratification (factors that predict lower risk)
‚Ä¢ History: age 60 y, no symptoms, no major comorbidities, cancer in remission, solid
tumor, no h/o fungal infection or recent antifungal Rx
‚Ä¢ Exam: temp 39 C, no tachypnea, no hypotension, no  MS, no dehydration
‚Ä¢ Studies: ANC 100 cells/L, anticipated duration of neutropenia 10 d, normal CXR
Initial antibiotic therapy (Clin Infect Dis 2002;34:730)
‚Ä¢ Empiric regimens should include a drug with antipseudomonal activity
‚Ä¢ PO abx may be used in low-risk Pts: cipro  amoxicillin-clavulanate (NEJM 1999;341:305)
‚Ä¢ IV antibiotics: no clearly superior regimen; monotherapy or 2-drug regimens can be used
Monotherapy: ceftazidime, cefepime, imipenem, or meropenem
2-drug therapy: aminoglycoside  antipseudomonal -lactam
PCN-allergic: levofloxacin  aztreonam or aminoglycoside
‚Ä¢ Vancomycin added in select cases (hypotension, indwelling catheter, severe mucositis,
MRSA colonization, h/o quinolone prophylaxis), discontinue when cultures   48 h
Modification to initial antibiotic regimen
‚Ä¢ Low-risk Pts who become afebrile w/in 3‚Äì5 d can be switched to PO antibiotics
‚Ä¢ Empiric antibiotics changed for fever 3‚Äì5 d or progressive disease (eg, add vancomycin)
‚Ä¢ Antifungal therapy is added for neutropenic fever 5 d
liposomal amphotericin B, caspofungin, micafungin, anidulafungin, voriconazole,
posaconazole all options (NEJM 2002;346:225 & 2007;356:348)
Duration of therapy
‚Ä¢ Known source: complete standard course (eg, 14 d for bacteremia)
‚Ä¢ Unknown source: continue antibiotics until afebrile and ANC 500 cells/L
‚Ä¢ Less clear when to d/c abx when Pt is afebrile but prolonged neutropenia
Role of hematopoietic growth factors (JCO 2005;23:4198 & 2006;24:3187)
‚Ä¢ Granulocyte (G-CSF) and granulocyte-macrophage (GM-CSF) colony-stimulating fac-
tors can be used as 1 prophylaxis when expected FN incidence 20% or as 2 pro-
phylaxis after FN has occurred in a previous cycle (to maintain dose-intensity for
curable tumors). CSF T rate of FN but have not been shown to impact mortality.
‚Ä¢ Colony-stimulating factors can be considered as adjuvant therapy in high-risk FN Pts
SPINAL CORD COMPRESSION
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Metastases located in vertebral body extend and cause epidural spinal cord compression

--- Fim da P√°gina 162 ---

‚Ä¢ Prostate, breast, and lung cancer are the most common causes, followed by renal cell
carcinoma, NHL, and myeloma
‚Ä¢ Site of involvement: thoracic (70%), lumbar (20%), cervical (10%)
‚Ä¢ Signs and symptoms: pain (96%, precedes neuro s), weakness, autonomic
dysfunction (urinary retention, T anal sphincter tone), sensory loss
Diagnostic evaluation
‚Ä¢ Always take back pain in Pts with solid tumors very seriously
‚Ä¢ Do not wait for neurologic signs to develop before initiating evaluation b/c duration &
severity of neurologic dysfunction before Rx are best predictors of neurologic outcome
‚Ä¢ Urgent whole-spine MRI is study of choice. Consider CT myelogram if unable to get MRI.
Treatment
‚Ä¢ Dexamethasone (10 mg IV S 4 mg IV or PO q6h)
initiate immediately while awaiting imaging if back pain  neurologic deficits
‚Ä¢ Emergent XRT or surgical decompression if confirmed compression / neuro deficits
‚Ä¢ Surgery  XRT superior to XRT alone for neuro recovery in solid tumors (Lancet 2005;366:643)
‚Ä¢ If pathologic fracture causing compression S surgery; if not surgical candidate S XRT
TUMOR LYSIS SYNDROME
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Large tumor burden or a rapidly proliferating tumor S spontaneous or chemotherapy-
induced release of intracellular electrolytes and nucleic acids
‚Ä¢ Most common w/ Rx of high-grade lymphomas (Burkitt‚Äôs) and leukemias (ALL, AML,
CML in blast crisis); rare with solid tumors; rarely due to spontaneous necrosis
‚Ä¢ Electrolyte abnormalities: c K, c uric acid, c PO4 S T Ca
‚Ä¢ Renal failure (urate nephropathy)
Prophylaxis
‚Ä¢ Allopurinol 300 mg qd to bid PO or 200‚Äì400 mg/m2 IV (adjusted for renal fxn) &
aggressive hydration prior to beginning chemotherapy or XRT
‚Ä¢ Rasburicase (recombinant urate oxidase) 0.15 mg/kg or 6 mg fixed dose (except in obese
Pts) & aggressive hydration prior to beginning chemotherapy or XRT (see below)
Treatment
‚Ä¢ Avoid IV contrast and NSAIDs
‚Ä¢ Allopurinol  aggressive IV hydration 	 diuretics to c UOP
‚Ä¢ Consider alkalinization of urine w/ isotonic NaHCO3 to c UA solubility & T risk of urate
nephropathy (controversial: may cause metabolic alkalosis or Ca3(PO4)2 precipitation).
‚Ä¢ Rasburicase (0.15‚Äì0.2 mg/kg/d  3‚Äì7 d) for severe c UA, espec in aggressive malig;
UA level must be drawn on ice to quench ex vivo enzyme activity ( JCO 2003;21:4402;
Acta Haematol 2006;115:35)
‚Ä¢ Treat hyperkalemia, hyperphosphatemia, and symptomatic hypocalcemia
‚Ä¢ Hemodialysis may be necessary; early renal consultation for Pts w/ renal insuffic. or ARF

‚Ä¢ ‚ÄúTypical‚Äù: acute onset of fever, cough w/ purulent sputum, dyspnea, consolidation on CXR

‚Ä¢ Reasons for failure to improve on initial Rx:

Insufficient drug levels: eg, vanco trough 15‚Äì20 g/mL (needed for lung penetration)

‚Ä¢ Pneumonia and influenza are the 8th leading cause of death in the U.S.
‚Ä¢ For low-risk Pts, can discharge immediately after switching to PO abx (CID 2007;44:527)

VIRAL RESPIRATORY INFECTIONS
Microbiology and Epidemiology
‚Ä¢ Typical pathogens: short, mild  rhinovirus, coronavirus; longer, more severe or com-
plicated  influenza, parainfluenza, respiratory syncytial virus (RSV), adenovirus
‚Ä¢ Seasonal flu: 365,000 hosp., 51,000 deaths per y in U.S.; most 65 y (NEJM 2008;359:2579)
‚Ä¢ Pandemic 2009 H1N1: more severe disease in younger Pts (JAMA 2009;302:1896)
Diagnosis
‚Ä¢
Primarily clinical: cough, fever, myalgias, arthralgias, rhinorrhea, pharyngitis
(in contrast, viral bronchitis p/w cough  low-grade temp; usually benign & self-limited)
‚Ä¢
Respiratory viral panel (flu, paraflu, RSV, adeno) on nasal washing or sputum/BAL 
‚Ä¢
Rapid influenza test on nasal swab: Se 50‚Äì70% (? lower for pandemic flu), Sp 95%
‚Ä¢
DFA (Se 85%), RT-PCR (gold standard) avail. for influenza (PCR distinguishes type)
Treatment
‚Ä¢
Seasonal influenza: M2 inhib. (amantadine, rimantadine) effective only against some type A;
neuraminidase inhib. (oseltamivir, zanamivir) effective vs.A & B, but resistance emerging
‚Ä¢
Pandemic H1N1 influenza: nearly 100% sensitive to oseltamivir, consider IV peramivir for
critically ill Pts unable to take PO (currently emergency use in U.S.); resistant to amantidine
‚Ä¢
Oseltamivir dosed 75 mg PO bid  5 d, effective only if started w/in 48 h of sx, but used
anytime in immunosupp. & considered in all Pts w/ or predisposed to severe influenza
‚Ä¢
Consider inhaled ribavirin for RSV in immunosupp. (eg, BMT, lung tx); limited adult data
Prevention
‚Ä¢ Inactivated influenza vaccine: available for seasonal and select pandemic flu, incl. H1N1
rec if 50 y, at risk for complic., HCW, caretakers of high-risk Pts; for all if surplus
‚Ä¢ Isolation, droplet precautions for inPts strongly recommended
‚Ä¢ Prophylaxis for high-risk contacts of confirmed influenza: oseltamivir 75 mg PO daily  10 d

FUNGAL
6-3
FUNGAL INFECTIONS
Candida species
‚Ä¢ Microbiology: normal GI flora; C. albicans & nonalbicans spp. (consider azole resistance
if prior Rx or nonalbicans; C. parapsilosis more likely to be echinocandin resistant)
‚Ä¢ Risk factors: neutropenia, immunosupp., broad-spectrum abx, intravascular
catheters (esp. if TPN), IVDU, abd surgery, DM, renal failure
‚Ä¢ Clinical manifestations
Mucocutaneous: cutaneous (eg, red, macerated lesions in intertriginous zones); oral
thrush (exudative, erythematous, or atrophic; if unexplained, r/o HIV); esophageal
(odynophagia;  oral thrush); vulvovaginal, balanitis
Candiduria: typically colonization due to broad-spectrum abx and/or indwelling catheter
Candidemia (#4 cause of nosocomial bloodstream infxn): r/o retinal involvement
(req c Rx); endocarditis rare but serious (esp. w/ nonalbicans & prosthetic valve)
Hepatosplenic: intestinal seeding of portal & venous circulation; seen in acute leukemics
Hematogenous dissemination S lung, brain, meninges, etc
Empiric Treatment
Mucocutaneous
Clotrimazole, nystatin, fluconazole, itraconazole
Candiduria
Fluconazole or intravesical ampho B if sx, severely 
immunosupp., or will undergo GU procedure
Candidemia w/o neutropenia
Echinocandin or fluconazole or ampho B
Febrile neutropenia
Echinocandin (eg, micafungin) or Ampho B 
Remove intravascular catheters (CID 2009;48:503)
Histoplasmosis
‚Ä¢ Epidemiology: hyperendemic in central & SE U.S. (esp. in areas w/ bird & bat 
droppings), present in river banks elsewhere including northeast
‚Ä¢ Clinical manifestations
Acute pulmonary: often subclinical, but may see mild to severe  cavitary 
Chronic pulmonary: c productive cough, wt loss, night sweats, infiltrates, cavitation
Disseminated (immunosupp.): fever, wt loss, HSM, LAN, oral ulcers, skin lesion
‚Ä¢ Treatment: itraconazole; amphotericin  steroids if severe or disseminated (CID 2007;45;807)
Coccidioidomycosis
‚Ä¢ Epidemiology: SW U.S. (San Joaquin or ‚ÄúValley‚Äù fever)
‚Ä¢ Clinical manifestations
Acute pulmonary: often subclinical; chest pain, cough, fever, arthralgias
Chronic pulmonary: cough, hemoptysis, fever, night sweats, wt loss
Chronic disseminated (in immunosupp., pregnant, & DM): fever, malaise, diffuse 
pulmonary process, bone, skin, & meningeal involvement 
‚Ä¢ Treatment for disseminated or high-risk 1
 pulmonary: fluconazole or itraconazole, or
amphotericin if severe (CID 2005;41:1217)
Blastomycosis
‚Ä¢ Epidemiology: south central, SE, and midwest U.S.
‚Ä¢ Clinical manifestations: often asx, acute PNA, chronic pneumonia;
extrapulmonary: verrucous & ulcerated skin lesions, bone & GU involvement, CNS
‚Ä¢ Treatment: itraconazole; ampho B if severe or immunosupp. (CID 2008;46:1801)
Aspergillus (Chest 2002;121:1988; CID 2008;46:327; NEJM 2009;360:1870)
‚Ä¢ ABPA; hypersensitivity pneumonitis: see ‚ÄúInterstitial Lung Disease‚Äù
‚Ä¢ Aspergilloma: usually in pre-existing cavity (from TB, etc.); most asx, but can lead
to hemoptysis; sputum cx  in 50%; CT S mobile intracavitary mass with air crescent
Rx: antifungals w/o benefit; embolization or surgery for persistent hemoptysis
‚Ä¢ Necrotizing tracheitis: white necrotic pseudomembranes in Pts w/ AIDS or lung Tx
‚Ä¢ Chronic necrotizing: seen in COPD, mild immunosupp; subacute sputum, fever, wt loss;
CT: infiltrate  nodule  thick pleura; lung bx S invasion; Rx  voriconazole  ampho B
‚Ä¢ Invasive/disseminated: seen if immunosupp. (neutropenia, s/p transplant, steroid Rx,
AIDS esp. w/ steroids or neutropenia); s/s PNA w/ chest pain & hemoptysis; CT: nodules,
halo sign, air crescent sign; lung bx if dx inconclusive; Rx  voriconazole  ampho B
Zygomycetes (eg, Mucor, Rhizopus)
‚Ä¢ Epidemiology: diabetes mellitus (70%), heme malignancy, s/p transplant, chronic
steroids, deferoxamine or iron overload
‚Ä¢ Clinical manifestations: rhinocerebral  periorbital/forehead pain (more extensive than
orbital cellulitis),  fever (may appear nontoxic at first), exophthalmos, decreased EOM,
may involve CNs (V VII); nasal turbinates  black eschar; Dx: careful ENT exam  bx
‚Ä¢ Treatment: Serial debridements, ampho.Very high mortality even if treated.

Cryptococcus (CID 2010;50:291)
‚Ä¢ Epidemiology: immunosupp. (esp.AIDS) most susceptible, but may occur in healthy host
‚Ä¢ Clinical manifestations 
CNS (meningitis)  HA, fever, meningismus, high ICP,  stupor. Dx: LP w/ CSF CrAg,
India ink stain, fungal cx (cell counts vary); serum CrAg 1:8 highly Se/Sp in AIDS.
Other forms: pulmonary, urinary, cutaneous, CNS cryptococcoma.With any evi-
dence of cryptococcal disease, exonerate CNS infxn w/ LP.
‚Ä¢ Treatment:
CNS disease (or non-CNS disease in immunosupp. Pts):
HIV : Induction: ampho (Ampho B 0.7‚Äì1.0 mg/kg/d, liposomal ampho B 3‚Äì4 mg/kg/d
or ABLC 5 mg/kg/d) and if bone marrow allows, flucytosine (100 mg/kg/d in 
4 divided doses)  2 wk; Consolidation: fluconazole 800 mg/d  8 wk;
Maintenance: 12 mo antifungal Rx and until immune recovery on ARVs
Transplant: Induction: liposomal ampho B and flucytosine  2 wks; Consolidation:
fluconazole 400 mg/d  8 wk; Maintenance: fluconazole  6‚Äì12 mo if able to 
reduce immunosuppressive Rx, o/w longer
Treat high ICP w/ repeat large-volume LPs or temp. lumbar drain; few require VP shunt
Non-CNS disease in healthy Pts: fluconazole vs. observation, based on clinical setting
Fungal diagnostics
‚Ä¢ Culture: Candida grows well in blood/urine cx, others (eg, Crypto, Histo) grow better
in fungal isolator BCx. Cx insensitive for Coccidioides.
‚Ä¢ Antibody detection: Histo, Blasto, Coccidio, Aspergillus. Se variable.
‚Ä¢ Antigen detection
Histo urine/serum Ag: Se of urine Ag 90% (serum Ag 80%) for disseminated
disease; Sp limited by cross-reactivity with other fungal infxns
1,3--D-glucan: Se for many fungal infxns (Candida, Aspergillus, Histo, Coccidio,
Fusarium, Pneumocystis, Sporothrix; but not Crypto, Blasto, Mucor, Rhizopus); not Sp
Galactomannan (GM): more specific for Aspergillus, but Se 50%
Crypto Ag (serum, CSF): serum Ag 90% Se & Sp in invasive infxn, lower for pulm only
‚Ä¢ Histopathologic exam (nb, no grinding of tissue if Zygomycetes suspected)

UTI
6-5
URINARY TRACT INFECTIONS (UTI)
Definitions
‚Ä¢ Anatomic
lower: urethritis, cystitis (superficial infection of bladder)
upper: pyelonephritis (inflammatory process of the renal parenchyma), renal or
perinephric abscess, prostatitis
‚Ä¢ Clinical
uncomplicated: cystitis in immunocompetent nonpregnant women w/o underly-
ing structural or neurologic disease
complicated: upper tract infection in women or any UTI in men or pregnant women
or UTI with underlying structural disease or immunosuppression
Microbiology
‚Ä¢ Uncomplicated UTI: E. coli (80%), Proteus, Klebsiella, S. saprophyticus (CID 2004;39:75)
‚Ä¢ Complicated UTI: E. coli (30%), enterococci (20%), Pseudomonas (20%), S. epidermidis
(15%), other GNR
‚Ä¢ Catheter-associated UTI: yeast (30%), E. coli (25%), other GNR, enterococci, S. epi
‚Ä¢ Urethritis: Chlamydia trachomatis, Neisseria gonorrhoeae, Ureaplasma urealyticum,
Trichomonas vaginalis, Mycoplasma genitalium, HSV
‚Ä¢ S. aureus: uncommon primary urinary pathogen in absence of catheter or recent
instrumentation; ‚à¥consider bacteremia w/ hematogenous seeding
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Cystitis: dysuria, urgency, frequency, hematuria, 	 in urine color/odor, suprapu-
bic pain; fever generally absent
‚Ä¢ Urethritis: may be identical to cystitis except urethral discharge may be present
‚Ä¢ Prostatitis
chronic: similar to cystitis except symptoms of obstruction (hesitancy, weak stream)
acute: perineal pain, fever, tenderness on prostate exam
‚Ä¢ Pyelonephritis: fever, shaking chills, flank or back pain, nausea, vomiting, diarrhea
‚Ä¢ Renal abscess (intrarenal or perinephric): identical to pyelonephritis except persistent
fever despite appropriate antibiotics
Diagnostic studies
‚Ä¢ Urinalysis: pyuria  bacteriuria  hematuria  nitrites
‚Ä¢ Urine Cx (from clean-catch midstream or straight-cath specimen)
significant bacterial counts: 105 CFU/mL in asx women, 103 CFU/mL in men,
102 CFU/mL in sx or catheterized Pts (hydration may falsely dilute counts)
pyuria &  UCx  sterile pyuria S urethritis, nephritis, renal tuberculosis, foreign body
‚Ä¢ Pregnant women & those undergoing urologic surgery: screen for asx bacteriuria
‚Ä¢ Blood cultures: in febrile and possibly complicated UTIs
‚Ä¢ DNA detection/cx for C. trachomatis/N. gonorrhoeae in high-risk Pts or sterile pyuria
‚Ä¢ 1st-void and midstream urine specimens, prostatic expressage, and post‚Äìprostatic
massage urine specimens in cases of suspected prostatitis
‚Ä¢ Abdominal CT to r/o abscess in Pts with pyelo who fail to defervesce after 72 h
‚Ä¢ Urologic workup (renal U/S w/ PVR, abd CT, voiding cystography) if recurrent UTIs in men
Treatment of UTIs
Clinical scenario
Empiric treatment guidelines*
Cystitis
FQ or TMP-SMX PO  3 d (uncomp.) or  10‚Äì14 d (complicated)
Asx bacteriuria in pregnancy or prior to urologic surgery S abx  3 d
Catheterized Pts
Abx as above and remove or exchange catheter
Urethritis
Treat for both Neisseria and Chlamydia
Neisseria: ceftriaxone 125 mg IM  1
Chlamydia: doxy 100 mg PO bid  7 d or azithromycin 1 g PO  1
Prostatitis
FQ or TMP-SMX PO  14‚Äì28 d (acute) or 6‚Äì12 wks (chronic)
Pyelonephritis
Outpatient: FQ or oral ceph. PO  14 d
Inpatient: ceftriaxone IV or FQ PO or aminoglycoside or 
ampicillin/sulbactam  14 d
(	 IV S PO when Pt improved clinically and afebrile  24‚Äì48 h and 
then complete 14-d course)
Renal abscess
Drainage  antibiotics as for pyelonephritis
*When possible, use organism-directed therapy, guided by in vitro susceptibilities, Pt‚Äôs past microbiology
data and recent antibiotic exposure, or local patterns of drug resistance.

SOFT TISSUE
6-6
SOFT TISSUE AND BONE INFECTIONS
CELLULITIS
Infection of superficial and deep dermis and subcutaneous fat
Microbiology (NEJM 2004;350:904; CID 2005;41:1373)
‚Ä¢ Primarily Strep and Staph, including MRSA; may include GNRs in diabetics/immunosupp.
‚Ä¢ Community-acquired MRSA (CA-MRSA, NEJM 2005;352:1485 & 2006;355:666):
Up to 75% of purulent skin/soft tissue infxns, depending on location (rapidly increasing)
Clinically indistinguishable from MSSA, though may be more aggressive/abscess-forming
High-risk groups: athletic teams, military, prison, MSM, communities w/ MRSA infxns
Often TMP-SMX sensitive; variably clindamycin sensitive (may falsely appear sus-
ceptible on lab testing, requires confirmation w/ D-test; NEJM 2007;357:380)
‚Ä¢ Special cases: cat bite S P. multocida; dog bite S P. multocida, C. canimorsus; penetrating
injury S Pseudomonas; gardening S Sporothrix; salt water S V. vulnificus (classically
from raw oysters), Erysipelothrix; fresh water S Aeromonas
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Erythema, edema, warmth, pain (rubor, tumor, calor, dolor)
‚Ä¢  Lymphangitis (proximal red streaking) and regional lymphadenopathy
‚Ä¢ P. multocida S rapid onset; C. canimorsus S sepsis w/ symmetric, peripheral gangrene
in splenectomized and other immunosupp. Pts; V. vulnificus S hemorrhagic bullae &
sepsis (especially in cirrhotics); Sporothrix S ulcerating nodules
‚Ä¢ Toxic shock syndrome can be seen w/ Staph or Strep infxn. Fever, HA, vomiting,
myalgias, pharyngitis, diarrhea, diffuse rash w/ desquamation. Hypotension, shock.
BCx may be .
Diagnosis
‚Ä¢ Largely clinical diagnosis; BCx low yield (Se 5% in simple cellulitis) but useful if 
‚Ä¢ Aspirate of bulla or pus from furuncle or pustule may provide dx
Treatment
‚Ä¢ Abx: PCNase-resist PCN or 1st-gen. ceph.; if MRSA risk: inPt S vanco; outPt S TMP-
SMX  agent for strep (eg, PCN, amox, clinda) or doxy (active against MRSA  strep) 
‚Ä¢ Limb elevation (erythema may worsen after starting abx b/c bacterial killing S inflamm.)
‚Ä¢ Worse outcomes if vasc. insuff., edema, immunosupp., resistant orgs., or deeper infxn
OTHER CUTANEOUS INFECTIONS
Definitions
‚Ä¢ Impetigo: superficial purulent lesions, usually on face/extrem,  bullae,  gold crust
‚Ä¢ Erisypelas: raised erythematous lesion with clear demarcation from normal skin
‚Ä¢ Folliculitis: superficial inflammation of hair follicles, limited to epidermis
‚Ä¢ Furunculosis: infxn of follicle extending to dermis (mult. coalescent furuncles  carbuncle)
Microbiology and treatment (CID 2005;41:1373)
‚Ä¢ Impetigo: Strep or Staph; Rx  topical mupirocin or other top. antibacterial usually sufficient
‚Ä¢ Erisypelas: mainly Grp A Strep; Rx  PCN unless Staph suspected
‚Ä¢ Folliculitis/furunculosis: S. aureus, Pseud. (‚Äúhot tub folliculitis‚Äù); Rx  warm compress
 I&D; abx controversial, give if assoc. cellulitis, lyphangitis, systemic sx, immunsosupp.
‚ÄúDIABETIC FOOT‚Äù
Infected neuropathic foot ulcer
Microbiology
‚Ä¢ Mild (superficial, no bone or joint involvement): usually S. aureus or aerobic streptococci
‚Ä¢ Limb- or life-threatening  deep, bone/joint involvement, systemic tox., limb ischemia
monomicrobial or polymicrobial with aerobes  anaerobes
aerobes  staphylococci, streptococci, enterococci, and GNR (including Pseudomonas)
anaerobes  anaerobic streptococci, Bacteroides, Clostridium (rare)
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Ulcer with surrounding erythema and warmth  purulent drainage
‚Ä¢ Tenderness may be absent due to neuropathy
‚Ä¢  Crepitus (indicating gas and ‚à¥mixed infection w/ GNR & anaerobes or Clostridium)
‚Ä¢  Underlying osteomyelitis
‚Ä¢  Systemic toxicity (fever, chills, leukocytosis, hyperglycemia)
‚Ä¢ #1 cause of DM-related hosp. days; #1 proximal cause of non-traumatic amputations in U.S.

‚Ä¢ Blood cx should be obtained in all Pts,  in 10‚Äì15%

probing to bone via ulcer/tract has high Sp but low Se; bone bx most reliable

NECROTIZING FASCIITIS
Definition
‚Ä¢ Infection and necrosis of superficial fascia, subcutaneous fat, and deep fascia (necrosis
of arteries and nerves in subcutaneous fat S gangrene)
‚Ä¢ Fournier‚Äôs gangrene: necrotizing fasciitis of the male genitalia (used by some to describe
involvement of male or female perineum)
Epidemiology
‚Ä¢ c risk in diabetes, PVD, alcohol abuse, IVDU, immunosuppression, cirrhosis
‚Ä¢ Can also affect healthy individuals
Microbiology
‚Ä¢ Group I (often after abd/perineal surgery or trauma): polymicrobial (anaerobe 
facultative anaerobe  GNR); often with DM, PVD and other comorbidities.
‚Ä¢ Group II (usually extrem): Strep pyogenes  Staph; often healthy w/o obvious portal
of entry; up to 1‚ÅÑ2 have toxic shock syndrome (TSS). CA-MRSA can rarely cause
monomicrobial necrotizing fasciitis.
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Most common sites: extremities, abdominal wall, and perineum, but can occur anywhere
‚Ä¢ Cellulitic skin 	s with poorly defined margins  rapid spread  systemic toxicity
‚Ä¢ Pain out of proportion to apparent cellulitis; skin hyperesthetic and later anesthetic
‚Ä¢ Bullae (serous S hemorrhagic); darkening of skin to bluish-gray S cutaneous 
gangrene  crepitus or radiographically visible gas
Diagnostic signs
‚Ä¢ Need high degree of clinical suspicion because of nonspecific physical exam
‚Ä¢ Aspiration of necrotic center; blood cultures; Gram stain; ‚úìCK for tissue necrosis
‚Ä¢ Imaging studies: MRI S best tissue contrast; plain radiographs S soft tissue gas;
CT S extent of infection, soft tissue gas 
‚Ä¢ Clinical diagnosis enough to initiate urgent surgical exploration
‚Ä¢ Microbiologic diagnosis from Gram stain and culture of surgical specimens
Treatment
‚Ä¢ Definitive treatment is surgical d√©bridement of necrotic tissue and fasciotomy
‚Ä¢ Type I: breadth of GNR coverage determined by host, prev hosp, prev Rx and initial
Gram stain; eg, carbapenem or (3rd-gen ceph  amp  [clinda or metronidazole])
‚Ä¢ Type II: PCN  clindamycin. If community-acquired MRSA a consideration,  vanco.
If TSS, add high dose IVIG.
‚Ä¢ Hyperbaric oxygen: adjunct, but should not delay definitive surgical treatment
Prognosis
‚Ä¢ Generally fatal if untreated; reported mortality 20‚Äì50%
CLOSTRIDIAL MYONECROSIS (GAS GANGRENE)
Definition
‚Ä¢ Life-threatening, fulminant clostridial infection of skeletal muscle
‚Ä¢ Usually muscle trauma (penetrating wound or crush injury)  wound contami-
nation w/ clostridial spores
‚Ä¢ Most commonly C. perfringens; C. septicum assoc w/ cancer (GI, heme), even w/o trauma

‚Ä¢ Incubation period 6 h to 2‚Äì3 d

OSTEOMYELITIS
Infection of bone due to hematogenous seeding or direct spread from contiguous focus
Microbiology (NEJM 1997;336:999)
‚Ä¢ Hematogenous: S. aureus; mycobacterial infection of vertebral body  Pott‚Äôs disease
‚Ä¢ Contiguous focus (may be acute or chronic)
open fracture, orthopedic surgery, etc.: S. aureus and S. epi
 vasc. insuffic. (eg, diabetic foot): polymicrobial (aerobic  anaerobic GPC & GNR)
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Surrounding soft-tissue compromise  fistula to superficial skin
‚Ä¢  Fever, malaise, and night sweats (more common in hematogenous than contiguous)
‚Ä¢ Vertebral osteomyelitis (seen in Pts over 50 y): unremitting, focal back pain, usually
fever (NEJM 2010;362:1022)
Diagnostic studies (JAMA 2008;299:806)
‚Ä¢ Identification of the causative organism is key
‚Ä¢ Culture data from tissue (surgical sampling/needle bx), not swabs of ulcers/fistulae
‚Ä¢ Blood cultures (more often  with acute hematogenous osteomyelitis)
‚Ä¢ ESR 70 greatly increases likelihood of osteo (JAMA 2008;299:806)
‚Ä¢ Imaging
plain radiographs: normal early in disease; lytic lesions seen after 2‚Äì6 wks
MRI: can detect very early changes (overall Se 90%, Sp 82%; Archives 2007;167:125)
CT: can demonstrate periosteal reaction and cortical and medullary destruction
CT & MRI very Se but not completely Sp; false  if contiguous focus w/ periosteal
reaction, Charcot changes
radionuclide imaging: very Se but non-Sp (false  if soft-tissue inflammation)
Treatment
‚Ä¢ Antibiotics (based on cx data)  4‚Äì6 wks
‚Ä¢ Surgery should be considered for any of the following: acute osteo that fails to respond
to medical Rx, chronic osteo, complications of pyogenic vertebral osteo (eg, early signs
of cord compression, spinal instability, epidural abscess), or infected prosthesis
EPIDURAL ABSCESS
Etiology
‚Ä¢ Hematogenous spread (2/3): skin infection, soft tissue (dental abscess), or endocarditis
‚Ä¢ Direct extension (1/3): vertebral osteomyelitis, sacral decubitus ulcer, spinal anesthesia
or surgery, lumbar puncture
‚Ä¢ Risk factors: diabetes, renal failure, alcoholism, IVDU, immunosuppression
‚Ä¢ S. aureus most common pathogen
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Back pain (unremitting including midline)  often fever  nerve root or cord signs
Diagnostic studies
‚Ä¢ MRI
‚Ä¢ Aspiration of abscess fluid for Gram stain & cx or operative Gram stain & cx 
‚Ä¢ Blood cx (frequently )
Treatment
‚Ä¢ Antibiotics  surgery (decompressive laminectomy and d√©bridement) for failure

ASEPTIC MENINGITIS
Definition
‚Ä¢ Negative bacterial microbiologic data, CSF pleocytosis with  appropriate

‚Ä¢ TB, fungal, spirochetal (Lyme, syphilis, leptospirosis), rickettsial, Coxiella, Ehrlichia

‚Ä¢ Neoplasms: intracranial tumors (or cysts), lymphomatous or carcinomatous meningitis
(CSF cytology or flow may be reactive and dx may require meningeal bx)

‚Ä¢ No abx if suspect viral (cell count 500 w/ 50% lymphs, TP 80‚Äì100 mg/dL, normal glc,
 Gram stain, not elderly/immunosupp.); o/w start empiric abx, wait for cx data
‚Ä¢ MTb: antimycobacterial Rx  dexamethasone (NEJM 2004;351:1741)
‚Ä¢ Fungal: amphotericin B or lipid formulation,  5-fluorouracil

VIRAL ENCEPHALITIS
Definition
‚Ä¢ Viral infection of the brain parenchyma with evidence of neurologic dysfunction
Etiologies (Lancet 2002;359:507; Neurology 2006;66:75; CID 2008;47:303)
‚Ä¢ HSV-1 (9%): all ages/seasons; MRI: temporal lobe lesions/edema; EEG: temporal focus
‚Ä¢ VZV (9%): 1
 or reactivation;  vesicular rash; all ages (favors elderly), all seasons
‚Ä¢ Arboviruses (9%): Eastern/Western equine,West Nile, St. Louis, Japanese, Powassan
W Nile (NEJM 2005;353:287): mosquito vector, bird reservoir; fever, HA, flaccid paralysis, rash
‚Ä¢ Enteroviruses (Coxsackie, echo): viral syndrome; peaks in late summer/early fall
‚Ä¢ Others: CMV, EBV, HIV, JC virus (PML), measles, mumps, rubella, rabies, flu, adenovirus
‚Ä¢ Nonviral mimics: bacterial endocarditis, brain abscess, toxoplasmosis,TB, toxins, vas-
culitis, hematologic malignancies, subdural hematoma, encephalomyelitis (eg,
ADEM), paraneoplastic syndromes, seizure, mitochondrial disorders
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Fever, HA, 	 MS,  seizures and focal neuro findings (latter atypical for viral meningitis)
Diagnostic studies (etiologic dx made in only about 25% of cases)
‚Ä¢ Lumbar puncture: lymphocytic pleocytosis; PCR for HSV (95% Se & Sp at 2‚Äì3 d),VZV,
CMV, EBV, HIV, JC, adeno/enterovirus, W Nile (60% Se); W Nile CSF IgM 80% Se
‚Ä¢ MRI (CT if MRI unavailable);W Nile w/ thalamic hyperintensity
‚Ä¢ EEG (to r/o seizure; findings in encephalitis are non-specific)
‚Ä¢ Dilated retinal exam
‚Ä¢ Serologies; vaccine history, ELISA or DFA of nasal or resp swabs for respiratory viruses
Treatment
‚Ä¢ HSV, VZV: acyclovir 10 mg/kg IV q8h (often empiric Rx given frequency of HSV/VZV)
‚Ä¢ CMV: ganciclovir  foscarnet; supportive care for most other etiologies
BELL‚Äôs PALSY
Definition & Etiology
‚Ä¢ Acute, idiopathic, unilateral facial nerve palsy (peripheral CN VII)
‚Ä¢ Postulated to be due to reactivation of HSV-1 in cranial nerve VII
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Unilateral facial muscle weakness, hyperacusis, decreased taste/lacrimation/salivation
Diagnosis
‚Ä¢ Dx of exclusion: r/o brainstem lesion, Lyme, zoster (incl sine herpete), HIV/AIDS, sarcoid
Treatment (NEJM 2007;357:1598 & JAMA 2009;302:985)
‚Ä¢ 80% recover spontaneously by 9 mos (much lower rate in diabetics)
‚Ä¢ Corticosteroids (prednisolone 25 mg PO bid  10 d) started w/in 72 h of sx onset
improve odds of recovery (note: no conclusive data for diabetics, immunosuppressed)
‚Ä¢ No conclusive data to support the use of acyclovir or valacyclovir, though often given
ZOSTER
Definition & Etiology
‚Ä¢ Zoster  herpes zoster  shingles: acute, unilateral, painful dermatomal skin eruption
‚Ä¢ VZV reactivation in peripheral nerve distribution from latency in dorsal root ganglion
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Neuritic pain in a dermatomal distribution, then acute dermatomal eruption of
clustered rash (vesicles  papules/pustules  macules) in varying stages of evolution
‚Ä¢ Consecutive dermatomes may be seen in all Pts; more widespread in immunosupp.
‚Ä¢ Lesions in V1 distribution of facial nerve require urgent ophthalmologic evaluation
‚Ä¢ Post-herpetic neuralgia (PHN)  severe pain lasting 90 d after episode; may last
mos to y, more frequent w/ c age and w/ delay of antiviral Rx
Diagnosis
‚Ä¢ Physical appearance of rash; most sensitive is DFA from scraping of unroofed vesicle,
Tzanck does not distinguish HSV or VZV, culture insensitive for VZV (unlike HSV)
Treatment
‚Ä¢ Rx if can initiate w/in 72 h of skin lesions in normal host or at any time dx in immunosupp.
‚Ä¢ Valacyclovir or famciclovir  7 d in normal host; acyclovir 10 mg/kg IV q8h if dissemi-
nated or high-risk Pt (medically ill, immunosupp.,V1 zoster w/ any ophthalmic s/s, etc).
‚Ä¢ Prevention: vaccine approved for Pts 60 y (T lifetime risk from 20% to 10%, also T PHN)

BACTERIAL ENDOCARDITIS
Definition
‚Ä¢ Infection of endothelium of heart (including but not limited to the valves) 
‚Ä¢ Acute (ABE): infection of normal valves with a virulent organism (eg, S. aureus, group
A or other beta-hemolytic strep, Strep pneumo)
‚Ä¢ Subacute (SBE): indolent infection of abnl valves w/ less virulent organism (eg, S. viridans)
Predisposing conditions
‚Ä¢ Abnormal valve
high-risk: prior endocarditis, rheumatic valvular disease,AoV disease (incl. bicuspid),
complex cyanotic lesions, prosthesis (annual risk 0.3‚Äì1%)
medium-risk: MV disease (including MVP w/ MR or leaflet thickening), HCMP
‚Ä¢ Abnormal risk of bacteremia: IVDU, indwelling venous catheters, poor dentition,
hemodialysis, DM, intracardiac devices (eg, pacemaker, ICD)

Diagnostic studies
‚Ä¢ Blood cultures (before abx): at least 3 sets (aerobic & anaerobic bottles) from dif-
ferent sites, ideally spaced 1 h apart. ‚úìBCx (at least 2 sets) after appropriate abx
have been initiated to document clearance; repeat q24‚Äì48h until .
‚Ä¢ CBC w/ diff (cWBC common in ABE; anemia in 90% SBE), ESR, RF, BUN/Cr, U/A & UCx
‚Ä¢ ECG (on admission and at regular intervals) to assess for new conduction abnormalities
‚Ä¢ Echocardiogram: obtain TTE if low clinical suspicion, expect good image quality;
TEE if (i) mod-to-high clinical suspicion, (ii) high-risk Pt (prosthetic valve, prior IE,
CHD), (iii) TTE nondx, (iv) TTE  but endocarditis strongly suspected, or (v) sus-
pect progressive or invasive infection (eg, persistent bacteremia or fever, new con-
duction abnl, intracardiac shunt, etc.) (Circ 2005;111:e394)

tion in prosthesis, as well as to improve structural integrity of tissue that will
receive prosthesis
‚Ä¢ Severe valvular dysfunction S refractory CHF: emergent if refractory cardio-

10 mm veg. & prior embolic event, or 15 mm veg.; risk of embolism 4.8/1000 Pt
days in 1st wk, 1.7/1000 Pt days thereafter; cerebral emboli no longer considered 
contraindic to surgery unless hemorrhage (then ideally wait 1 mo) or severe stroke 
‚Ä¢ PVE, especially with valve dysfunction or dehiscence or S. aureus or GNR infection
Prognosis
‚Ä¢ NVE: non-IVDU S. aureus S 30‚Äì45% mortality; IVDU S. aureus (typically right-sided)
S 10‚Äì15% mortality; SBE S 10‚Äì15% mortality 
‚Ä¢ PVE S 23% mortality
‚Ä¢ Aortic valve worse prognosis than mitral valve

Endocarditis Prophylaxis
Cardiac
Prosthetic valve; previous NVE; congenital heart disease (CHD) 
conditions*
including unrepaired or incompletely repaired cyanotic CHD (palliative 
shunts or conduits), 1st 6 mos after completely repaired CHD using 
prosthetic material; cardiac transplant recipients w/ valvulopathy
(Prophylaxis no longer rec. in acquired valvular dysfxn,
bicuspid AoV, MVP with leaflet thickening or regurgitation, HCMP)
Procedures*
Dental: that involve manipulation of gingival tissue or periapical region
of teeth or perforation of oral mucosa (eg, extractions, periodontal
procedures, implants, root canal, cleanings)
Respiratory: incision or biopsy of respiratory mucosa
(Prophylaxis no longer rec. for GI or GU procedures)
Regimens
Oral: amoxicillin 2 g 30‚Äì60 min before
Unable to take PO: amp 2 g IM/IV or cefazolin or cftx 1 g IM/IV
PCN-allergic: clindamycin 600 mg PO/IM/IV
*Pts should meet both indications (conditions and procedure) to qualify for prophylaxis. (Circ 2007;116:1736)
BACTEREMIA
Etiologies
‚Ä¢ 1
 infxn due to direct inoculation of the blood, frequently assoc. w/ intravascular catheters.
Catheter-related bloodstream infection  same org from peripheral cx and (cath
tip cx or cx drawn from catheter) (CID 2009;49:1).
‚Ä¢ 2
 infxn due to infection in another site (eg, UTI, lung, biliary tree, skin) spreading to blood
Microbiology
‚Ä¢ 1
 infxn/indwelling catheters (CID 2004;39:309): coagulase-neg staphylococci (includes 
S. epidermidis and others) 31%, Staphylococcus aureus 20%, enterococci 9%, Candida
species 9%, E. coli 6%, Klebsiella species 5%
‚Ä¢ 2
 infxn: dependent on source
Risk factors for true bacteremia (JAMA 1992;267:1962)
‚Ä¢ Pt: fever, shaking chills, IVDU, major comorbidities, immunosupp, indwelling catheter
‚Ä¢ Organism
higher risk: S. aureus, -hemolytic strep, enterococci, GNR, S. pneumonia, Neisseria
lower risk: coag-neg staph (10%), diphtheroids, & Propionibacterium (0%)
‚Ä¢ Time to growth: 24 h S higher risk, 72 h S lower risk (except for slow-
growing organisms such as HACEK group)
‚Ä¢ Confirmatory cultures: draw prior to first abx dose in stable Pts if possible
‚Ä¢ Factors favoring endocarditis: bacteremia that is high-grade w/o identifiable source, per-
sisting after line removal or drainage of focal source, in hosts at risk for endocarditis, or
w/ organisms known to cause IE (Duke criteria); emboli
Treatment

empiric therapy for GPC: vanco to cover coag-neg staph and MRSA while awaiting sensi

TUBERCULOSIS
Epidemiology
‚Ä¢ U.S.: 10‚Äì15 million infected (10 c risk if foreign-born or minority); worldwide: 2 billion 
‚Ä¢ After resurgence in U.S. 1984‚Äì1992, rates have declined, though slower than CDC goals
‚Ä¢ Pt is more likely to develop TB disease if:
High-prevalence populations (more likely to be exposed to & infected w/ bacillus):
immigrant from high-prevalence area, homeless or medically underserved, resident or
worker in jail or long-term facility, HCW at facility w/ TB, close contact to Pt w/ active TB
High-risk populations (more likely to progress from infxn S active disease): HIV 
or other immunosupp, chronic renal failure, DM, organ Tx, IVDU, EtOH, mal-
nourished, malignancy, gastrectomy, on biologics (eg,TNF inhibitors, rituximab)
Microbiology and natural history
‚Ä¢ Transmission of Mycobacterium tuberculosis via small-particle aerosols (ie, droplet nuclei)
‚Ä¢ 90% of infected normal hosts will never develop clinically evident disease, 10% will 
‚Ä¢ Localized disease: healing & calcification or progressive 1
 TB (at site of infection)
‚Ä¢ Hematogenous spread: latent infection  reactivation TB or progressive disseminated TB
‚Ä¢ Two-thirds of clinically evident disease in U.S. due to reactivation
Screening for prior infection
‚Ä¢ Whom to screen: high-prevalence and high-risk populations (HIV  Pts should
have PPD testing as part of initial evaluation and annually thereafter)
‚Ä¢ How to screen: Mantoux tuberculin test (ie, purified protein derivative or PPD)
inject 5-TU (0.1 mL) intermed. strength PPD intradermally S wheal; examine 48‚Äì72 h
‚Ä¢ How to interpret PPD: determine max diameter of induration by palpation
Size of reaction

False 

Booster effect
c induration due to immunologic boost by prior skin test in previously
sensitized individual (by TB or NTM, or BCG).Test goes from  S 
but does not represent true conversion due to recent infection. 2nd 
test is Pt‚Äôs true baseline. Can be 1 y after initial test.
(NEJM 2002;347:1860)
‚Ä¢ IFN- release assays (IGRA): (Ag-stimulated IFN- release from Pt‚Äôs T-cells): may
be used for screening where you would use PPD; may have c Sp, esp in BCG Rx‚Äôd
Pts (Annals 2008;149:177). Relies on host immune fxn, so Se still limited in immuno-
supp. Lack of gold standard for latent TB infxn compromises Se/Sp estimates (J Clin
Epi 2010;63:257). One-step test, but more expensive than PPD.
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Primary TB pneumonia: middle or lower lobe consolidation,  effusion,  cavitation
‚Ä¢ TB pleurisy: can occur w/ primary or reactivation. Due to breakdown of granuloma
w/ spilling of contents into pleural cavity and local inflammation. Pulmonary
effusion  pericardial and peritoneal effusions (tuberculous polyserositis).
‚Ä¢ Reactivation TB pulmonary dis.: apical infiltrate  volume loss  cavitation
‚Ä¢ Miliary TB: acute or insidious; due to widespread hematogenous dissemination; usu-
ally in immunosupp, DM, EtOH, elderly or malnourished. Constitutional sx
(fever, night sweats, weight loss) usually prominent. Pulm disease w/ small millet
seed‚Äìlike lesions (2‚Äì4 mm) on CXR or chest CT (latter more Se), present in
60‚Äì80% of those w/ miliary TB.
‚Ä¢ Extrapulmonary TB: lymphadenitis, pericarditis, peritonitis, meningitis, nephritis  sterile
pyuria, osteomyelitis (vertebral  Pott‚Äôs disease), hepatitis, splenitis, cutaneous, arthritis
‚Ä¢ TB and HIV: HIV-infected & other immunosupp Pts at c risk for infxn, progressive 1
infxn, and reactivation. Risk of progression from infxn to disease 8‚Äì10%/y. Can occur at
any CD4 count, but more likely to disseminate at lower CD4 counts. Reinfection (includ-
ing w/ drug-resistant strains) is clinically significant, particularly in hyperendemic areas.
‚Ä¢ Multi-drug resistant (MDR) TB: resistant to isoniazid (INH) and rifampin (RIF)
‚Ä¢ Extensively drug resistant (XDR) TB: resistant to INH, RIF, quinolone, & 2nd-line injectables

Diagnostic studies for active TB (high index of suspicion is key!)
‚Ä¢ Acid-fast smear (rapid dx) and culture (c Se and allows susceptibility testing) of
sputum, bronchoscopic alveolar lavage, pleura, or other clinical specimens; avoid FQ
if considering dx of TB, as they can compromise dx yield
‚Ä¢ PCR: 94‚Äì97% Se c/w smear; 40‚Äì77% Se c/w culture (JAMA 2009;301:1014)
‚Ä¢ CXR: classically fibrocavitary apical disease in reactivation vs. middle & lower lobe
consolidation in 1
 TB, but distinction imperfect and HIV  strongly assoc. with non-
apical disease, regardless of timing (JAMA 2005;293:2740)
‚Ä¢ Adenosine deaminase (ADA): useful in extrapulmonary sites, best validated for ascites
Preventive therapy (JAMA 2005;293:2776; Annals 2009;150:ITC6-1)
‚Ä¢ Appropriate prophylaxis reduces incidence of subsequent disease by 65‚Äì75%
‚Ä¢ Treat Pts who are  based on screening guidelines listed above, or any exposed HIV  Pt
‚Ä¢ R/O active disease in any Pt w/ suggestive s/s before starting INH. If HIV , routinely ask
if cough, fever, or night sweats; if yes S ‚úìsputum smear, CXR, CD4 (NEJM 2010;362:707).

Treatment of active tuberculosis (JAMA 2005;293:2776; Annals 2009;150:ITC6-1)

‚Ä¢ Screen for HIV in all Pts in whom initiating anti-TB Rx; if indicated, should initiate

‚Ä¢ Paradoxical worsening of sx can occur after starting Rx. More common w/ extrapulm TB 
(eg, tuberculoma, LAN), likely due to hypersensitivity response to killing of bacilli. More
frequent/severe w/ concurrent immune reconstitution (eg, HIV  Pts started on ARVs,
Pts taken off immunosuppressants, etc). Must r/o treatment failure (repeat Cx, imaging, etc).

HIV/AIDS
