HIV/AIDS
Definition
‚Ä¢ AIDS: HIV  CD4 count 200/mm3 or opportunistic infection (OI) or malignancy
Epidemiology
‚Ä¢ 1 million Americans infected w/ HIV; 6th leading cause of death in 25‚Äì44 y-old age group 
‚Ä¢ 33.4 million individuals infected worldwide
‚Ä¢ Routes: sexual (risk is 0.3% for male-to-male, 0.2% for male-to-female, 0.1% for female-to-
male transmission), IVDA, transfusions, needle sticks (0.3%), vertical (15‚Äì40% w/o ARV)
‚Ä¢ Postexposure (risk infxn 0.3%) ppx: 2 NRTIs ( PI or NNRTI if high-risk)  4 wks 
Acute retroviral syndrome (ARS)
‚Ä¢ Occurs in 40‚Äì90% of Pts 2‚Äì6 wks after infxn;  ELISA,  viral load (2 wks after infxn)
‚Ä¢ Mononucleosis-like syndrome (c incid mucocut. & neuro manifestations c/w EBV or CMV)
Diagnostic studies
‚Ä¢ ELISA for HIV-1 Ab:  1‚Äì12 wks after acute infection; 99% Se; 1
 screen test
‚Ä¢ Western blot:  if 2 bands from diff regions of HIV genome; 99% Sp; confirmatory
after  ELISA
‚Ä¢ Rapid preliminary tests: 4 Ab tests; use saliva, plasma, blood, or serum; 99% Se &
96‚Äì99% Sp (Annals 2008;149:153); PPV in low prev populations as low as 50% 
‚Ä¢ PCR (viral load): detects HIV-1 RNA in plasma; assay range is 48‚Äì10 million copies/mL
2% false , but usually low # copies; in contrast, should be very high (750 k) in 1
 infxn
‚Ä¢ When testing, obtain informed consent for ELISA,Western, and PCR
‚Ä¢ HIV screening is recommended for all Pts in all health care settings (MMWR Sept 22, 2006)
‚Ä¢ CD4 count: not a dx test per se, as may be HIV  and have a normal CD4 count or
may have a low CD4 count and not be HIV ; many other illnesses impact CD4 count
Initial approach to HIV  Pt
‚Ä¢ Document HIV infection (if adequate documentation is not available, repeat dx studies)
‚Ä¢ H&P (mucocutaneous, neurocognitive issues, OIs, malignancies, STDs); review all
ARVs and other meds
‚Ä¢ Lab evaluation: CD4 count, viral load, HIV genotype, CBC w/ diff., Cr, lytes, LFTs, fasting
glc and lipids; PPD or IGRA, syphilis serology; toxoplasmosis & CMV IgG; HAV, HBV, &
HCV serologies; Chlamydia & gonorrhea screening; baseline CXR; Pap smear in 

LYME DISEASE
Microbiology
‚Ä¢ Infection with spirochete Borrelia burgdorferi (consider coinfection w/ Ehrlichia, Babesia)
‚Ä¢ Transmitted by ticks (Ixodes, deer tick); animal hosts include deer and mice
‚Ä¢ Infection usually requires tick attachment 36‚Äì48 h
Epidemiology
‚Ä¢ Most common vector-borne illness in U.S.; peak incidence in summer (May‚ÄìAug)
‚Ä¢ Majority of cases in NY, NJ, CT, RI,WI, PA, MA, ME, NH, MI, MD, DE, northern CA
‚Ä¢ Humans contact ticks usually in fields with low brush near wooded areas

Stage
Manifestations
Stage 1
Due to local effects of spirochete. General: flu-like illness
(early localized)

wks after infection
central clearing, size 6‚Äì38 cm; lymphocytomas; regional LAN
Stage 2
Due to spirochetemia and immune response
(early dissem.)
General: fatigue, malaise, LAN, HA; fever uncommon
wks to mos after

infection

meningitis, mononeuritis multiplex ( pain), transverse myelitis

Stage 3
Due to chronic infection or autoimmune response

mos to y after

Diagnostic studies
‚Ä¢ In general, a clinical diagnosis, but rigorous dx requires confirmatory testing (per IDSA)
‚Ä¢ Serology (in right clinical setting): screen w/ ELISA, but false  due to other spirochetal
diseases, SLE, RA, EBV, HIV, etc.; false  due to early abx therapy or w/in 6 wks of infxn
confirm  ELISA results w/ Western blot (c Sp)
‚Ä¢ ‚úìCSF if suspected neuro disease:  intrathecal Ab if (IgGCSF/IgGserum)/(albCSF/albserum) 1
Treatment (NEJM 2006;354:2794)
‚Ä¢ Prophylaxis (best prevention is tick avoidance): protective clothing, tick ‚úìq24h, DEET
Chemoprophylaxis w/ doxycycline 200 mg PO  1 only if all of the following:
1. Ixodes scapularis tick attached 36 h

ROCKY MOUNTAIN SPOTTED FEVER (RMSF)
Microbiology & Epidemiology
‚Ä¢ Infection with Rickettsia rickettsii (Gram  obligate intracellular bacterium)
‚Ä¢ Transmitted by Dermacentor variabilis, Dermacentor andersoni
‚Ä¢ Coastal mid-Atl, New Engl, midwest, northwest, southeast, Canada, Mexico, Central &
South America
‚Ä¢ Peak incidence spring and early summer
Clinical manifestations (typically w/in 1 week of tick exposure)
‚Ä¢ Fever, HA, 	MS, myalgias, N/V, occasionally abdominal pain
‚Ä¢ Rash (2‚Äì5 d after onset)  centripetal: starts on ankles and wrists S trunk, palms &
soles; progresses from macular to maculopapular to petechial
‚Ä¢ Severe cases progress to vasculitis, hypoperfusion/shock, end-organ damage
‚Ä¢ Up to 75% mortality if untreated, 5‚Äì10% even w/ Rx (esp. if delayed) (NEJM 2005;353:551)
Diagnosis
‚Ä¢ Usually a clinical diagnosis; requires early clinical suspicion given risks of delayed Rx
‚Ä¢ During acute illness can dx by examining skin bx for rickettsiae (Se 70%)
‚Ä¢ 7‚Äì10 d after onset of sx, serology (indirect fluorescent antibody test) turns 
Treatment
‚Ä¢ Doxycycline 100 mg PO bid (give empirically if clinical suspicion)
EHRLICHIOSIS/ANAPLASMOSIS
Microbiology
‚Ä¢ Infection with Gram  obligate intracellular bacterium
‚Ä¢ Human monocytic ehrlichiosis (Ehrlichiosis chaffeensis) (HME)
‚Ä¢ Human granulocytic anaplasmosis (Anaplama phagocytophilum) (HGA)
‚Ä¢ Transmission: HME by Amblyomma americanum, Dermacentor variabilis; HGA by Ixodes
Epidemiology
‚Ä¢ Majority of HGA cases found in RI, MN, CT, NY, MD
‚Ä¢ Majority of cases of HME found in SE, southcentral, and mid-Atlantic regions of U.S.
‚Ä¢ Peak incidence spring and early summer
Clinical manifestations (typically w/in 3 wks of tick exposure)
‚Ä¢ Fever, myalgias, malaise, HA, occasional cough, dyspnea; onset often acute
‚Ä¢ Laboratory: leukopenia, thrombocytopenia, renal failure, c aminotransferases, LDH, A
Diagnosis
‚Ä¢ Start Rx based on clinical suspicion; however, definitive dx requires confirmation
‚Ä¢ Acute illness: intraleukocytic morulae on peripheral blood smear (rare); PCR; later: serology
Treatment
‚Ä¢ Doxycycline 100 mg PO bid (often  10 d); should defervesce in 48 h, else reconsider dx
BABESIOSIS
Microbiology & Epidemiology
‚Ä¢ Infection with parasite Babesia microti (U.S.), Babesia divergens (Europe)
‚Ä¢ Transmitted by Ixodes
‚Ä¢ Europe & U.S. (more commonly coastal areas & islands off of MA, NY, RI, CT)
‚Ä¢ Peak incidence spring and summer
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Range from asx to fevers, sweats, myalgias, & HA to severe hemolytic anemia,
hemoglobinuria, & death (degree of parasitemia correlates roughly with severity)
‚Ä¢ Risk factors for severe disease include asplenia, T cellular immunity, c age, pregnancy
Diagnosis
‚Ä¢ Clinical syndrome  blood smear with intraerythrocytic parasites; PCR; serology (late)
Treatment
‚Ä¢ [Atovaquone  azithromycin] (1st line) or [clindamycin  quinine] (for more severe cases)
‚Ä¢ Exchange transfusion if parasitemia 10%, severe hemolysis, or SIRS
TULAREMIA
Microbiology
‚Ä¢ Infxn w/ Francisella tularensis via contact w/ animal tissue, tick/insect bite, ? aerosol
Clinical manifestations (typically w/in 2‚Äì10 d of infxn)
‚Ä¢ Acute onset of fever, HA, nausea; ulcer w/ black eschar at site of entry; LAN; PNA
Diagnosis & Treatment
‚Ä¢ Hazardous to cx. Serology  by 2nd week.
‚Ä¢ Streptomycin or gentamicin  7‚Äì14 d

Workup

‚Ä¢ Laboratory evaluation

‚Ä¢ Pursue abnormalities raised by above w/u (eg, bx, MRI, etc., for dx, not screening)
‚Ä¢ Duke‚Äôs criteria for endocarditis (qv) have good Se & Sp in Pts with FUO
‚Ä¢ Consider temporal artery bx if c ESR and age 60, particularly if other s/s
‚Ä¢ ? Bone marrow aspirate & bx (esp if signs of marrow infiltration) or liver bx (espec. if c A):
even w/o localizing s/s, yield may be up to 24% (path and culture) (Archives 2009;169:2018)
‚Ä¢ More likely to make a dx if: continuous fever, duration 180 d, c ESR/CRP/LDH,

‚Ä¢ Empiric antibiotics are not indicated (unless Pt neutropenic)
‚Ä¢ Empiric glucocorticoids not indicated unless strong suspicion for specific rheumatologic dx
‚Ä¢ 5‚Äì15% of FUO resolve on their own (wks to mos) w/o dx

PITUITARY DISORDERS
HYPOPITUITARY SYNDROMES
Panhypopituitarism
‚Ä¢ Etiologies
Primary: surgery, radiation, tumors (primary or metastatic), infection, infiltration
(sarcoid, hemochromatosis), autoimmune, ischemia (including Sheehan‚Äôs syndrome
caused by pituitary infarction intrapartum), carotid aneurysms, cavernous sinus
thrombosis, trauma
Secondary (hypothalamic dysfunction or stalk interruption): tumors (including
craniopharyngioma), infection, infiltration, radiation, surgery, trauma
‚Ä¢ Clinical manifestations
Hormonal: acute S weakness, easy fatigability, hypotension, polyuria and polydipsia;
chronic S bradycardia, sexual dysfxn, loss of axillary & pubic hair, wt loss, amenorrhea
Mass effect: headache, visual field s, cranial nerve palsies, galactorrhea
Apoplexy (pituitary hemorrhage or infarction, usually w/ underlying pituitary adenoma):
sudden headache, N/V, visual field s, cranial nerve palsies, meningismus,  MS,
hypoglycemia, hypotension
‚Ä¢ Diagnostic studies
Hormonal studies
chronic: T target gland hormone  T or normal trophic pituitary hormone
acute: target gland hormonal studies may be normal
partial hypopituitarism is more common than panhypopituitarism
Pituitary MRI
‚Ä¢ Treatment
Replace deficient target gland hormones
Most important deficiencies to recognize and treat in inpatients are adrenal insufficiency
and hypothyroidism; if both present, treat with glucocorticoids first, then replace
thyroid hormone so as not to precipitate adrenal crisis
T ACTH
‚Ä¢ Adrenal insufficiency similar to 1 (see ‚ÄúAdrenal Disorders‚Äù) except:
no salt cravings or hypokalemia (b/c aldo preserved)
no hyperpigmentation (b/c ACTH/MSH is not c)
T TSH
‚Ä¢ Central hypothyroidism similar to 1 (see ‚ÄúThyroid Disorders‚Äù) except absence of goiter
‚Ä¢ Dx with free T4 in addition to TSH, as TSH may be low or inappropriately normal
T PRL
‚Ä¢ Inability to lactate
T GH
‚Ä¢ c chronic risk for osteoporosis, fatigue, weight gain
‚Ä¢ Dx with failure to c GH w/ appropriate stimulus (eg, insulin tolerance test, glucagon
stimulation)
‚Ä¢ GH replacement in adults controversial
T FSH & LH
‚Ä¢ Clinical manifestations: T libido, impotence, oligomenorrhea or amenorrhea, infertility
‚Ä¢ Physical examination: T testicular size; loss of axillary, pubic, and body hair
‚Ä¢ Dx with: T a.m. testosterone or estradiol and  T or normal FSH/LH (all levels
T in acute illness, ‚à¥do not measure in hospitalized Pts)
‚Ä¢ Treatment: testosterone or estrogen replacement vs. correction of the underlying cause
T ADH (hypothalamic or stalk disease): diabetes insipidus
‚Ä¢ Clinical manifestations: severe polyuria, mild hypernatremia (severe if T access to H2O)
‚Ä¢ Diagnostic studies: see ‚ÄúDisorders of Sodium Homeostasis‚Äù

HYPERPITUITARY SYNDROMES
Pituitary tumors
‚Ä¢ Pathophysiology: adenoma S excess of trophic hormone (if tumor fxnal, but 30‚Äì40%
not) and potentially deficiencies in other trophic hormones due to compression;
cosecretion of PRL and growth hormone in 10% of prolactinomas
‚Ä¢ Clinical manifestations: syndromes due to oversecretion of hormones (see below)
 mass effect: headache, visual s, diplopia, cranial neuropathies
‚Ä¢ Workup: MRI, hormone levels,  visual field testing, consider MEN1 (see below)
if 10 mm, ‚àÖmass effect, no hormonal effects, can f/up q 3‚Äì6 mos
Hyperprolactinemia (NEJM 2010;362:1219)
‚Ä¢ Etiology
prolactinoma (50% of pituitary adenomas)
stalk compression due to nonprolactinoma S T inhibitory dopamine S c PRL (mild)
‚Ä¢ Physiology: PRL induces lactation and inhibits GnRH S T FSH & LH
‚Ä¢ Clinical manifestations: amenorrhea, galactorrhea, infertility, T libido, impotence
‚Ä¢ Diagnostic studies: c PRL, but elevated in many situations, ‚à¥r/o pregnancy or
exogenous estrogens, hypothyroidism, dopamine agonists (psych meds, antiemetics),
renal failure (T clearance), cirrhosis, stress, c carb diet. MRI to evaluate for tumor,
visual field testing if MRI shows compression of optic chiasm.
‚Ä¢ Treatment
If asx (no HA or hypogonadal sx) and microadenoma (10 mm), follow with MRI
If sx or macroadenoma (10 mm) options include:
medical with dopamine agonist such as bromocriptine (70‚Äì100% success rate) or
cabergoline (better tolerated); side effects include N/V, orthostasis, nasal congestion
surgical: transsphenoidal surgery (main indications: failed medical Rx, GH co-secretion,
or neurologic sx not improving); 10‚Äì20% recurrence rate
radiation: if medical or surgical therapy have failed or are not tolerated
Acromegaly (c GH; 10% of adenomas; NEJM 2006;355:2558)
‚Ä¢ Physiology: stimulates secretion of insulin-like growth factor 1 (IGF-1)
‚Ä¢ Clinical manifestations: c soft tissue, arthralgias, jaw enlargement, headache, carpal tunnel
syndrome, macroglossia, hoarseness, sleep apnea, amenorrhea, impotence, diabetes
mellitus, acanthosis/skin tags, c sweating, HTN/CMP, colonic polyps
‚Ä¢ Diagnostic studies: no utility in checking random GH levels because of pulsatile secretion
c IGF-1 (somatomedin C);  c PRL; pituitary MRI to evaluate for tumor
oral glc tolerance test S GH not suppressed to 1 (0.3 if newer assay) ng/mL by 2 h
‚Ä¢ Treatment: surgery, octreotide (long- and short-acting preparations), dopamine 
agonists (if PRL co-secretion), pegvisomant (GH receptor antagonist), radiation
‚Ä¢ Prognosis: w/o Rx there is 2‚Äì3 c mortality, risk of pituitary insufficiency, colon cancer
Cushing‚Äôs disease (c ACTH): 10‚Äì15% of adenomas; see ‚ÄúAdrenal Disorders‚Äù
Central hyperthyroidism (c TSH, c -subunit): extremely rare; see ‚ÄúThyroid Disorders‚Äù
c FSH & LH: usually non-fxn, presents as hypopituitarism b/c of compression effects

HYPOTHYROIDISM
Etiologies
‚Ä¢ Primary (
90% of cases of hypothyroidism; T free T4, c TSH)
Goitrous: Hashimoto‚Äôs thyroiditis, recovery after thyroiditis, iodine defic., Li, amiodarone
Nongoitrous: surgical destruction, s/p radioactive iodine or XRT, amiodarone
‚Ä¢ Central (T free T4,low/nl or slightly high TSH):hypothalamic or pituitary failure (TSH levels T or
‚Äúnormal,‚Äù can be slightly c although functionally inactive due to abnormal glycosylation)
Hashimoto‚Äôs thyroiditis
‚Ä¢ Autoimmune destruction with patchy lymphocytic infiltration
‚Ä¢ Associated with other autoimmune disease and may be part of PGA syndrome type II
‚Ä¢  antithyroid peroxidase (anti-TPO) and antithyroglobulin (anti-Tg) Abs in 
90%
Clinical manifestations (Lancet 2004;363:793)
‚Ä¢ Early: weakness, fatigue, arthralgias, myalgias, headache, depression, cold intolerance,
weight gain, constipation, menorrhagia, dry skin, coarse brittle hair, brittle nails, carpal
tunnel syndrome, delayed DTRs (‚Äúhung up‚Äù reflexes), diastolic HTN, hyperlipidemia
‚Ä¢ Late: slow speech, hoarseness, loss of outer third of eyebrows, myxedema
(nonpitting skin thickening due to c glycosaminoglycans), periorbital puffiness,
bradycardia, pleural, pericardial, & peritoneal effusions, atherosclerosis
‚Ä¢ Myxedema coma: hypothermia, hypotension, hypoventilation,  MS
Diagnostic studies
‚Ä¢ T FT4; c TSH in primary hypothyroidism;  antithyroid Ab in Hashimoto‚Äôs thyroiditis
‚Ä¢ May see hyponatremia, hypoglycemia, anemia, c LDL, T HDL, and c CK
‚Ä¢ Screening recommended for pregnant women
Treatment of overt hypothyroidism
‚Ä¢ Levothyroxine (1.5‚Äì1.7 g/kg/d), re ‚úìTSH q5‚Äì6wks and titrate until euthyroid;
sx can take mos to resolve; lower starting dose (0.3‚Äì0.5 g/kg/d) if at risk for
ischemic heart disease; advise Pt to keep same formulation of levothyroxine;
c dose typically needed if: pregnancy (30% c by wk 8), initiation of estrogen 
replacement, poor GI absorption (concomitant Fe or Ca suppl., PPI, sucralfate,
celiac disease, IBD)
‚Ä¢ Myxedema coma: load 5‚Äì8 g/kg T4 IV, then 50‚Äì100 g IV qd; b/c peripheral 
conversion impaired, may also give 5‚Äì10 g T3 IV q8h if unstable w/ bradycardia 
and/or hypothermia (T3 more arrhythmogenic); must give empiric adrenal replace-
ment therapy first as T adrenal reserves in myxedema coma
Subclinical hypothyroidism (NEJM 2001;345:260)
‚Ä¢ Mild c TSH and normal free T4 with only subtle or no sx
‚Ä¢ If c titers of antithyroid Abs, progression to overt hypothyroidism is 4%/y
‚Ä¢ Rx controversial: follow expectantly or treat to improve mild sx or dyslipidemia
most initiate Rx if TSH 
10 mU/L, goiter, pregnancy, or infertility
HYPERTHYROIDISM
Etiologies (Lancet 2003;362:459)
‚Ä¢ Graves‚Äô disease (60‚Äì80% of thyrotoxicosis)
‚Ä¢ Thyroiditis: thyrotoxic phase of subacute (granulomatous) thyroiditis or painless
(lymphocytic) thryoiditis
‚Ä¢ Toxic adenomas (single or multinodular goiter) 
‚Ä¢ TSH-secreting pituitary tumor or pituitary resistance to thyroid hormone (cTSH, c free T4)
‚Ä¢ Misc: amiodarone, iodine-induced, thyrotoxicosis factitia, struma ovarii (3% of ovarian
dermoid tumors and teratomas), hCG-secreting tumors (eg, choriocarcinoma),
large deposits of metastatic follicular thyroid cancer
Graves‚Äô disease (NEJM 2008;358:2594)
‚Ä¢ Female:male ratio is 5‚Äì10:1, most Pts between 40 and 60 y at dx
‚Ä¢  thyroid antibodies: TSI or TBII ( in 80%), anti-TPO, antithyroglobulin; ANA
‚Ä¢ Clinical manifestations in addition to those of hyperthyroidism (see below):
goiter: diffuse, nontender, w/ thyroid bruit
ophthalmopathy (NEJM 2009;360:994): Seen in 50%; up to 90% if formally tested.
Periorbital edema, lid retraction, proptosis, conjunctivitis, diplopia (EOM infiltration);
associated w/ smoking. Stare and lid lag seen in any type of hyperthyroidism.
pretibial myxedema (3%): infiltrative dermopathy

Clinical manifestations of hyperthyroidism
‚Ä¢ Restlessness, sweating, tremor, moist warm skin, fine hair, tachycardia, AF, weight loss,
c frequency of stools, menstrual irregularities, hyperreflexia, osteoporosis, stare and
lid lag (due sympathetic overactivity)
‚Ä¢ Apathetic thyrotoxicosis: seen in elderly who can present with lethargy as only sx
‚Ä¢ Thyroid storm (extremely rare): delirium, fever, tachycardia, systolic hypertension but
wide pulse pressure and T MAP, GI symptoms; 20‚Äì50% mortality
Laboratory testing
‚Ä¢ c FT4 and FT3; T TSH (except in TSH-secreting tumors)
‚Ä¢ RAIU scan is very useful study to differentiate causes (see table on page 7-3)
‚Ä¢ Rarely need to ‚úìfor autoantibodies except in pregnancy (to assess risk of fetal Graves‚Äô)
‚Ä¢ May see hypercalciuria  hypercalcemia, c A, anemia
Treatment
‚Ä¢ -blockers: control tachycardia (propranolol also T T4 S T3 conversion)
‚Ä¢ Graves‚Äô disease: either antithyroid drugs or radioactive iodine (NEJM 2005;352:905)
methimazole: 70% chance of recurrence after 1 y; side effects include pruritus,
rash, arthralgia, fever, N/V, and agranulocytosis in 0.5%. PTU: 2nd line (risk of
hepatocellular necrosis; TID dosing; slower effect). For both, need to ‚úìLFTs,
WBC, TSH at baseline and in follow-up.
radioactive iodine (RAI): preRx selected Pts w/ cardiovascular disease or elderly
w/ antithyroid drugs to prevent c thyrotoxicosis, stop 3 d before to allow RAI
uptake; 
75% of treated Pts become hypothyroid
surgery: less commonly chosen for Graves‚Äô, usually for Pts w/ obstructive goiter
or ophthalmopathy
‚Ä¢ Toxic adenoma or toxic multinodular goiter: RAI or surgery (methimazole preRx for
surgery, in selected patients before RAI)
‚Ä¢ Thyroid storm: -blocker, PTU, iopanoic acid or iodide (for Wolff-Chaikoff effect) 
1 h
after PTU,  steroids (T T4 S T3)
‚Ä¢ Ophthalmopathy: can worsen after RAI, prevented by prophylactic Rx w/ prednisone in
high-risk patients; can be Rx‚Äôd w/ radiation and/or surgical decompression of the orbits
Subclinical hyperthyroidism (NEJM 2001;345:512)
‚Ä¢ Mild T TSH and normal free T4 with only subtle or no sx
‚Ä¢ 15% will develop overt hyperthyroidism in 2 y; c risk of AF & osteoporosis
‚Ä¢ Rx controversial: consider if TSH 0.1 mU/L and c risk for CV disease or osteopenic
THYROIDITIS (NEJM 2003;348:2646)
‚Ä¢ Acute: bacterial infection (very rare in U.S. except postsurgical)
‚Ä¢ Subacute: transient thyrotoxicosis S transient hypothyroidism S normal thyroid fxn
painful (viral, granulomatous, or de Quervain‚Äôs): fever, c ESR; Rx 	 NSAIDs,ASA, steroids
silent (postpartum, autoimmune, or lymphocytic): painless,  TPO Abs; if

other: amiodarone, palpation thyroiditis, after radiation
‚Ä¢ Chronic: Hashimoto‚Äôs (hypothyroidism), Riedel‚Äôs (idiopathic fibrosis)
NONTHYROIDAL ILLNESS (SICK EUTHYROID SYNDROME)
‚Ä¢ TFT abnormalities in Pts w/ severe nonthyroidal illness (‚à¥in acute illness, ‚úìTFTs only
if c concern for thyroid disease); may have acquired transient central hypothyroidism
‚Ä¢ If thyroid dysfxn suspected in critically ill Pt, TSH alone not reliable; must measure total

‚Ä¢ Mild illness: T T4 S T3 conversion, c rT3 ‚áíT T3; in severe illness: T TBG & albumin, cc rT3
‚áíTT T3, c degradation of T4, central T TSH ‚áíTT T3, TT T4, T FT4, T TSH

‚Ä¢ Replacement thyroxine not helpful or recommended for critically ill Pts w/ T T3 and T4

AMIODARONE AND THYROID DISEASE
Risk of thyroid dysfunction is lower with lower doses
‚úìTSH prior to therapy, at 4-mo intervals on amio, and for 1 y after if amio d/c‚Äôd
Hypothyroidism (occurs in 10%; more common in iodine-replete areas)
‚Ä¢ Pathophysiology
(1) Wolff-Chaikoff effect: iodine load T I uptake, organification, and release of T4 & T3
(2) inhibits T4 S T3 conversion
(3) ? direct/immune-mediated thyroid destruction

‚Ä¢ Normal individuals: T T4; then escape Wolff-Chaikoff effect and have c T4, T T3, c TSH;

ADRENAL
7-7
ADRENAL DISORDERS
Cushing‚Äôs Syndrome (Hypercortisolism)
Definitions
‚Ä¢ Cushing‚Äôs syndrome  cortisol excess
‚Ä¢ Cushing‚Äôs disease  Cushing‚Äôs syndrome 2 to pituitary ACTH hypersecretion
Etiologies of hypercortisolism
‚Ä¢ Most common cause is iatrogenic Cushing‚Äôs syndrome caused by exogenous glucocorticoids
‚Ä¢ Cushing‚Äôs disease (60‚Äì70%): pituitary adenoma (usually microadenoma) or hyperplasia
‚Ä¢ Adrenal tumor (15‚Äì25%): adenoma or (rarely) carcinoma
‚Ä¢ Ectopic ACTH (5‚Äì10%): SCLC, carcinoid, islet cell tumors, medullary thyroid cancer, pheo
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Nonspecific: glucose intolerance or DM, HTN, obesity, oligomenorrhea, osteoporosis
‚Ä¢ More specific: central obesity w/ extremity wasting, dorsocervical fat pads, rounded facies
‚Ä¢ Most specific: spontaneous bruising, proximal myopathy, wide striae, hypokalemia
‚Ä¢ Other: depression, insomnia, psychosis, impaired cognition, facial plethora, acne,
hirsutism, hyperpigmentation (if c ACTH), fungal skin infxns, nephrolithiasis, polyuria
Figure 7-3 Approach to suspected Cushing‚Äôs syndrome
CRH, corticotropin-releasing hormone; DST, dexamethasone suppression test; UFC, urinary free cortisol
Overnight 1 mg DST  give 1 mg at 11 pm; ‚úì8 am serum cortisol (suppression if 1.8 g/dL); 1‚Äì2% false 
(primarily used to evaluate subclinical Cushing‚Äôs in adrenal ‚Äúincidentalomas‚Äù) (JCEM 2008;93:1526)
11 pm salivary cortisol  abnl if level c; 24-h UFC  abnl if level c,  4  ULN virtually diagnostic
48-h LD DST  CRH  0.5 mg q6h  2 d,then IV CRH 2 h later; ‚úìserum cortisol 15 min later (  1.4 g/dL)
48-h LD DST  0.5 mg q6h  2 d; ‚úì24-h UFC at base. & during last 24 h of dex (suppress if 10% of base)
48-h HD DST  2 mg q6h  2 d; ‚úì24-h UFC as per LD DST
O/N HD DST  8 mg at 11 pm; ‚úì9 am serum cortisol (suppression if 32% of baseline)

Treatment of Cushing‚Äôs syndrome
‚Ä¢ Surgical resection of pituitary adenoma, adrenal tumor, or ectopic ACTH-secreting tumor
‚Ä¢ If transsphenoidal surgery (TSS) not successful S pituitary XRT, medical adrenalectomy
w/ mitotane, or bilat surgical adrenalectomy; ketoconazole ( metyrapone) to T cortisol
‚Ä¢ Glucocorticoid replacement therapy  6‚Äì36 mos after TSS (lifelong glucocorticoid 
mineralocorticoid replacement if medical or surgical adrenalectomy)
HYPERALDOSTERONISM
Etiologies
‚Ä¢ Primary (adrenal disorders, renin independent increase in aldosterone)
adrenal hyperplasia (70%), adenoma (Conn‚Äôs syndrome, 25%), carcinoma (5%)
glucocorticoid-remediable aldosteronism (GRA; ACTH-dep. rearranged promoter)
‚Ä¢ Secondary (extra-adrenal disorders, c aldosterone is renin dependent)
Primary reninism: renin-secreting tumor (rare)
Secondary reninism
renovascular disease: RAS, malignant hypertension
edematous states w/ T effective arterial volume: CHF, cirrhosis, nephrotic syndrome
hypovolemia, diuretics,T2D, Bartter‚Äôs (defective Na/K/2Cl transporter  receiving loop
diuretic), Gitelman‚Äôs (defective renal Na/Cl transporter  receiving thiazide diuretic)
‚Ä¢ Nonaldosterone mineralocorticoid excess mimics hyperaldosteronism
11-HSD deficiency (S lack of inactivation of cortisol, which binds to otherwise
nonselective mineralocorticoid receptor)
Black licorice (glycyrrhizinic acid inhibits 11-HSD), extreme hypercortisolism
(overwhelming 11-HSD), exogenous mineralocorticoids
Liddle‚Äôs syndrome (constitutively activated/overexpressed distal tubular renal Na channel)
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Mild to moderate HTN (11% of Pts w/ HTN refractory to 3 drugs; Lancet
2008;371:1921), headache, muscle weakness, polyuria, polydipsia; no peripheral edema
because of ‚Äúescape‚Äù from Na retention; malignant HTN is rare
‚Ä¢ Classically hypokalemia (but often normal), metabolic alkalosis, mild hypernatremia
Diagnostic studies
‚Ä¢ 5‚Äì10% of Pts w/ HTN; ‚à¥screen if HTN  hypokalemia, adrenal mass, or refractory HTN
‚Ä¢ Screening: aldosterone (

PAC/PRA ratio; ‚à¥avoid. -blockers generally best to control HTN during dx testing.

ADRENAL INSUFFICIENCY
Etiologies
‚Ä¢ Primary 	 adrenocortical disease 	 Addison‚Äôs disease
autoimmune: isolated or in assoc w/ PGA syndromes (see table on page 7-2)
infection: TB, CMV, histoplasmosis
vascular: hemorrhage (usually in setting of sepsis), thrombosis, and trauma
metastatic disease: (90% of adrenals must be destroyed to cause insufficiency)
deposition diseases: hemochromatosis, amyloidosis, sarcoidosis
drugs: ketoconazole, etomidate (even after single dose), rifampin, anticonvulsants
‚Ä¢ Secondary 	 pituitary failure of ACTH secretion (but aldosterone intact b/c RAA axis)
any cause of primary or secondary hypopituitarism (see ‚ÄúPituitary Disorders‚Äù)
glucocorticoid therapy (can occur after 2 wks of ‚Äúsuppressive doses‚Äù; dose effect
variable; 10 mg prednisone daily chronically can be suppressive) 
megestrol (a progestin with some glucocorticoid activity)
Clinical manifestations (NEJM 1996;335:1206)
‚Ä¢ Primary or secondary: weakness and fatigability (99%), anorexia (99%),
orthostatic hypotension (90%), nausea (86%), vomiting (75%), hyponatremia (88%)
‚Ä¢ Primary only (extra s/s due to lack of aldosterone and c ACTH): marked orthostatic
hypotension (because volume-depleted), hyperpigmentation (seen in creases, mucous
membranes, pressure areas, nipples), hyperkalemia
‚Ä¢ Secondary only:  other manifestations of hypopituitarism (see ‚ÄúPituitary Disorders‚Äù)
Diagnostic studies
‚Ä¢ Early a.m. serum cortisol: 3 g/dL virtually diagnostic; 18 g/dL rules it out
(except in severe septic shock‚Äîsee below)
‚Ä¢ Standard (250 g) cosyntropin stimulation test (testing ability of ACTH S c cortisol)
normal 	 60-min post-ACTH cortisol 18 g/dL
abnormal in primary b/c adrenal gland diseased and unable to give adequate output
abnormal in chronic secondary b/c adrenals atrophied and unable to respond
(very rarely, may be normal in acute secondary b/c adrenals still able to respond;
early a.m. cortisol can be used rather than post-stim value in these cases)
‚Ä¢ Low-dose (1 g) cort stim: ? more Se than high-dose test (controversial)
‚Ä¢ Other tests to evaluate HPA axis (w/ guidance by endocrinologist): insulin-induced
hypoglycemia (measure serum cortisol response); metyrapone (blocks cortisol synthesis
and therefore stimulates ACTH, measure plasma 11-deoxycortisol and urinary 
17-hydroxycorticosteroid levels)
‚Ä¢ Other lab abnormalities: hypoglycemia, eosinophilia, lymphocytosis,  neutropenia
‚Ä¢ ACTH: c in 1, T or low-normal in 2
‚Ä¢ Imaging studies to consider
pituitary MRI to detect anatomical abnormalities
adrenal CT: small, noncalcified adrenals in autoimmune, enlarged in metastatic disease,
hemorrhage, infection, or deposition (although they may be normal-appearing)
Adrenal insufficiency & critical illness (NEJM 2003;348:727; JAMA 2009;301:2362)
‚Ä¢ Perform ACTH stim ASAP in hypotensive Pt suspected to have absolute adrenal insuffic. 
‚Ä¢ Initiate corticosteroids early: use dexamethasone 2‚Äì4 mg IV q6h  fludrocortisone 50 g
daily prior to ACTH stim; change to hydrocortisone 50‚Äì100 mg IV q6‚Äì8h once
test completed.
‚Ä¢ Rx of relative adrenal insufficiency controversial (see ‚ÄúSepsis‚Äù).
Treatment
‚Ä¢ Acute insufficiency: volume resuscitation w/ normal saline  hydrocortisone IV as above 
‚Ä¢ Chronic insufficiency
Hydrocortisone: 20‚Äì30 mg PO qd (2‚ÅÑ3 a.m. 1‚ÅÑ3 early p.m.) or prednisone 5 mg PO qam
Fludrocortisone (not needed in 2 adrenal insufficiency): 0.05‚Äì0.1 mg PO qam
back-up dexamethasone 4 mg IM prefilled syringe given to Pt for emergency situations

PHEOCHROMOCYTOMA
Clinical manifestations (five Ps)
‚Ä¢ Pressure (hypertension, paroxysmal in 50%, severe and resistant to therapy)
‚Ä¢ Pain (headache, chest pain)
‚Ä¢ Palpitations (tachycardia, tremor, wt loss, fever)
‚Ä¢ Perspiration (profuse)
‚Ä¢ Pallor (vasoconstrictive spell)
‚Ä¢ ‚ÄúRule of 10‚Äù: 10% extra-adrenal (known as paraganglioma), 10% in children,
10% multiple or bilateral, 10% recur (c in paraganglioma), 10% malignant (c in
paraganglioma), 10% familial, 10% incidentaloma
‚Ä¢ Emotional stress does not trigger paroxysms, but abdominal manipulation can trigger
catecholamine release; some reports of IV contrast causing paroxysms
‚Ä¢ Associated with MEN 2A/2B, Von Hippel Lindau, neurofibromatosis type 1, familial
paraganglioma (mutations in succinate dehydrogenase gene, B, C and D)
Diagnostic studies
‚Ä¢ 24 urinary fractionated metanephrines & catechols: 90% Se, 98% Sp ( JCEM 2003;88:553).
Screening test of choice if low-risk (as false  with severe illness, renal failure, OSA,
labetalol due to assay interference, TCAs, medications containing sympathomimetics)
‚Ä¢ Plasma free metanephrines: 99% Se, 89% Sp (JAMA 2002;287:1427). Screening test of choice
if high-risk, but c rate of false  in low-preval. population.
‚Ä¢ Adrenal CT or MRI; consider MIBG scintigraphy if CT/MRI , PET can be used to
localize nonadrenal mass, but usually easy to find
‚Ä¢ Consider genetic testing in appropriate circumstances (bilateral, young Pt,  FHx,
extra-adrenal)
Treatment
‚Ä¢ -blockade first (usually phenoxybenzamine)  -blockade (often propranolol) S surgery
ADRENAL INCIDENTALOMAS
Epidemiology
‚Ä¢ 4% of Pts undergoing abdominal CT scan have incidentally discovered adrenal mass;
prevalence c with age
Differential diagnosis
‚Ä¢ Nonfunctioning mass: adenoma, cysts, abscesses, granuloma, hemorrhage, lipoma,
myelolipoma, primary or metastatic malignancy
‚Ä¢ Functioning mass: pheochromocytoma, adenoma (cortisol, aldosterone, sex hormones),
nonclassical CAH, other endocrine tumor, carcinoma
‚Ä¢ Nonadrenal mass: renal, pancreatic, gastric, artifact
Workup (NEJM 2007;356:601)
‚Ä¢ Rule out subclinical Cushing‚Äôs syndrome in all Pts using 1 mg overnight DST 
(Sp 91%). Abnormal results require confirmatory testing.
‚Ä¢ Rule out hyperaldosteronism if hypertensive w/ plasma aldo & renin (see above).
‚Ä¢ Rule out pheochromocytoma in all Pts (b/c of morbidity unRx‚Äôd pheo) using 24-h
urine fractionated metanephrines and catecholamines or plasma free metanephrines.
‚Ä¢ Rule out metastatic cancer and infection by history or CT-guided biopsy if suspicious
(in Pts w/ h/o cancer, 50% of adrenal incidentalomas are malignant)
‚Ä¢ CT and MRI characteristics may suggest adenoma vs. carcinoma
Benign features: size 4 cm; smooth margins, homogenous and hypodense appearance;
unenhanced CT 10 Hounsfield units or CT contrast-medium washout 
50% at
10 min. Can follow such incidentalomas w/ periodic scans.
Suspicious features: size 
4 cm or c size on repeat scan; irregular margins,
heterogeneous, dense, or vascular appearance; h/o malignancy or young age
(incidentaloma less common). Such incidentalomas warrant FNA biopsy, repeat
scan in 3 mos, or resection.

Pitfalls in measuring calcium
‚Ä¢ Physiologically active Ca is free or ionized (ICa). Serum Ca reflects total calcium
(bound  unbound) and ‚à¥influenced by albumin (main Ca-binding protein)
‚Ä¢ Corrected Ca (mg/dL) 	 measured Ca (mg/dL)  {0.8  [4 - albumin (gm/dL)]}
‚Ä¢ Alkalosis will cause more Ca to be bound to albumin (‚à¥total Ca may be normal but T ICa)
‚Ä¢ Best to measure ionized Ca directly

DIABETES

DIABETES MELLITUS
Definition (Diabetes Care 2003;26:S33 & 2009;32:1327)
‚Ä¢ Fasting glc 126 mg/dL  2; random glc 200 mg/dL  2 or  1 if severe 
hyperglycemia and acute metabolic decompensation; or 75 g OGTT w/ 2-h glc
200 mg/dL (routine OGTT not recommended)
‚Ä¢ Blood glc higher than normal, but not frank DM (‚Äúprediabetics,‚Äù 40% U.S. population)
Impaired fasting glc (IFG): 100‚Äì125 mg/dL
Impaired glc tolerance (IGT): 140‚Äì199 mg/dL 2 h after 75 g OGTT
Preventing progression to DM: diet & exercise (58% T), metformin (31% T), TZD (60% T)
‚Ä¢ c HbA1C (no accepted criterion yet, 6.5% recommended by intl expert cmte)
Categories
‚Ä¢ Type 1: islet cell destruction; absolute insulin deficiency; ketosis in absence of insulin
prevalence 0.4%; usual onset in childhood but can occur throughout adulthood; c risk
if  FHx; HLA associations; anti-GAD, anti-islet cell & anti-insulin autoantibodies 
‚Ä¢ Type 2: insulin resistance  relative insulin deficiency
prevalence 8%; onset generally later in life; cc risk if  FHx; no HLA associations
risk factors: age,  FHx, obesity, sedentary lifestyle
‚Ä¢ Type 2 DM p/w DKA (‚Äúketosis-prone type 2 diabetes‚Äù): most often seen in nonwhite,
 anti-GAD Ab, eventually may not require insulin (Endo Rev 2008;29:292)
‚Ä¢ Mature-Onset Diabetes of the Young (MODY): autosomal dom. forms of DM due to
defects in insulin secretion genes; genetically and clinically heterogeneous (NEJM 2001;345:971)
‚Ä¢ Secondary causes of diabetes: exogenous glucocorticoids, glucagonoma (3 Ds 	
DM, DVT, diarrhea), pancreatic (pancreatitis, hemochromatosis, CF, resection),
endocrinopathies (Cushing‚Äôs disease, acromegaly), gestational, drugs (protease
inhibitors, atypical antipsychotics)
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Polyuria, polydipsia, polyphagia with unexplained weight loss; can also be asymptomatic

Type 1: carb counting;Type 2: wt reduction diet  exercise

T hepatic gluconeogenesis, T HbA1C 1.5%

Complications
‚Ä¢ Retinopathy
non-proliferative: ‚Äúdot & blot‚Äù and retinal hemorrhages, cotton-wool/protein exudates
proliferative: neovascularization, vitreous hemorrhage, retinal detachment, blindness
treatment: photocoagulation, surgery
‚Ä¢ Nephropathy: microalbuminuria S proteinuria 	 nephrotic syndrome S renal failure
diffuse glomerular basement membrane thickening/nodular pattern (Kimmelstiel-Wilson)
usually accompanied by retinopathy; lack of retinopathy suggests another cause
treatment: strict BP control using ACE inhibitors (NEJM 1993;329:1456 & 35:1941; Lancet
1997;349:1787) or ARBs (NEJM 2001;345:851, 861), low-protein diet, dialysis, or transplant
‚Ä¢ Neuropathy
symmetric peripheral: symmetric distal sensory loss, paresthesias, 	 motor loss
autonomic: gastroparesis, constipation, neurogenic bladder, erectile dysfxn, orthostasis
mononeuropathy: sudden-onset peripheral or CN deficit (footdrop, CN III VI  IV)
‚Ä¢ Accelerated atherosclerosis: coronary, cerebral, and peripheral arterial beds
‚Ä¢ Infections: UTI, osteomyelitis of foot, candidiasis, mucormycosis, necrotizing external otitis
‚Ä¢ Dermatologic: necrobiosis lipoidica diabeticorum, lipodystrophy, acanthosis nigricans
Outpatient screening and treatment goals (Diabetes Care 2009;32:193 & S1:S13)
‚Ä¢ ‚úìHbA1C q3‚Äì6mo, goal 7% for most Pts (NEJM 2008;358:2545, 2560); microvascular &
macrovascular complications T by strict glycemic control in T1D (NEJM 1993;329:997 &
2005;353:2643) & T2D (Lancet 1998;352:837; NEJM 2008;359:1577; Lancet 2009;373:1765; Annals 2009;151:394)
‚Ä¢ Microalbuminuria screening yearly with spot microalbumin/Cr ratio, goal 30 mg/g
‚Ä¢ BP 130/80; LDL 100,TG 150, HDL 40; benefit of statins even w/o overt CAD
(Lancet 2003;361:2005 & 2004;364:685);ASA if age 50 () or 60 () or other cardiac risk

‚Ä¢ Dilated retinal exam yearly; comprehensive foot exam qy (Diabetes Care 2009;32:51, 513)
Management of hyperglycemia in inpatients 
‚Ä¢ Identify reversible causes/exacerbaters (dextrose IVF, glucocorticoids, postop, c carb diet)
‚Ä¢ Dx studies: BG fingersticks (fasting, qAC, qHS; or Q6h if NPO), HbA1C
‚Ä¢ Treatment goals: avoid hypoglycemia, extreme hyperglycemia (180 mg/dL)
‚Ä¢ Modification of outPt treatment regimen: In T1D, do not stop basal insulin (can cause DKA).
In T2D: stopping oral DM meds generally preferred to avoid hypoglycemia or med
interaction (except if short stay, excellent outPt cntl, no plan for IV contrast, nl diet) 
‚Ä¢ InPt insulin: can use outPt regimen as guide; if insulin na√Øve:

DIABETIC KETOACIDOSIS (DKA)
Precipitants (the Is)
‚Ä¢ Insulin defic. (ie, failure to take enough insulin); Iatrogenesis (glucocorticoids)
‚Ä¢ Infection (pneumonia, UTI) or Inflammation (pancreatitis, cholecystitis)
‚Ä¢ Ischemia or Infarction (myocardial, cerebral, gut); Intoxication (alcohol, drugs)
Pathophysiology
‚Ä¢ Occurs in T1D (and in ketosis-prone T2D); c glucagon and T insulin
‚Ä¢ Hyperglycemia due to: c gluconeogenesis, c glycogenolysis, T glucose uptake into cells
‚Ä¢ Ketosis due to: insulin deficiency S mobilization and oxidation of fatty acids,
c substrate for ketogenesis, c ketogenic state of the liver, T ketone clearance
Clinical manifestations (Diabetes Care 2003;26:S109)
‚Ä¢ Polyuria, polydipsia, & dehydration S c HR, HoTN, dry mucous membranes, T skin turgor 
‚Ä¢ Nausea, vomiting, abdominal pain (either due to intra-abdominal process or DKA), ileus
‚Ä¢ Kussmaul‚Äôs respirations (deep) to compensate for metabolic acidosis with odor of acetone
‚Ä¢  MS S somnolence, stupor, coma; mortality 1% even at tertiary care centers
Diagnostic studies
‚Ä¢ c anion gap metabolic acidosis: can later develop nonanion gap acidosis due to
urinary loss of ketones (HCO3 equivalents) and fluid resuscitation with chloride
‚Ä¢ Ketosis:  urine and serum ketones (acetoacetate measured by nitroprusside, but
predominant ketone is -OH-butyrate; urine ketones may be  in fasting normal Pts)
‚Ä¢ c serum glc; c BUN & Cr (dehydration 	 artifact due to ketones interfering w/ some assays)
‚Ä¢ Pseudohyponatremia: corrected Na  measured Na  [2.4  (measured glc ‚Äì100)/100]
‚Ä¢ T or c K (but even if serum K is elevated, usually total body K depleted); T total body phos
‚Ä¢ Leukocytosis, c amylase (even if no pancreatitis)

HYPOGLYCEMIA
Etiologies in diabetics
‚Ä¢ Excess insulin, oral hypoglycemics, missed meals, renal failure (T insulin & SU clearance)
‚Ä¢ -blockers can mask symptoms of hypoglycemia
Etiologies in nondiabetics
‚Ä¢ c insulin: exogenous insulin, sulfonylureas, insulinoma, anti‚Äìinsulin antibodies
‚Ä¢ T glucose production: hypopituitarism, adrenal insufficiency, glucagon deficiency,
hepatic failure, renal failure, CHF, alcoholism, sepsis
‚Ä¢ c IGF-II: non‚Äìislet tumor
‚Ä¢ Postprandial, esp. postgastrectomy or gastric bypass: excessive response to glc load
‚Ä¢ Low glc w/o sx can be normal
Clinical manifestations (glucose 55 mg/dL)
‚Ä¢ CNS: headache, visual s,  MS, weakness, seizure, LOC (neuroglycopenic sx)
‚Ä¢ Autonomic: diaphoresis, palpitations, tremor (adrenergic sx)
Evaluation in nondiabetics (J Clin Endocrinol Metab 2009;94:709)
‚Ä¢ If clinically ill: take measures to avoid recurrent hypoglycemia; ‚úìBUN, Cr, LFTs,TFTs;
IGF-I/IGF-II ratio when appropriate
‚Ä¢ If otherwise healthy: 72-h fast w/ monitored blood glc; stop for neuroglycopenic sx
‚Ä¢ At time of hypoglycemia: insulin, C peptide (c w/ insulinoma and sulfonylureas, T w/
exogenous insulin), -OH-butyrate, sulfonylurea levels
‚Ä¢ At end of fast, give 1 mg glucagon IV and measure response of plasma glc before feeding
Treatment
‚Ä¢ Glucose tablets, paste, fruit juice are first-line Rx for Pts who can take POs
‚Ä¢ If IV access available, give 25‚Äì50 g of D50 (50% dextrose)
‚Ä¢ If no IV, can give glucagon 0.5‚Äì1 mg IM or SC (side effect: N/V)

ARTHRITIS
8-1
ARTHRITIS‚ÄîOVERVIEW
Approach to patient with joint pain
‚Ä¢ Hx: distinguish joint vs. soft tissue pain, inflammatory vs. noninflammatory pain
Features suggestive of inflammatory pain: swelling in specific joint (w/o h/o trauma),
persistence over days to weeks, morning stiffness, improvement of pain and stiffness
w/ motion & exercise, improvement w/ NSAIDs or steroids
‚Ä¢ Physical exam (see later): localize complaint and determine if there are objective
signs of inflammation (arthritis, bursitis, tendinitis) or noninflammatory pain
(arthralgia, myofascial pain)
‚Ä¢ Osteoarthritis can p/w bony enlargement or crepitus, w/ or w/o noninflammatory
effusion

RA
8-3
RHEUMATOID ARTHRITIS (RA)
Definition and epidemiology
‚Ä¢ Chronic, symmetric, debilitating, and destructive polyarthritis caused by inflammatory,
proliferative synovial tissue (pannus) formation in affected joints
‚Ä¢ Genetic factors: c incidence in Pts w/ shared epitope on class II MHC DRB1 and DR4
‚Ä¢ Environmental factors: smoking, silica dust exposure
‚Ä¢ c risk w/ shared epitope & smoke b/c gene‚Äìenvironment interaction (Ann Rheum Dis 2010;69:70)
‚Ä¢ Prevalence  1% of adults; female:male  3:1; onset 35‚Äì50 y; worldwide
Clinical manifestations (Lancet 2001;358:903)
‚Ä¢ Pain, swelling, and impaired function of joints (typically PIPs, MCPs, wrists, knees,
ankles, MTPs, and cervical spine) with morning stiffness for 1 h
‚Ä¢ Polyarticular in 75% (60% small joints, 30% large joints, 10% both), monoarticular in
25% (knee, shoulder, wrist)
‚Ä¢ Joint immobilization, muscle shortening, bone & cartilage destruction, joint deformities:
ulnar deviation, swan neck (MCP flexion, PIP hyperextension, DIP flexion),
boutonni√®re (PIP flexion, DIP hyperextension), cock-up deformities (toes)
‚Ä¢ C1‚ÄìC2 instability S myelopathy, ‚à¥‚úìC-spine flex/ext films prior to elective intubation
‚Ä¢ Rheumatoid nodules (20‚Äì30%; usually in sero- Pts): SC nodules on extensor 
surfaces along tendon sheaths and in bursae; also occur in lung, heart, and sclera
‚Ä¢ Constitutional symptoms: fever, weight loss, malaise
‚Ä¢ Ocular: scleritis, episcleritis, keratoconjunctivitis sicca (associated Sj√∂gren‚Äôs)
‚Ä¢ Pulmonary (20% of the time precedes joint manifestations)
ILD: COP, fibrosis, nodules, Caplan‚Äôs syndrome (pneumoconiosis  rheumatoid nodules)
pleural disease: pleuritis, pleural effusions (classically low glucose)
pulmonary hypertension
airway disease: obstruction (cricoarytenoid arthritis), bronchiolitis, bronchiectasis
‚Ä¢ Cardiac: pericarditis (effusion in 13 of sero-), myocarditis, nodules can cause valvular
and/or conduction disease. Associated with c risk of cardiovascular death com-
pared with that of general population (Rheum 2009;48:1309).
‚Ä¢ Heme: anemia of chronic inflammation, leukemia, lymphoma
‚Ä¢ Vascular: small nail fold infarcts, palpable purpura, leukocytoclastic vasculitis
‚Ä¢ Renal: glomerulonephritis (membranous, mesganigal, MPGN); nephrotic syndrome
due to AA amyloidosis; Rx-induced, including NSAIDs (AIN, membranous GN),
gold, MTX 
‚Ä¢ Longstanding seropositive, erosive RA:
Felty‚Äôs syndrome (1%): neutropenia, RF , splenomegaly; c risk of NHL
large granular lymphocyte syndrome: neutropenia, lymphocytosis blood/marrow
‚Ä¢ Remember that rheumatoid joints can become superinfected
Laboratory and radiologic studies
‚Ä¢ RF (IgM anti-IgG Ab) in 85% of Pts; nonspecific as also seen in other rheumatic 
diseases (SLE, Sj√∂gren‚Äôs), chronic inflammation (SBE, hepatitis,TB), type II cryoglob-
ulinemia, 5% of healthy population
‚Ä¢ ACPA (anti‚Äìcitrullinated peptide Ab) or anti-CCP (Ab to cyclic citrullinated peptide): simi-
lar Se (80%), more Sp (90%) than RF particularly for early RA (Arth Rheum 2009;61:1472)
‚Ä¢ c ESR and CRP;  ANA in 15%; c globulin during periods of active disease; anemia
‚Ä¢ Radiographs of hands and wrists: periarticular osteopenia, bone erosions, and deformities
ACR/EULAR Classification Criteria (Arth Rheum 2010; in press)
‚Ä¢ Use for Pts with 1 joint with synovitis not better explained by another disease

Management (Lancet 2009;373:659)
‚Ä¢ Early dx and Rx w/ frequent follow-up and escalation of Rx as needed S T disease
activity and radiographic progression, c physical fxn and quality of life 
‚Ä¢ Initial therapy:nonselective NSAIDs (? c CV adverse events) or COX-2 inhibitors (c CV
adverse events w/ some): sx control as indicated; glucocorticoids (joint injection
or low-dose oral): acutely T inflammation; physical and occupational therapy
‚Ä¢ Start Disease-Modifying Anti-Rheumatic Drug (DMARD) w/in 3 mo if established
disease and ongoing inflammation (Annals 2007;146:406); all take 1 mo to have an effect

RELAPSING POLYCHONDRITIS
Definition & Epidemiology
‚Ä¢ Inflammatory destruction of cartilaginous structures
‚Ä¢ Defined by chondritis in 2 of the following sites: auricular, nasal, or laryngotracheal;
or chondritis in 1 of these sites  2 other manifestations (below) (Annals 1986;104:74)
‚Ä¢ 40% of cases a/w immunologic disorder (eg, RA, SLE, vasc., Sj√∂gren‚Äôs), cancer, or MDS  
‚Ä¢ Mean age at diagnosis is 47 y, men  women
Clinical manifestations (Curr Opin Rheumatol 2004;16:56)
‚Ä¢ Subacute onset of red, painful, and swollen cartilage; ultimately atrophic & deformed
‚Ä¢ Relapsing-remitting pattern
‚Ä¢ Frequency of involved cartilage: external ear (89%), migratory asymmetric nonerosive
arthropathy (72%), episcleritis/scleritis (59%), laryngotracheal sx (55%), inner ear
(28%), nasal cartilage, saddle deformity (25%), skin (25%), laryngotracheal stricture
(23%), kidney (22%), heart valves (12%), AI  MR
‚Ä¢ Ddx: Infxn (eg, Pseudomonal external otitis),Wegener‚Äôs, IBD chondritis, trauma
Diagnosis
‚Ä¢ Clinical diagnosis based on exam with multiple sites of cartilaginous inflammation
‚Ä¢ Labs: c ESR & CRP, leukocytosis, eosinophilia, anemia of chronic inflammation
‚Ä¢ Bx (not req for dx): proteoglycan depletion, perichondrial inflammation and replacement
with granulation tissue and fibrosis; immunofluorescence with Ig and C3 deposits
Evaluation and Treatment
‚Ä¢ Screen for pulm (PFTs, CXR/CT,  bronch) and cardiac (ECG,TTE) involvement
‚Ä¢ Therapy guided by disease activity and severity: steroids first line; NSAIDs, dapsone
for sx control of arthralgias and mild disease; MTX or AZA for steroid-sparing;
cyclophosphamide for organ-threatening disease

8-4

GOUT
8-5
CRYSTAL DEPOSITION ARTHRITIDES
GOUT
Definition & Pathobiology (Lancet 2010;375:318)
‚Ä¢ Monosodium urate (MSU) crystal deposition in joints and other tissues  
‚Ä¢ Activate cryopyrin inflammasome S IL-1	 S inflammation (Nature 2006;440:237)
Epidemiology
‚Ä¢ More common in men than in women (9:1); peak incidence 5th decade
‚Ä¢ Most common cause of inflammatory arthritis in men over 30 y
‚Ä¢ Rare in premenopausal women (estrogens promote renal urate excretion)
‚Ä¢ Risk factors: c serum uric acid (UA) related to metabolic syndrome; HTN; chronic kid-
ney disease; c intake of meat, seafood, and EtOH (Lancet 2004;363:1277; NEJM 2004;350:1093) 
Etiologies
‚Ä¢ Uric acid (UA) is end product of purine catabolism and is renally excreted

Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Acute arthritis: sudden onset (freq. nocturnal) of painful monoarticular arthritis
location: MTP of great toe (‚Äúpodagra‚Äù), feet, ankles, knees; occasionally polyarticular
overlying skin is warm, tense, dusky red; Pt may be febrile
precipitants: rapid

‚Ä¢ Tophi: deposits of urate crystals in SC tissue & joints; commonly in joints of fingers, wrists,
knees; also on pinna,Achilles tendon, and pressure areas, eg, ulnar aspect of forearm
‚Ä¢ Bursitis: olecranon, patellar (must be differentiated from intra-articular effusion)
‚Ä¢ Chronic tophaceous gout: deforming arthritis from tophus formation S pain, joint erosion
‚Ä¢ Renal: uric acid stones; urate nephropathy (interstitial deposits)
‚Ä¢ Asymptomatic hyperuricemia: serum UA 6.8 mg/dL w/o disease manifestations
Diagnostic studies

Chronic treatment
‚Ä¢
T urate production by T intake of meat and seafood (note: high-purine vegetables a/w 
c risk); c intake low-fat dairy products; T alcohol (esp. beer); wt control
‚Ä¢ Avoid dehydration and hyperuricemia-promoting drugs (eg, thiazide and loop diuretics)
‚Ä¢ Prophylaxis if frequent attacks and when starting antihyperuricemic therapy:
daily low-dose colchicine (50% T risk of acute flare; J Rheum 2004;31:2429) 
or NSAIDs (less evidence of effectiveness; Ann Rheum Dis 2006;65:1312)
‚Ä¢ Antihyperuricemic therapy for tophi, frequent attacks, nephrolithiasis; goal UA 6 mg/dL
however, do not start w/o prophylaxis and until 2‚Äì4 wk after acute attack as 
 T in serum
UA concentration can precipitate an attack
allopurinol (xanthine oxidase inhibitor); side effects: hypersensitivity, rash, diarrhea,
dyspepsia, headache, renal failure, BM suppression, and hepatitis; monitor CBC &
LFTs; dose adjustment necessary in Pts concurrently taking AZA
febuxostat (nonpurine xanthine oxidase inhibitor): consider if allopurinol intolerance/
failure or CKD; side effects: liver abnl, rash, arthralgias, nausea; monitor LFTs
(Arth Rheum 2008;59:1540); dose adjustment also necessary for AZA
probenecid (uricosuric) for underexcreters (urine UA 600 mg/24 h)
CALCIUM PYROPHOSPHATE DIHYDRATE (CPPD)
DEPOSITION DISEASE
Definition
‚Ä¢ Deposition of CPPD crystals w/in tendons, ligaments, articular capsules, synovium,
cartilage
‚Ä¢ Acute inflammation due to CPPD crystals in a joint is termed pseudogout
‚Ä¢ Chondrocalcinosis: calcification of cartilage visible on radiographs, resulting from
CPPD deposition in articular cartilage, fibrocartilage, or menisci
Epidemiology
‚Ä¢ More common in elderly: 20% over age 60 y have knee chondrocalcinosis in autopsy studies
Etiologies
‚Ä¢ Most cases idiopathic, but consider search for underlying cause, especially in the young
‚Ä¢ Metabolic: the 3 Hs: hemochromatosis, hypothyroidism, hyperparathyroidism;
diabetes, hypomagnesemia, alkaline phosphatase deficiency, familial hypocalciuric
hypercalcemia, gout, Gitelman‚Äôs syndrome, X-linked hypophosphatemic rickets
‚Ä¢ Joint trauma (incl. previous surgery); joint injections with hyaluronate can precipitate attacks
‚Ä¢ Familial chondrocalcinosis (autosomal dominant disorder)
Pathogenesis
‚Ä¢ c synovial & joint fluid levels of inorganic pyrophosphate produced by articular chon-
drocytes from ATP hydrolysis in response to various insults or inherited defects
favors CPPD crystallogenesis and deposition in the cartilage matrix
‚Ä¢ Crystals activate cryopyrin inflammasome S IL-1	 S inflammation (Nature 2006;440:237) 
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Pseudogout: acute mono- or asymmetric oligoarticular arthritis, indistinguishable from
gout except through synovial fluid exam for crystals
location: knees, wrists, and MCP joints
precipitants: surgery, trauma, or severe illness
‚Ä¢ ‚ÄúPseudo-RA‚Äù: chronic polyarticular arthritis with morning stiffness;  RF
‚Ä¢ Premature OA: destruction of articular cartilage and bony overgrowths S degen. of joints
Diagnostic studies
‚Ä¢ Arthrocentesis
take care not to tap through an infected area thus introducing infxn into joint space
polarized microscopy S rhomboid-shaped, weakly positively birefringent crystals
(yellow perpendicular and blue parallel to axis marked on polarizer)
WBC 2000‚Äì100,000/mm3, 50% polys
infection can coexist with acute attacks, ‚à¥always check Gram stain and culture
‚Ä¢ Screen for associated metabolic diseases when dx a new case: ‚úìCa, Mg,TSH, Fe, glc, UA
‚Ä¢ Radiographs: though not a prerequisite for the diagnosis of CPPD disease,
chondrocalcinosis appears as punctate and linear densities in articular cartilage, menisci,
triangular fibrocartilage of the wrist, small joints of fingers, and symphysis pubis
Treatment
‚Ä¢ Acute therapy for pseudogout: same as for gout, though colchicine not as effective
‚Ä¢ Chronic therapy: treat predisposing disease
‚Ä¢ Low-dose daily colchicine may be effective prophylaxis in some Pts

SERONEG
8-7
SERONEGATIVE SPONDYLOARTHRITIS
GENERAL
Definition (Annals 2002;136:896)
‚Ä¢ A spectrum of systemic inflammatory arthritides w/ predilection for the spine,
entheses, sacroiliac, and peripheral joints; affects 0.5‚Äì2% of population
‚Ä¢ 5 subtypes: ankylosing spondylitis, reactive, psoriatic, IBD-associated, and
undifferentiated (does not meet criteria for other subtypes, wide clinical spectrum)
‚Ä¢ Notable for absence of rheumatoid factor or autoantibodies;  c ESR
‚Ä¢ Synovial fluid of affected joints shows an inflammatory, nonseptic picture
Pathogenesis (Semin Arthritis Rheum 2008;38:83)
‚Ä¢ c prevalence of HLA-B27:  in 50‚Äì90% of Pts, but common in general pop. (6‚Äì8%) 
‚Ä¢ HLA-B27 accounts for 30% of attributable genetic risk, but not used for dx
‚Ä¢ Other gene associations: IL23R (26% of attributable risk) and ARTS1 (9%)
‚Ä¢ Environmental factors likely critical for disease, esp. reactive arthritis (eg, infection)
ANKYLOSING SPONDYLITIS
Epidemiology
‚Ä¢ Onset in teens or mid-20s; onset after age 40 y very unusual; male:female ratio 
3:1; HLA-B27  in 90%
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Gradual onset of chronic, intermittent bouts of lower back pain and stiffness
‚Ä¢ Morning stiffness that improves with hot shower and exercise
‚Ä¢ Progressive limitation of motion in cervical, thoracic, and/or lumbar spine over time
Severity of lumbar flexion deformity assessed by  modified Wright-Schober
test (4 cm c in distance between a point at lumbosacral jxn and another point
10 cm above, when going from standing to maximum forward flexion)
T-spine mobility (extension) and kyphosis severity measured by occiput-to-wall distance 
‚Ä¢ Enthesitis: inflammation at site of tendon/ligament insertion into bone, eg,Achilles
tendinitis, plantar fasciitis, rigidity of spine (bamboo spine by x-ray) 
‚Ä¢ Arthritis in peripheral joints can occur, eg, hips, shoulders, knees
‚Ä¢ Acute anterior uveitis (25‚Äì40% at some time during disease): presents with unilateral 
blurred vision, lacrimation, and photophobia
‚Ä¢ Cardiovascular disease (5%): ascending aortitis,AI, conduction system abnormalities
‚Ä¢ Neurologic complications: spinal fractures, C1/2 subluxation, or cauda equina syndrome
Imaging studies
‚Ä¢ Radiographs of spine to assess progression of disease:
Sacroiliac joint disease with erosions and sclerosis
Calcification of spinal ligaments with bridging syndesmophytes (‚Äúbamboo spine‚Äù)
Squaring and generalized demineralization of vertebral bodies,‚Äúshiny corners‚Äù
‚Ä¢ MRI spine to assess inflammation in sacroiliac joint, esp. early in disease course
Treatment (Lancet 2007;369:1379; Curr Opin Rheumatol 2009;21:324)
‚Ä¢ Supportive: physical therapy, NSAIDs, steroid injection
‚Ä¢ Anti-TNF shown to improve symptoms and function (Ann Rheum Dis 2006;65:423)
‚Ä¢ MTX: somewhat effective for peripheral arthritis, but little or no effect on spinal sx;
SAS may provide benefit in Pts w/o peripheral arthritis (Ann Rheum Dis 2006;65:1147)
REACTIVE ARTHRITIS
Epidemiology
‚Ä¢ Ages 20‚Äì40 y; male:female ratio  5:1; more common in Caucasians
Pathogenesis
‚Ä¢ Immune-mediated aseptic synovitis in a genetically susceptible host post-GU or GI infxn
‚Ä¢ Bacteria associated with disease
GU: Chlamydia and Ureaplasma urealyticum
GI: Shigella, Salmonella, Yersinia, Campylobacter, C. difficile
Clinical presentation
‚Ä¢ Originally described as a triad of seronegative arthritis, nongonococcal
urethritis, and noninfectious conjunctivitis (Reiter‚Äôs syndrome)
‚Ä¢ Arthritis: 10‚Äì30 d post‚Äìinciting infection S mild constitutional sx, low back pain, asym-
metric, mono- or oligoarticular arthritis of primarily large joints (knees, ankles, feet),
enthesopathy, and sacroiliitis. Can develop sausage digits (dactylitis) of extremities.

--- Fim da P√°gina 211 ---

‚Ä¢ Urethritis/cervicitis: usually Chlamydia infection preceding arthritis, but also can see
sterile urethritis in postdysenteric reactive arthritis
‚Ä¢ Conjunctivitis: noninfectious, unilateral or bilateral and  uveitis, iritis, and keratitis
‚Ä¢ Cutaneous manifestations (may go unnoticed by Pt)
circinate balanitis: shallow, painless ulcers of glans penis and urethral meatus
keratoderma blenorrhagica: hyperkeratotic skin lesions on soles of feet, scrotum,
palms, trunk, scalp
stomatitis and superficial oral ulcers
‚Ä¢ GI: diarrhea and abdominal pain either w/ or w/o infectious agent
‚Ä¢ CV:AI from inflammation and scarring of aorta and valve; conduction defects
Radiographs
‚Ä¢ Early: soft tissue swelling and effusions around affected joints
‚Ä¢ Late: asymmetric proliferation of bone at site of inflammation
‚Ä¢ Asymmetric sacroiliitis in 70%
Diagnostic studies
‚Ä¢ PCR of urine or genital swab for Chlamydia, stool cultures, C. difficile toxin, etc., but 
studies do not rule out
Treatment and prognosis
‚Ä¢ NSAIDs, steroid injection for mono- or oligoarthritis, SAS if inflammation persists
‚Ä¢ Antibiotics if evidence of active or antecedent infection, as cx may be 
‚Ä¢ Arthritis may persist for months to years, and recurrences are common
PSORIATIC ARTHRITIS
Epidemiology
‚Ä¢ Seen in 20‚Äì30% of Pts w/ psoriasis (and not necessarily those with severe skin disease)
‚Ä¢ Arthritis may precede onset of skin disease, even by years; a/w nail changes
‚Ä¢ 20‚Äì40% of Pts with psoriatic arthritis have spinal or sacroiliac involvement
‚Ä¢  Men and women are affected equally and most Pts in 30s and 40s
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Several clinical patterns of arthritis:
monoarticular/oligoarticular (eg, large joint, DIP joint, dactylitic digit): most
common initial manifestation
polyarthritis (small joints of the hands and feet, wrists, ankles, knees, elbows):
indistinguishable from RA, but often asymmetric
arthritis mutilans: severe destructive arthritis with bone resorption, esp. hands
axial disease: similar to ankylosing spondylitis  peripheral arthritis
‚Ä¢ Enthesopathies, tendinitis
‚Ä¢ Fingernails: pitting, transverse depressions, onycholysis, subungal hyperkeratosis
‚Ä¢ Eye inflammation (30%): conjunctivitis, iritis, episcleritis, and keratoconjunctivitis sicca
‚Ä¢ Psoriatic skin lesions
Radiographs
‚Ä¢ ‚ÄúPencil-in-cup‚Äù deformity seen at DIP joints, erosive changes
‚Ä¢ Spinal involvement, sacroiliitis
Treatment
‚Ä¢ Symptom control: NSAIDs; intra-articular glucocorticoid injections
‚Ä¢ Anti-TNF (etanercept, infliximab, adalimumab) T progression of disease
‚Ä¢ Sulfasalazine: only DMARD shown to improve sx, but not progression of disease
‚Ä¢ Other: MTX, leflunomide, CsA,AZA, PUVA, antimalarials, gold
ENTEROPATHIC (IBD-ASSOCIATED)
Epidemiology
‚Ä¢ Seen in 20% of Pts w/ IBD; more frequently seen in Crohn‚Äôs than UC
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Peripheral, migratory, asymmetric, nondeforming oligoarthritis: abrupt
onset, large joints, course parallels GI disease
‚Ä¢ Spondylitis: associated more strongly with HLA-B27, course does not parallel GI disease
‚Ä¢ Sacroiliitis
‚Ä¢ Erythema nodosum, pyoderma gangrenosum ( neutrophilic dermatosis S painful ulcers w/
violaceous border; Ddx incl. idiopathic, IBD, RA, myelogenous leukemia), anterior uveitis
Treatment
‚Ä¢ 5-ASA compounds, etc., for underlying IBD (see IBD)

INFECTIOUS ARTHRITIS & BURSITIS
DIAGNOSIS AND EMPIRIC TREATMENT OF INFECTIOUS ARTHRITIS
Diagnosis
‚Ä¢ Arthrocentesis should be performed as soon as suspected
‚Ä¢ Take care not to tap through an infected area thus introducing infxn into joint space
‚Ä¢ Send fluid for cell count, gram stain, bacterial culture, crystals
WBC 50,000 with poly predominance suspicious for bacterial infection
(crystals do not rule out septic arthritis!)
Initial therapy
‚Ä¢ Prompt empiric antibiotics guided by gram stain
‚Ä¢ If gram stain negative, empiric Rx w/ cefazolin (eg, low risk of MRSA and gonorrhea)
or vancomycin (IVDU, MRSA risk factors); add cefepime if elderly, immunosupp.
‚Ä¢ Modify antibiotics based on culture results and clinical course

BACTERIAL (NONGONOCOCCAL) ARTHRITIS
Epidemiology and risk factors
‚Ä¢ Immunocompromised host (eg, diabetics, HIV, elderly, SLE)
‚Ä¢ Damaged joints: RA, OA, gout, trauma, prior surgery/prosthetic, prior arthrocentesis (rare)
‚Ä¢ Bacterial seeding
bacteremia secondary to IVDU, endocarditis, or skin infection
direct inoculation or spread from contiguous focus (eg, cellulitis, septic bursitis, osteo)
Clinical manifestations (Lancet 2010;375:846)
‚Ä¢ Acute onset of monoarticular arthritis (80%) with pain, swelling, and warmth
‚Ä¢ Location: knee (most common), hip, wrist, shoulder, ankle. In IVDA, tends to involve
other areas, eg, sacroiliac joint, symphysis pubis, sternoclavicular and manubrial joints
‚Ä¢ Constitutional symptoms: fevers, chills, sweats, malaise, myalgias, pain
‚Ä¢ Infection can track from initial site to form fistulae, abscesses, or osteomyelitis
‚Ä¢ Septic bursitis must be differentiated from septic intra-articular effusion
Additional diagnostic studies
‚Ä¢ Synovial fluid: WBC usually 50,000 (but can be as low as 1,000), 90% polys
gram stain  in 75% of Staph, 50% of GNR; culture  in 90% of cases
‚Ä¢ Leukocytosis with neutrophilic predominance  bandemia
‚Ä¢ Blood cultures  in 50% of cases
‚Ä¢ Conventional radiographs usually normal until after 2 wk of infection when bony
erosions, joint space narrowing, osteomyelitis, periostitis can be seen
‚Ä¢ CT and MRI useful especially for suspected hip infection or epidural abscess
Definitive treatment (for nonprosthetic joints)
‚Ä¢ Antibiotics (as earlier)
‚Ä¢ Surgical drainage/lavage indicated in many cases, especially for larger joints
‚Ä¢ Prognosis: 10‚Äì50% mortality depending on virulence of organism, time to Rx, host

DISSEMINATED GONOCOCCAL INFECTION (DGI)
Epidemiology
‚Ä¢ Most frequent type of infectious arthritis in sexually active young adults
‚Ä¢ Caused by Neisseria gonorrhea
‚Ä¢ Normal host as well as Pts w/ deficiencies of terminal components of complement
‚Ä¢ Female:male ratio  4:1. c incidence during menses, pregnancy, and postpartum period.
c incidence in homosexual males. Rare after age 40 y.
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Preceded by mucosal infection (eg, endocervix, urethra, or pharynx) that is often asx
‚Ä¢ Usually presents as two distinct syndromes:
Joint localized: purulent arthritis (40%) usually of knees, wrists, hands, or ankles
Bacteremia: triad of polyarthritis, tenosynovitis, skin lesions
prodrome: fever, malaise, migratory polyarthralgias (wrist, knees, ankles, elbows)
acute onset of tenosynovitis (60%) in wrists, fingers, ankles, toes
rash (50%): gunmetal gray pustules with erythematous base on extremities & trunk
‚Ä¢ Rare complications: Fitz-Hugh-Curtis syndrome (perihepatitis), pericarditis, meningitis,
myocarditis, osteomyelitis
Additional diagnostic studies
‚Ä¢ Leukocytosis with increased neutrophils; c ESR
‚Ä¢ Synovial fluid: WBC 50,000 (but can be 10,000), poly predominant
Gram stain  in 25% of cases
culture  in up to 50% of cases if culture anaerobically on Thayer-Martin media
PCR for gonococcal DNA can improve Se (not widely available or standardized)
‚Ä¢ Blood culture: more likely  in tenosynovitis; rarely in joint localized disease
‚Ä¢ Gram stain and culture of skin lesions occasionally 
‚Ä¢ Cervical, urethral, pharyngeal, rectal cultures on Thayer-Martin media indicated; check for
Chlamydia
Treatment
‚Ä¢ Ceftriaxone or cefotaxime  7 d w/ empiric doxycycline for possible concur-
rent Chlamydia (fluoroquinolones no longer recommended due to resistance)
‚Ä¢ Joint aspiration or arthroscopy/lavage may be required for Pts with purulent arthritis
OLECRANON AND PREPATELLAR BURSITIS
Epidemiology and risk factors (Infect Dis North Am 2005;19:991)
‚Ä¢ 150 bursae in the body; two most commonly infected are olecranon and prepatellar
‚Ä¢ Most commonly due to direct trauma, percutaneous inoculation, or contiguous
spread from adjacent infection (eg, cellulitis)
‚Ä¢ Other risk factors: recurrent noninfectious inflammation (eg, gout, RA, CPPD), diabetes
‚Ä¢ S. aureus (80%) most common, followed by streptococci
Diagnosis
‚Ä¢ Physical exam: discrete bursal swelling, erythema, maximal tenderness at center of
bursa with preserved joint range of motion
‚Ä¢ Aspirate bursa if concern for infxn, ‚úìcell count, gram stain, bacterial cx, crystals
WBC 20,000 with poly predominance suspicious for bacterial infection, but
lower counts common (crystals do not rule out septic bursitis!)
‚Ä¢ Assess for adjacent joint effusion, which can also be septic
‚Ä¢ Take care not to tap through infected skin thus introducing infxn into bursa
Initial therapy
‚Ä¢ Prompt empiric coverage for staphylococci and streptococci: cefazolin or oxacillin,
vancomycin if concern for MRSA, broaden spectrum based on other risk factors
‚Ä¢ PO antibiotics acceptable for mild presentation
‚Ä¢ Modify antibiotics based on gram stain, culture results, and clinical course
‚Ä¢ Duration of therapy is 1‚Äì4 wk
‚Ä¢ Serial aspirations every 1‚Äì3 d until sterile or no reaccumulation of fluid
‚Ä¢ Surgical intervention if unable to drain bursa through aspiration, evidence of foreign
body or necrosis, recurrent or refractory bursitis with concern for infection of
adjacent structures

SYSTEMIC SCLEROSIS AND SCLERODERMA DISORDERS
Definition and epidemiology
‚Ä¢ Scleroderma refers to the presence of tight, thickened skin
‚Ä¢ Localized scleroderma  morphea (plaques of fibrotic skin), linear (fibrotic bands),
‚Äúen coup de saber‚Äù (linear scleroderma on one side of scalp and forehead ‚âàsaber scar)
‚Ä¢ Systemic sclerosis (SSc)  scleroderma  internal organ involvement. Subgroups:
SSc w/ limited cutaneous disease (hands, arms, face): CREST syndrome, pulm HTN;
renal and cardiac manifestations rare
SSc w/ diffuse cutaneous disease (incl. proximal extremities & trunk): rapidly pro-
gressing disorder affecting skin, one or more internal organs
SSc sine scleroderma (visceral disease without skin involvement, rare)
‚Ä¢ Peak onset of SSc between ages 30‚Äì50; more common in women than men
‚Ä¢ 1‚Äì2/100,000 annual incidence of systemic disease in the U.S.
‚Ä¢ Pathogenesis: immune damage to endothelial cells and reactive O2 species produc-
tion S persistent oxidative stress S perivascular inflammation S fibroblast activa-
tion and fibrosis. Cytokines, growth factors, and autoantibodies (against PDGF
receptor, endothelial cells, and fibroblasts) all contribute (NEJM 2009;360:1989).
Classification criteria (1 major or 2 minor; 97% Se, 98% Sp; Arth Rheum 1980;23:581)
‚Ä¢ Major: skin findings extend proximal to MCP or MTP joints
‚Ä¢ Minor: sclerodactyly (skin findings limited to the fingers)
digital pitting scars from loss of substance on the finger pad
bibasilar pulmonary fibrosis
‚Ä¢ Other causes of thickened skin: diabetes (scleredema ‚â†scleroderma), hypothyroidism,
nephrogenic systemic fibrosis, eosinophilic fasciitis, amyloidosis, GVHD, drug or toxin
Diagnostic studies
‚Ä¢ Autoantibodies
 anti-Scl-70 (anti-topoisomerase 1): 40% of diffuse, 15% of limited
 anti-centromere: 60‚Äì80% of limited, 5% of diffuse
 ANA (90%),  RF (30%)
‚Ä¢ If renal involvement S c BUN and Cr, proteinuria
‚Ä¢ If pulmonary involvement S interstitial pattern on CXR/chest CT, restriction and/or
T DLCO on PFTs; PHT revealed by echocardiography
‚Ä¢ Skin bx not routine, but helpful to assess other possible causes for skin thickening

Clinical Manifestations of Systemic Sclerosis
Skin
Tightening and thickening of extremities, face, trunk (bx not req for dx)

Treatment (organ-based approach)
‚Ä¢ Pulmonary

‚Ä¢ Renal: monitor BP monthly, intervene early to avoid HTN crisis; dipstick for protein

offer only minimal to modest benefit for skin fibrosis.
INFLAMMATORY MYOPATHIES
Definition and epidemiology (Lancet 2003;362:971)
‚Ä¢ Polymyositis (PM): T cell-mediated muscle injury S skeletal muscle inflam. & weakness
‚Ä¢ Dermatomyositis (DM): immune complex deposition in blood vessels with complement
activation S skeletal muscle inflam. & weakness  skin manifestations 
‚Ä¢ Inclusion body myositis (IBM): T cell-mediated muscle injury, vacuole formation with
amyloid deposition S skeletal muscle inflam. & weakness
‚Ä¢ 10% of PM and 15% of DM associated with malignancy (NEJM 1992; 326:363)
‚Ä¢ PM/DM: onset typically 40s and 50s; more common in women than men
‚Ä¢ IBM: onset after age 50; men  women; often misdiagnosed as polymyositis
CTD
8-12

Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Muscle weakness: gradual, progressive, often painless, symmetric, and proximal;
typically difficulty climbing stairs, arising from chairs, brushing hair;  tenderness of
affected areas; asymmetry and distal weakness more common in IBM than PM/DM
‚Ä¢ Dermatologic
erythematous rash on sun-exposed skin: neck & shoulders (shawl sign), face, chest
heliotrope rash (purplish discoloration) over upper eyelids  periorbital edema
Gottron‚Äôs papules (pathognomonic): violaceous often scaly areas symmetrically over
dorsum of PIP and MCP joints, elbows, patellae, medial malleoli
subungal erythema, dilation and dropout of nailbed capillaries, cuticular telangiec-
tases,‚Äúmechanic‚Äôs hands‚Äù (skin cracks on digits)
dermatologic features without muscle disease  DM sine myositis
‚Ä¢ Polyarthralgias or polyarthritis
‚Ä¢ Vasculitis of skin, muscle, GI tract and eyes; Raynaud‚Äôs (30%, usu. DM and overlap CTD)
‚Ä¢ Visceral involvement
pulmonary: acute alveolitis, chronic ILD, weakness of respiratory muscles
cardiac (33%): myocarditis, pericarditis, arrhythmias; HF uncommon; c CK-MB & Tn
(J Rheumatol 2009;36:2711)
GI: dysphagia, aspiration
‚Ä¢ Ddx: drug-induced myopathy (statins, cocaine, steroids, colchicine); infxn (HIV, EBV,
CMV); metabolic (hypothyroid, hypo K, hypo Ca); neuromuscular disease (eg, myas-
thenia gravis); glycogen storage disease; mitochondrial myopathy; muscular dystrophy
Diagnostic studies
‚Ä¢ c CK, aldolase, SGOT, and LDH;  c ESR & CRP
‚Ä¢ Autoantibodies:  ANA (75%),  RF (33%)
 anti-Jo-1 (25%), a/w nonerosive polyarthritis, Raynaud‚Äôs, ILD, mechanic‚Äôs hands
 anti-Mi-2 (5-10%), more common with DM, may have better prognosis
 anti-SRP (signal recognition peptide), seen in PM, indicates more aggressive disease
‚Ä¢ EMG: c spontaneous activity, T amplitude, polyphasic potentials with contraction
‚Ä¢ Muscle biopsy: all with muscle fiber necrosis, degeneration & regeneration
PM: endomysial inflam. (CD8 T cells) surrounds non-necrotic fibers, c MHC class I
DM: perimysial, perivascular inflam. (B & CD4 T cells), complement in vessels
IBM: same as PM with eosinophilic inclusions and rimmed vacuoles (EM)
Treatment (PM and DM, no effective treatment for IBM)
‚Ä¢ High-dose steroids, add MTX or AZA if tapering fails at 2‚Äì3 mo
‚Ä¢ For resistant disease: IVIg (DM  PM), MMF, rituximab, CsA, tacrolimus,
cyclophosphamide (esp. if ILD or vasculitis) 
‚Ä¢ IVIg for life-threatening esophageal or respiratory muscle involvement
‚Ä¢
‚úì for occult malignancy; monitor respiratory muscle strength with spirometry

SJ√ñGREN‚ÄôS SYNDROME
Definition and epidemiology
‚Ä¢ Chronic dysfunction of exocrine glands due to lymphoplasmacytic infiltration
‚Ä¢ Can be primary or secondary (a/w RA, scleroderma, SLE, PM, hypothyroidism, HIV)
‚Ä¢ More prevalent in women than in men; typically presents between 40 and 60 y of age
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Dry eyes (keratoconjunctivitis sicca): T tear production; burning, scratchy sensation
‚Ä¢ Dry mouth (xerostomia): difficulty speaking/swallowing; dental caries; xerotrachea; thrush
‚Ä¢ Parotid gland enlargement or intermittent swelling (bilateral)

MIXED CONNECTIVE TISSUE DISEASE (MCTD)
Definition
‚Ä¢ MCTD pts have features of SLE, systemic sclerosis, and/or polymyositis, often
evolving a dominant phenotype of SLE or systemic sclerosis
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Raynaud‚Äôs phenomenon typical presenting symptom
‚Ä¢ Hand edema:‚Äúpuffy hands,‚Äù sclerodactyly, RA-like arthritis without erosions
‚Ä¢ Pulmonary involvement (85%) with pulmonary hypertension, fibrosis
‚Ä¢ GI dysmotility (70%)
‚Ä¢ Low risk for renal HTN crisis or glomerulonephritis; if either, reconsider diagnosis of MCTD

‚Ä¢ As per specific rheumatic diseases detailed above
RAYNAUD‚ÄôS PHENOMENON
Clinical manifestations (NEJM 2002;347:1001)
‚Ä¢ Episodic, reversible digital ischemia, in response to cold or stress, classically: blanching
(ischemia) S cyanosis (venule dilatation) S rubor (resolution with reactive
hyperemia); color change usually well demarcated; affects fingers, toes, ears, nose
‚Ä¢ Associated sx include cold, numbness, & paresthesias S throbbing & pain
Primary  Raynaud‚Äôs disease (50%; excluded all secondary causes)
‚Ä¢ Onset age 20‚Äì40 y, female:male  5:1
‚Ä¢ Clinical: mild, symmetric episodic attacks; no evidence of peripheral vascular disease, no
tissue injury, normal nailfold capillary examination,  ANA, normal ESR
Secondary  Raynaud‚Äôs phenomenon (50%)
‚Ä¢ Typically, Pts 35 y of age
‚Ä¢ Collagen vascular disease: SSc, SLE, RA, PM-DM, MCTD, Sj√∂gren‚Äôs (abnl nailfold exam)
exaggerated vascular reactivity ultimately leads to tissue ischemia & injury
‚Ä¢ Arterial disease: peripheral atherosclerosis, thromboangiitis obliterans (abnormal pulses)
‚Ä¢ Hematologic: cryoglobulinemia,Waldenstr√∂m‚Äôs, antiphospholipid syndrome
‚Ä¢ Trauma (vibration or repetitive motion injury) & drugs (ergot alkaloids)
Treatment
‚Ä¢ All: avoid cold, maintain warmth of digits & body; avoid cigarettes, drugs, and trauma
‚Ä¢ Mild to moderate: long-acting CCB, -blockers, topical nitrates, low-dose ASA
‚Ä¢ Moderate to severe: sildenafil, bosentan (esp. w/ PHT); consider digital sympathectomy 
‚Ä¢ Digit-threatening: IV prostaglandins, digital sympathectomy
‚Ä¢ Others:ARBs, fish oil (primary RP only; Am J Med 1989;86:158)

VASCULITIS
LARGE-VESSEL VASCULITIS
Takayasu‚Äôs arteritis (‚Äúpulseless disease‚Äù)
‚Ä¢ Systemic granulomatous vasculitis involving Ao and branches; most often subclavian
and innominate arteries (90%), as well as carotid, renal, pulmonary (~50%)
‚Ä¢ Most common in Asia and in young women of reproductive age
‚Ä¢ Clinical manifestations
Phase I: inflammatory period with fever, arthralgias, weight loss
Phase II: vessel pain and tenderness, ‚Üìand unequal pulses in extremities, bruits,
limb claudication, renovascular hypertension (50%), neurogenic syncope. Ao
aneurysm and AI may accompany aortic involvement.
Phase III: burnt out, fibrotic period
‚Ä¢ Dx studies: ‚ÜëESR (75%), CRP; arteriography ‚Üíocclusion, stenosis, irregularity and
aneurysms; carotid Doppler studies; MRI/MRA; pathology ‚Üífocal panarteritis,
cellular infiltrate with granulomas and giant cells. MRI useful for monitoring.
‚Ä¢ Classification criteria (3 of 6 is 90.5% Se & 97.8% Sp; Arth Rheum 1990;33:1129)
1. age 40 y at dis. onset
4. systolic BP difference 10 mmHg between arms
2. claudication of extremities
5. bruit over subclavian arteries or aorta
3. ‚Üìbrachial artery pulse     
6. arteriogram abnormality (Ao, primary branches,
or prox. large arteries in extremities)
‚Ä¢ Treatment: steroids, MTX, antiplatelet Rx, surgical/endovascular revasc (Circ 2008;69:70)
Giant cell arteritis (GCA) (NEJM 2003;349:160)
‚Ä¢ Vasculitis affecting cranial branches of aortic arch, especially temporal artery 
(thus also called temporal arteritis), but can cause aortitis as well
‚Ä¢
90% of Pts 60 y, rare 50 y; female:male ratio  2:1
‚Ä¢ Clinical manifestations (JAMA 2002;287:92)
constitutional sx: low-grade fevers, fatigue, weight loss, myalgias, anorexia
headache, tender temporal arteries and scalp and absent temporal artery pulsation
ophthalmic artery (20%) ‚Üíoptic neuritis, diplopia, amaurosis fugax, and blindness
facial arteries ‚Üíjaw claudication 
Raynaud‚Äôs phenomenon; intermittent claudication of extremities; thoracic Ao aneurysm
‚Ä¢ Dx studies: ‚ÜëESR (though in ~5% ESR <40 even before Rx); ‚ÜëCRP, anemia
(ESR related to fgbn & Ig in blood; Ddx for 100: malignancy esp. multiple myeloma,
lymphoma; GCA or other vasculitis; ESRD; endocarditis,TB, osteomyelitis)
temporal artery bx: 3‚Äì5 cm, bilat. ‚Üëyield (J Rheum 2009;36:794); look for vasculitis, granul.
if suspect Ao involvement: MRI/MRA or CT-PET to identify stenoses, aneurysms, inflam.
‚Ä¢ Classification criteria (3 of 5 is 93.5% Se & 91.2% Sp; Arth Rheum 1990;33:1122)
1. age 50 y
4. ‚ÜëESR 50 mm/h
2. new headache
5. biopsy ‚Üívasculitis & granulomas
3. temp artery tenderness or ‚Üìpulsation
‚Ä¢ Polymyalgia rheumatica (seen in 50% GCA Pts; 15% of Pts w/ PMR develop GCA)
no universal or validated diagnostic criteria exist; instead follow general guidelines:
age 50 y; ESR 40 mm/h (and/or elevated CRP)
bilateral aching and morning stiffness (30 min  1 mo), involving 2 of following 3
areas: neck or torso, shoulders or prox. arms, hips or prox. thighs; nighttime pain
exclude other causes of sx (eg, RA); CK should be normal
‚Ä¢ Treatment: steroids (if vision threatened do not await path results before starting Rx);
40‚Äì60 mg/d for GCA; 10‚Äì20 mg/d for PMR; follow clinical status and ESR  CRP
MEDIUM-VESSEL VASCULITIS
Polyarteritis nodosa (‚Äúclassic‚Äù PAN) ( JAMA 2002;288:1632)
‚Ä¢ Acute or chronic systemic necrotizing vasculitis, typically of renal and other visceral
arteries, without granuloma formation
‚Ä¢ More common in men; average age of onset ~50 y; strongly associated with HBV
‚Ä¢ Clinical manifestations (Cupps and Fauci. The Vasculitides. Philadelphia:WB Saunders, 1981)
constitutional sx: weight loss, fevers, fatigue
musculoskeletal (64%): myalgias, arthralgias, arthritis
renal involvement (60%) with active urinary sediment, hypertension, renal failure
nervous system (51%): peripheral neuropathies, mononeuritis multiplex, stroke
GI (44%): abd pain, GIB/infarction, cholecystitis; GU (25%): ovarian or testicular pain
cutaneous lesions (43%): livedo reticularis, purpura, nodules, Raynaud‚Äôs
cardiac (36%): coronary arteritis, cardiomyopathy, pericarditis
if lung involvement, suspect other vasculitis

‚Ä¢ Dx studies: ‚ÜëESR & CRP, ‚ÜëWBC, rare eosinophilia, HBsAg ( in ~30%),  ‚ÜìC,  ANCA

Differential diagnosis of ANCA
‚Ä¢ c-ANCA (anti-PR3): Wegener‚Äôs granulomatosis, Churg-Strauss, microscopic polyangiitis
‚Ä¢ p-ANCA (anti-MPO): microscopic polyangiitis, Churg-Strauss,Wegener‚Äôs

‚Ä¢ atypical ANCA patterns: drug-induced vasculitis, nonvasculitic rheumatic diseases,

Wegener‚Äôs granulomatosis
‚Ä¢ Necrotizing granulomatous inflammatory disease with systemic vasculitis, particularly
involving the upper and lower respiratory tract, and kidney
‚Ä¢ Can occur at any age, but ‚Üëincidence in young and middle-aged adults
‚Ä¢ Clinical manifestations
