lower: pleurisy, pulmonary infiltrate, nodules, hemorrhage, hemoptysis

ocular (50%): episcleritis, uveitis, & proptosis from orbital granulomas, corneal ulcer
neurologic: cranial and peripheral neuropathies, mononeuritis multiplex
hematologic: ‚Üëincidence DVT/PE (20) when disease active (Annals 2005;142:620)
‚Ä¢ Dx studies: 90%  ANCA (80‚Äì95% c-ANCA, remainder p-ANCA)
CXR or CT ‚Üínodules, infiltrates, cavities; sinus CT ‚Üísinusitis
‚ÜëBUN & Cr, proteinuria, hematuria; sediment w/ RBC casts, dysmorphic RBCs

‚Ä¢ Classification criteria (2 of 4 criteria is 88% Se & 92% Sp; Arth Rheum 1990;33:1101)
1. nasal or oral inflammation: oral ulcers, purulent or bloody nasal discharge
2. CXR showing nodules, fixed infiltrates, or cavities
3. microscopic hematuria or urinary red cell casts
4. granulomatous inflammation on biopsy
‚Ä¢ Treatment (NEJM 2003;349:36; Annals 2009;150:670)
Induction: cyclophosphamide PO (2 mg/kg/d  3‚Äì6 mo or pulse 15 mg/kg/d q2‚Äì3 wk)
& prednisone (1‚Äì2 mg/kg/d taper over 6‚Äì18 mo)
RPGN: consider adding plasma exchange to regimen (J Am Soc Nephrol 2007;18:2180)

Microscopic polyangiitis (MPA)
‚Ä¢ Necrotizing small-vessel vasculitis ‚Üíglomerulonephritis, pulmonary capillary

‚Ä¢ Clinical manifestations: similar to Wegener‚Äôs but renal  respiratory involvement;

Churg-Strauss syndrome
‚Ä¢ Eosinophil-rich granulomatous inflammation involving lung, peripheral nerves,
heart, kidneys, and skin
‚Ä¢ Rare condition that can present at any age, but typically 30‚Äì40 y; a/w HLA-DRB4
‚Ä¢ Clinical manifestations
asthma and allergic rhinitis (new asthma in an adult raises suspicion)
eosinophilic infiltrative disease or eosinophilic pneumonia
systemic small-vessel vasculitis with granulomas
neuropathy (incl. mononeuritis multiplex), glomerulonephritis

(Medicine 2009;88:236)
dermatologic: palpable purpura, petechiae, subcutaneous nodules
‚Ä¢ Dx studies: 50%  ANCA (c-ANCA or p-ANCA), eosinophilia (5‚Äì10 k/L, 80%),

1. asthma
4. migratory or transitory pulm. infiltrates
2. eosinophilia 10%
5. paranasal sinus abnormality
3. mono- or polyneuropathy
6. extravascular eosinophils on biopsy
‚Ä¢ Treatment: high-dose corticosteroids ( cyclophosphamide or other DMARDs if nec.)

Henoch-Sch√∂nlein purpura (HSP)
‚Ä¢  Systemic vasculitis characterized by palpable purpura, arthralgia, abd pain, hematuria
‚Ä¢ Epidemiology: male  female, children  adults, onset in winter  summer

2. age 	20 y at disease onset
3. bowel angina

Cutaneous leukocytoclastic angiitis
‚Ä¢ Heterogeneous group of clinical syndromes due to immune complex deposition
in capillaries, venules, and arterioles; includes hypersensitivity vasculitis
‚Ä¢ Overall the most common type of vasculitis
‚Ä¢ Etiologies
drugs: penicillin, aspirin, amphetamines, thiazides, chemicals, immunizations
infections: strep throat, bacterial endocarditis,TB, hepatitis, staphylococcal infections
tumor antigens
foreign proteins (serum sickness)
‚Ä¢ Clinical manifestations: abrupt onset of palpable purpura, cutaneous ulceration, and
transient arthralgias after exposure to the offending agent, variably accompanied by
fever, arthralgias, and other organ involvement; peripheral neuropathy
‚Ä¢ Dx studies: ‚ÜëESR, ‚Üìcomplement levels, eosinophilia; skin biopsy ‚Üí
leukocytoclastic vasculitis with neutrophils, nuclear fragments 2 to karyorrhexis,
Ig  complement deposition on direct immunofluorescence 
perivascular hemorrhage and fibrinoid deposits 
distinguished from HSP by absence of IgA deposition in skin, and from cryoglobulinemic
vasculitis by absence of cryoglobulins
‚Ä¢ Classification criteria (3 of 5 criteria is 71% Se & 84% Sp; Arth Rheum 1990;33:1108)
1. age 16 y
2. medication taken at disease onset
3. palpable purpura
4. maculopapular rash
5. biopsy showing granulocytes in a perivascular or extravascular location
‚Ä¢ Treatment: withdrawal of offending agent  rapid prednisone taper
Beh√ßet‚Äôs syndrome
‚Ä¢ Multisystem vasculitis that may involve small-, medium- and large-sized vessels,
characterized by recurrent oral and genital ulcers with variable manifestations
affecting the skin, eye, CNS, and musculoskeletal system
‚Ä¢ Associated with HLA B51, highest prevalence on the old Silk Road (Turkey) and
other Asian countries
‚Ä¢ Classification criteria (#1  2 others is 91% Se & 96% Sp; Lancet 1990;335:1078)
1. recurrent oral aphthous ulceration (at least 3 times in one year)
2. recurrent genital ulceration
3. eye lesions: uveitis (with hypopyon), scleritis, retinal vasculitis, optic neuritis
4. skin lesions: pustules, papules, folliculitis, erythema nodosum
5.  pathergy test (prick forearm with sterile needle ‚Üípustule)
‚Ä¢ Other clinical manifestations
arthritis: mild, symmetric, chronic and nondestructive, involving knees and ankles
neurologic: focal deficits, pleocytosis, inflammatory infiltrates w/o vasculitis
vascular: superficial or deep vein thrombosis (25%); arterial stenosis, occlusion, and
aneurysm can also occur
‚Ä¢ Evaluation: ulcer bx, cerebral angio (rarely necessary); slitlamp exam and funduscopy
‚Ä¢ Treatment (Rheumatology 2007;46:736; Ann Rheum Dis 2009;68:1528)
mucocutaneous
mild: colchicine, topical steroids, dapsone
severe: oral steroids,AZA, thalidomide (males), MTX, CsA, anti-TNF
arthritis: NSAIDs, colchicine, steroids,AZA, anti-TNF, IFN-2a
ocular: steroids, AZA, infliximab, IFN-2a, CsA, cyclophosphamide, chlorambucil
vascular: large artery (esp pulmonary), high-dose steroids  cyclophosphamide then
AZA maintenance; for venous thrombosis, control inflammation  anticoagulation
CNS
parenchymal: steroids, MTX,AZA, infliximab, adalimumab, cyclophosphamide,
chlorambucil 
dural sinus thrombosis: steroids and anticoagulation
AZA early helps prevent ocular disease and ulcerations, and improves prognosis

Etiologies
‚Ä¢ Infections (type II & III): viral (HCV, HBV, HAV, EBV, CMV, HIV), bacterial (endocarditis,

‚Ä¢  Hematologic diseases (type I): MM, NHL, HL, CLL, CML,TTP, myelodysplasia

Pathophysiology

Diagnostic studies

‚Ä¢ Must distinguish from cryofibrinogenemia  proteins that precipitate from plasma only
(eg, fibrin, fibrinogen). Separate disorder that can be seen in CTD, infection, malig-
nancy. Usually asx or may promote thrombosis.
‚Ä¢  rheumatoid factor (RF)
‚Ä¢ False elevations in WBC or plt count on automated CBC, due to cryoprecipitation
‚Ä¢ T C4 levels, variable C3 levels, c ESR
‚Ä¢ Must keep blood warmed to 37C at all times en route to lab; early cooling causes
false  cryoglobulin, loss of RF and TT complement
‚Ä¢ In HCV-associated type 2 cryoglobulinemia:  HCV RNA,  anti-HCV Ab
‚Ä¢ Biopsy of affected tissue (skin, kidney)
Treatment
‚Ä¢ Treat underlying disorder:
chemotherapy and/or radiation for lymphoproliferative disorders
antiviral therapy and/or rituximab for HCV (Arth Rheum 2009;60:2531)
DMARDs for rheumatic disease
‚Ä¢ NSAIDs for control of mild symptoms for Pts with normal renal function
‚Ä¢ Prednisone  other immunosuppressants (eg, cyclophosphamide) for major organ
involvement
‚Ä¢ Plasmapheresis in severe disease

CHANGE IN MENTAL STATUS
Definitions (nb, description of state better than imprecise use of terms)
‚Ä¢ Confusion (encephalopathy): unable to maintain coherent thought process
‚Ä¢ Delirium: waxing & waning confusional state w/ additional sympathetic signs
‚Ä¢ Drowsiness: T level of consciousness, but rapid arousal to verbal or noxious stimuli
‚Ä¢ Stupor: impaired arousal to noxious stimuli, but some preserved purposeful movements
‚Ä¢ Coma: sleeplike state of unresponsiveness, with no purposeful response to stimuli

Initial evaluation
‚Ä¢ History (typically from others): previous or recent illnesses, including underlying

‚Ä¢ General physical examination: evaluate for asterixis, signs of trauma, stigmata of

‚Ä¢ Neurologic examination (if possible, off sedatives/paralytics)

quick horizontal nystagmus away) imply brainstem intact

ŒîMS
9-2
Diagnostic studies
‚Ä¢ Head CT; radiographs to r/o C-spine fracture; CXR to r/o PNA (in elderly)
‚Ä¢ Laboratory: electrolytes, BUN, Cr, ABG, LFTs, CBC, PT, PTT, NH3, tox screen,TSH, U/A
‚Ä¢ Lumbar puncture to r/o meningitis
‚Ä¢ EEG to r/o nonconvulsive seizures
ANOXIC BRAIN INJURY
Prevalence
‚Ä¢ Pts w/ at least 5 min of cerebral hypoxia at risk
‚Ä¢ 1.5 million cardiac arrests per year in U.S.; 30% survive, but only 10‚Äì20% return to
independence
Initial evaluation
‚Ä¢ Neuro exam: focus on coma exam S cranial nerves, motor response to pain
‚Ä¢ Imaging: usually not informative w/in first day after arrest, but should be done prior
to initiating hypothermia if patient found down or witnessed to hit head
Coma Exam Checklist
Cranial nerves 
Pupillary response, extraocular movements, corneal reflex 
Motor 
Spontaneous limb movements, posturing 
Sensory
Response to painful stimuli
Reflexes
Deep tendon reflexes, Babinski
Induced hypothermia (NEJM 2002;346:549, 559)
‚Ä¢ Indications: comatose w/in 6 h following cardiac arrest (not isolated resp. arrest).
Fully studied only in VT/VF, but acceptable to perform after asystole or PEA arrest.
‚Ä¢ Contraindications: active bleeding, including cerebral; known sepsis; recent surgery or
trauma (relative); CV instability; clear improvement in neurologic exam (purposeful
movements, vocalizations)
‚Ä¢ Method: target temperature 32‚Äì34C  24 h (from time of initiation of cooling)
cold saline infusions; ice packs to the head, neck, and torso; cooling blankets
may use cooling vest or endovascular catheter if available
‚Ä¢ Complications
cardiac dysrhythmias (bradycardia most common): if significant dysrhythmia or
hemodynamic instability, d/c cooling and actively rewarm patient (this is only cir-
cumstance in which active rewarming should be performed; o/w rewarm no
faster than 0.5C per h)
coagulopathy: Pts can receive fibrinolytics, GP IIb/IIIa inhibitors, etc., and still
undergo cooling. ‚úìPT and PTT.
infection: ‚úìsurveillance blood cultures during cooling
hyperglycemia
hypokalemia during cooling, hyperkalemia w/ rewarming; keep K 4‚Äì5 mEq/L
Ongoing evaluation
‚Ä¢ Neuro exam: daily focus on coma exam, cranial nerves, GCS score. Pt needs to be
off sedation for adequate time to evaluate (depends on doses used, duration of
Rx, metabolic processes in the individual Pt).
‚Ä¢ Labs: daily CBC, PT/PTT, electrolytes. Serum neuron-specific enolase (NSE) on day 1‚Äì3
‚Ä¢ Imaging: noncontrast CT 24 h after arrest; if unrevealing, MRI around day 3‚Äì5
‚Ä¢ EEG: should be performed in any Pt w/ seizures or myoclonus (to r/o status epilepti-
cus); should be considered in all unresponsive Pts (to r/o nonconvulsive seizures)
‚Ä¢ Somatosensory evoked potentials (SSEP): helpful for prediction of poor outcome if
absent cortical responses bilaterally; should not be performed earlier than 48 h
after arrest (72 h if cooled)
Prognosis (Neuro 2006;67:203; NEJM 2009;361:605)
‚Ä¢ Uniformly poor prognosis can be predicted at 72 h only in Pts who have absent
pupillary and corneal reflexes, and no motor response to pain; also with absent
SSEPs at 48 h
‚Ä¢ Otherwise, requires multifactorial approach, considering neuro exam, age and
comorbid diseases, and ancillary data (serum NSE, neuroimaging, EEG, SSEP)
‚Ä¢ When in doubt, err on the side of giving more time (especially in younger Pts and
induced hypothermia Pts)

SEIZURES
9-3
SEIZURES
Definitions (NEJM 2003;349:1257)
‚Ä¢ Seizure  abnormal, paroxysmal, excessive discharge of CNS neurons; occurs in
5‚Äì10% of the population; clinical manifestations can range from dramatic to subtle
‚Ä¢ Epilepsy  recurrent seizures due to an underlying cause; 0.5‚Äì1.0% of population
‚Ä¢ Generalized seizures (involves brain diffusely)
Tonic-clonic (grand mal): tonic phase (10‚Äì20 sec) with contraction of muscles (caus-
ing expiratory moan, cyanosis, pooling of secretions, tongue biting) S clonic
phase (30 sec) with intermittent relaxing and tensing of muscles
Absence (petit mal): transient lapse of consciousness w/o loss of postural tone
Myoclonic (infantile spasms & juvenile myoclonic epilepsy): sudden, brief contraction
‚Ä¢ Partial or focal seizures (involves discrete areas, implies a focal, structural lesion)
Simple: without impairment of consciousness; may be motor, sensory, or autonomic
Complex: with impairment of consciousness  automatisms
Partial with secondary generalization: starts focal, becomes diffuse
Ddx
‚Ä¢ Syncope
Feature
Seizure
Syncope

Diaphoresis, nausea, tunnel vision

Skin pallor, clamminess
‚Ä¢ Nonepileptic seizure (NES, aka ‚Äúpsychogenic‚Äù): may see side-to-side head turning,
asymmetric large-amplitude limb movements, diffuse twitching w/o LOC, and crying
or talking during event
‚Ä¢ Other: metabolic disorders (eg, alcoholic blackouts, hypoglycemia); migraines; TIAs;
narcolepsy; nonepileptic myoclonus; tics; asterixis
Etiologies
‚Ä¢ Alcohol withdrawal, illicit drugs, meds (eg, -lactams, bupropion, tramadol, metro-
nidazole, meperidine, CsA, antidep., clozapine can lower seizure threshold)
‚Ä¢ Brain tumor or penetrating trauma
‚Ä¢ Cerebrovascular disease, including subdural hematomas, hypertensive encephalopathy
‚Ä¢ Degenerative disorders of the CNS (eg, Alzheimer‚Äôs)
‚Ä¢ Electrolyte (hyponatremia) & other metabolic (eg, uremia, liver failure, hypoglycemia)
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Aura (sec to mins): premonition consisting of abnormal smells/tastes, unusual behav-
ior, oral or appendicular automatisms
‚Ä¢ Ictal period (sec to mins): tonic and/or clonic movements of head, eyes, trunk, or extrem.
‚Ä¢ Postictal period (mins to h): slowly resolving period of confusion, disorientation,
and lethargy. May be accompanied by focal neurologic deficits (Todd‚Äôs paralysis).
‚Ä¢ Status epilepticus: continuous tonic-clonic seizure 30 min, or repeated seizures
such that there is no resolution of postictal periods. Complications include neu-
ronal death, rhabdomyolysis, and lactic acidosis.
‚Ä¢ Nonconvulsive status epilepticus alteration of awareness (ranging from confu-
sion to coma) w/o motor manifestations. Dx with EEG.
Clinical evaluation
‚Ä¢ Seizure: patient usually w/o recollection, must talk to witnesses
unusual behavior before seizure (ie, an aura)
type & pattern of abnl movements, incl. head turning & eye deviation (gaze prefer-
ence usually away from seizure focus)
loss of responsiveness
‚Ä¢ HPI: recent illnesses/fevers, head trauma, sleep deprivation, medication compliance
‚Ä¢ PMH: prior seizures or  FHx, prior meningitis/encephalitis, prior stroke or head trauma
‚Ä¢ Medications, alcohol, and illicit drug use
‚Ä¢ General physical exam should include the skin, looking for neuroectodermal disorders
(eg, neurofibromatosis, tuberous sclerosis) that are a/w seizures
‚Ä¢ Neurologic exam should look for focal abnormalities S underlying structural abnormality

(eg, spikes or sharp waves), but seen in up to 2% of normal population; sleep

Treatment (Lancet 2006;367:1087 & 2007;369:1000, 1016; NEJM 2008;359:166)
‚Ä¢ Treat any underlying causes, including CNS infections, intoxication, or withdrawal, etc.
‚Ä¢ Antiepileptic drug (AED) therapy is usually reserved for Pts w/ underlying structural

‚Ä¢ For Pts w/ infrequent seizures, early (vs. delayed) intervention w/ AED c time to seizure

‚Ä¢ AED choice dependent on type of seizure, side effects, cost, and drug interactions
‚Ä¢ Introduce gradually, monitor carefully
‚Ä¢ May consider withdrawal if seizure-free (typically for at least 1 y) and normal EEG
‚Ä¢ Individual state laws mandate seizure-free duration before being allowed to drive

ALCOHOL WITHDRAWAL
Pathophysiology
‚Ä¢ Alcohol is CNS depressant
‚Ä¢ Chronic use S insensitivity to inhibitory neurotransmitter -aminobutyric acid (GABA)
‚Ä¢ Abrupt alcohol cessation S CNS overactivity
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Minor withdrawal sx (6‚Äì48 h after last drink): mild anxiety, tremulousness, HA
‚Ä¢ Withdrawal seizures: typically w/in 48 h after last drink; if unRx‚Äôd, 1‚ÅÑ3 S delirium tremens
‚Ä¢ Alcoholic hallucinosis: isolated hallucinations (typically visual) 12‚Äì48 h after last drink
‚Ä¢ Delirium tremens (DT): disorientation, agitation, hallucinations, c HR & BP, fever,
diaphoresis; begins 48‚Äì96 h after last drink, lasts 5‚Äì7 d
‚Ä¢ Need to consider other dx: CNS infxn, CNS bleed, drug O/D, acute liver failure, GIB

STROKE
ISCHEMIC (70%)
Etiologies
‚Ä¢ Embolic (75%): artery S artery, cardioembolic, paradoxical (NEJM 2007;357:2262), cryptogenic
‚Ä¢ Thrombotic (25%): lacunar (arteriolar, seen in HTN & DM) or large vessel
‚Ä¢ Other: dissection, vasculitis, vasospasm, hyperviscosity, watershed
Clinical Manifestations
Embolic: rapid onset, sx maximum at onset
Thrombotic: progression of sx over hrs to days possibly with stuttering course
Artery
Deficits

Amaurosis fugax (transient monocular blindness)
ACA
Hemiplegia (leg  arm)
Confusion, abulia, urinary incontinence, primitive reflexes
MCA
Hemiplegia (arm & face  leg); hemianesthesia; homonymous hemianopia
Aphasia if dom. hemisphere: sup. div. S expressive; inf. S receptive
Apraxia and neglect if nondominant hemisphere
Drowsiness & stupor seen later (due to brain swelling)
PCA
Thalamic syndromes with contralateral hemisensory disturbance, aphasia
Macular-sparing homonymous hemianopia
Vertebral
Wallenberg syndrome  numbness of ipsilateral face and contralateral 
limbs, diplopia, dysarthria, ipsilateral Horner‚Äôs
Basilar
Pinpoint pupils, long tract signs (quadriplegia and sensory loss), cranial 
nerve abnormalities, cerebellar dysfunction
Cerebellar
Vertigo, nausea/vomiting, diplopia, nystagmus, ipsilateral limb ataxia
Lacunar
Pure hemiplegia, pure hemianesthesia, ataxic hemiparesis, or
dysarthria 
 clumsy hand
Transient ischemia attacks (TIAs) are sudden neurologic deficits caused by cerebral
ischemia w/o evidence of infarction on imaging; sx typically resolve w/in 24 h (usually w/in 1 h);
a harbinger of stroke.
Ddx: seizure, migraine, syncope, hypoglycemia, anxiety
Physical examination
‚Ä¢ General including rhythm, murmurs, carotid & subclavian bruits, signs of peripheral emboli
‚Ä¢ Neurologic including NIH stroke scale (NIHSS)
Diagnostic studies
‚Ä¢ Laboratory: electrolytes, Cr, glc, CBC, PT, PTT, LFTs, ESR, tox screen, BCx (if suspicion for
endocarditis); once stable, lipids, HbA1c,TSH, homocysteine, Lp(a), hypercoag w/u (if
65 y or in those w/ cryptogenic strokes; ideally drawn before anticoagulation initiated)
‚Ä¢ ECG
‚Ä¢ Urgent CT is usually the initial imaging study because of its rapidity and availability
first, noncontrast CT to r/o hemorrhage (Se for ischemic 	s is 20% w/in 12 h)
then, CT angio/perfusion to evaluate cerebrovascular patency and areas of reversible
ischemia (if intra-arterial/catheter-based interventions are being considered)
‚Ä¢ MRI offers superior imaging but may not identify acute hemorrhage (although data sug-
gest may be equivalent; JAMA 2004;292:1823) and may be falsely  for small brainstem
strokes w/in 1st 3 h; should be delayed if Pt is unstable or will delay therapy
‚Ä¢ Carotid Doppler U/S, transcranial Doppler (TCD)
‚Ä¢ Holter monitoring to assess for paroxysmal AF
‚Ä¢ Echocardiography w/ bubble study to r/o PFO or atrial septal aneurysm (confer 4
c risk of stroke; NEJM 2001;345:1740), cardiac thrombus, valvular vegetations
Treatment of TIA (NEJM 2002;347:1687)
‚Ä¢ Immediate evaluation and treatment as clinically indicated (Lancet 2007;370:1432)
‚Ä¢ Consider heparin IV S warfarin if: known or presumptive cardioembolic TIAs or if
bridging to mechanical intervention (CEA, stenting) for large vessel atherothrombotic dis.
‚Ä¢ Antiplatelet therapy with ASA, clopidogrel, or ASA 
 dipyridamole
‚Ä¢ Carotid revascularization if sx 70% ipsilateral stenosis (see later)

Treatment of ischemic stroke (NEJM 2008;359:1317; Lancet 2010;375:1695)
‚Ä¢ Thrombolysis (IV): 0.9 mg/kg (max 90 mg), w/ 10% as bolus over 1 min, rest over 1 h
consider if onset w/in 4.5 h, large deficit, ‚àÖhemorrhage, and ‚àÖcontraindication to lysis.
For Pts Rx‚Äôd w/in 3 h, 12% absolute c in excellent functional outcome, 5.8%
absolute c ICH, trend toward 4% absolute T mortality (NEJM 1995;333:1381)
‚Ä¢ Intra-arterial therapy with thrombolysis (JAMA 1999;282:2003) or catheter-based tech-
niques promising (66% rate of recanalization) but still experimental;
currently reserved for occlusion of a major vessel (ICA, MCA, basilar)
‚Ä¢ Anticoag w/ UFH of no proven benefit w/ c risk of hemorrhagic transformation 
consider infusion w/o bolus if Pt not thrombolysed and having progressive sx
long-term warfarin if embolic stroke; no role in nonembolic stroke (NEJM 2001;345:1444)
‚Ä¢ Antiplatelet therapy
ASA T death & recurrent stroke (Stroke 2000;31:1240) and is superior to warfarin
alone (NEJM 2005;352:1305)
dipyridamole  ASA superior to ASA alone (Lancet 2006;367:1665)
clopidogrel  ASA not more effective than ASA alone and c bleeding (Lancet 2004;364:331)
dipyridamole  ASA ‚âàclopidogrel alone in risk of recurrent stroke, and T bleeding
w/ clopidogrel, but study limited by preponderance of small-vessel subtype (NEJM
2008;359:1238)
‚Ä¢ BP should not be lowered acutely unless severe (SBP 200) or evidence of MI or CHF
if considering thrombolysis, then lower to 180/110 with nitrates or labetalol
‚Ä¢ DVT prophylaxis: enoxaparin more efficacious than UFH (Lancet 2007;369:1347)
‚Ä¢ Cerebral edema peaks at 3‚Äì4 d poststroke S c ICP requiring elevated head of bed
30; intubation & hyperventilation to PaCO2 30 (transient benefit);
osmotherapy with mannitol IV 1 gm/kg S 0.25 g/kg q6h;  hypertonic saline
surgical decompression
‚Ä¢ Statin S T in recurrent stroke & T MACE (Lancet 2002;360:7; NEJM 2006;355:549)
Carotid revascularization
‚Ä¢ Carotid endarterectomy (if institutional morbidity & mortality 6%) indicated for:
sx stenosis 70% (? 50‚Äì69% if male, age 75 y, or recent sx) S 65% T
stroke (NEJM 1991;325:445; Lancet 2004;363:915)
asx stenosis 70% & 75 y S 50% T stroke (Lancet 2004;363:1491)
‚Ä¢ Superiority and even noninferiority of carotid stenting remains controversial (NEJM
2004;351:1493; Lancet 2006;368:1239; NEJM 2006;355:1660 & 2008;358:1572; Lancet 2010;375:985)
Patent foramen ovale (PFO) (NEJM 2005;353:2361)
‚Ä¢ Present in 27% of population; may be a/w stroke, but yearly risk 0.1% in healthy pop.
‚Ä¢ Features a/w c risk of stroke: 4 mm separation, R S L shunting at rest, c septal mobility

HEMORRHAGIC (30%)
Etiologies
‚Ä¢ Intracerebral (90%): HTN (brainstem/cerebellum, basal ganglia), AVM, amyloid
angiopathy (lobar), anticoagulation/thrombolysis, venous thrombosis, tumors
‚Ä¢ Subarachnoid (SAH, 10%; Lancet 2007;369:306): ruptured aneurysm, trauma

‚Ä¢ Impairment in level of consciousness, vomiting  headache, may cause progressive focal

Diagnostic studies
‚Ä¢ CT or ? MRI (JAMA 2004;292:1823)
‚Ä¢ Angiography (CT or conventional) to determine the source of bleeding (aneurysm, AVM)
‚Ä¢ LP to ‚úìfor xanthochromia if no evidence of hemorrhage on CT and suspicious for SAH
Treatment
‚Ä¢ Reverse any coagulopathies
‚Ä¢ Platelets: keep 100k; unclear if transfusions necessary for patients on ASA
‚Ä¢ Recombinant activated factor VII is currently investigational, but may T hematoma

‚Ä¢ Strict BP control w/ goal SBP 140, unless risk for hypoperfusion b/c critical carotid sten.

‚Ä¢ Cerebral venous thrombosis: paradoxically, requires anticoagulation with IV heparin

Etiologies
‚Ä¢ Mononeuropathy (one nerve): entrapment, compression, trauma, DM, Lyme
‚Ä¢ Mononeuropathy multiplex (axonal loss of multiple, separate, noncontiguous nerves):

‚Ä¢ Polyneuropathy (multiple symmetric nerves, generally length dependent)

GUILLAIN-BARR√â Syndrome (GBS)
Definition and epidemiology
‚Ä¢ Acute inflammatory demyelinating polyneuropathy (AIDP)
‚Ä¢ Incidence 1‚Äì2 per 100,000; most common acute / subacute paralysis
‚Ä¢ Precipitants: viral illness (EBV, CMV, HSV, HIV), URI (Mycoplasma), gastroenteritis
(Campylobacter), Lyme, surgery, older immunizations
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Ascending paralysis over hours to days
‚Ä¢ Hypoactive then absent reflexes
‚Ä¢ Sensory dysesthesias and numbness often first symptoms, back pain also common
‚Ä¢ Respiratory failure requiring ventilatory assistance occurs in 30%;
autonomic instability and arrhythmias occur in 50%
‚Ä¢ Fisher variant: ophthalmoplegia, ataxia, areflexia; associated with anti-GQ1b antibodies
Diagnostic studies (results may be normal in first several days)
‚Ä¢ LP: albuminocytologic dissociation  c protein w/o pleocytosis (20 lymphs)
‚Ä¢ EMG & NCS: T nerve conduction velocity and conduction block
‚Ä¢ FVC & NIF: to assess for risk of respiratory failure (cannot rely on PaO2 or SaO2)
Treatment
‚Ä¢ Plasma exchange (Neuro 1985;35:1096) or IVIg (NEJM 1992;326:1123)
no additional benefit with both (Lancet 1997;349:225)
‚Ä¢ Supportive care with monitoring in ICU setting if rapid progression or resp. failure
‚Ä¢ Watch for autonomic dysfunction: labile BP, dysrhythmias (telemetry)
‚Ä¢ Most recover near baseline; axonal variant (5%) with incomplete recovery; 3‚Äì5% mortality

MYASTHENIA GRAVIS
Definition and epidemiology
‚Ä¢ Autoimmune disorder with Ab directed against acetylcholine receptor (AChR) in NMJ
‚Ä¢ Prevalence: 1 in 7,500; affects all ages, peak incidence 20s‚Äì30s (women), 60s‚Äì70s (men)
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Fluctuating weakness w/ fatigability (worse w/ repetitive use, relieved by rest)
‚Ä¢ Cranial muscles involved early S ocular (ptosis, diplopia) in 50%; bulbar (difficulty
chewing, dysarthria, dysphagia) in 15%. Often later progresses to generalized weakness.
‚Ä¢ Limb weakness proximal  distal; DTRs preserved; minimal / no atrophy
‚Ä¢ Exacerbations triggered by stressors such as URI, surgery, pregnancy or postpartum,
meds (eg, aminoglycosides, procainamide, phenytoin); prednisone can worsen acutely
‚Ä¢ Myasthenic crisis  exacerbation S need for respiratory assistance
‚Ä¢ Cholinergic crisis  weakness due to overtreatment with anticholinesterase medications;
may have excessive salivation, abdominal cramping, and diarrhea; rare at normal doses
Diagnostic studies
‚Ä¢ Bedside: ptosis after 30 seconds of sustained upgaze, improved with ice pack over eyes
‚Ä¢ Neostigmine test: temporary c strength; false  &  occur; premedicate w/ atropine
‚Ä¢ EMG: T response with repetitive nerve stimulation (vs. c response in Lambert-Eaton)
‚Ä¢ Anti-AChR Ab: Se 80%, 50% if ocular disease only; Sp 90%; muscle specific receptor
tyrosine kinase (MuSK) Ab account for most AchR Ab  cases
‚Ä¢ CT or MRI of thorax to evaluate thymus (65% hyperplasia, 10% thymoma)
Treatment
‚Ä¢ Anticholinesterase medications (eg, pyridostigmine)
‚Ä¢ Thymectomy if thymoma; may lead to improvement in up to 85% Pts w/o thymoma
‚Ä¢ Immunosuppression: prednisone  azathioprine, cyclophosphamide
‚Ä¢ Myasthenic crisis: treat precipitant
consider d/c anticholinesterase if suspect cholinergic crisis
aggressive immunosuppression with glucocorticoids (but watch for initial worsening)
IVIg, plasmapheresis
ICU if rapid or severe (follow FVC, NIF)
MYOPATHIES
Etiologies
‚Ä¢ Hereditary: Duchenne, Becker, limb-girdle, myotonic, metabolic, mitochondrial
‚Ä¢ Endocrine: hypothyroidism, hyperparathyroidism, Cushing syndrome
‚Ä¢ Toxic: statins, fibrates, glucocorticoids (critical illness myopathy), zidovudine, alcohol,
cocaine, antimalarials, colchicine, penicillamine
‚Ä¢ Infectious: HIV, HTLV-1, trichinosis, toxoplasmosis 
‚Ä¢ Inflammatory (see Rheumatology): polymyositis, dermatomyositis, inclusion body myositis
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Progressive or episodic weakness (not fatigue)
‚Ä¢ Weakness most often symmetric, proximal  distal (stairs, rising from sitting, etc.)
‚Ä¢  Myalgias (though not prominent or frequent)
‚Ä¢ May develop either pseudohypertrophy (dystrophies) or mild muscle atrophy
Diagnostic studies
‚Ä¢ CK, aldolase, LDH, electrolytes,ALT/AST, PTH,TSH, ESR, HIV
‚Ä¢ Autoantibodies (anti-Jo1, antisynthetase, anti-Mi-2, anti-SRP,ANA, RF)
‚Ä¢ EMG/NCS: low-amplitude, polyphasic units with early recruitment,  fibrillation
potentials
‚Ä¢ Muscle biopsy, molecular genetic testing (where indicated)

HEADACHE
9-10
HEADACHE
Primary headache syndromes
‚Ä¢ Tension: associated with muscle contraction in neck or lower head; treat with NSAIDs
‚Ä¢ Migraine: see later
‚Ä¢ Cluster: periodic, paroxysmal, brief, sharp, orbital headache that may awaken from sleep
 lacrimation, rhinorrhea, conjunctival injection, or unilateral Horner‚Äôs syndrome.
Acute treatments: oxygen, triptans; chronic prophylaxis: calcium-channel blocker.
Secondary causes of headaches
‚Ä¢ Vascular: stroke, intracerebral hemorrhage, SAH, subdural hematoma,AVM, unruptured
aneurysm, arterial hypertension, venous thrombosis
‚Ä¢ Infection: meningitis, encephalitis, abscess
‚Ä¢ Brain tumor
‚Ä¢ Pseudotumor cerebri (idiopathic intracranial hypertension)
‚Ä¢ CSF disorder: c (hydrocephalus) or T (s/p LP)
‚Ä¢ Trigeminal neuralgia
‚Ä¢ Extracranial: sinusitis,TMJ syndrome, temporal arteritis
‚Ä¢ Medication (analgesic) overuse
Clinical evaluation (JAMA 2006;296:1274)
‚Ä¢ History: quality, severity, location, duration, time of onset, precipitants/relieving factors
‚Ä¢ Associated symptoms (visual 	s, nausea, vomiting, photophobia)
‚Ä¢ Focal neurologic symptoms
‚Ä¢ Head or neck trauma, constitutional symptoms
‚Ä¢ Medications, substance abuse
‚Ä¢ General and neurologic examination
‚Ä¢ Warning signs that should prompt neuroimaging:
worst ever, worsening over days, wakes from sleep
vomiting, aggravated by exertion or Valsalva
age 50 y, fever, abnl neurologic exam, aura, cluster-type headache, unilateral
MIGRAINE
Epidemiology
‚Ä¢ Affects 15% of women and 6% of men; onset usually by 30 y
Clinical manifestations (Lancet 2004;363:381; JAMA 2006;296:1274)
‚Ä¢ Unilateral or bilateral, retro-orbital, throbbing or pulsatile headache; lasts 4‚Äì72 h
‚Ä¢ Often accompanied by nausea, vomiting, photophobia
‚Ä¢ ‚ÄúPOUNDing‚Äù: Pulsatile; duration 4‚Äì72 hOurs; Unilateral; Nausea & vomiting; Disabling
LR 3.5 if 3 criteria are met, LR 24 if 4 criteria are met
‚Ä¢ Classic (18%)  visual aura (scotomata with jagged or colored edge) precedes
headache
‚Ä¢ Common (64%)  headache without aura
‚Ä¢ Complicated  accompanied by stereotypical neurologic deficit that may last hrs
‚Ä¢ Precipitants: stress, hunger, foods (cheese, chocolate) and food additives (MSG),
fatigue, alcohol, menstruation, exercise
Treatment (NEJM 2002;346:257)
‚Ä¢ Eliminate precipitants
‚Ä¢ Prophylaxis:TCA, B, CCB, valproic acid, topiramate (JAMA 2004;291:965)
‚Ä¢ Abortive therapy
ASA, acetaminophen, caffeine, high-dose NSAIDs
metoclopramide IV, prochlorperazine IM or IV
5-HT1 agonists (‚Äútriptans‚Äù); contraindic. if complicated migraine, CAD, prior stroke
combo of triptan 
 NSAID more efficacious than either alone (JAMA 2007;297:1443)
ergotamine, dihydroergotamine; use with caution in Pts with CAD

SPINE
9-11
BACK AND SPINAL CORD DISEASE
Ddx of back pain
‚Ä¢ Musculoskeletal: musculoligamentous ‚Äústrain‚Äù (experienced by up to 80% of popula-
tion at some time), OA, RA, spondylolisthesis, vertebral compression fx, inflamma-
tory spondyloarthritis (ankylosing spondylitis, reactive, psoriatic)
‚Ä¢ Spinal cord (myelopathy) / nerve root (radiculopathy):
Degenerative/traumatic: disc herniation, spondylosis, fracture
Neoplastic: lung, breast, prostate, multiple myeloma, lymphoma
Infectious (also see ID section): osteomyelitis, epidural abscess, zoster, Lyme, CMV, HIV
‚Ä¢ Referred pain from visceral disease: (quality of pain can be important to distinguish)
GI: PUD, cholelithiasis, pancreatitis, pancreatic cancer
GU: pyelonephritis, nephrolithiasis, uterine or ovarian cancer, salpingitis
Vascular: aortic dissection, leaking aortic aneurysm
Initial evaluation
‚Ä¢ History: location, radiation, neurologic symptoms, infection, malignancy
‚Ä¢ General physical examination: local tenderness, ROM, signs of infection or malignancy,
signs of radiculopathy (experienced as sharp/lancinating pain radiating into limb):
Spurling sign (radicular pain w/ downward force to extended & ipsilaterally rotated head)
straight leg raise (radicular pain at 30‚Äì70): ipsilateral: 95% Se, 40% Sp; crossed 
(contralateral leg raised): 25% Se, 90% Sp
‚Ä¢ Neurologic examination: full motor (including sphincter tone), sensory (including
perineal region), and reflexes including anal (S4) and cremasteric (L2)
‚Ä¢ Laboratory (depending on suspicion): CBC, ESR, Ca, PO4, A, CSF
‚Ä¢ Neuroimaging: low yield if nonradiating pain, high false  rate (incidental spondylosis)
depending on suspicion: x-rays, CT or CT myelography, MRI, bone scan
‚Ä¢ EMG/NCS may be useful to distinguish root/plexopathies from peripheral neuropathies
SPINAL CORD COMPRESSION
Clinical manifestations
‚Ä¢ Acute: flaccid paraparesis and absent reflexes (‚Äúspinal shock‚Äù)
‚Ä¢ Subacute-chronic: spastic paraparesis and hyperactive reflexes
‚Ä¢ Posterior column dysfunction in legs (loss of vibratory sense or proprioception)
‚Ä¢ Sensory loss below level of lesion
‚Ä¢ Bilateral prominent Babinski responses  ankle clonus
Evaluation and treatment
‚Ä¢ Empiric spine immobilization (collar, board) for all trauma patients
‚Ä¢ STAT MRI (at and above clinical spinal level, pre- and postgadolinium) or CT myelogram
‚Ä¢  Emergent neurosurgical and/or neurology consultation
‚Ä¢ Urgent radiation therapy  surgery for compression if due to metastatic disease
‚Ä¢ High-dose steroids depending on cause:
Tumor: dexamethasone 10‚Äì100 mg IV  1 then 4‚Äì24 mg every 6 hr  
Trauma: controversial (may have slight benefit but c risk of infection, poor healing)
? methylprednisolone 30 mg/kg IV over 15 min then 45 min later: 5.4 mg/kg/h  23 h

‚Ä¢ Radicular pain aggravated by activity (esp. bending, straining, coughing), relieved by lying

Shape
anisocytosis S unequal RBC size; poikilocytosis S irregular RBC shape
acanthocytes  spur cells (irregular sharp projections) S liver disease 
bite cells (removal of Heinz bodies by phagocytes) S G6PD deficiency
echinocytes  burr cells (even, regular projections) S uremia, artifact 
pencil cell S long, thin, hypochromic - very common in adv. iron deficiency
rouleaux S hyperglobulinemia (eg, multiple myeloma)
schistocytes, helmet cells S MAHA (eg, DIC,TTP/HUS), mechanical valve
spherocytes S HS,AIHA; sickle cells S sickle cell anemia
stomatocyte S central pallor appears as curved slit S liver disease, EtOH
target cells S liver disease, hemoglobinopathies, splenectomy
tear drop cells  dacryocytes S myelofibrosis, myelophthisic anemia,
megaloblastic anemia, thalassemia

FORMULAE
10-11
Warfarin-heparin overlap therapy
‚Ä¢ Indications: when failure to anticoagulate carries c risk of morbidity or mortality
(eg, DVT/PE, intracardiac thrombus)
‚Ä¢ Rationale: (1) Half-life of factor VII (3‚Äì6 h) is shorter than half-life of factor II (60‚Äì72 h);
‚à¥warfarin can elevate PT before achieving a true antithrombotic state
(2) Protein C also has half-life less than that of factor II;
‚à¥theoretical concern of hypercoagulable state before antithrombotic state
‚Ä¢ Method: (1) Therapeutic PTT is achieved using heparin
(2) Warfarin therapy is initiated
(3) Heparin continued until INR therapeutic for 2 d and 4‚Äì5 d of warfarin
(roughly corresponds to 2 half-lives of factor II or a reduction to 25%)

1 Normal PA CXR. The convex right cardiac border is formed by the right atrium
(straight arrows), and the curved arrows indicated the location of the superior vena cava.
The left cardiac and great vessels border what might be considered as four skiing moguls.
From cephalad to caudad, the moguls are the aortic arch, the main and left pulmonary arter-
ies, the left atrial appendage, and the left ventricle. (Radiology 101, 3rd ed, 2009.)
